============================================================
Factory 001 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Contracts & Human Resources (Audit Code: CHR-08)**
During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's transition in ownership, finalized in August 2023, has resulted in a systemic lapse in the execution of legally compliant employment contracts. Inspector observations confirmed that approximately 60% of the workforce (estimated at 240 workers based on current headcount) are working under expired contracts issued by the previous ownership entity, with no new contracts presented for signature despite the change in operational control. Follow-up interviews with a randomized selection of 17 production line workers on the audit date corroborated this finding, with multiple workers stating they had "received no new paperwork" and were unsure of their current terms of employment, although wage payments have continued. This constitutes a failure to meet the fundamental requirement for a documented and mutually agreed employment relationship, exposing both workers and the new management to significant legal and operational risk. **Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FO-2023-014**
**Compliance Area: C.1 Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

Following the recent change in ownership and ongoing management transition noted in the factory profile, a review of documentation and worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, revealed systemic deficiencies in the implementation of policies guaranteeing freedom of association. During interviews with 32 workers selected from various production departments, 19 individuals (approximately 59%) reported being unaware of any functioning worker representative structure or collective bargaining agreement, and expressed uncertainty regarding their right to organize. This was corroborated by management's inability to produce any minutes from worker-management meetings for the past eight months, nor any valid, democratically elected worker representative committee documentation post-ownership transfer. Furthermore, a posted "Code of Conduct" in the main cafeteria, dated August 2023, was found to omit all clauses pertaining to freedom of association and collective bargaining, which were present in the version provided to the audit team by headquarters. This combination of absent procedural documentation, lack of worker awareness, and incomplete policy communication constitutes a failure to effectively communicate and uphold these fundamental rights within the operational environment.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Required: Corrective Action Plan (CAP) must address policy dissemination, establishment of a representative election procedure, and management training.**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Finding Reference: EP-004-23**
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness
**Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

This finding relates to the factory's emergency response planning and training protocols, which were reviewed following the recent change in ownership and management transition noted in the factory profile. Inspector observation on the date of assessment confirmed that while evacuation route maps are posted, the emergency assembly point designated in the documented "Site Emergency Plan" (Revision 2, dated August 2023) is the previously used yard, an area that is now partially obstructed by new construction material storage, reducing its effective capacity. Interviews with a cross-section of 14 production workers from various floors conducted on October 26 revealed that only 3 were aware of any change in assembly procedures, and the most recent full facility drill was logged over 11 months ago, pre-dating the ownership change. This constitutes a failure to update and communicate essential life safety information and to conduct required periodic drills, thereby exposing personnel to unnecessary risk during an emergency event. **Severity: Moderate** - A follow-up review is required within 90 days to verify the designation of a suitable assembly area and the execution of a documented drill.

[Harassment & Abuse]
During the scope of the audit conducted on 12-15 October 2023, with particular attention to the workplace environment following the recent change in ownership and managerial transition noted in the factory profile, the audit team identified systemic gaps in the implementation of policies and procedures designed to prevent harassment and abuse. Investigator observations, supported by confidential interviews with 27 production line operators, revealed a recurring theme wherein three recently promoted floor supervisors (identified in internal records as FS-08, FS-11, and FS-14) routinely used derogatory language and intimidating behavior, including public shaming for productivity shortfalls, with multiple workers providing consistent, first-hand accounts of such incidents occurring as recently as the week prior to the audit. While a written policy against harassment exists, it was found not to have been communicated to the workforce in the local language post-transition, and no functional, confidential grievance mechanism was operational, leaving workers with no trusted avenue to report abuses without fear of retaliation. This constitutes a failure to ensure a workplace free from harassment and psychological abuse as per requirement SA8000:2014 Section 5.2 and the client’s Code of Conduct, clause 7.3, necessitating immediate corrective action including manager re-training, policy re-communication, and establishment of a protected reporting channel. Severity: Moderate.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
**Finding Reference: WB-2023-001-04**

During the review of wage records for the period covering March 1, 2023, to May 31, 2023, and corroborated through confidential worker interviews conducted on June 15, 2023, it was found that the factory has not established a functional mechanism for the calculation and payment of legally mandated overtime premiums. Specifically, while base wages for the standard 40-hour workweek were found to be compliant, the investigation, which included a review of 22 randomly selected payroll files and interviews with 17 production line workers, revealed that overtime hours worked beyond the legal weekly threshold were compensated at the standard hourly rate, in direct violation of Section 12(b) of the national labor law requiring a premium of not less than 1.5 times the regular rate. This practice was informally acknowledged by the interim payroll supervisor, who attributed the discrepancy to "ongoing reconciliation issues with the new payroll software" implemented post-ownership transition. The finding is systemic, affecting an estimated 95% of the production workforce, and represents a significant financial detriment to workers. **Severity: Critical** A follow-up review of the corrected payroll system implementation is required within 30 days.


============================================================
Factory 002 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding AEP-002-2023-17 (Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness)**
During the follow-up inspection conducted on 23 October 2023, the audit team noted significant improvements to the fire safety systems in Building A, including the completion of the mandated second emergency staircase. However, observations and worker interviews revealed a persistent deficiency in the site-wide emergency preparedness protocol. While documented evacuation drills for the permanent day-shift workforce are conducted semi-annually, the procedure fails to incorporate the contractual night-shift cleaning crew of 34 personnel, as confirmed in separate interviews with three members of that crew on 24 October 2023. This omission constitutes a failure to ensure all personnel are trained and accounted for in emergency scenarios, as per section 7.5 of the client's Emergency Preparedness Standard. A corrective action plan must address the inclusion of all shift patterns and temporary staff in drill schedules. Severity: Moderate

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FA-002-2023-08-FOA.001**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**
**Date of Assessment:** October 26-27, 2023

The audit team acknowledges the factory's established profile of an active union and generally positive industrial relations. During document review, the collective bargaining agreement (CBA) dated January 15, 2023, was presented and found to be comprehensive. However, during confidential interviews conducted on October 27 with 14 workers selected from various production lines, including 8 union members, a recurring concern was identified regarding the implementation of Article 6.2 of the CBA, which stipulates that union members serving on the workers' welfare committee shall be granted a minimum of four paid hours per month for committee duties. Worker testimony and cross-referenced timecard records for the months of August and September 2023 indicate that these hours were not consistently allocated or were deducted from the production bonuses of three identified committee members, constituting a failure to fully implement the bargained agreement. Management confirmed awareness of the discrepancy, attributing it to an administrative oversight in payroll processing, and has committed to rectification and back-pay.
**Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-002-2023-08-HA-01
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (CC Code: HSA.01)
**Background & Context:** The audit scope for this area, conducted on 23-25 October 2023, included a review of the factory's Harassment Prevention Policy (Revision 3, dated Jan 2023), documented procedures, training records, and confidential worker interviews. The factory profile indicates a mature Industrial Relations environment with an active union and a jointly managed Worker Welfare Committee.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** The policy is comprehensive and prominently displayed. Training records show 100% of line workers (423 employees) completed mandatory harassment prevention training in Q1 2023. During confidential interviews with 38 workers across departments and shifts, 6 workers independently and unprompted referenced a specific incident in August 2023 where a line supervisor was alleged to have used verbally abusive language. The factory's established grievance mechanism was utilized; the matter was reported via the union representative, investigated jointly by management and the Worker Welfare Committee within 72 hours, and resolved with documented disciplinary action against the supervisor, including mandatory re-training and a final written warning. All interviewed workers expressed confidence in the reporting channels and noted a respectful overall environment.

**Analysis & Conclusion:** The documented evidence confirms the factory's procedural controls are effectively implemented. The isolated incident was handled promptly, transparently, and through the agreed-upon formal channels, demonstrating the robustness of the grievance mechanism and good worker-management relations. No systemic failure or policy gap was identified.
**Status: Compliant.** A follow-up note is appended to verify the completion of the supervisor's re-training by 30 November 2023.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area: 1.4 Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: WH-O-002-2024-07)**

During the review of payroll records for the period April 1 - June 30, 2024, and subsequent worker interviews, it was noted that while the standard workweek is correctly maintained at 48 hours, the payment for overtime hours worked beyond this threshold is not fully compliant. Inspector observations of timecards and corresponding wage slips for 17 sampled workers from the sewing and finishing departments revealed a consistent pattern wherein overtime premiums were calculated at a rate of 1.5 times the regular hourly rate, as confirmed in interviews with eight (8) workers on July 15, 2024. However, national legislation mandates that the first two hours of overtime per day be paid at 1.25 times the regular rate, with only hours beyond that qualifying for the 1.5 times rate. This administrative error in payroll calculation has resulted in underpayment for the initial two hours of daily overtime for affected employees, constituting a violation of wage regulation B7.2. The factory's otherwise strong grievance mechanism had not identified this systemic payroll error, though management has been cooperative in the review. A follow-up note is required to determine the scope of the financial impact and the timeline for remediation and back-pay. Severity: Moderate.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-002-2023-F14 | Compliance Area: C.6 Discrimination & Harassment | Audit Date: October 26-27, 2023**

During the review of human resources policies and procedures, and subsequent confidential worker interviews, the audit team identified an issue requiring corrective action. While the factory has a strong, active union and a demonstrably effective grievance mechanism for most labor issues, the investigation revealed a procedural inconsistency in hiring practices. Specifically, the review of recruitment records for the period of July-September 2023 and interviews with 8 workers from the cutting department indicated that three female candidates of a particular ethnic minority group were not advanced to the skills assessment stage for sewing operator positions, despite having comparable application credentials to candidates from other demographics who were advanced. Management clarified this was an oversight by a previous hiring officer, who is no longer employed, and there was no evidence of systemic or overt discrimination within the existing workforce. The factory is directed to revise its hiring checklist to include a documented, criteria-based review of all applications prior to shortlisting and to provide refresher training on non-discrimination to all HR personnel by Q1 2024. **Severity: Minor**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-002-2023-08**

**Background & Methodology:** During the scheduled unannounced audit conducted on August 22-24, 2023, the audit team, in line with Protocol FL-01.4, specifically examined policies and practices related to forced labor, including but not limited to freedom of movement, retention of identity documents, and the existence of any form of bonded labor. The review encompassed a document check of HR policies for 145 production floor operators hired within the last 12 months, physical inspection of dormitory facilities and factory gates, and confidential interviews conducted via a randomized selection process with 28 workers across all production departments.

**Observations & Interview Notes:** The audit team confirmed through payroll cross-referencing and worker testimonials that all personnel are in possession of their own government-issued identity documents, which are neither withheld by management nor required as a condition of employment. Furthermore, the established and active worker union, verified through meeting minutes and interviews with five union representatives, provides a robust additional layer of oversight. Interviews conducted on August 23rd consistently reflected an understanding of the grievance mechanism, with no worker reporting coercion or a sense of indebtedness. Review of timecards and wage slips for a sample of 30 workers showed no evidence of unexplained deductions or the retention of wages as a disciplinary measure.

**Analysis & Follow-up:** While the initial document review raised no flags, the on-site verification of worker testimonies against payroll records and the demonstrable functionality of the grievance channel are considered conclusive. The factory's cooperative stance and transparent record-keeping further support this assessment. A follow-up note is made to commend the union liaison for their role in the documented worker orientation sessions on rights and remedies, which directly supports this compliance area.

**Status: Compliant**


============================================================
Factory 003 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: OSH-2024-003-07**  
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health**  
**Date of Inspection: May 22, 2024**  

During the site walkthrough conducted on the aforementioned date, it was observed that the factory's transition in ownership and management has resulted in a lapse of systematic oversight regarding machine guarding protocols. Specifically, in the cutting and sewing sections, seven out of twelve inspected industrial sewing machines were found to be operating without functional needle guards, and the guarding on two fabric cutting machines had been removed to facilitate faster material feeding, as verbally confirmed by two line supervisors. Subsequent interviews with a cross-section of 14 production workers on May 23 revealed a pattern of concerns, with several workers noting the absence of recent safety training and expressing unfamiliarity with lock-out/tag-out procedures for machine maintenance, a deficiency corroborated by a review of training records which showed no safety drills conducted since the ownership change in Q1 2024. This constitutes a failure to uphold section 7.3 of the applicable code of conduct and presents a direct and serious risk of laceration or amputation injuries. **Severity: Critical**  

**Follow-up Note:** The corrective action plan must include immediate reinstatement of all missing guards, mandatory safety training for all machine operators, and the establishment of a documented machinery safety checkpoint system under the new management structure.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB.01-09)**
**Finding Reference:** WB-003-24-008

Following the recent change in ownership and during the ongoing management transition, a review of payroll records for the period of March 1-15, 2024, alongside confidential worker interviews conducted on April 10, 2024, revealed systemic discrepancies in overtime compensation. Inspector observations of time cards and production data for 14 sampled workers from the cutting and sewing departments indicated consistent overtime work exceeding the legal monthly limit. However, interviews with 8 of these workers confirmed that all overtime hours were compensated at the standard regular hourly rate, in direct violation of statutory requirements for premium pay. This practice, affecting an estimated 85% of the production workforce, represents a failure to correctly calculate and pay for overtime work and constitutes a wage underpayment. **Severity: Critical.** A follow-up note has been issued to management requiring immediate rectification and back-pay calculation for the previous three months.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-003-2024-07-B**

During the review of documentation and worker interviews conducted on-site between October 22-24, 2024, the audit team identified significant irregularities in the area of employment contracts within the context of the factory’s recent ownership transition. Background checks confirmed the change in legal entity occurred approximately three (3) months prior to the audit. The inspection revealed that for an estimated 45% of the workforce (approximately 135 of 300 current employees), no valid, signed employment contract under the new legal entity name was present in personnel files; only outdated contracts from the previous ownership were on record. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 12 randomly selected workers from the sewing and finishing departments, 8 of whom stated they had not been issued a new contract or received any written documentation outlining their continued terms of employment since the takeover, expressing uncertainty regarding their current leave entitlements and grievance procedures. The absence of legally binding contracts for a substantial portion of the workforce constitutes a failure to establish clear terms of employment and exposes workers to potential rights violations. A follow-up note has been issued to management requesting immediate rectification and a full review of all personnel records.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 004 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FO-004-2023-12**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (CBA - 02.01)**
**Background & Context:** The audit of Factory 004, conducted on 12-14 October 2023, included a review of policies and procedures related to worker representation, alongside confidential interviews with 28 workers selected from various production departments. The factory operates in a rural jurisdiction with a predominantly migrant workforce, which typically presents heightened risks regarding awareness and exercise of associational rights.

**Observations & Interviews:** While a policy document referencing freedom of association was present in the HR office, no evidence of an active worker committee, union, or collective bargaining agreement was found. During worker interviews, 22 of the 28 workers stated they were unaware of any mechanism to form a representative body. Three workers separately indicated in their notes a belief that management would view such activities unfavorably, with one worker (Interview #17) paraphrasing a line supervisor's comment from June 2023 that "forming groups causes trouble and slowdowns." Furthermore, the available grievance procedure (Doc. GRV-004-A) channels all complaints solely through hierarchical management lines, with no independent worker representation in the process.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** The factory has failed to establish, communicate, or facilitate the practical means for workers to freely associate and bargain collectively, creating an environment that implicitly discourages the exercise of these rights. This constitutes a systemic failure in providing a fundamental worker right. A corrective action plan must be developed to include formal, management-supported communications on these rights, training for all levels of management, and the establishment of a democratically elected worker representative body without interference.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
During the announced audit conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the area of Harassment & Abuse (Ref. SA8000: Sec. 8.2 / Audit Protocol HRA-04) was reviewed through document examination, management interviews, and confidential worker consultations. The review of the factory's disciplinary policies and grievance procedures (Doc. HR-007, Rev. 2) found them to be formally compliant; however, interviews with 14 migrant line workers, conducted through an interpreter on 24 October, revealed a consistent pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by two identified line supervisors in the cutting and sewing departments. Multiple workers described being frequently subjected to public shouting, demeaning comments regarding their work speed, and threats of dismissal or punitive overtime assignments for minor errors, with one worker providing a specific account from the week prior (approx. 17 October). Despite the existence of a grievance box, workers expressed a unanimous and credible fear of retaliation due to their migratory status and the remote location of the factory, which limits alternative employment and access to independent legal counsel. Management was unable to provide any records of investigated complaints or supervisor training on appropriate disciplinary techniques for the past 12 months, indicating a systemic failure in implementation and remedy. Severity: Moderate.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Point Reference:** OSH-2023-004-07 **Date of Inspection:** 17-18 October 2023
**Background & Context:** The inspection of the cutting and sewing sections, alongside the boiler room and chemical storage area, revealed systemic shortcomings in the factory's safety management systems. This is of particular concern given the high turnover and predominantly migrant workforce, who may lack comprehensive safety training or be reluctant to report hazards due to their vulnerable employment status.
**Observations & Interviews:** The audit team observed unguarded drive belts on seven industrial sewing machines in Section B, and the emergency exit in the west wing was found partially obstructed by excess WIP storage. During confidential interviews with 12 production workers, 9 confirmed they had never received formal training on lockout-tagout procedures or chemical handling, despite the presence of aerosol adhesives and cleaning solvents in the warehouse. Furthermore, the first-aid kit inspected on the mezzanine level (Kit #3) was found to be missing several critical components, including antiseptic wipes and burn dressings, and its log showed it had not been restocked since June 2023.
**Follow-up Note:** Management cited geographic isolation and difficulties in sourcing specialized safety equipment as contributing factors; however, these do not constitute a valid exemption from providing a safe workplace as per local law and the client's code of conduct.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-004-2023-08**
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1, B.1.2)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26-27, 2023

A review of payroll records for the period September 1-30, 2023, combined with cross-referenced timecard data and separate, confidential interviews with 14 production line workers, reveals systemic non-compliance with mandated working hour limits. It was observed that during the peak production week for order #20487 (September 18-24), workers routinely clocked 13-hour shifts across a seven-day workweek, resulting in a confirmed 91-hour work week, far exceeding the legal maximum of 60 hours. Worker interviews substantiated this, with multiple interviewees stating that refusal of overtime was implicitly linked to threats of contract non-renewal, a significant concern given the predominantly migrant workforce with limited alternative employment or access to legal recourse in this rural location. While management cited order deadlines as a contributing factor, the documented pattern indicates this is a regular operational practice rather than an exceptional occurrence, demonstrating a failure of managerial due diligence to implement adequate production planning and staffing to comply with statutory limits.
**Severity: Critical**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: CL-2023-004-02**
**Compliance Area:** Prohibition of Child Labor
**Background & Context:** During the routine document review conducted on 23 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's hiring records, while generally complete, showed several instances of missing copies of primary age verification documents for workers hired during the peak production period of July-August 2023. This procedural gap, coupled with the factory's location in a rural zone with a known reliance on migrant labor, prompted a targeted physical inspection of workstations and follow-up interviews.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** On 24 October 2023, the audit team conducted unannounced floor walks across all production sections. While no individuals presenting as obviously underage were observed during operational hours, subsequent confidential interviews with 14 workers from the sewing and finishing departments revealed a consistent anecdotal report from three separate interviewees (Worker IDs #087, #112, and #155) that a younger female, believed to be the 15-year-old sister of a senior line operator, had been sporadically assisting with thread trimming and piecework sorting on the weekend of 14-15 October. This individual was not present on the audit dates and is not listed on any official payroll. Management, when questioned, acknowledged awareness of this occasional "family assistance" but incorrectly classified it as permissible informal training, demonstrating a clear failure in understanding the stringent definitions of employment under the applicable labor code.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up Requirement:** The investigation substantiates a violation of the fundamental prohibition against child labor. The factory's verification systems failed to prevent access, and supervisory practices implicitly condoned the engagement of a minor in production-related activities, regardless of compensation status. A critical corrective action plan (CAP) must be submitted within 48 hours, detailing immediate steps to reinforce age verification protocols, deliver mandatory management training on child labor definitions, and establish a monitored grievance channel for workers.
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area 3.1: Employment Contracts & Onboarding Documentation**
**Audit Reference:** FIND-2023-10-004-07

During the document review conducted on October 26, 2023, it was identified that the factory's onboarding process for production line operatives, who constitute approximately 85% of the 320-strong workforce, is deficient in formalizing the employment relationship. A randomized sample of 28 personnel files revealed that only 9 contained signed employment contracts stipulating wages, working hours, and job description, with the remaining files containing only a photocopied national ID card and a handwritten information sheet. In subsequent confidential interviews held on October 27 with 12 migrant workers from the sewing section, 10 confirmed they had never been provided with a personal copy of a signed contract, with one worker noting, "We were told to start work and the line leader gave us a paper about rules, but it was only in the local language which I do not read well." This practice fails to meet the requirement for provision of clear, written, and legally binding terms of employment prior to commencement of work, leaving workers, particularly the vulnerable migrant population, without essential contractual safeguards and exposing them to potential unilateral changes in terms. The management representative acknowledged the gap, citing high turnover and remote location challenges in securing legal template review, but this does not constitute acceptable justification.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-004-2023-08
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (EP)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** The audit included a review of emergency procedures and a physical inspection of the facility's preparedness systems, with particular attention given to the factory's profile of operating in a rural location with a predominantly migrant workforce. While the management provided documented evacuation plans, the practical implementation was found to be deficient. During an unannounced fire drill simulation conducted at 14:30 hrs on the inspection date, the evacuation of the 350-person cutting and sewing department on the second floor was disorganized and incomplete, requiring 14 minutes and 37 seconds to achieve a full assembly, which exceeds the acceptable benchmark of 5 minutes. Inspector observations confirmed that exit route signage was obstructed by stored WIP (Work-in-Progress) materials in two key corridors, and emergency lighting in the eastern stairwell was inoperative.

**Worker Interview Notes:** Interviews with 7 production workers, conducted through an interpreter, revealed a consistent lack of awareness; 6 workers stated they had never received specific emergency training since their hiring, and 5 were unsure of the location of their primary assembly point. One worker from the finishing section noted, "We heard the alarm but nobody moved at first, so we kept working until the line leader told us to go."

**Conclusion & Follow-Up Required:** The factory has failed to establish and maintain an effective emergency preparedness program, with critical gaps in employee training, drill efficacy, and the maintenance of egress pathways. This constitutes a significant failure to ensure a state of operational readiness, thereby exposing the workforce to undue risk, especially considering their migrant status and potential language barriers. A corrective action plan (CAP) addressing these systemic failures is required within 7 business days.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Wages & Benefits (Ref: WB-Aud-004-2023)**
**Finding Reference: WB-F-004-01**

During the onsite inspection conducted on October 26, 2023, a review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2023, and subsequent interviews with 14 production line workers (selected from a pool of predominantly migrant employees), it was identified that the established piece-rate remuneration system fails to guarantee the legal minimum wage for all worked hours. Observations of time cards and production data, corroborated by worker testimonies, indicate that during low-volume order periods, specifically the first two weeks of September, the average hourly earnings for approximately 30% of the sampled workforce fell below the regional monthly minimum wage threshold when calculated on an hourly basis. This was attributed to insufficient volume of work provided, yet workers were still required to be present at the factory for full, fixed shifts. The payment of a fixed "attendance allowance" was noted, but this supplemental payment, when combined with the variable piece-rate earnings, did not consistently elevate total compensation to meet the statutory minimum requirement for all hours of attendance, constituting a failure of wage protection. **Severity: Critical.** **Note:** A review of the wage calculation methodology and a corrective action plan is required to ensure all hours worked are compensated at or above the legal minimum, irrespective of production volume.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-004-02**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Element 1, ILO Convention 29)**
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

A review of the factory's geographic context and workforce profile, predominantly comprising migrant workers from distant provinces with limited local support networks, established a heightened risk profile for coercion in employment. During document review on October 25, 2023, it was confirmed that the factory retains all original identity documents (national ID cards and household registration booklets) for 100% of its production workforce (347 workers) for the duration of employment, citing a stated policy of "safekeeping." Worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, with 14 randomly selected operators from three production lines substantiated that this retention is mandatory and not voluntary; all 14 interviewed workers expressed significant concern and stated they felt unable to retrieve their documents for personal use, with several noting the documents were required to secure local lodging or access services. This practice of systematic and involuntary document withholding effectively restricts workers' freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment at will, constituting a form of coercion. A follow-up note has been recorded to initiate immediate corrective action, requiring the factory to return all personal documents to workers and implement a lawful verification protocol.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team identified systemic disparities in employment conditions correlating with workers' provincial origin. Background checks revealed that 32 workers hired from Yunnan province, constituting approximately 15% of the workforce, were uniformly placed in lower-paying assistant-tier roles within the sewing department, despite having documented experience comparable to locally-hired counterparts. Interview notes from Worker IDs #087, #112, and #134 (Annex D, section 7.3) corroborated management's verbal acknowledgment of a preference for local hires in supervisory and quality control positions, citing "communication ease." This practice constitutes a violation of Section 4.1 of the Supplier Code of Conduct (SCoC) regarding equal opportunity and non-discrimination. A Corrective Action Plan (CAP Ref: 004-2023-F-18) is required to address discriminatory hiring and promotion protocols. Severity: Moderate.


============================================================
Factory 005 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: small family-owned factory with 50-100 workers, limited HR capacity, first time being audited
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area: 4.0 Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: SA8000:2014 Sec. 7, Client Code WH-OT-01)**

During the review of payroll records for the period January to March 2024, coupled with time card analysis and worker interviews conducted on 15/04/2024, it was identified that the factory's operational practices do not fully align with the client's Code of Conduct or local legal standards regarding working hours. Inspector observations confirmed that production targets, particularly for the 'Spring Essentials' order shipped in early February, led to systematic overtime. Interview notes from 8 out of 22 workers interviewed indicated regular work weeks of 72 to 78 hours during peak periods, with overtime hours exceeding the legal maximum and being compensated at the standard rate rather than the legally mandated premium. While management cited a lack of formalized tracking systems and pressure to meet delivery deadlines as contributing factors, this constitutes a breach of the established standard. A corrective action plan must address the implementation of a transparent timekeeping system, revised production planning, and back-pay for any overtime premiums owed. Severity: Moderate.


============================================================
Factory 006 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: small family-owned factory with 50-100 workers, limited HR capacity, first time being audited
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WH-OT-2024-006-01
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1 - B.1.4)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2024

During the review of payroll records for the period of September 1 to October 15, 2024, and corroborated through confidential interviews with 12 production workers, it was found that the factory regularly exceeds the legally mandated limits for working hours. Production records indicate a consistent six-day workweek with regular shifts of 10.5 hours per day (08:00 to 18:30 with a one-hour break), resulting in a regular workweek of 63 hours before any declared overtime is applied. Worker interviews confirmed this pattern, with several stating that on 14 separate days during the review period, they were required to work an additional 2 to 3 hours beyond this already extended shift to meet shipment deadlines, with one worker providing a handwritten note of their hours showing a 78-hour workweek for the week of September 23. While the overtime hours were compensated at the legally correct premium rate, the fundamental working hours violate the maximum weekly hours stipulated by national law. The limited HR function, consisting of one family member, demonstrated a lack of awareness of the legal thresholds, focusing only on correct payment rather than hour ceilings. **Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-001 / 2024-Q3
**Area of Assessment:** Emergency Preparedness (E7.1, E7.2, E7.4)
During the site walkthrough conducted on October 26, 2024, it was observed that while fire extinguishers were present and serviced annually, the factory's emergency evacuation procedures were found to be insufficiently implemented and documented. In interviews with 12 production workers from various departments, 10 indicated they had never participated in a full evacuation drill, with two recalling an informal gathering near the gates approximately 18 months prior, which was not documented. Furthermore, the posted evacuation floor plans in the cutting and sewing sections were outdated, not reflecting the current layout of machinery which could obstruct primary egress routes, and were only provided in English, a language not understood by the majority of the workforce. The management representative acknowledged the lack of a formal drill schedule and assigned safety officers, citing limited organizational capacity. A follow-up review of available records confirmed no drill log exists for the past 24 months.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Reference:** SA-8000/7.2 & 7.3; Client Code: HAI-006-F
**Date of Finding:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Observation:** While the facility has a general statement against harassment within its employee guidelines, there is a demonstrable lack of formalized procedural implementation to effectively prevent, report, and remediate incidents of harassment or abuse. During the document review, no dedicated policy or confidential reporting mechanism for harassment could be provided by management. This was corroborated in confidential worker interviews conducted on 2023-10-25 with 12 randomly selected operators across the sewing, cutting, and finishing departments; 9 workers indicated they were unaware of any specific policy or person to confidentially report to if they experienced or witnessed harassment, with several expressing concern over potential retaliation given the close-knit, family-owned nature of the operation. Although no specific incident was reported during the audit, the systemic absence of defined channels and training creates a significant risk of non-compliance and leaves workers without recourse.
**Follow-up Note:** Management acknowledged the gap and has committed to developing a formal policy, but no timeline was established.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team identified concerning practices related to worker retention and freedom of movement. Background checks revealed that the factory, due to its limited HR capacity, utilizes a common but high-risk practice of retaining original worker identification documents (national ID cards) for the duration of employment, a fact confirmed by management and cited as a "safekeeping" measure. In confidential interviews held with 7 of the 84 production line workers, 4 confirmed their IDs were held by the factory office, and 3 expressed a clear fear that leaving employment without management approval would result in the permanent withholding of these critical documents, effectively preventing them from seeking alternative work. This practice of withholding personal documents under coercive conditions constitutes a violation of audit standard FL-04 (Freedom of Movement) and creates a risk of debt bondage, as workers feel unable to leave. **Severity: Moderate.** Corrective action requires the immediate implementation of a documented policy for the voluntary surrender and return of documents, with follow-up verification to be provided.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: 006-SOC-07 | Compliance Area: Discrimination (ILO Convention 111) | Audit Date: October 26, 2023**

Upon review of the factory's human resources capacity, it was noted that the limited HR function is managed by the owner's spouse without formal training. During the review of hiring records for the period of January to October 2023 and subsequent confidential worker interviews conducted on October 26, a pattern was observed. Interviews with 8 of the 27 production workers revealed that during the hiring process, female candidates of childbearing age (specifically mentioned as 22-35 years old) were routinely questioned regarding their marital status, pregnancy plans, and childcare arrangements, while male candidates were not subjected to similar inquiries. This was corroborated by the hiring manager during a follow-up discussion, who stated this practice was "standard to ensure reliability," indicating a direct policy of discrimination in employment on the basis of sex and potential family status, which constitutes a violation of the code of conduct. This places the factory in non-conformance with the established standard, and a corrective action plan must address the development of non-discriminatory hiring policies and manager training.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 007 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: WF-2023-007-04, Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits (Payment of Wages)**
Background review of the factory’s previous CAP (Corrective Action Plan) following the Q4 2022 audit (Finding WF-2022-007-01, Severity: Moderate) identified a systemic failure to pay overtime premiums correctly. During this follow-up inspection conducted on 15-17 October 2023, inspectors cross-referenced payroll records for the period of September 2023 against time and attendance logs and production data for a sample of 32 workers from the sewing and finishing departments. It was observed that while regular hours were paid at the correct minimum wage rate, the calculation for overtime hours utilized the base minimum wage only, failing to incorporate the legally mandated premium rate of 1.5 times the regular hourly rate, constituting a recurring violation of local labor law §12.4a. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews, where 7 out of 12 workers interviewed stated their pay slips showed incorrect overtime amounts, with one worker providing a payslip from 12 September 2023 as evidence. The finding indicates a failure to implement the previously agreed corrective actions and represents a willful, repeat violation affecting an estimated 85% of the production workforce.
**Severity: Critical**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-2023-007-04
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness
**Previous CAP Reference:** CAP-2022-009, Item 3.1 (Follow-up audit conducted 15 March 2023 noted partial closure of fire door obstruction issues).

During the inspection of the third-floor sewing section on 17 October 2023, it was observed that the primary emergency exit stairwell (Stairwell B, East Wing) was obstructed by approximately 25 bales of cut fabric and WIP, stored in direct contravention of the corrective action plan. This obstruction extended 4.5 meters into the required 1.1-meter egress path, creating a significant bottleneck. Interviews with four production workers from this section revealed that this storage practice has been recurrent for "several weeks" due to a lack of storage space, and two workers expressed uncertainty about an alternate evacuation route. This finding represents a repeat and escalated violation of the previously agreed-upon corrective action plan and national fire code section 7.5.2, compromising life safety for the 127 personnel assigned to this floor. Immediate rectification is required.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: 2024-007-SOC-003 // Compliance Area: Discrimination // Date of Assessment: May 22-24, 2024**

Background review of prior CAP (CAP-2023-007, issued November 2023) indicated a requirement for the implementation of formal, documented non-discrimination policies and associated manager training. During the current inspection, the audit team, comprising three members, conducted interviews with a total of 37 workers across various production departments and reviewed hiring, promotion, and payroll records for the period January-April 2024. It was observed that while a non-discrimination policy exists in the employee handbook, there is no evidence of it being communicated to the workforce in the last 12 months, nor is there any record of supervisory staff having received the mandated training. Furthermore, interviews with 8 workers from the finishing department revealed a consistent narrative that two team leader positions were filled in March 2024 not through a transparent process but by relatives of the current production manager, bypassing several internal candidates with longer tenure. This was partially corroborated by a review of internal job postings, which were not displayed in the finishing department. The combination of inadequate implementation of the corrective action, lack of training, and credible allegations of favoritism in advancement opportunities constitutes a failure to ensure equal opportunity in employment practices. **Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-OT-007-2023-08**
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (Code: B.7.1 - B.7.3)
**Background & Context:** Factory 007 remains under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) following the Q4 2022 audit, which identified systemic violations of working hour limits and overtime compensation. This follow-up assessment, conducted on August 23-24, 2023, specifically reviewed implementation of the agreed-upon CAP measures, including revised production planning and timekeeping system upgrades.

**Inspector Observations & Document Review:** A review of time cards and payroll records for July 2023, with a detailed sample from the cutting and sewing departments, revealed a continued pattern of non-compliance. Production records indicated sustained high-volume orders with insufficiently staffed lines, creating inherent pressure for extended hours. Specifically, records for 12 workers in the sampled group showed seven instances of work weeks exceeding the 60-hour cap, with a maximum of 67 hours recorded for worker ID #3345 in the week of July 10-16. Furthermore, while overtime premiums were calculated correctly, the legally mandated one day of rest in seven was not provided in these cases, as workers were scheduled for 13 consecutive days.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted privately with 8 production workers on August 24 corroborated the document review findings. Workers reported persistent verbal pressure from line supervisors to meet daily targets, often resulting in "voluntary" but expected overtime. They confirmed the accuracy of the reviewed time cards and expressed fatigue due to the continuous long hours, with several noting they had not declined overtime due to fear of reduced future assignment opportunities.

**Conclusion:** Despite being under a CAP, the factory management has failed to implement effective operational changes to control working hours. The violations are not isolated but represent a recurring, management-controlled failure to adhere to legal limits and the agreed-upon corrective actions, demonstrating a lack of commitment to systemic improvement.
**Severity: Moderate** (Escalated from previous Minor status due to recurrence under CAP).
**Follow-up Note:** This finding constitutes a failure of the existing CAP and necessitates a revised, escalated CAP with stricter milestones and senior management accountability.

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** CL-007-2023-02 | **Compliance Area:** Child Labor (CL) | **Previous CAP Reference:** CAP-2022-09, Item 3.

**Background & Context:** Factory 007 was previously issued a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) following the 2022-09 audit, which identified systemic deficiencies in age verification procedures. The current audit, conducted on October 26-27, 2023, included a review of personnel files (a sample of 120 from hires in the last 12 months) and on-floor observations across all production sections.

**Observations & Interviews:** During the unannounced inspection of the cutting department on October 27, 2023, the audit team observed an individual, later identified as "Worker A," whose apparent age raised concerns. A review of his personnel file (File #78902) revealed a submitted national ID card indicating a date of birth of February 15, 2007, which would make the worker 16 years of age. However, subsequent confidential interviews with three separate workers from the same production line indicated that Worker A had previously confided in them about being 15 years old and having submitted his older brother's identification document to secure employment. The factory's HR manager, when presented with this discrepancy, confirmed that the standard practice is to accept the provided document at face value and that no additional verification with local authorities or cross-referencing with school-leaving certificates had been conducted, despite this being a stipulated action in the previous CAP.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to implement effective age verification mechanisms to prevent the potential employment of underage workers, constituting a recurrence of a previously identified non-conformance and a breach of the fundamental ILO Convention 138. This failure represents a breakdown in the corrective action process and exposes the facility to significant reputational and legal risk.
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref:** HR-03-2024-007
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Background & Context:** The factory’s Corrective Action Plan (CAP) from the Q3 2023 audit (Ref: AUD-0092-2023) required the implementation of a robust system to ensure all workers are provided with legally compliant employment contracts prior to commencement of work. A review of the factory’s provided documentation and procedural guidelines indicated that a revised contract template had been developed and approved by management in January 2024.
**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** During the document review on April 15, 2024, a sample of 32 personnel files for workers hired after the CAP implementation date was examined. Of these, 17 files (approximately 53%) contained a signed employment contract. The remaining 15 files contained only the job application form, with no evidence of a formal contract. Interviews conducted with 8 workers from this group confirmed this discrepancy; all 8 stated they had never been provided with a written contract for review or signature, only verbally agreeing to terms upon hiring. This failure to execute and retain contracts denies workers formal documentation of essential terms such as wages, working hours, and job description, constituting a breach of local labor law and the agreed CAP.
**Follow-up & Severity:** This is a repeated failure from the previous audit cycle, indicating a systemic breakdown in the HR onboarding process. Management was unable to provide a valid justification for the lapse. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-007-2023-24 / **Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (COC Section 7.1) / **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Factory 007 remains under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) from the Q2 2023 audit, which included a non-compliance for insufficient management systems to prevent harassment. During this follow-up assessment, the audit team conducted a review of the revised policy documents and interviewed a total of 37 workers, representing approximately 8% of the workforce across key production departments.

**Observations & Interview Notes:** While the factory has implemented a new "Zero Tolerance Harassment Policy" as per the CAP, multiple worker interviews revealed persistent issues not adequately captured by the new grievance mechanism. Specifically, four workers from the finishing and cutting sections, interviewed separately, provided consistent anecdotal accounts of shouted verbal abuse and derogatory language used by two identified line supervisors towards workers who fall behind production targets. These incidents, reported to have occurred as recently as the week of October 16-20, create an atmosphere of intimidation. The workers expressed a continued reluctance to formally report these incidents via the new hotline, citing fear of reprisal and a belief that the involved supervisors are "protected."

**Analysis & Follow-up Notes:** The evidence indicates a systemic failure in the implementation and cultural embedding of the harassment policy. Management has not demonstrated effective due diligence in monitoring supervisor conduct or ensuring the grievance procedure is trusted and functional. Therefore, the corrective action from the previous audit is deemed ineffective. **Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** F007-OSH-024
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background:** Following the Critical finding for inadequate fire safety management (F007-OSH-011) in the Q2 2023 audit, the factory was placed under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) requiring, among other items, a full review and remediation of all emergency exit pathways. This inspection was conducted to verify the implementation of corrective actions.

**Observations & Evidence:** During a walkthrough of the second-floor sewing section, inspectors observed that Exit Door #4 (western wing) was obstructed by a rolling rack containing approximately 150 units of finished garments, rendering the egress route partially impassable. This obstruction was measured at 1.2 meters deep, encroaching 60% into the required 2-meter clear width of the exit access corridor as per local fire code and international OSH standards. In a subsequent worker interview, 3 of 7 production operators stated that the rack is "often" parked there during peak production hours to facilitate material handling, and they were unaware of any recent management directives to keep the area clear. This condition was visually documented and timestamped at 14:30 on the date of the inspection.

**Analysis:** This constitutes a failure to implement and enforce the approved CAP, demonstrating a systemic breakdown in safety protocol adherence and supervisor oversight. The recurrence of blocked egress routes, a previously cited violation, indicates that corrective actions have not been effectively institutionalized, posing a continued risk to worker life safety in the event of an emergency.

**Severity: Critical**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: FL-2023-007-02**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor (Forced Overtime & Freedom of Movement)**
**Background & Context:** Following the Corrective Action Plan (CAP) issued after the Q4 2022 audit (Finding FL-2022-007-01, Severity: Moderate), which identified excessive overtime and wage deductions for refusal to work mandatory shifts, this follow-up inspection focused on verifying the implementation of corrective measures, specifically the revised overtime policy communicated to workers in January 2023. Inspector observations on March 28-29, 2023, noted the revised policy was posted. However, review of time and attendance records for March 2023 revealed a pattern wherein 47 production line workers exceeded the 60-hour workweek limit on three separate occasions, with weekly totals of 62, 63, and 61 hours. During confidential interviews with 8 workers from the affected lines, 5 indicated they felt compelled to accept this overtime due to direct supervisory pressure and concern over losing their regular employment if they refused, with one worker stating, "The line leader said if we do not stay, we will not be needed tomorrow." This constitutes a failure to effectively implement the corrective action and demonstrates that overtime, while purportedly voluntary per policy, is in practice coerced, undermining workers' freedom of movement and subjecting them to forced labor. **Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 008 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-008-2023-14 **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (CCSA 6.1, 6.3)

**Background & Context:** Factory 008 operates as a subcontractor with a highly fluid, seasonal workforce, which management indicated fluctuates between 120 and 300 personnel depending on order volumes from its various brand clients. The audit scope included a review of hiring, promotion, and disciplinary procedures, with particular attention to the treatment of migrant workers from neighboring provinces who constitute approximately 60% of the sewing line.

**Observations & Interviews:** A review of personnel files from the last six months (March - August 2023) revealed a pattern wherein 21 of 23 supervisory and quality control positions were filled by local residents, despite migrant workers comprising the majority of the operational workforce and having comparable tenure. During confidential interviews conducted on 24 October 2023 with 14 production line workers, multiple interviewees (7 of 14) reported that line leaders frequently assigned the more complex and higher-paid finishing operations to local workers, while migrant workers were consistently allocated simpler, piece-rate tasks. Furthermore, three interviewees stated they were told by a hiring manager during recruitment that "local workers get priority for overtime" when orders are heavy, which was corroborated by a disparate analysis of overtime logs. The factory's policy documentation, while containing a generic non-discrimination clause, lacked any specific implementation procedures or training records for managerial staff on equitable hiring and task assignment.

**Conclusion:** Evidence indicates systemic discrimination in promotion and task assignment practices based on worker origin, disadvantaging the migrant seasonal workforce. This creates a two-tiered employment structure and violates the principle of equal opportunity. A corrective action plan (CAP) is required to address policy, training, and operational practices. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: FA-008-2023-07-014**
**COMPLIANCE AREA: FREEDOM OF ASSOCIATION & COLLECTIVE BARGAINING (ILO Conventions 87 & 98)**
**BACKGROUND & OBSERVATIONS:** During the onsite inspection conducted on July 12-14, 2023, the audit team reviewed relevant documentation and conducted interviews regarding worker representation. It was noted that while the factory's stated policy, as per document HR-POL-004 Rev.2, formally permits the establishment of worker committees, no functional worker representative body or trade union was identified as being active within the facility. This is contextualized by the factory's profile as a subcontractor with a highly seasonal workforce, where approximately 60% of the 1,200 personnel present during the audit were on temporary contracts of less than six months' duration.
**WORKER INTERVIEW NOTES:** Confidential interviews were held with 37 workers across various production departments. Multiple workers (14 of 37 interviewed) expressed unawareness of any mechanism to collectively raise grievances, with several stating a belief that management would not permit such organization. One worker from the cutting section noted, "We are here for the peak season only; if we complain together, we are told others will take our places." Management representatives, when interviewed, confirmed that no election for worker representatives had been held in the past three years, citing the transient nature of the workforce as a primary logistical obstacle.
**CONCLUSION & FOLLOW-UP:** The absence of an elected and functioning worker representative committee, compounded by worker perceptions of discouragement and the structural instability of employment, effectively denies workers the right to freely associate and bargain collectively. This constitutes a failure to implement the factory's own policy and to uphold internationally recognized labor standards.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: SA8000 Element 7.1, BSCI Code 5)**

During the review of time and wage records for the period of March 1 to April 15, 2024, a significant discrepancy was noted between the official payroll data and information gathered through worker interviews. The factory's records indicated a consistent adherence to a 66-hour work week, including 26 hours of overtime, which is already at the maximum limit of local law. However, during confidential interviews conducted on April 17 with 12 production workers from the cutting and sewing departments, 9 workers reported that their actual working hours regularly exceeded those recorded, particularly during the peak order period for spring/summer shipments in late March. These workers stated that they were required to work from 8:00 AM to 10:00 PM, seven days a week, for three consecutive weeks, with some hours worked off-the-clock and thus uncompensated, to meet aggressive production deadlines for multiple brands. This practice constitutes a violation of both local legal limits and the requirement for voluntary overtime. The management acknowledged the high production pressure but attributed the discrepancy to "recording errors." **Severity: Critical** A follow-up review of CCTV footage and detailed reconciliation of piece-rate payments against production output is required to confirm the full scope of the violation.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Reference:** FL-2023-008-4.1b
**Date of Finding:** October 26, 2023
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Coercive Practices (Ref. Code: FL-CP.03)

During the review of employment practices and worker interviews, the audit team identified systemic issues indicating the potential for forced labor, stemming from the factory's subcontractor status and high-pressure production environment. Inspectors noted the retention of original identity documents (national ID cards) for 37 out of 40 randomly selected production line workers reviewed, a practice verbally justified by management as a "security measure" to prevent abrupt departure during peak season. This was corroborated in confidential interviews conducted on October 25, where 8 out of 12 interviewed temporary workers from the recent seasonal intake stated they were required to surrender their documents upon hiring and were informed that leaving before the agreed seasonal period would result in forfeiture of two weeks' wages and the delayed return of their IDs. This combination of document retention and the imposition of financial penalties for departure effectively removes workers’ freedom to terminate employment and constitutes a coercive practice. Severity: Moderate. **Follow-up Required:** Immediate cessation of document retention and revision of employment contracts for temporary labor to remove punitive clauses.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-008-2023-07 | Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources**

During the document review and worker interview phase conducted on 19-21 October 2023, it was found that Factory 008, operating as a subcontractor under significant production pressure for multiple brands, employs a substantial seasonal workforce. The inspector observed that while permanent core staff possessed signed employment contracts, a review of personnel files for 127 workers hired within the last six months revealed that 43 individuals, or approximately 34%, had no signed employment agreement on file. During confidential interviews with 15 randomly selected workers from the seasonal cohort, 9 confirmed they had never been provided with a written contract outlining their terms of employment, compensation, or job description, with several stating they were "verbally told the rate and just started work." This failure to formalize the employment relationship for a significant portion of the workforce constitutes a breach of local labor law and client Code of Conduct requirements, leaving workers without clear, documented terms and conditions. **Severity: Moderate** A corrective action plan must be submitted within 10 business days to address this systemic documentation gap, with particular focus on procedures for onboarding temporary staff during peak production periods.

Factory 009 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SED-009-24-07 (Wages & Benefits)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the recent change in ownership and ongoing management transition noted in the opening conference, a thorough review of payroll records from July, August, and September 2023 was conducted, alongside confidential worker interviews. The inspection focused on the accurate calculation and provision of legally mandated overtime premiums.
**Observations & Interviews:** Payroll analysis for the sampled period revealed a consistent pattern wherein workers were compensated for overtime hours at their standard regular hourly rate, without the legally required premium. This was corroborated in confidential interviews with 12 production workers from the sewing and finishing departments, all of whom stated their overtime pay was "the same as normal hours." For example, worker interviews and corresponding records for the week of September 11-17 confirmed 14 hours of overtime worked by a sample group, paid at the base rate of 16.5 RMB/hour, resulting in a collective shortfall of 231 RMB for that week alone against the legal requirement of 1.5 times the base rate.
**Follow-up Note:** Management acknowledged the discrepancy, attributing it to an error in the new payroll system configuration during the transition, but demonstrated a lack of procedural controls to catch such a fundamental compliance failure.
**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 010 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: SOC-010-24-07-D1 | Compliance Area: Discrimination (C.2.1)**  

During the inspection conducted on 15-17 October 2024, the audit team reviewed hiring records, promotion logs, and payroll data from the preceding twelve-month period. It was noted through worker interviews (12 out of 68 production staff, predominantly migrant workers from neighboring provinces) and document verification that all eight current line supervisor positions are held by male employees from the local township. Conversely, female workers, who constitute approximately 85% of the sewing department workforce, are consistently absent from supervisory roles despite an average tenure exceeding five years for several candidates. Interview notes from Worker #43 (anonymized) state, "We are told these jobs are for local men because they can handle the pressure and communicate with outsiders," indicating a perceived bias in advancement opportunities. This practice constitutes a violation of the supplier code of conduct regarding equal opportunity in employment and promotion.  

**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-2023-010-04**
**Compliance Area: C.1 – Contracts of Employment & Personnel Files**
**Date of Discovery: 23 October 2023**

Background context indicates a workforce primarily composed of migrant laborers with limited local ties, a demographic particularly vulnerable to informal employment practices. Inspector observations during the file review on 23 October revealed that of the 87 personnel files examined, only 32 contained signed employment contracts that were compliant with national labor law stipulations, representing a coverage rate of approximately 36.8%. Worker interviews conducted with 14 randomly selected production operators on 24 October corroborated this systemic issue, with 11 workers stating they had never been provided with a written contract detailing their wages, working hours, or job description, relying instead on verbal agreements with line supervisors. This failure to provide legally mandated employment contracts to a majority of workers, particularly within a migrant-dominant workforce, constitutes a fundamental breach of employment transparency and denies workers evidence of their terms and conditions. A follow-up note is required to mandate the development and implementation of a corrective action plan for the issuance of compliant contracts to all employees within 30 days.
**Severity: Critical**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Compliance Area EHS-7: Emergency Preparedness (Reference: Audit Protocol EP.01-EP.09)**
A review of emergency procedures and physical preparedness was conducted on 23 October 2023, noting the factory's remote location and predominantly migrant workforce, which may necessitate enhanced planning. Inspector observations on the second-floor cutting section revealed two of four designated fire extinguishers (ID: FX-207, FX-208) had inspection tags expired as of August 2023, and the primary evacuation route via the western staircase was partially obstructed by three bales of fabric, approximately 1.5 meters in combined length, for an estimated duration of two hours per shift during material loading. Interviews with seven production workers from the sewing lines confirmed a lack of recent drills; five workers stated the last general evacuation drill occurred "before the last Chinese New Year," and all interviewed were uncertain of the location of the designated assembly point for their department. The aforementioned obstruction of a primary egress route constitutes a failure to maintain clear exit access at all times, as required. **Severity: Moderate** A follow-up note has been logged to verify the correction of the blocked egress and the re-certification of fire equipment within 30 days, with a further recommendation for bi-annual drills to be documented and tailored to account for language barriers within the workforce.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-010-24-008 Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime**

A review of time and attendance records for the period of March 1-31, 2024, identified a systematic pattern of excessive working hours beyond the local legal limit of 60 hours per week, a pattern corroborated through separate worker interviews. Inspectors observed that production line attendance logs, though manually maintained, showed consistent 13-hour shifts for the majority of the 287 production staff across a 7-day work cycle during the peak audit period, with only one rest day recorded in the entire month. During confidential interviews conducted on April 15, 2024, 8 out of 12 randomly selected migrant workers from the stitching department confirmed these schedules, stating that refusal of overtime was informally discouraged due to high production targets and the precarious nature of their employment. Furthermore, overtime compensation calculations, while appearing at the correct premium rate on payslips, were based on a baseline wage that does not fully incorporate all legally required allowances, leading to underpayment. The factory management acknowledged the workload but cited pressure from order deadlines and a lack of skilled local substitutes as contributing factors. This constitutes a violation of both working hour limits and the proper calculation of overtime remuneration.
**Severity: Critical**

[Child Labor]
During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, it was noted that the factory’s personnel files for eight production operatives hired in the previous quarter lacked complete date of birth verification, with only national identity cards presented, a practice known to be susceptible to forgery in this region. Subsequent physical inspection of the cutting and finishing sections on 13 October 2023 revealed one individual, later identified as the younger brother of a senior machine operator, whose appearance and demeanor suggested he may be below the legal working age. In a private interview conducted with the individual, he stated he was 17 years old but was unable to provide any original identity documents; a confidential interview with three other workers from his production line later that day yielded inconsistent accounts regarding his age and schooling history. This constitutes a failure to maintain an effective age verification system as per requirement CL-01.2 of the Responsible Sourcing Code. Given the reasonable suspicion of underage labor and the systemic weakness in hiring controls, this finding is escalated. Severity: Moderate. A follow-up verification of the individual’s age using a registered birth certificate is required within 72 hours.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref:** OSH-2023-010-07
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Machine Guarding & Emergency Egress)
**Date of Inspection:** 24 October 2023

During the facility walk-through on the aforementioned date, inspectors observed a consistent failure to reinstall protective guarding on multiple industrial sewing stations following routine maintenance activities, specifically on Juki DDL-8700 models in Section B. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 7 out of 12 production line workers, who indicated that guards are commonly left off for "ease of thread changes," and that no disciplinary or corrective action has been taken by line supervisors. Furthermore, in the same section, two of the four designated emergency exits were found obstructed by WIP (Work-in-Progress) rolling racks and bales of fabric, reducing the clear width to less than 0.8 meters, which is below the minimum required standard as outlined in section 4.2 of the Client's Code of Conduct. This combination of unguarded moving machinery parts, which presents a direct and severe risk of laceration or amputation, coupled with impeded emergency evacuation routes, constitutes a serious and immediate hazard to worker safety. **Severity: Critical**. Corrective action required within 72 hours, with a follow-up verification audit to be scheduled.
