Factory 011 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-011-24-003 | Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources | Date of Discovery: October 26, 2023**

A review of personnel files and subsequent worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, identified systemic non-conformities regarding employment contracts. While the factory maintains signed contracts for all 347 employees on file, inspection of a sample of 28 files for production line workers revealed that all contracts were only in the Mandarin language. Interviews with 19 migrant workers from rural regions of Guizhou and Yunnan provinces confirmed that 17 of them did not fully comprehend the contractual terms, citing limited literacy and the use of complex legal terminology. This practice fails to ensure informed consent and violates Section 7.1 of the Client's Code of Conduct, which mandates that employment agreements be communicated in a language understood by the worker. Given the factory's remote location and its workforce profile consisting predominantly of vulnerable migrant workers with limited access to legal resources, this deficiency obstructs their ability to understand fundamental terms of employment. Severity: Moderate. **Follow-up Note: Corrective Action Plan (CAP) must include translation of contracts into appropriate local dialects and provision of verbal explanations prior to signing, with documented worker acknowledgements.**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-011-2023-08
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1, B.1.3)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26-27, 2023

A review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2023, combined with timecard data and worker interviews, indicates systemic non-compliance with legal limits on working hours and overtime compensation. Production records show a sustained peak order period throughout the month, and inspector observations confirmed operational activity on all Sundays. Interviews with 14 randomly selected production line workers, predominantly migrant workers from distant provinces, revealed a consistent pattern of working 13-hour shifts from 7:00 to 21:00, including a 2-hour cumulative break period, for 28 consecutive days in September. This results in a recorded 77 hours of overtime per worker for the month, far exceeding the legal maximum of 36 hours, and corroborates management's admission that the stipulated weekly rest day was not provided. Furthermore, while overtime premiums were paid at the correct rate, the excessive hours constitute a severe infringement of worker welfare and local labor law. **Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: FAC-011-24-WB-003**
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (WB)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

During the review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2023, and subsequent worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, it was found that the factory's remuneration practices for overtime work are not in conformity with national labor law. Inspector observations of time cards and corresponding pay slips for a sample of 12 production line workers revealed a consistent pattern of overtime hours being compensated at the standard hourly rate, rather than at the legally mandated premium rate. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 8 of the 15 workers spoken to, who stated that they have "never received extra pay for overtime," with one worker providing a pay slip indicating 98 regular hours and 52 overtime hours, yet total gross pay was calculated using a single, flat rate. The practice constitutes a systemic failure to provide legally prescribed wages for all hours worked. **Severity: Critical** A follow-up note has been issued to management requiring immediate rectification and a retrospective payout calculation for the previous six months, to be submitted for review.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref:** EP-011-2023-04 | **Compliance Area:** 3.2 Emergency Preparedness & Response | **Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** The facility's documented Emergency Preparedness Plan, reviewed on-site, was found to be generic and not site-specific, lacking crucial elements such as designated assembly point maps or procedures for visitor accountability. During an unannounced audible alarm test of the fire evacuation system at 14:30 hours, conducted from the second-floor cutting department, egress was severely hampered due to multiple obstructions, including bundled fabric and partially assembled rolling racks within the primary west staircase landing, reducing the effective width to approximately 0.7 meters. Furthermore, subsequent worker interviews with 7 employees from the sewing section, conducted on October 27, revealed a systemic lack of training; 6 of the 7 workers interviewed could not identify their designated assembly area and stated they had never participated in a full facility drill, with their last recorded training being over 14 months prior, which constitutes a failure in maintaining procedural competency.

**Follow-up Note:** Management attributed the obstructed egress to temporary production overflow and the training deficit to high worker turnover, but these do not mitigate the immediate and severe risk to personnel safety. This represents a failure to ensure and maintain effective emergency readiness, exposing a significant portion of the workforce, particularly a vulnerable migrant population with potential language barriers, to undue hazard.

**Severity: Critical**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FA-011-24-008 **Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Category 3.0)

**Background & Context:** The audit scope included a review of policies, management interviews, and worker interviews conducted on-site between March 12-14, 2024, to assess the practical implementation of freedom of association principles. Given the factory's rural location and a workforce comprising approximately 92% migrant workers, particular attention was paid to potential barriers to collective organization and the presence of any form of worker representation.

**Observations & Evidence:** Management confirmed there is no active labor union or worker-elected committee, stating workers have "never expressed interest." During confidential interviews with 32 workers selected from various production departments, 28 workers demonstrated a lack of awareness of their right to associate freely, with several expressing fear of termination or non-renewal of their contracts if they were to engage in collective activities. Furthermore, review of the "Code of Conduct" document (version 2.1, dated Jan 2023) provided to workers omitted all clauses related to freedom of association, though it is included in the management-held copy. A posted notice in the canteen, dated February 28, 2024, outlining "Worker Responsibilities," contained a clause discouraging "disruptive group gatherings" without defining the term, which could reasonably be interpreted to deter peaceful assembly.

**Conclusion:** The combination of the workforce's vulnerable demographic profile, the absence of any mechanism for collective voice, the dissemination of incomplete policy documents, and the presence of a discouraging public notice creates an environment that actively inhibits the right to freedom of association and collective bargaining. **Severity: Moderate.** **Follow-up Required:** The supplier must revise and uniformly distribute the Code of Conduct, remove the ambiguous notice, and initiate a management-sponsored education program on worker rights, with evidence of worker attendance, to be verified at the next surveillance audit.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-011-04**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (ILO Convention 29)**
**Date of Discovery: 23 October 2023**

Through document review and confidential worker interviews conducted on-site between 23-25 October 2023, the audit team identified systemic practices indicative of involuntary labor. Background verification revealed that 89% of the production workforce consists of migrant workers from distant provinces, a population particularly vulnerable to coercion. During interviews with 17 workers from the sewing section, a consistent narrative emerged regarding the unlawful retention of original identity documents, including national ID cards and household registration booklets, by factory management under the pretext of "safekeeping and processing." This practice, confirmed through cross-reference with the HR department's own records, effectively restricts workers' freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment at will, as they fear being unable to secure alternative housing or travel without these documents. Furthermore, interview notes from Worker #11 (anonymized) disclosed that management has, on at least three documented occasions in Q3 2023, threatened to withhold these documents and final wage payments if workers attempted to resign before the end of an informally imposed 24-month "service period," which is not stipulated in their employment contracts. This constitutes a clear deprivation of liberty and constitutes forced labor.
**Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: 011-24-HA-003 | Compliance Area: HARASSMENT & ABUSE (CCS Section 7.1)**
During the inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team reviewed the factory's policies and conducted confidential interviews with 37 production line workers, approximately 65% of whom are migrant workers residing in on-site dormitories. While a written policy against harassment is documented, implementation and worker awareness are severely deficient. Multiple workers interviewed (12 of 37) described a persistent pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by two line supervisors in the cutting and sewing departments, including frequent use of derogatory language, public humiliation for quality errors, and threats of punitive overtime assignments or dismissal for those who complain. The workers, citing their migrant status and fear of job loss, expressed no confidence in the existing, opaque grievance mechanism. This constitutes a failure to provide a workplace free from harassment and abuse and to ensure effective access to remedy. **Severity: Moderate.** *Follow-up Note: Corrective Action Plan must include supervisor re-training, a communicated and trusted reporting procedure, and re-interview of affected workers within 60 days.*

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref: OSH-2023-011-07**  
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health**  
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**  

During the site walkthrough conducted on the aforementioned date, it was observed that the fabric cutting section, which employs approximately 45 workers, lacked adequate machine guarding on seven (7) operational band saws and two (2) circular cutters, exposing moving blades and pinch points. This condition was further corroborated during private interviews with twelve (12) workers, three of whom cited minor laceration incidents in the past six months that were treated onsite but not formally recorded in the factory’s injury log. The absence of documented risk assessments for these specific machines (per requirement OSH-5.03) and the failure to implement hierarchy of controls constitutes a significant deviation from the mandated safety protocols. The management representative acknowledged awareness of the issue but cited budgetary constraints and difficulty sourcing technical parts in the rural location as contributing factors to the delay in remediation. **Severity: Moderate**

Factory 012 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FA-012-2023-08-FOA-001
**Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Section 7.0 of Code of Conduct)
**Date of Assessment:** 23-24 October 2023

**Background & Observations:** While Factory 012 maintains ISO 9001:2015 certification and a generally robust procedural framework for social compliance, the assessment of Freedom of Association and Collective Bargaining uncovered a procedural inconsistency. A review of the posted policies in all production halls and the canteen confirmed the presence of the legally required notification of workers' rights to associate, per local legislation. However, during confidential interviews conducted on 24 October with 23 production workers selected from various departments, a recurring theme emerged. Eight workers (approximately 35% of those interviewed) expressed uncertainty regarding the function and protection afforded to the in-house Worker Welfare Committee, with one interviewee stating, "We know the committee exists, but we are not sure if they can help with disputes about management, or if we would face problems for talking to them." Further document review revealed that the minutes from the last three quarterly Committee meetings, while maintained, contained only summaries of welfare discussions (e.g., cafeteria menus) with no recorded items, discussions, or resolutions pertaining to collective bargaining matters or the handling of worker grievances related to terms and conditions of employment.

**Follow-up Note:** The Compliance Officer was able to present the Committee's charter, which does formally include grievance handling, indicating a failure in implementation and communication rather than a complete absence of structure.

**Severity: Minor**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-012-2023-04**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (WH)**
**Date of On-Site Verification: October 26-27, 2023**

Background context indicates a generally well-managed facility with a documented commitment to compliance, as evidenced by its ISO certifications and the presence of a dedicated compliance officer. A review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2023, and time cards for four production lines (A, C, D, and F) initially reflected adherence to the 60-hour maximum workweek. However, during confidential interviews conducted on October 27 with 14 workers from the cutting and sewing departments, a discrepancy was identified. Eight workers reported that during a peak production period in mid-September, they were instructed to continue working after clocking out to meet a shipping deadline, effectively working "off-the-clock" for approximately 2-3 hours per night over four consecutive days, which was not captured in the official records. This practice, while reportedly isolated, constitutes a breach of accurate working hour tracking and voluntary overtime provisions. The Compliance Officer was notified of this allegation prior to the exit meeting and has initiated a preliminary internal review.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref: OSH-2023-012-F07**
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health**
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**

During the walkthrough of the finishing and packing department on October 26, 2023, the audit team observed three separate emergency exit doors (EX-04, EX-07, and EX-09) which were found to be partially obstructed by temporary storage of packaged finished goods, with the obstruction at EX-07 measuring approximately 1.2 meters into the required egress path. This condition was confirmed through photographs and floor plan verification. Interviews with four workers from the adjacent sewing section revealed a lack of recent egress drills specific to this area, with one worker noting the obstructions had been present "for a few days" due to a surge order. While the factory maintains a generally good standard of housekeeping and the exits remained functionally unlocked, this presents a clear deviation from Section 4.2.1 of the factory's own Emergency Preparedness Plan and international life safety standards, as the consistent blockage of designated exits, even temporarily, compromises a fundamental requirement for safe evacuation. **Severity: Moderate** A corrective action request has been issued with a follow-up verification date set for November 9, 2023.

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area 1.0 – Child Labor & Young Workers**
**Finding Reference:** CL-012-2023-02

An inspection of the factory's recruitment and human resources management systems was conducted, including a review of personnel files for 30 randomly selected production workers from the April 2023 hiring cohort. While the factory maintains a robust documentary verification process, a discrepancy was identified during worker interviews on October 26, 2023. One worker, presented with a valid national ID card indicating an age of 18, was observed during a walkthrough of the dormitory facilities with personal items (a secondary school notebook and a birthday card) suggesting a more recent secondary education completion and a younger age. A follow-up, discreet interview with two of the worker's peers corroborated concerns that the individual may have presented a sibling's documentation. The factory's compliance officer was immediately notified and initiated a formal re-verification process. This isolated incident represents a breakdown in the age verification protocol for a single hire, not indicative of systemic use of child labor, but it highlights a vulnerability in relying solely on documentary evidence without supplementary verification measures.
**Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Area: 4.0 - Harassment & Abuse (Code: HA)**
**Reference: AUD-012-2023-047**

Background context acknowledges Factory 012's established compliance framework, including ISO 9001:2015 certification and the presence of a dedicated Compliance Officer, Mr. Lee, who was interviewed on 17 October 2023. However, during confidential worker interviews conducted on 18-19 October 2023 with 43 production operators (approximately 12% of the production floor workforce), a repeated theme emerged regarding supervisory conduct in the finishing department. Specifically, three separate workers from Line 7 described instances where line leader Mr. Chen employs verbally abusive language, including shouting and using derogatory terms such as "useless" when production targets are missed, creating an environment of intimidation. While no physical abuse was alleged or observed, and the factory's policy prohibiting harassment was found to be posted, these verified verbal abuse allegations indicate a gap between policy and practice in a specific department. The Compliance Officer was unaware of these specific allegations, as no formal grievances had been lodged, suggesting potential inadequacies in the grievance mechanism's effectiveness or employee trust. **Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref:** HR-CON-012-24-008
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observation:** While the factory maintains a generally robust HR administration system, as attested by its ISO certification and the presence of a dedicated Compliance Officer, a sample review of employment contracts for 30 workers hired between July and September 2023 revealed an inconsistency. The contracts, while comprehensive on most fronts, uniformly omitted a legally mandated clause specifying the precise number of working hours per day and rest days per week, as required by national labor law Article 24(1)(c). This omission was confirmed across multiple departments.

**Interviews & Records:** Interviews with 5 of the affected newly hired workers indicated they had received verbal explanations of their schedules during orientation, but acknowledged the written contract lacked this detail. The HR Manager provided the shift policy documentation, which complies with legal limits, confirming this was a systematic drafting error rather than an intent to mislead.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** Although actual working hours were found to be within legal limits during the audit period, the absence of this fundamental term in the binding employment contract constitutes a violation of statutory requirements and introduces potential risk for misinterpretation. The factory is required to revise the contract template and re-issue corrected contracts to all affected personnel.
**Severity: Minor**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SSA-2023-012-F06 **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (including hiring, promotion, remuneration, and termination) **Date of On-Site Verification:** October 26, 2023.

**Background & Observation Notes:** During the review of hiring and personnel records for the preceding twelve-month period, alongside worker interviews, the audit team identified a concerning pattern inconsistent with the factory's otherwise strong compliance profile and ISO 9001 certification. While the facility maintains a formal non-discrimination policy and has a dedicated compliance officer, interviews with 37 production line workers across three departments revealed a unanimous and specific narrative regarding management-tier promotions. All interviewed workers from Group D (comprising 28 female and 9 male operators) stated that the three most recent promotions to Line Supervisor positions, documented as occurring in Q2 2023, were all awarded to male employees, despite there being several female candidates with equal or greater seniority and performance metrics noted in their files.

**Worker Interview Excerpt:** One female worker with nine years of tenure (Worker ID #4472) stated, "We are told the policy is fair, but when a supervisor job opens, they always say it is a job needing a man to handle the pressure and the night shift. We have done the same job for years." This sentiment was echoed in multiple interviews.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Required:** This constitutes evidence of gender-based discrimination in promotion practices, creating a discriminatory barrier to advancement. The factory management is required to provide a corrective action plan addressing this systemic issue, including revised, objective promotion criteria, retraining for HR and management personnel, and a review of the past two years' promotion decisions for potential remediation. **Severity: Moderate.**

Factory 013 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: SOC-07-HA-2024-013**

During the audit conducted on 23-24 October 2024, which included a review of policies, direct observation on the production floor across three departments, and confidential worker interviews with a stratified sample of 37 workers, a systemic gap was identified in the effective implementation of the factory's "Zero Tolerance for Harassment" policy (POL-HR-004, Rev.3). While a formal policy exists and is posted, investigator observations and multiple worker testimonies indicated that the current grievance mechanism is perceived as inaccessible and ineffective. Specifically, five separate interviewees from the cutting and finishing sections reported instances of verbal abuse and intimidation by line supervisors, particularly during high-pressure production peaks, with one worker providing a detailed account of a threatening remark made on or around October 15th; these workers expressed a unanimous lack of confidence in reporting such incidents, fearing retaliation and citing a belief that management would side with supervisory staff. Furthermore, the review of grievance logs for the past 12 months revealed only two formally recorded complaints under the harassment policy, which is statistically inconsistent with the size of the workforce and the findings from the interview process, indicating significant under-reporting due to a deficient complaint-handling culture. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan (CAP) must address the revitalization of the grievance mechanism, including supervisor re-training, anonymous reporting options, and demonstrated non-retaliation protections to be verified at follow-up.

[Discrimination]
During the review of personnel files and payroll records from January to March 2024, coupled with interviews conducted on 20-MAR-2024 with 32 production operators (15 male, 17 female) across three product lines, the audit team identified a systemic pattern of gender-based discrimination in both hiring and advancement. Observation of the current organizational chart and review of promotion histories over the past 24 months indicate that all 28 line supervisor positions and 5 production manager positions are held exclusively by male employees, despite a qualified female workforce constituting approximately 65% of the sewing department. Worker interviews consistently noted a perceived "glass ceiling," with several experienced female operators (with 5+ years tenure) stating they had been passed over for supervisory training in favor of less experienced male colleagues, with management citing a preference for males in "authority roles" during a follow-up clarification meeting on 21-MAR-2024. This constitutes a violation of the client's Code of Conduct, section 3.2 (Equal Opportunity), and relevant local labor law provisions. Severity: Moderate. Corrective Action Plan required within 30 days to address discriminatory promotion practices and implement a transparent, merit-based advancement policy.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-03:** During the review of payroll records for the period January to March 2024, coupled with confidential worker interviews conducted on 12 April 2024, it was identified that the factory retains possession of workers' original identification documents, including national ID cards and passports, for the duration of their employment. This practice was confirmed in separate interviews with 7 out of 22 randomly selected production line workers, who stated that management secures these documents in the main administrative office ostensibly for "safekeeping and processing," and that requesting their return prior to resignation would be "very difficult." This policy effectively restricts workers' freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment at will, creating a situation of undue reliance and potential coercion. While management cited a procedural rationale, the retention of these critical personal documents constitutes a violation of international labor standards regarding the prohibition of forced labor. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan is required to institute an immediate policy change whereby documents are only held for legally mandated registration periods, with clear receipts provided, after which they must be returned to worker possession.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: SOC-02 / FOACB-2024-013**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2024**

During the review of factory policies and subsequent worker interviews, it was established that while the factory has a formally registered trade union with an elected committee, its operational effectiveness is severely constrained. Management confirmed the union's existence and provided meeting minutes; however, interviews conducted on October 25 with 14 workers from three production departments revealed a consistent pattern of obstruction. Five workers separately reported that union committee members have been subtly advised by line supervisors not to discuss wage-related grievances during union meetings, and two workers stated they were discouraged from attending the last annual general meeting under the premise of urgent production targets. This constitutes interference in the legitimate activities of the workers’ organization and restricts the union’s ability to engage in meaningful collective bargaining on key issues. A follow-up note is required to review the terms of the collective bargaining agreement and interview the union president off-site.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
Audit Finding Ref: WH-013-24-08. Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Code: C.3.1). During the review of payroll records for the period of January to March 2024, alongside timecard data and worker interviews conducted on 15 April 2024, it was identified that the factory's production schedule, particularly for the woven outerwear line, regularly necessitates overtime exceeding the stipulated limits. Examination of records for a sample of 112 workers from that line revealed a consistent pattern of 78-82 working hours per week during peak periods, with interviewed workers (12 of 18 interviewed) confirming they had worked seven consecutive days for several weeks. Although management provided documentation of written consent for excess overtime, this does not constitute a lawful exception for the systematic and prolonged exceedance of the weekly hour cap as outlined in the benchmark standards. Severity: Critical. A corrective action plan must address systemic scheduling to ensure legal compliance.

Factory 014 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: 5.1 - Harassment, Abuse & Discipline (Audit Code: HR-08)**
**Background & Context:** During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 23-24 October 2023, it was noted that Factory 014, due to its recent establishment, has yet to fully implement a formal management system to address workplace harassment. The factory's policy documentation, while containing a basic anti-harassment clause, lacks detailed procedures for reporting, investigation, and remediation. **Observations & Interview Notes:** Interviews with 17 randomly selected workers from the sewing and finishing sections revealed a recurring theme regarding supervisory conduct. Multiple workers (6 of 17 interviewed) described an environment where line leaders and one specific floor supervisor frequently use derogatory language, shouting, and intimidation to enforce production targets. One worker recounted an incident on approximately October 12th where a supervisor publicly berated and threw a garment at an operator for repeated stitching errors, causing significant embarrassment. No formal grievance mechanism was known to the workers, who expressed fear of retaliation if they complained. **Finding:** The factory is in violation of the requirement to provide a workplace free from harassment, abuse, and harsh or inhumane treatment. The absence of a robust, communicated policy coupled with credible verbal testimony of abusive conduct indicates a systemic failure in protecting worker dignity and well-being. **Follow-up Required:** Management must immediately issue a clear directive against such behavior, develop and implement a comprehensive anti-harassment policy with confidential reporting channels, and conduct mandatory training for all managerial staff.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: FL-014-2024-003 | Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Section 1 / ILO Convention 29)**
Background context indicates this facility is in a developmental phase regarding its social compliance management systems. During the document review on 12 October 2024, it was confirmed that the company policy on forced labor, while extant, has not been formally communicated or trained upon for the majority of the operational workforce. Inspector observations on the production floor and in dormitory facilities noted no visible indicators of confinement; however, during confidential worker interviews conducted with 17 randomly selected employees (approximately 12% of the workforce), 3 workers in the cutting department provided consistent, unsolicited testimony that their original identification documents (national ID cards) are being held by the factory's administrative office under a stated policy of "safekeeping" for the duration of their employment. This retention of personal documents was verified through cross-reference with management, who confirmed the practice is intended to "reduce turnover," effectively restricting workers' freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment at will, which constitutes a bonded labor risk. A follow-up note is required for the immediate cessation of this practice and the return of all documents, coupled with revised policy implementation and worker verification.
**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 015 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref. WAGE-2024-015-03**

**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Code: WB.1.1 - Minimum Wage Compliance)

**Background & Observations:** During the audit conducted on March 12-14, 2024, a review of payroll records for the period January-February 2024 and time cards for a sample of 45 production workers was performed. The factory operates under a collective bargaining agreement (CBA) ratified in November 2023 with the recognized union. The audit team noted that the base wage for all regular workers meets the regional statutory minimum. However, during cross-referencing of individual worker pay slips (for 8 workers selected from line 7) with their approved overtime records, a consistent discrepancy was identified.

**Interviews & Evidence:** In interviews with 15 workers from various departments, including 4 union stewards, it was confirmed that the calculation of overtime premiums for hours worked on designated public holidays, specifically the New Year's Day holiday on January 1, 2024, was not performed at the legally mandated rate of 300% of the regular hourly rate, as stipulated in national labor law Article 44. Instead, workers confirmed they were compensated at the standard overtime rate of 150%. This was corroborated by the examined pay slips for January. The union representatives stated the issue had been formally raised via the grievance mechanism on January 25, and management was engaged in corrective discussions at the time of the audit.

**Follow-up Note:** Factory management acknowledged the administrative error in their payroll software configuration for holiday pay and provided evidence of a pending software patch. A commitment was made to perform a back-pay calculation for all affected workers (estimated 289 personnel) by April 15, 2024, with reconciliation to be verified by the union committee.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Reference:** SA-015-24-Q2-OSH-03
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Date of Inspection:** May 17, 2024

**Finding Details:** During the walkthrough of the cutting and spreading sections on Level 2, the audit team observed a significant deviation from the documented OSH procedures regarding machine guarding. Specifically, the protective interlock device on the primary automatic fabric spreading machine (Model TX-7, Serial #47821) had been deliberately overridden, as evidenced by a metal bracket rigged to hold the safety switch in the closed position, allowing the machine barrier to be opened while the machine was in operation. This condition was confirmed to be a persistent workaround, in place for an estimated three weeks according to the shift supervisor, to expedite fabric loading. Interviews with four operators from the section revealed awareness of the bypass, with two expressing unease about the associated pinch-point hazard, though they had not formally reported it via the grievance mechanism, assuming it was a management-sanctioned modification for productivity. This constitutes a failure to maintain effective engineering controls and exposes operators to unguarded moving parts.
**Severity: Critical**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-015-2023-087 | Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: CBA.01)** 

During the onsite assessment conducted on 23-25 October 2023, the audit team reviewed documentation related to industrial relations and conducted interviews with a total of 37 workers, including 8 members of the elected Worker Welfare Committee (WWC) and 5 members of the factory's recognized union executive committee. Background checks confirm the union has been formally registered and active at the site for over four years. Interview notes from multiple worker discussions (e.g., Worker IDs #112, #089, #203) consistently reflected a positive perception of the grievance mechanism, with no reports of anti-union discrimination or interference in union activities. Management provided verified minutes of the last four collective bargaining agreement (CBA) negotiations, demonstrating good-faith engagement, and the current CBA was found to be posted prominently in all major workspaces in accordance with procedural requirements. A review of disciplinary records from the past 12 months showed no cases related to union activities, supporting the observation of a functional and cooperative dialogue channel between worker representatives and factory management. **Status: Compliant.**

Factory 016 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
Audit Area C.1: Contracts of Employment & Personnel Files. Background: The factory maintains a centralized HR department with documented procedures for onboarding. During the file review on 12 October 2023, a sample of 35 personnel files for production operators hired within the last 12 months was examined against the standard requirement for signed, individualized contracts stipulating terms of employment. Observation: While all files contained signed contracts, the audit team noted that for 8 files (approximately 23% of the sample), the contracts were undated, and in 3 instances, the stated job title and department did not align with the worker’s actual assignment as verified through payroll and floor supervisor interviews. Interviews with a random selection of 7 workers from the affected lines confirmed they had signed documents during induction but were not provided with a copy for their personal records, and two expressed uncertainty about their official job classification. This constitutes a failure to ensure transparent and mutually understood terms of employment as per the applicable code of conduct section COC.7.2.1. Follow-up action requires HR to rectify all deficient contracts and implement a supervisory check prior to filing. Severity: Minor.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the review of time and attendance records for the period of March 1-31, 2024, and cross-referenced with payroll data, it was identified that a significant portion of the workforce, specifically 127 employees within the cut-and-sew departments, exceeded the local legal maximum of 60 hours of work per week. Inspector observations on the production floor, supported by timecard printouts (Ref: WH/OT-016-03-27), confirmed continuous 13-hour shifts from 7:00 to 21:00, including mandated but unpaid meal breaks, across a seven-day work week to fulfill a pressing shipment order. Interviews conducted with eight workers from lines 4 and 7 on March 28 revealed unanimous confirmation of the extended schedules, with expressed fatigue, though they acknowledged receiving legally prescribed overtime premiums for hours beyond the standard 44-hour workweek. This constitutes a systematic failure of management controls to plan production capacity within legal working hour limits, as outlined in section 7.1 of the Responsible Sourcing Code (Code: RS-7.1B). A follow-up note is required to review production planning protocols with senior management. Severity: Critical.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-016-02**
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Element 2; ILO Convention 29)
**Background & Observations:** During the document review on 12 October 2023, it was noted that Factory 016 utilizes a third-party labor agent for the recruitment of migrant production line workers, constituting approximately 40% of the workforce. While management provided the standard agency contract, auditor inspection of individual employment contracts for a sample of 22 such workers revealed that all contained a clause stipulating a punitive "early termination fee" equivalent to three months' gross salary, which is to be paid by the worker to the agency should they resign prior to the completion of a 36-month contract period. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews conducted on 13 October 2023 with 8 migrant workers from this cohort, all of whom expressed a clear understanding of this fee as a significant barrier to leaving their employment, with one worker (Interview #7) stating, "I cannot leave, I owe too much money to the agent already." This practice effectively restricts workers’ freedom to terminate employment at their own will, constituting a form of bonded labor.
**Severity: Critical**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Compliance Area: EP3 – Emergency Preparedness & Response**
**Reference:** Audit Protocol Section 7.2; Findings Log #2024-016-EP-01

During the site inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, the audit team reviewed the factory's emergency preparedness documentation and observed conditions on the production floors and in the dormitory block. The documented emergency evacuation plan, last revised in Q4 2022, was found to be comprehensive on paper. However, observation of the second-floor cutting section revealed that two of the four primary evacuation routes were partially obstructed by temporary storage of WIP materials and a relocated cutting table, reducing the clear egress width to approximately 0.7 meters, below the 1.1-meter standard outlined in the factory's own plan. Furthermore, interviews with a randomly selected group of 14 workers from various departments indicated a significant lack of practical drill familiarity; while 12 workers could identify the general assembly point, only 3 could accurately describe the alternate evacuation route for their primary workstation, and no unannounced full-scale drill had been conducted in the past 12 months, contrary to the semi-annual requirement stipulated in the factory's procedures. This discrepancy between documented protocols and implemented practice, particularly regarding egress maintenance and drill frequency, constitutes a failure in maintaining operational readiness. **Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 017 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-017-04 | Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (ILO Core Convention 29)** Following a review of payroll records from March to May 2023 and interviews conducted on 17 May 2023 with 32 production workers across all departments, the audit team identified a procedural inconsistency regarding the voluntary nature of overtime. While management demonstrated a clear policy of overtime being voluntary, corroborated by the active workers' union committee, investigator observations of the overtime sign-up sheet for the week of 8 May 2023 revealed that the sheet was posted only on the day prior to the scheduled overtime, providing insufficient notice. Interviews with seven workers indicated that two felt indirect pressure to consent due to the short notice and departmental production targets, creating a perceived obligation, though no instances of direct coercion or disciplinary action for refusal were documented. This constitutes a failure in implementing robust systems to ensure all overtime is genuinely voluntary, a requirement under the client’s Code of Conduct, section 3.2. A corrective action plan (CAP) must address the overtime notification process. Severity: Minor

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref:** EP-2024-017-03
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (EP)
**Date of Inspection:** 23 April 2024

**Background & Observations:** The audit team, while acknowledging the factory's generally robust industrial relations framework, conducted a thorough review of emergency protocols and physical preparedness. Inspection of the primary cut-and-sew facility on 23 April 2024 revealed that the posted evacuation route maps in the western production wing (Building A, Sections 4-7) were outdated and did not reflect the current layout following the machinery reconfiguration completed in Q4 2023. Furthermore, during a documented review of training records, it was noted that the emergency response team (ERT) drill for the aforementioned wing was last conducted on 15 November 2023, exceeding the internal policy and client requirement for semi-annual drills.

**Worker Interview Context:** Interviews conducted with 12 workers from the affected sections, facilitated by union representatives on 24 April 2024, provided corroborating testimony. While workers expressed confidence in the overall grievance mechanism, several interviewees noted uncertainty regarding the precise assembly point for their section in the event of an emergency, with one worker (Interview ID: W-INT##-441) stating, "We know to run to the yard, but our old meeting spot is now blocked by the new storage containers."

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** This deficiency constitutes a failure to maintain clear and current emergency information and to conduct drills at the required frequency, potentially compromising a safe and orderly evacuation. The factory management is required to submit a corrective action plan (CAP) to update all evacuation plans and conduct a refresher ERT drill for Building A by 31 May 2024.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
During the review of human resources policies and procedures conducted on 17 March 2024, with follow-up worker interviews held between 18-19 March, the audit team identified a procedural inconsistency in the application of the factory's non-discrimination policy (HR-011). While management provided documented evidence of a strong grievance mechanism and generally positive labor relations, interviews with 8 female workers from the finishing department revealed a pattern of exclusion from a recent operator training program for automated cutting machines. The training, which took place in February 2024, was offered exclusively to 12 male employees, with supervisors citing physical demands as the primary rationale, despite no such requirement being listed in the official job description for the newly created positions (Ref: Job Code CUT-OP-03). This constitutes a failure to provide equal opportunity for advancement based on gender, a violation of Section 5.2 of the Client's Code of Conduct. **Severity: Moderate**. A corrective action plan must address the revision of selection criteria and include a remedial training opportunity for affected workers.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SAF-017-2023-014
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

During the comprehensive facility walkthrough, the audit team noted generally good housekeeping standards and clear emergency egress routes. However, a review of the chemical storage area for the screen printing section revealed improper handling of hazardous substances. Specifically, three 20-liter containers of flammable ink thinner (Product Code: TH-202) were observed stored directly on the floor without secondary containment, adjacent to an electrical panel, contravening section 7.3 of the factory's own Chemical Management Policy based on the local regulatory code. Interviews with four workers from the printing department on the same date confirmed they had received annual Hazard Communication training but expressed uncertainty about the specific risks associated with the thinner storage arrangement, relying primarily on supervision for handling instructions. The factory's joint safety committee, noted as active in the profile, had not flagged this issue in their last two monthly meeting minutes. This constitutes a failure to implement adequate control measures for identified fire and health risks. **Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: CL-2023-017-02 | Compliance Area: 3.1 Prohibition of Child Labor | Date of Discovery: October 26, 2023**

During a review of personnel files for a sample of 35 production workers, the audit team identified one individual, employed as a junior sewing machine helper in the knitwear section, whose provided national ID card, cross-referenced against the factory's own hiring records dated July 15, 2023, indicated a date of birth that would make the worker 14 years old at the time of employment, which is below the minimum age of 15 as stipulated by national law and relevant ILO conventions. Interview notes from a private discussion with the worker (Worker ID# 4482) on the audit date confirmed the individual commenced full-time work immediately after the documented hiring date and had not participated in any authorized light work or apprenticeship program. While management, upon immediate notification, demonstrated full cooperation and initiated the documented remedial procedures outlined in their strong grievance mechanism, which includes continued financial support for the child’s education, the presence of an underage worker in the production environment constitutes a direct breach of fundamental compliance protocols. **Severity: Critical**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-OT-017-2024-008
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1 & B.1.3)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2024

During the review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2024, and corroborated through electronic timeclock data extracts, it was observed that a significant portion of the workforce in the cutting and sewing departments exceeded the weekly overtime limits stipulated in both national law and the applicable Brand Code of Conduct. Specifically, for the week ending September 20, 2024, 127 out of 312 workers (approximately 41%) in these departments were recorded as having worked 78 hours, which constitutes 18 hours of overtime against a maximum permitted limit of 12 hours. Worker interviews conducted on October 26 with 14 employees from the affected departments confirmed this pattern, with several noting that while the overtime was voluntary and compensated correctly at the premium rate, the high volume of orders led to management encouraging extended shifts. It is noted that the factory's otherwise strong grievance mechanism, including union consultation, was utilized, with the union formally raising concerns about sustained excessive hours in a meeting with management on September 25, as per documented minutes; however, effective corrective action had not been implemented by the time of the audit. This represents a systemic failure in operational planning to manage order flow within legal working hour parameters.
**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 018 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
During the announced audit conducted on 12-14 March 2024, a review of payroll records for the 847 production workers identified a systematic pattern of wage deductions used as a disciplinary measure, a practice which constitutes a form of compelled labor under local law and international standards (refer to audit code FL-03). Investigator observations of posted workshop rules in the Sewing Department (Line 7) and subsequent confidential interviews with 14 workers, including 3 from the newly established embroidery unit, confirmed management routinely imposes fines for infractions such as imperfect stitching or talking during work hours, with deductions documented on pay slips as "quality penalties." This practice, while defended by the experienced management team as essential for maintaining product standards, effectively withholds earned wages to compel performance and instills a climate of economic coercion. Severity: Moderate. A corrective action plan must address the immediate cessation of this practice and the reimbursement of any such deductions levied within the last payroll cycle.

[Child Labor]
During the announced audit conducted on 12-14 November 2023, the audit team reviewed personnel files for a sample of 120 production workers hired within the preceding 12 months, as per standard procedure SCP-07A. While the majority of documentation was in order, the team identified a discrepancy regarding one individual, Mr. A. Rahman (Employee ID #018-3477), hired on 15 May 2023 for the finishing department. His provided national ID card, while appearing authentic, listed a date of birth that would make him 17 years and 8 months old at the time of hiring, which is below the minimum age of 18 as stipulated in the Factory’s own Code of Conduct and international standard ILO Convention 138. Interviews conducted with three workers from the same production line (Worker Interviews #45, #46, #47) corroborated the individual’s youthful appearance and suggested he had previously stated he was still in school the prior year. Management was unable to provide a verified birth certificate upon request during the closing meeting, citing provincial administrative delays. This constitutes a failure in the age verification process during recruitment. **Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: H.01 - Harassment & Abuse (incl. Harsh or Inhumane Treatment)**
**Ref: AUD-018-2024-F3.2.1**

**Background & Context:** The factory's documented Social Compliance Manual, version 4.1, dated January 2024, includes a clear policy prohibiting all forms of harassment, abuse, and corporal punishment, which was communicated to all employees via mandatory orientation sessions as per management records. During the inspection conducted on 17-18 April 2024, the audit team focused on verifying the implementation and worker understanding of these mechanisms through document review, management interviews, and confidential worker interviews.

**Observations & Interviews:** While management provided a grievance procedure flowchart and reported no formal complaints logged in the dedicated register for the current quarter, confidential interviews with 47 workers across sewing, finishing, and quality departments revealed a consistent pattern of concerns. Multiple workers (approximately 12 of those interviewed) described instances where line supervisors, particularly in the woven trousers section, used verbally abusive language, including shouting, public humiliation, and derogatory remarks regarding work speed, as a routine method of line management. One worker provided a specific account from 12 April 2024, where a supervisor threatened disciplinary action, including the withholding of the monthly attendance bonus, for a worker who requested a bathroom break outside the scheduled group break period. This creates an environment of intimidation and fear of retaliation, which likely suppresses the formal reporting of such incidents through the official channels.

**Finding:** The factory is in violation of the Harassment and Abuse standard. The evidence indicates that the established policy is not effectively implemented or enforced at the supervisory level, permitting a pattern of verbal abuse and threats that constitute psychological coercion and a hostile work environment. The fear of retaliation undermines the integrity of the grievance procedure. A formal corrective action plan is required to address supervisory training, implementation monitoring, and the cultivation of a speak-up culture.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: OS&H-018-07**
During the inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team focused on the maintenance of mechanical guarding on production equipment, a previously cited item from the 2022 audit. It was observed that while the majority of guarding on high-risk machinery (e.g., cutting presses, band saws) in the cutting and sewing sections remained compliant, a follow-up inspection of the five industrial washing machines in the wet processing area revealed that the interlocking safety guards on machines #3 and #5 had been deliberately bypassed using zip-ties, a condition confirmed by the maintenance supervisor to be in place for "at least two weeks" to expedite routine loading and unloading. This was corroborated in separate, confidential interviews with three operators from that section, who expressed awareness of the hazard but felt pressured by production targets to not report the issue. This constitutes a failure to maintain effective engineering controls to prevent contact with moving parts, creating an immediate and serious risk of laceration or amputation. **Severity: Critical** Corrective action must be initiated prior to the resumption of operations on this equipment, with verification required.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Audit Code: WH.01)**
A review of time and attendance records for the period of January 1 to March 15, 2024, indicated a systemic pattern of non-compliance with local legal limits and client Code of Conduct requirements regarding maximum weekly working hours. Inspector observations and payroll reconciliation for the cut-and-sew department (approximately 320 workers) revealed that for seven of the eleven weeks reviewed, a significant portion of the workforce exceeded the 60-hour weekly threshold, with peaks of 78 hours recorded during the third week of February to meet a shipping deadline for a key buyer. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews conducted on March 18, where 8 of 12 randomly selected operatives from that department stated their regular work schedules included mandatory, but unofficially recorded, overtime on Sundays. While management provided historical data demonstrating a downward trend, the current findings represent a failure of management systems to control production planning and accurately capture all hours worked, leading to a high risk of worker fatigue. **Severity: Moderate**. A corrective action plan must address root causes in production scheduling and timekeeping procedures.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FAC-018-24-FOA-001**  
During the assessment period of October 15-18, 2024, in the compliance area of Freedom of Association and Collective Bargaining, it was noted that while the factory maintains a formal, management-established Worker Representative Committee (WRC) as per local law, our review uncovered substantive gaps in genuine collective bargaining mechanisms. Inspector observations and document review revealed that the current WRC election process, last held in March 2023, was conducted without a secret ballot and management retains approval authority over candidate nominations, thereby potentially influencing the body's independence. This was corroborated in confidential worker interviews, where 7 out of 12 randomly selected production operators from Sewing Lines 3 and 5 stated they were unaware of any alternative means to independently organize or associate, and expressed no confidence in the WRC's ability to negotiate terms of employment without management interference. Although no active trade union is present, the procedural restrictions and lack of demonstrable training for worker representatives on their rights under international standards constitute a failure to promote and facilitate the principles of freedom of association in practice.  
**Severity: Moderate** — Corrective Action Plan required within 30 days to revise election protocols and implement awareness programs.
