Factory 019 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Discrimination]
During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 15-16 May 2024, the audit team identified systemic hiring practices that appear to constitute discrimination on the basis of gender and regional origin. A review of the last six months of personnel files (Ref: HR-019-01-2024 to HR-019-06-2024) indicated that 97% of sewing machine operator positions are filled by female workers, while all supervisory, maintenance, and warehouse roles are held exclusively by male employees. Furthermore, interviews with 21 workers, predominantly migrants from Guizhou and Yunnan provinces, revealed a consistent anecdotal pattern that local applicants from the surrounding county are preferentially hired for less physically demanding and higher-paying auxiliary positions, a practice confirmed in discussion with the HR manager who cited "communication efficiency and stability." This constitutes a violation of Code of Conduct section 4.2 on Equal Opportunity. A follow-up note has been issued to management to revise hiring policies and implement corrective action training. Severity: Moderate.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: 019-OSH-04**
During the onsite inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team focused on the fire safety provisions within the cutting and sewing departments. It was observed that of the twelve emergency exits mapped on the factory's provided floor plan, three exits (designated #4, #7, and #9) were found to be partially obstructed by stored WIP materials and, more critically, exit #7 was secured with a padlocked chain during operational hours, a condition confirmed during both day and night shift observations. Interviews conducted with a sample of 14 production line workers, predominantly migrants from neighboring provinces, revealed a general lack of awareness of emergency evacuation procedures, with only two workers able to correctly identify their primary assembly point. This constitutes a clear violation of section 7.2 of the Client's Code of Conduct and local statutory fire safety regulations, presenting an imminent and serious risk to worker safety in the event of an emergency, particularly given the high occupant load and combustible materials present. **Severity: Critical** A follow-up verification must confirm the immediate removal of all obstructions and the permanent unencumbered access to all emergency egress routes, supported by documented worker training.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-019-04**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (CC Code: A1.1)**
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** During the document review and physical inspection of the dormitory facilities, it was noted that the factory employs a predominantly migrant workforce, a profile with documented vulnerability to forced labor indicators. While no systematic retention of identity documents was observed in a central location, subsequent worker interviews revealed a concerning pattern. Interview notes from three out of eight randomly selected production line workers (Worker IDs #87, #112, and #203) indicated that their original national identity cards are held by the factory's security department under a stated policy of "safekeeping," with access to retrieve them granted only with supervisory approval, which was reported by one worker as being routinely delayed for several days. This practice, coupled with the factory's remote rural location and the acknowledged limited access to external legal support, effectively restricts workers' freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment at will, creating a situation of coercion.

**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 020 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: 2024-WB-020-003 | Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits | Audit Date: October 23-24, 2024**

During the review of wage records for the period of September 2024, a significant discrepancy was noted between the factory's payroll register and the attendance logs for the peak production month. It was observed that for 127 seasonal workers hired specifically for the Fall/Winter collection rush, overtime hours exceeding the legal weekly limit were not compensated at the statutory premium rate, as mandated by national labor law. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews conducted on October 24, wherein 8 out of 12 workers from the cutting and sewing departments stated they consistently worked 12-hour days, 7 days a week during the last three weeks of September, yet their pay slips reflected only the standard hourly rate for all hours worked, with no distinction for overtime premiums. Furthermore, cross-referencing with production orders confirmed intense pressure from multiple brands to meet concurrent deadlines during this period. The factory management acknowledged the practice, citing the subcontractor model and fluctuating order volumes as contributing factors, but failed to provide any remedial action plan for back-payment. **Severity: Critical**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-020-2023-08-FOA-001
**Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FA & CB)
**Date of Assessment:** October 26-27, 2023

**Background & Context:** Factory 020 operates as a subcontractor for multiple mid-market apparel brands, resulting in fluctuating order volumes and a pronounced seasonal production cycle. Management indicated a current workforce of approximately 1,200 employees, with an estimated 40% classified as temporary or seasonal workers hired within the last three months to meet peak demand. The factory's stated policy, as documented in the employee handbook revision 2.1, acknowledges workers' rights to associate freely.

**Observations & Interviews:** During a physical inspection of the premises, no evidence of any trade union presence, elected worker representatives, or posted information regarding FA & CB rights was observed in common areas, cafeterias, or notice boards. Interviews were conducted with 27 workers from various production departments, selected at random. Of those interviewed, 18 were seasonal workers. A consistent narrative emerged wherein 22 workers expressed unawareness of any mechanism to form or join a worker representative body. Several senior sewing operators, with over two years of tenure, noted in separate interviews that "discussion of group complaints" is discouraged by line supervisors, with one operator (interview #14) quoting a supervisor as stating, "this kind of talk is for permanent factories, not for temporary workers like us." Management was unable to provide any records of meetings, elections, or communications pertaining to collective bargaining or worker representation structures.

**Conclusion:** The combination of a transient workforce, the absence of any functional representative committee or union, and managerial practices that implicitly dissuade collective activity effectively nullifies workers' rights to freedom of association and collective bargaining as per local law and international standards. This constitutes a systemic failure.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Required:** Corrective Action Plan (CAP) must detail steps to establish a democratically elected worker representative committee inclusive of seasonal staff, followed by formal management training and documented worker awareness sessions.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources (AUD-REF: HRC-07)**
**Finding Reference:** F020-HR-2024-003

**Background & Context:** The audit scope for this area included verification of employment contracts, clarity of terms, and adherence to local labor law provisions for all worker categories. Factory 020 operates as a subcontractor with a pronounced seasonal workflow, employing a significant proportion of temporary staff during peak production periods preceding major shipping deadlines, as noted in the production planning documents reviewed.

**Observations & Evidence:** During the document review on 14 March 2024, a sample of 37 personnel files for workers hired within the last six months was examined. It was observed that 23 of these files, all pertaining to temporary seasonal workers engaged for the Autumn/Winter 2024 collection, contained only a basic, one-page "Registration Form" signed by the worker. This form lacked numerous statutory elements required under local labor law, including but not limited to: a specified duration of employment, detailed job description, explicit grounds for termination, and a clear outline of social insurance contributions. The management representative indicated that formal contracts are typically issued only to "core" permanent staff, citing the administrative burden and transient nature of seasonal labor.

**Worker Interview Corroboration:** Interviews conducted with a randomized selection of 12 temporary workers from the cutting and finishing departments on the same date confirmed this practice. Nine interviewed workers stated they had signed only a single paper upon hiring and were unsure of their exact employment status or notice period. Several expressed concern regarding the lack of written confirmation of their wage rate for overtime hours, noting they relied on verbal assurances from line supervisors.

**Conclusion:** This constitutes a failure to establish a lawful employment relationship as defined by national labor regulations, depriving workers of written certainty regarding essential terms and conditions. The practice is systemic for the seasonal workforce, which constitutes approximately 60% of the current headcount. A formal corrective action plan is required to align employment documentation with legal standards for all personnel.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** CL-020-2024-01
**Compliance Area:** Child Labor
**Factory Code:** 020
**Date of On-Site Inspection:** 16 October 2024

During the documentation review of personnel files for the 87 production line workers employed during the peak season (August-October 2024), a discrepancy was identified concerning one individual, Mr. Chen Wei (Employee ID: PL-447). While his provided national ID card, photocopied and filed, indicates a date of birth placing him above the minimum working age, subsequent confidential interviews conducted with a random sample of 12 workers from the sewing section on 16 October 2024 yielded conflicting testimony. Two separate workers, interviewed independently, indicated that the individual in question is known to be the younger brother of a senior line mechanic and is believed to be only 15 years old, having been brought in to assist with meeting urgent shipment deadlines for a major outerwear brand. This allegation, combined with the factory's acknowledged profile as a subcontractor utilizing a transient seasonal workforce under high production pressure, creates reasonable doubt regarding the effectiveness of age verification procedures and constitutes a potential violation of local labor law and international standards. **Severity: Critical** A formal age verification follow-up, requiring original documentation and potentially a guardian interview, must be conducted immediately, and the factory's recruitment and document checking protocols require systemic review.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**COMPLIANCE AREA: OCCUPATIONAL SAFETY & HEALTH (OSH) - REF: AUDIT-020-OSH-03**
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Factory 020 operates as a subcontractor with a fluctuating, seasonal workforce, which necessitates robust and consistently enforced safety onboarding and hazard control protocols. During the inspection of the cutting and sewing sections, the auditor observed a significant disconnect between documented procedures and on-floor implementation. Specifically, the inspection focused on machine guarding and personal protective equipment (PPE) compliance, as these are high-risk areas given the production pressure and frequent turnover of operational staff.

**Observations & Interviews:** A walkthrough of the production floor on the aforementioned date revealed that 7 out of 12 inspected industrial sewing machines had their needle guards either removed or intentionally tied back, ostensibly to facilitate faster threading and operation, a practice verbally confirmed by two line supervisors to reduce bottlenecks. Furthermore, during worker interviews conducted with 8 randomly selected operators from the sewing section, 6 confirmed they had not received any formal safety training specific to the equipment they were currently operating since being hired for the peak season, with two stating they were "shown quickly by a colleague." This is corroborated by training records which show no OSH induction for 60% of the workforce hired within the last 90 days.

**Finding:** The factory has failed to ensure the effective implementation of its stated machine safety policies, creating an imminent risk of laceration and amputation injuries due to unguarded moving parts, compounded by a systemic lack of safety training for a significant portion of the seasonal workforce. This constitutes a failure to provide a safe working environment and meet the requirements of section 7.8 of the applicable code of conduct.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: SA-020-2023-09-D01 | Compliance Area: Discrimination (Category: Hiring & Employment Practices) | Audit Date: September 12-14, 2023**

During the review of hiring and personnel records, and subsequent worker interviews, the audit team identified patterns indicative of discriminatory hiring practices based on age and gender for specific production roles. Background context notes that Factory 020 operates with a highly seasonal workforce and faces significant pressure to ramp up production for multiple concurrent orders. Inspector observations confirmed a near-total segregation of the workforce in the cutting and finishing sections, with all 27 operatives observed in the cutting section being male. Conversely, interviews with 15 workers across three production lines revealed that female applicants are routinely directed toward sewing and packing roles, with four interviewees stating they were explicitly told the cutting section was "not suitable for women" during their hiring process in June/July 2023. This is further supported by a review of the last 120 hiring tickets, which showed zero female placements into the cutting department over the past eight months, despite applications. This constitutes a violation of the factory's own code of conduct and international labor standards regarding equal opportunity. **Severity: Moderate**. *Follow-up Required: Management must review and revise hiring protocols and provide documented training to all hiring personnel on non-discrimination policies.*

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref:** FL-020-2023-08
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor / Coercive Labor Practices
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Context:** Factory 020 operates as a subcontractor for several mid-market apparel brands, characterized by a high-volume, short-lead-time production model. The facility employs a significant seasonal workforce, with a noted increase of approximately 60% during peak periods. The audit focus included a review of recruitment practices, wage payment, and freedom of movement.

**Observations & Interviews:** During document review, it was noted that 127 of 400 production line workers (32%) had submitted government-issued identity documents to factory management, ostensibly for "safekeeping and registration purposes," with no clear written policy or receipt provided for such deposits. In parallel confidential interviews conducted on October 25-26 with 23 randomly selected workers from this group, 14 individuals reported that their documents were being withheld without their explicit consent. Several workers expressed concern that retrieving their documents prior to the end of their seasonal contract, which coincides with the Lunar New Year period, would result in the forfeiture of two months' "retention bonuses" and unpaid overtime accrued, a mechanism described internally as "loyalty compensation." This practice effectively creates a situation of economic coercion, restricting workers' ability to voluntarily terminate employment due to the significant potential financial loss and lack of access to personal documentation.

**Conclusion:** The withholding of personal identity documents coupled with a wage deduction scheme that penalizes early departure constitutes a violation of the Forced Labor principle of freedom to terminate employment without penalty. This is exacerbated by the factory's seasonal model, which places disproportionate pressure on migrant workers. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan must address the immediate return of all documents, revision of the bonus policy to be non-coercive, and implementation of a documented, receipt-based personal effects policy.


============================================================
Factory 021 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: small family-owned factory with 50-100 workers, limited HR capacity, first time being audited
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** CL-2023-021-01F **| Compliance Area:** Child Labor Prevention (CLP) **| Date of On-Site Verification:** October 26, 2023.

During a review of personnel files for a sample of 30 production workers, cross-referenced with observed workers on the sewing floor, it was noted that Employee ID# 021-047, presented as being 18 years of age, appeared significantly younger. Subsequent inspection of supporting documentation revealed that the government-issued identification card provided was of poor visual quality, raising authenticity concerns. In a private interview conducted on the same date, the worker in question, when separated from the line supervisor (a relative of the owner), disclosed a date of birth that would make them 15 years of age, which was later corroborated by a birth certificate provided by the worker's parent. The factory management, upon confrontation, stated they were unaware of the falsified document due to a lack of formal age verification procedures and reliance on familial referrals. This constitutes a confirmed instance of child labor. **Severity: Critical.** Follow-up required: Immediate remediation and implementation of a rigorous age verification protocol.

[Emergency Preparedness]
During the site inspection conducted on October 26, 2023, it was noted that while the factory has posted a basic evacuation plan at the main entrance, the overall state of emergency preparedness is underdeveloped and does not meet the required standard as per clause 8.2 of the client's Code of Conduct. Observations included obstructed access to two of the four identified fire extinguishers (units #FE-03 and #FE-07 in the cutting and storage sections, respectively), a lack of documented, role-specific emergency response procedures, and no visible designation of assembly points outside the building. Interviews with 7 randomly selected production workers confirmed that no emergency drills have been conducted within the last 12 months, with several workers expressing uncertainty about evacuation routes from their workstations. The absence of a drill program and impeded access to life-saving equipment constitutes a significant failure in providing a minimum level of worker safety. **Severity: Moderate.** Corrective actions must include the immediate clearing of extinguisher access, the development and communication of a formal emergency plan, and the scheduling of a full facility evacuation drill with documented participation.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FOACB-021-01**

During the audit conducted on March 12, 2024, it was established that while no formal trade union is present, the management has not demonstrated proactive steps to ensure the effective implementation of workers' rights to freedom of association as per client code of conduct section 7.1 and local law. Investigator observations and a review of HR documentation confirmed the absence of any policy or communication to workers regarding these rights. Furthermore, in confidential interviews held with 12 production workers on the audit date, 10 indicated they were unaware of their right to form or join a workers' association and expressed concern about potential management retaliation should they attempt to organize, citing the factory's small, familial structure. No evidence of past disciplinary actions related to association was found, but the pervasive lack of awareness and fear constitute a failure in the factory's duty to inform and protect. **Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-021-2023-08-F07 **| Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SA8000:2014 Section 5) **| Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Context:** During the document review and worker interviews conducted as part of the initial social compliance audit, the audit team sought to evaluate the factory's policies and practices regarding non-discrimination in hiring, compensation, access to training, promotion, termination, and retirement. The factory's provided Employee Handbook, dated January 2023, contains a broad non-discrimination clause. However, during interviews with 12 production workers from various departments on October 26, 2023, a consistent pattern emerged regarding hiring practices for stitching operator positions. **Inspector Observations & Worker Interview Notes:** Three (3) separate workers, with tenures ranging from 8 months to 3 years, independently stated during confidential interviews that the Production Manager (Mr. Chen, owner's relative) has a stated preference for hiring female workers for all sewing machine operator roles, based on a belief that "women have more patience and thinner fingers for this delicate work." Review of hiring records for the last 12 months showed that 28 of 29 new hires for operator positions were female, while all 4 hires for loading, maintenance, and cutting helper roles were male. This constitutes a *de facto* hiring policy based on gender, which is not justified by bona fide occupational requirements and contravenes the factory's own policy and international standards. The management representative, when presented with this preliminary finding, acknowledged the pattern but cited tradition and efficiency as contributing factors, indicating a need for formal policy implementation and training.

**Severity: Moderate**

**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan required to include a revised, detailed non-discrimination policy, documented training for all hiring managers, and a review of current job descriptions to ensure they are gender-neutral.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref:** OSH-2023-021-04
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Observations:** During the walkthrough of the production floor, the audit team observed a persistent and systemic failure in the control of hazardous energy (lockout/tagout procedures). Specifically, at three separate power press stations (Machine IDs: PP-07, PP-09, PP-12), maintenance and cleaning activities were witnessed on 10/26/2023 where the machines were stopped but not isolated from their energy sources, with the main power switches left in the "on" position and no personal padlocks or danger tags applied. This was corroborated by the Production Supervisor, who stated that for quick cleaning of jams, formal lockout is not typically followed due to time pressures.
**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted with five (5) machine operators confirmed this practice is routine, with one operator noting, "We just make sure it's off and tell others not to touch it." There is no documented lockout/tagout procedure available on the floor, and workers, including the sole part-time maintenance technician, have received no formal training on the control of hazardous energy, as verified by a review of training records.
**Conclusion:** This constitutes a serious and imminent danger, exposing workers to potential amputation or crushing injuries from the unexpected energization of machinery during servicing. The factory has not established an effective energy control program as required.
**Severity: Critical**

Factory 022 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: SA-022-D-04 | Compliance Area: A.7 Non-Discrimination** | **Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023 | **Reference:** Company Code of Conduct, Section 3.2; Local Labor Law, Article 15.

Following the fire incident on August 12, 2023, and the subsequent rebuilding of the western cutting section, the audit team reviewed hiring and assignment practices for the period of August to October. While management provided policy documentation affirming non-discrimination, interviews conducted on October 26 with 17 production workers from the rebuilt section revealed a consistent pattern. Twelve of the interviewed workers, all of whom are over the age of 45, reported being excluded from reassignment to the new, automated cutting machines, with supervisory staff citing a need for "younger, faster learners who can handle new technology." These positions were instead filled by workers under 35, constituting a de facto policy of age-based discrimination in placement and training opportunities during the recovery period. The factory failed to demonstrate objective criteria for these reassignments, thereby violating established standards for equal opportunity. **Severity: Moderate.** **Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan must include retraining for HR and production supervisors on non-discriminatory practices and the establishment of a transparent, skill-based reassignment procedure.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2024-022-03A
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Emergency Preparedness & Fire Safety)
**Date of Inspection:** 17 May 2024

Following the significant fire incident of 12 April 2024, which resulted in a full production stoppage and subsequent special oversight by the local Fire Safety Bureau, this audit focused heavily on the factory's remediation and restoration of fire safety protocols. While management has demonstrated a clear intent to address structural damage, observations on the date of inspection confirmed that the newly installed fire hose reels on the reinstated second-floor sewing lines (Blocks C and D) lacked adequate water pressure, with a flow test yielding less than 30 liters per minute, which is below the minimum standard required for effective fire suppression. Interviews with four workers from these blocks revealed they had not received any practical training on the use of this new equipment, with one worker noting, "We saw them put the hoses in last week, but no one has shown us how they work." Although the factory remains under heightened external monitoring, the failure to ensure operational firefighting equipment and provide corresponding employee training in a post-fire recovery context constitutes a serious lapse. **Severity: Critical**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FL-022-08-2024
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Section 1, ILO Convention 29)
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident on 12 July 2024, which resulted in the partial closure of Dyeing Workshop B, the audit team paid specific attention to workforce management and freedom of movement, given the factory's stated pressures to recover production capacity and its current status under special monitoring by district authorities. Our review focused on the contractual and practical conditions for the 127 workers reassigned from the damaged workshop.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** Documentation review identified that all reassigned workers had signed a revised job assignment addendum; however, the addendum text, particularly in Clause 4.1, was vague on the right to refuse reassignment. During unannounced inspections of the dormitory facility on 21 August 2024, it was noted that 14 identity cards (belonging to workers from the affected workshop) were being held by line supervisors under a stated "temporary safekeeping" policy initiated post-fire. In confidential interviews conducted with 9 of these workers on 22 August 2024, three stated they felt compelled to accept overtime on rest days due to management references to the "special recovery period" and concerns that refusal could affect their re-employment once the workshop is rebuilt. While no evidence of physical coercion or bonded labor was found, the combination of document retention and the exploitative use of the factory's vulnerable post-disaster circumstances to pressure overtime constitutes a coercion risk.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to ensure that work is voluntary, as the retention of personal documents and the applied psychological pressure, leveraging the workers' precarious situation post-incident, undermines the principle of free consent. A corrective action plan must address the immediate return of all personal documents and the revision of policies to explicitly prohibit such practices, even during recovery operations.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Code:** CHR-022-07
**Date of On-site Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident of August 15, 2023, which resulted in significant operational disruption, the audit focused on post-incident workforce management and contractual adherence, given the factory's status under special monitoring by local labor authorities. **Inspector Observations:** A review of personnel files for 87 workers re-hired or newly hired post-August 15th revealed that 23 files (approximately 26%) lacked a signed employment contract, contrary to local labor law Article 12. **Worker Interview Notes:** Interviews conducted on October 26 with 15 affected workers confirmed they had commenced work without receiving or signing a formal contract, though they acknowledged receiving verbal terms of employment and their first month's wages. While management attributed this lapse to the urgent need to restart production and re-establish the workforce, the absence of written contracts constitutes a failure to formalize the terms of employment, depriving workers of critical legal protections, especially during a recovery period. **Follow-up Required:** Management must immediately rectify this omission for all current employees and implement a documented onboarding procedure to prevent recurrence. Severity: Moderate.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref:** FA-022-2023-08-FOA-001
**Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: FOA.01, FOA.03)
**Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

Following the fire incident on August 15, 2023, and the subsequent special monitoring regime implemented by local authorities, the audit team conducted a detailed review of industrial relations practices. While management provided documentation of a legally registered enterprise-level trade union, interviews with 17 production workers across three departments revealed a substantive gap in practical implementation. Multiple workers, speaking on condition of anonymity, indicated that union representatives had been informally discouraged from conducting meetings or discussing collective grievances since the factory resumed partial operations in September, with one representative stating, "We were told by the production manager that now is not the time for internal disputes." This was corroborated by the absence of any minutes from union meetings post-August 2023, despite the union constitution requiring monthly sessions. The situation constitutes a restriction of workers' right to organize freely, particularly during a critical recovery period. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up verification is required within 90 days to confirm the union is able to operate without interference and that collective bargaining mechanisms are fully functional.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-2023-022-004 | Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Local Labour Law, Article 28 & Buyer Code of Conduct, Section 7.2)**
Following the fire incident on 15 March 2023 and the subsequent production delays, the audit focused on reviewing working hour practices for the period of April to June 2023, a period of acknowledged recovery and heightened shipment pressure. Inspector observations of production line attendance logs from the week of 22 May 2023, cross-referenced with payroll data, indicated a pattern of excessive overtime, with approximately 65% of the sewing line workforce exceeding the 36-hour monthly overtime limit stipulated by local law. Interviews conducted on 12 July 2023 with eight (8) production workers from Department B corroborated this, with four workers stating they had worked 13 consecutive days without a rest day in early June, and all interviewed workers confirming verbal management pressure to meet revised deadlines. While management cited the extraordinary circumstances post-fire and the need for business continuity, the systematic nature of the violations, lack of documented voluntary consent for excess overtime, and failure to provide compensatory time-off constitute a breach of fundamental requirements. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up review of corrective actions, including revised production planning and time-off schedules, is required within 60 days.

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding CL-003: Verification of Worker Age Eligibility**
Following the fire incident on 15.03.2024 and subsequent workforce restructuring noted in the factory’s submitted remediation plan, a targeted review of hiring protocols and worker age documentation was conducted. During floor observation on 24.10.2024, the audit team noted two individuals in the finishing section whose physical appearance raised initial concerns regarding potential minimum age non-compliance. Cross-referencing with the factory’s register and conducting confidential interviews with 12 production workers, one worker, presented as "Trainee Helper," was unable to produce original identification. The factory’s copied record indicated an age of 16, but the provided document was of poor quality and lacked necessary security features, preventing reliable verification. As the factory is under special monitoring, the failure to maintain a reliable system for age verification constitutes a significant breach of protocol, despite no conclusive evidence of underage labor being established.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: SA-022-2023-HR-04 | Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (CCS Section 7.0) | Date of Discovery: 17 October 2023**

Following the fire incident of Q3-2023 and the subsequent relocation of production lines to a temporary facility, the audit team conducted a review of management systems and worker welfare pertaining to harassment and abuse. Inspector observations confirmed the existence of a revised code of conduct posted in common areas and a dedicated grievance box at the main entrance. However, during confidential interviews conducted on 17-18 October with a randomized sample of 32 production line workers (approximately 15% of the workforce), 7 workers (constituting 21.9% of interviewees) disclosed a pervasive atmosphere of heightened verbal abuse and intimidation by certain line supervisors, particularly in the sewing section. These workers reported that supervisory staff, under perceived pressure to meet accelerated recovery production targets, frequently used derogatory language, threats of dismissal, and public humiliation tactics towards operators making errors. The factory's newly appointed HR officer acknowledged awareness of "increased tensions" but cited ongoing crisis management priorities as having delayed the implementation of planned supervisory training on appropriate disciplinary methods. While a formal grievance procedure is documented, the interviewed workers expressed a unanimous lack of confidence in its anonymity or efficacy, fearing retaliation. This constitutes a failure to ensure a workplace free from harassment and abuse, exacerbated by the post-crisis operational environment. **Severity: Moderate.** *Follow-up Note: Required Corrective Action Plan must include immediate, targeted training for all supervisory staff, re-communication of zero-tolerance policy via worker meetings, and establishment of a follow-up interview schedule with the workers who came forward, to be submitted to the auditor by 30 November 2023.*

[Wages & Benefits]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: WG-FI-022-09-2024**
**COMPLIANCE AREA: WAGES & BENEFITS (PAYROLL PERIOD: AUGUST 2024)**
**BACKGROUND & CONTEXT:** Following the fire incident on July 15, 2024, and the subsequent partial operational shutdown, the audit focused on verifying that wage payments during the recovery period were made accurately and in a timely manner, particularly for workers engaged in cleaning and rehabilitation work, which fell outside normal production cycles. Inspector observations of posted wage statements and a detailed review of payroll registers for August revealed significant discrepancies. Interviews conducted on September 10, 2024, with a sample of 17 workers from the cleaning crews confirmed that while their regular August salaries were paid on September 5th (a delay acknowledged and attributed to bank processing issues), the separate daily allowances for the hazardous cleanup work, verbally agreed at 150% of the normal daily rate, were not included in the payment and no separate payslip or accounting for these hours was provided, constituting a failure to compensate for all hours worked at the correct rate.
**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 023 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-023-07: Review of Policies and Practices Related to Forced Labor and Involuntary Work**
Following the significant fire incident on March 15, 2024, which damaged the western cutting section, the audit team conducted a focused assessment of forced labor indicators, particularly concerning the factory's recovery operations and the status of its workforce. During document review on June 10-11, 2024, it was confirmed that all 127 production workers have been retained on payroll and are presenting for work; however, interviews with a randomized selection of 18 employees from the sewing department revealed consistent statements that management has withheld all identity documents (national IDs and work permits) since the fire, citing a need for "administrative reprocessing and verification with local authorities." This practice, confirmed in a separate interview with the HR Manager, removes workers' freedom of movement and creates a situation of undue coercion, as workers expressed fear that leaving employment would result in permanent loss of their crucial documents. While the factory's challenging circumstances are acknowledged, the retention of personal documents is a clear violation of the benchmark code FL.01 and constitutes a severe infringement on worker liberty.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note: Corrective Action Plan (CAP) required within 48 hours, detailing immediate document return process and revised policy submission to the audit body.**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2024-023-08
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSHA 29 CFR 1910, Local Fire Code Article VII)
**Background & Context:** The facility, which is under enhanced oversight by the Municipal Fire Department following a significant structural fire on 12 March 2024, was observed to be in a state of active reconstruction in the western cutting section. The audit focus was to evaluate the restoration of minimum life safety protocols and general occupational health conditions during this recovery period.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** During the site walkthrough on 22 April 2024, it was noted that temporary electrical cabling, powering reactivated sewing lines adjacent to the damaged area, was routed across primary pedestrian aisles and secured to floor surfaces with duct tape, creating pronounced trip hazards and presenting potential cable insulation damage. Furthermore, three of five newly reinstalled emergency exit doors in the packing warehouse were found to be obstructed by staged pallets of finished goods, and a fourth was functionally compromised as its panic bar mechanism was jammed with debris. Interviews conducted with seven (7) production workers from the affected departments revealed that the mandatory monthly emergency evacuation drill, stipulated in the factory's own Corrective Action Plan submitted to authorities, has not been conducted since the incident, with two workers explicitly stating they were unaware of any updated assembly points. Measurements of the primary aisle in the sewing hall showed a width of 28 inches, falling below the 36-inch minimum required for unobstructed egress.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up Note:** Despite being under special monitoring, the factory has failed to maintain basic housekeeping and egress integrity during its reconstruction phase, resulting in multiple imminent fire safety and life safety hazards that were directly contributory to the cause of the previous major incident. Management must immediately clear all exit routes, secure all temporary utilities in an approved manner, and conduct a full emergency drill. A re-inspection of these items is required within 14 days.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: SA-023-2024-DF-07 // Compliance Area: C7 - Discrimination & Harassment // Date of Finding: October 26, 2024**
During the review of human resources policies and subsequent worker interviews conducted on October 24-25, 2024, a pattern of differential treatment was identified regarding workers affected by the fire incident of August 2024. Factory management, in an effort to expedite recovery of production capacity in Sewing Section B, has instituted a mandatory overtime policy applicable only to a specific demographic group; namely, 37 female workers who were transferred from the finishing department post-incident. This policy was not applied to male workers in the same section performing identical tasks. Interviews with 8 of the affected workers confirmed they were threatened with reassignment to less desirable cleaning duties or termination for non-compliance, while male counterparts were offered voluntary overtime with premium pay. This constitutes discriminatory practice based on gender, creating a hostile work environment and violating section 4.2 of the company's Code of Conduct and local labor law provisions on equal treatment. **Severity: Moderate.** *Follow-up Note: This finding is particularly concerning given the factory's status under special monitoring by authorities; corrective action plans must be submitted for review by the oversight committee.*

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: EP-023-07-2024**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date(s) of Inspection: July 15-16, 2024**

**Context & Observations:** Given the factory's profile as an entity recovering from a recent significant fire incident (noted in Q2 2024) and currently under special monitoring by district authorities, the audit team placed heightened scrutiny on emergency preparedness protocols. During the physical inspection on July 15, it was observed that while primary exit routes in the rebuilt sewing section were clear, secondary egress routes in the fabric warehouse and the cut-and-pack department on the west wing were partially obstructed by stored WIP materials and roll cages, impeding a clear path of egress. Furthermore, a review of evacuation drill records for the current calendar year indicated only one documented full facility drill was conducted post-incident, dated May 30, 2024, which is non-compliant with the mandated quarterly frequency per the client's Code of Conduct and local regulatory requirements for high-risk facilities under enhanced surveillance.

**Interviews & Documentation:** Interviews conducted with 11 workers across two shifts on July 16 revealed inconsistent understanding of revised assembly point locations, with 7 workers incorrectly identifying the primary muster area. The designated emergency warden for the cutting section was unaware of his responsibilities, as confirmed through role-specific questioning. Despite the presence of newly installed fire suppression equipment, the documented emergency response plan had not been updated to reflect post-renovation floor plans and revised personnel assignments, creating a critical gap in procedural readiness.

**Conclusion:** The failure to maintain unobstructed secondary exits, conduct required quarterly drills, ensure comprehensive worker training on revised procedures, and maintain an updated emergency plan constitutes a systemic failure in emergency preparedness, particularly egregious given the facility's recent history and special monitoring status. This presents an imminent risk to worker safety.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan (CAP) required within 48 hours; verification of cleared egress routes and a conducted drill must be submitted with photographic evidence prior to the re-audit window.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB-3.1, 4.2, 5.7)**
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident on 15 February 2024 and the subsequent placement of the facility under special monitoring by the District Labor Office, this audit included a heightened review of wage records for the preceding three pay periods to assess stability of income for workers during the recovery period. Inspector observations of payroll ledgers for March 2024, cross-referenced with time cards and bank transfer statements, revealed a consistent pattern of non-compliance regarding overtime remuneration. Specifically, for all overtime hours worked beyond the legal weekly threshold, the factory applied the standard hourly rate instead of the legally mandated premium rate, a violation confirmed in interviews with 14 randomly selected production workers on 2 April 2024, who verified their payslips did not reflect the correct overtime calculation. This constitutes a failure to comply with Section 12.4 of the national labor law and represents a systemic, rather than isolated, deduction of worker earnings during a period of acknowledged operational duress.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-023-2024-08
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1 - B.1.3)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2024

**Finding:** During the review of time and attendance records for the period of September 1 to October 25, 2024, and through corroborating worker interviews, it was found that management has instituted a mandatory extended work schedule to recover production capacity lost during the fire incident of August 15. Specifically, in the cutting and sewing departments, all 127 operative personnel have been required to work 12-hour shifts, seven days per week, for the last seven consecutive weeks. Verified payroll records show an average of 98 hours of overtime per worker per week during this period, far exceeding the legal limit of 36 overtime hours per month. Interview notes from 14 workers across two production lines confirmed this schedule was not voluntary, with one worker stating, "We were told we must all work to save the factory, and we are afraid to refuse." While the factory's recovery efforts are noted, the systematic and prolonged nature of this violation, which also infringes upon the weekly rest day requirement, constitutes a significant breach. **Severity: Critical**

**Follow-Up Note:** This finding has been escalated for immediate corrective action planning with the Special Monitoring Authority.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FO-023-07-2024-014 | Compliance Area: C.2 Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**

Background context indicates that, following the fire incident on May 12, 2024, and the subsequent special monitoring regime imposed by local labor authorities, factory management committed to restoring all operational and social compliance frameworks. During the inspection conducted on July 22-23, 2024, it was observed that while efforts to rebuild physical infrastructure are proceeding, the mechanisms for worker-management dialogue remain severely impaired. Interviews held with 17 production workers across three departments revealed a consistent narrative that pre-fire worker representative elections have been declared null and void by management, with no process initiated to hold new elections, under the rationale of "temporary operational disruption." Furthermore, review of meeting minutes and notice boards found no evidence of any collective bargaining activities or communications regarding the re-establishment of worker representation since the incident, despite the factory operating at approximately 65% capacity. Management's stated intention to address this "once full production resumes" constitutes a failure to uphold the principle of continuous dialogue and effectively denies workers their right to organize during a critical recovery period. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up verification is required within 90 days to confirm the election schedule has been formally posted and the process initiated.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-023-2023-C7.1 / **Compliance Area:** C7 – Harassment & Abuse (Zero Tolerance) / **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

**Context & Background:** The assessment of this compliance area was conducted against the backdrop of significant operational disruption following a fire incident in Workshop B on August 15, 2023, which has necessitated production consolidation into remaining workspaces and placed the facility under heightened scrutiny by local labor authorities (Reference: LL Dept. Order #GH-782). This compressed working environment was noted as a potential stressor impacting management-worker interactions. **Observations & Interviews:** During the review of policies and procedures, the Harassment Grievance Protocol was found to be documented and posted. However, unannounced floor walks on October 26th revealed several instances of supervisory staff, particularly in the relocated sewing section, using verbally abusive language and derogatory remarks to催促 workers expediting post-fire backlog orders. Confidential interviews conducted with 14 workers from this section (approx. 30% of the section's workforce) substantiated these observations, with 9 workers providing specific, consistent accounts of being shouted at, called incompetent, and threatened with punitive overtime assignments or job loss for failing to meet accelerated targets. The interview notes from Worker #11 (anonymized) stated, "Since the fire, the line leader yells every day that we are too slow and are 'useless burdens.' We are afraid to report because we need the work now more than ever." No records of any harassment-related grievances filed in the past six months were presented, indicating a likely non-functional grievance mechanism or climate of fear. **Conclusion:** While the documented policy framework is nominally compliant, the evidence indicates a systemic pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by supervisory personnel, which has been exacerbated by post-incident production pressures and is not being captured or addressed through the management system. This constitutes a failure of the zero-tolerance principle and creates an environment of coercion. **Follow-up Required:** Immediate anti-harassment training for all management and supervisory staff, re-communication of grievance procedures with guaranteed anonymity, and implementation of a management accountability matrix must be verified. Severity: Moderate

[Contracts & Human Resources]
