Factory 025 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: WH-OT-2024-025-A)**

Following a review of time and attendance records for the period of January to March 2024 and conducting interviews on-site on April 15, 2024, the audit team notes that while the factory maintains a generally proactive approach to schedule management and possesses a robust, union-negotiated Collective Bargaining Agreement (CBA), a discrepancy was identified concerning weekly working hour limits. Inspector observations of detailed electronic punch records for Production Line B, specifically for weeks 20240401 and 20240408, indicated that a group of 37 workers engaged in a high-priority order consistently exceeded 60 hours of total work, with a recorded peak of 63 hours in the first week. Interviews with five workers from this line, conducted separately with union representation available, confirmed the excessive hours but noted that all overtime was voluntary and paid at the correct CBA-stipulated rate of 2.0 times the regular hourly wage for Sundays. Although the factory's strong grievance mechanism and cooperative union relationship were evident, and no worker reported coercion, this constitutes a breach of both local legal standards and the client's Code of Conduct, which stipulates a maximum 60-hour week. The management has been advised to review production planning to prevent recurrence. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-025-2023-08-CON**
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources**
**Observation Date: October 26, 2023**

A review of personnel files for a representative sample of 30 production workers hired within the last fiscal year, selected randomly across three production lines, was conducted alongside interviews with 15 workers from the same cohort. While the factory demonstrates generally robust HR practices and a positive industrial relations climate facilitated by the active union, a procedural inconsistency was identified. It was observed that for 7 out of the 30 files reviewed (approximately 23%), the signed employment contracts, though containing all legally required clauses and the correct wage rates, were dated and executed on average 5 calendar days after the worker's documented start date, contrary to section 4.2 of the Client's Code of Conduct which mandates contracts be provided prior to commencement of work. Interviewed workers confirmed receiving and understanding their contracts, and none reported any discrepancy between agreed and actual terms, indicating the issue is administrative rather than substantive. The factory management has been advised to implement a pre-employment orientation checklist to ensure contract finalization is completed proactively.
**Severity: Minor**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-025-2023-014 | **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (CCP Section 7.1) | **Date of Evaluation:** October 26-27, 2023

**Background & Context:** The audit scope included a review of policies, records, and worker interviews to assess the factory's compliance with non-discrimination standards. Factory 025 maintains a generally positive industrial relations climate, with a recognized workers' union and a functional grievance mechanism, as noted in prior assessments. The review of the factory's written policy, dated January 2023, was found to be fully aligned with local law and brand standards, explicitly prohibiting discrimination in hiring, remuneration, access to training, promotion, termination, or retirement based on race, caste, national origin, religion, disability, gender, sexual orientation, union membership, political affiliation, or age.

**Observations & Interviews:** During worker interviews conducted on October 27, a total of 32 workers from various production lines and departments were consulted in confidential settings. Interview notes consistently reflected a positive perception of equitable treatment. No worker raised any instance of discriminatory practice. This sentiment was corroborated in a separate interview with three members of the union executive committee, who confirmed that discrimination has not been raised as a formal or informal issue through the grievance channel in the past twelve months. A subsequent review of 15 personnel files for hires and promotions over the last quarter showed no discrepancies based on the protected categories listed in policy.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** Based on document review, worker testimony, and union feedback, no instances of discriminatory practice were identified. The factory's active union and strong grievance mechanism provide a robust corrective framework, which was verified as being utilized for other issues but not for discrimination-related complaints, indicating a lack of prevalent issues in this area. The finding for this compliance area is fully satisfactory.
**Status: Compliant.**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-025-2023-HA-01
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (HA)
**Date of On-Site Verification:** October 26, 2023

During the review of policies and worker interviews conducted on the aforementioned date, the audit team noted the factory's generally positive industrial relations climate, facilitated by an active union presence and a functional bipartite committee. However, interviews with 37 workers across cutting, sewing, and finishing departments revealed a recurring, albeit isolated, theme. Three workers in the sewing section, interviewed separately, described instances where a line supervisor, identified as Supervisor #12, used intimidating language and threw small garment pieces (e.g., collars, cuffs) on the floor near workers' feet when production targets were missed, actions the workers characterized as humiliating. While the factory's grievance mechanism was known and trusted, and no physical abuse was alleged, these reported behaviors create a climate of fear and disrespect, constituting verbal abuse and a form of psychological harassment under the applicable code of conduct. The management representative was immediately informed, and a follow-up note requires the factory to conduct a focused investigation into these allegations, provide remedial training for the supervisor in question, and submit documented evidence of corrective action by November 30, 2023.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 026 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
During the verification of payroll records for the period of January to March 2024, auditors identified a systemic practice requiring all production line workers (approximately 720 individuals) to submit their original government-issued identity cards and household registration documents to the factory's Security and Administration Department upon hire. These documents are retained in a locked cabinet within the HR office (observed on-site 12 April 2024) and are only returned to the worker upon termination of employment, a policy explicitly stated in the individual employment contracts reviewed (Ref: FL-026-03). Interviews with 14 randomly selected workers from various departments confirmed unanimous and consistent testimony that they do not have possession of their own legal documents, with several expressing concern that leaving the factory would be impossible without them, thus restricting their freedom of movement. This practice of unlawful retention of personal identity documents constitutes a strong indicator of forced labor, as it can be used to coerce workers to remain in employment against their will. **Severity: Critical** A corrective action plan must be submitted within 48 hours outlining the immediate, unconditional return of all documents and a revised contractual policy.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
**Finding Reference:** WB-026-2024-01

A review of wage records for the period of January to March 2024, alongside interviews conducted on April 16 and 17 with 37 production workers across various product lines, identified a systemic discrepancy in overtime remuneration. While the factory correctly applies the statutory rate for regular workday overtime, it was observed through payroll cross-checking that all overtime performed on weekly rest days (Sundays) is calculated at a rate of 1.5 times the regular hourly rate, rather than the legally mandated 2.0 times rate as stipulated in national labor law. This practice was confirmed in sample records for 14 workers in the cutting and sewing departments, and verbally acknowledged by the payroll coordinator during the document review. This constitutes a violation of legal wage payment requirements affecting all 800+ employees when such overtime is worked. **Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref:** DISC-026-2023-08
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (including but not limited to hiring, remuneration, promotion, and access to training)
**Background & Context:** The audit scope included a review of policies, payroll records for a six-month period, and structured interviews to assess the factory's adherence to non-discrimination principles as outlined in the applicable code of conduct and local law. Factory management provided their written Equal Opportunity Policy, last revised in 2021, which explicitly prohibits discrimination based on gender, ethnicity, religion, or age.

**Observations & Evidence:** During document review on 14 October 2023, a comparative analysis of payroll data for the Sewing and Packing departments (approximately 620 workers) against the Quality Control and Supervisory staff (approximately 180 workers) revealed a pronounced demographic disparity. While 92% of the sewing and packing line workers are female, 95% of the QC and supervisory positions are held by male employees. Worker interviews conducted on 15 October 2023 with 14 randomly selected female line workers from different product lines corroborated this systemic pattern; eight workers stated they had applied for internal QC vacancies over the past two years but were informed such roles were "unsuitable" or told they were "better suited to sewing," with no transparent application process provided. Furthermore, interview notes from three male employees in the Cutting section indicated they had received mandatory machine maintenance training not offered to their female counterparts in similar roles.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** The evidence indicates a de facto practice of gender-based occupational segregation, limiting female employees' access to higher-paying and skilled positions, which constitutes a violation of the established policy and international standards. Management must develop and implement a corrective action plan with clear, measurable goals to promote equitable access to advancement and training.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Area 3.0: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**
**Reference: SA8000:2014 Section 4; Client Code: FOI-7.2**
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

During the review of personnel policies and subsequent confidential worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, with 42 workers across various production departments, it was established that while the factory has a formally registered collective bargaining agreement (CBA) on file, dated January 15, 2023, there exists a significant gap in practical implementation and awareness. Management representatives confirmed the CBA's existence and the presence of a worker-elected trade union committee; however, 35 out of the 42 workers interviewed (approximately 83%) stated they had never received a copy or been briefed on the CBA's contents, and several were unclear on the identity of their union representatives. This lack of effective communication and transparency undermines the principle of good faith bargaining and inhibits workers' ability to fully understand and exercise their rights under the agreement. Furthermore, the union committee president, interviewed separately, indicated that monthly meetings with management had not been consistently held over the past six months, with three meetings cancelled by management due to "production pressures."
**Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref:** EHS-AUD-2023-026-EP-004 | **Compliance Area:** 4.2 Emergency Preparedness & Response (Fire) | **Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** During the walkthrough of the main production building (Block A, Floors 1-3), it was noted that while fire extinguishers are present and annually serviced (last serviced September 2023 per tags), the documented emergency evacuation drill conducted on July 15, 2023, was found to be non-representative of a full-shift scenario. The drill log indicated participation by only 127 personnel, primarily consisting of line supervisors and administrative staff from the day shift, which does not reflect the conditions of a full occupancy event with the factory's stated capacity of over 800 workers across multiple shifts. Interviews conducted with 14 randomly selected production workers from the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments confirmed that they had not participated in any full-building evacuation drill within the last 12 months, with several stating they were unaware of alternate escape routes from their workstations. This constitutes a failure to ensure all workers are adequately trained and familiarized with evacuation procedures under varied working conditions, as required by the International Fire Safety Standard IFSS-EP 2.1. The factory must implement and document a comprehensive drill encompassing all active shifts and production zones.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)**
**Reference: OSH-07 / Incident Investigation & Corrective Action**

The audit team conducted a review of the factory's incident reporting and investigation procedures on 17 October 2023. While the documentation for recordable injuries from the past 12 months was largely complete, a significant lapse was identified concerning a near-miss incident. On 5 August 2023, a mechanical jam on a cutting press (Serial #CP-026-44) required a maintenance technician to manually clear the machine while power was isolated; however, the assigned lockout/tagout device was found to be non-compliant (a generic combination lock without a uniquely assigned key). This event was logged in the near-miss register but no root cause analysis was initiated, and no corrective actions to inspect and replace all non-compliant lockout devices were documented. Interviews with three maintenance staff on the audit date confirmed this was a known issue with several older locks in circulation, and they were unaware of any subsequent procedural changes or equipment audits. This failure to investigate a near-miss with clear potential for severe injury represents a systemic deficiency in the corrective action process. **Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
Compliance area 3.04: Harassment & Abuse (Code: SA8000/7, ILO Conventions 100 & 111). During the review of the factory's social management systems on 12 October 2023, it was noted that while a formal anti-harassment policy exists within the employee handbook, the implementation and awareness of its grievance mechanisms were found to be insufficient. Interviews conducted with a cross-section of 37 workers from the sewing, cutting, and finishing departments revealed that 8 workers (approximately 22%) reported experiencing or witnessing derogatory comments and verbal intimidation by line supervisors, particularly during peak production periods, with one interviewee providing a specific account from late September regarding threats of dismissal for failing to meet unilateral production targets. Furthermore, the dedicated harassment complaint channel, a locked box near the HR office, was observed to be placed directly under a security camera, creating a perceived lack of confidentiality and deterring reporting, a point corroborated in three separate worker interviews. The management team, while cooperative, acknowledged that supervisor training on this policy has lapsed for over 18 months, and no investigation records for harassment complaints were available for review for the current calendar year, indicating a systemic failure in operationalizing the written policy. Severity: Moderate. Follow-up required: Factory must submit a corrective action plan detailing supervisor re-training, relocation of the complaint box, and awareness sessions for all workers within 14 days.

[Child Labor]
During the document review for the period covering January to April 2024, the personnel files for all 812 active production workers were examined for age verification, with particular attention paid to 45 hires from the past six months. The review identified two individuals, hired in February 2024 as helpers in the finishing section, whose provided national ID cards appeared questionable upon scrutiny, indicating birth years that would make them 15 years of age. Subsequent confidential interviews conducted on [Audit Date] with the individuals in question, separated from management, confirmed they were aged 15 and 16, and further interviews with three co-workers in the same section corroborated their regular attendance on the production floor for the past ten weeks. This constitutes a breach of CLS-001 (Prohibition of Child Labor) and national labor law, necessitating immediate remedial action including their removal from production duties, provision of support for educational enrollment, and reimbursement of lost wages until they reach legal working age. **Severity: Critical**


============================================================
Factory 027 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: SOC-03-2024-027**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SOC-2.1, SOC-2.3)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2024

During the review of hiring and promotion records from Q1-Q3 2024, coupled with confidential worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2024, the audit team identified a discrepancy between the factory's generally positive industrial relations climate and its practices in one specific department. While the established grievance mechanism is functional and well-utilized for other issues, interviews with 7 of 32 workers in the cutting section revealed a consistent perception that supervisory staff preferentially assign overtime and more desirable cutting patterns to workers from the same regional background as the department head. This pattern, though not evidenced in formal hiring or wage documents, was confirmed through cross-referenced worker statements and overtime allocation logs, creating a de facto disadvantage and potential hostile environment for workers from other regions. The management has been advised to conduct targeted anti-discrimination training for cutting section supervisors and to implement a transparent, rotational system for overtime and task assignment, with a follow-up review scheduled for Q1 2025.
**Severity: Minor**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-027-2023-08-FOA-001**

During the onsite audit conducted on August 22-24, 2023, the auditors reviewed the factory's policies and procedures related to Freedom of Association and conducted confidential interviews with a total of 37 workers, including 8 members of the elected union executive committee. Background documentation confirms the presence of an active and legally recognized union, "Garment Workers' Unity," in operation since 2019, with a collective bargaining agreement (CBA) currently in force. Worker interviews and management confirmation indicated a generally positive relationship, with a functional grievance mechanism that includes union representation. However, a review of the minutes from the last four Joint Consultative Committee (JCC) meetings, dated between March and July 2023, revealed that while worker representatives raised three separate proposals for discussion on shift premium adjustments, management unilaterally tabled these items without providing a substantive written response or initiating good-faith bargaining, citing ongoing market studies. This failure to engage in meaningful dialogue on terms and conditions of employment, as required by the CBA's framework and international standards, constitutes an impediment to the collective bargaining process. **Severity: Minor**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: FL-2023-027-02**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor (SA8000:2014 Element 2, Client Code of Conduct Section 8.1)**
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

During the review of payroll records for the period of September 16-30, 2023, and subsequent confidential worker interviews, a procedural inconsistency was identified concerning employee departure. The audit team, having conducted interviews with 17 workers selected from various production lines, noted through three separate worker statements that the factory's practice of holding the final paycheck until the exit interview is completed and the factory-issued ID card is physically returned creates an undue restriction on workers' freedom of termination. While management clarified that this policy is intended to ensure a proper offboarding process and recovery of company property, and while no evidence of physical confinement or debt bondage was present, the withholding of earned wages constitutes a form of compulsive labor, as it can coercively delay or influence a worker's decision to leave employment. The factory's generally strong industrial relations and functional grievance mechanism mitigate the overall risk, but this practice is a clear deviation from the principle that employment is voluntary. A corrective action plan (CAP) must address the revision of this procedure to ensure final wages are paid on the worker's last day of work, irrespective of administrative completion.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
During the review of personnel files on 17 October 2023, an inconsistency was identified concerning employee ID 027-884, hired on 12 August 2023 as a helper in the finishing section. The submitted national ID card, photocopied and retained in the file (Document Ref: CL-027-884), indicated a date of birth calculating to an age of 17 years and 9 months at the time of hiring, which is below the minimum age of 18 as stipulated in Section 4.1 of the Code of Conduct and local labor law. Interviews conducted with the HR manager and the direct line supervisor confirmed the individual's employment and duties, though they cited an administrative oversight during the recruitment verification process. The factory's otherwise robust grievance mechanism and union presence were noted as positive factors in promptly addressing this issue upon discovery. **Severity: Minor**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref. OS&H-2023-027-04**
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OS&H)
**Date of Inspection:** 17 October 2023
**Background & Context:** The audit reviewed the general safety conditions within the cutting and sewing sections, with particular attention to machine guarding, emergency egress, and general housekeeping, as per the requirements of section 4.1 of the Client's Code of Conduct and local regulatory standards.
**Observations & Interviews:** During the walk-through, the auditor noted that the main aisles leading to the designated fire exits in the western wing of the sewing hall were partially obstructed by temporary storage of WIP (Work-in-Progress) carts and bulk fabric rolls, reducing the clear width to approximately 0.8 meters in several locations, which is below the mandated minimum of 1.1 meters. This condition was present during both the initial (09:30) and follow-up (14:00) walk-throughs. Interviews with 7 production workers from that section confirmed this is a recurring issue during peak production periods, though they expressed confidence in reporting such issues via the established grievance mechanism.
**Analysis:** While the factory demonstrates strong procedural frameworks for OS&H and worker-management dialogue, this physical obstruction of egress routes constitutes a failure to maintain a fundamental engineering control for life safety. The condition creates a tangible hazard in the event of an emergency requiring evacuation.
**Follow-Up Note:** Management was notified verbally of this finding during the inspection and initiated immediate clearance of the aisles.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: EP-2023-027-04**
**Compliance Area: A.4 Emergency Preparedness & Response**
**Audit Date(s):** October 26-27, 2023

**Background & Observation:** A review of the factory's emergency preparedness protocols was conducted, including an inspection of all four production floors and two warehouse areas, alongside a documented fire drill record from September 15, 2023. While the factory maintains a comprehensive written Emergency Response Plan (ERP) and the recent drill was executed with satisfactory evacuation times, a significant discrepancy was identified during physical verification of escape routes. On the third-floor west wing, serving cutting and sewing sections with an approximate headcount of 127 workers, the primary designated exit stairwell (Stairwell B) was found to be partially obstructed. Inspector observation at 10:45 on October 26 confirmed that a pallet of packaged finished goods and several roll cages were stored within the stairwell, reducing the clear egress width to approximately 85cm at one point, which is below the minimum required width stipulated in the client's Code of Conduct and local fire regulations. This condition was photographically documented (Ref: Img_EP027_1026_012).

**Worker Interview Corroboration:** Interviews with 8 workers from the affected section, conducted separately on October 27, revealed that while they were aware of evacuation procedures and assembly points, 5 of them spontaneously noted that the clutter in Stairwell B was a "regular occurrence" and expressed concern it would slow escape during a real emergency. This corroborates the physical finding of a systemic housekeeping failure impacting a critical life safety system.

**Follow-Up & Root Cause:** Management was immediately notified and rectified the obstruction during the audit. However, the recurrence of this issue, as indicated by worker testimony, points to a breakdown in routine safety inspections of egress routes. This finding is classified as a violation due to the direct and serious compromise of a primary exit during normal operating conditions.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FB-027-2023-08-WB-002
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Code: WB.1.1 - Payment of Wages)
**Background & Context:** The audit of wage payments for the period of June 2023 was conducted with a focus on verifying the accurate and timely disbursement of the government-mandated minimum wage, inclusive of all legal allowances, against provided attendance, production, and payroll records. Factory 027 maintains a documented collective bargaining agreement (CBA) effective January 2023.
**Observations & Interviews:** A review of payroll for 147 production workers for the last three pay periods revealed that while base wages were correctly calculated, the attendance-based *Attendance Bonus* of BDT 500 per month, as stipulated in Clause 4.2 of the active CBA, was not disbursed to 32 workers in the June payroll who had met the required 95% attendance threshold. This was confirmed through cross-referencing signed attendance registers with individual pay slips. During confidential interviews conducted on 14 August 2023 with 7 affected workers from Sewing Section B, all corroborated the non-payment of this contractual benefit, though they expressed confidence in the union's ability to address the issue through the established grievance mechanism, which they had not yet utilized for this specific matter.
**Conclusion & Follow-up:** This constitutes a failure to pay wages and benefits in accordance with a legally binding collective bargaining agreement. Management attributed the error to a clerical oversight during payroll processing for a specific line and has committed to rectification. A corrective action plan requiring the back-payment of the owed bonus with statutory interest, plus a review of payroll software validations, is to be submitted by 30 August 2023.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-027-2024-08 / **Compliance Area:** HAR-01 Harassment & Abuse (incl. Verbal, Physical, Sexual)
**Background & Context:** The audit included a review of the factory's harassment prevention policy (rev. Jan 2024), documented grievance mechanism records from the past 12 months, and interviews conducted with 32 workers across various departments on 14-15 August 2024. The factory's profile indicates an active union and historically strong worker-management relations, which establishes a higher expected standard for procedural adherence.
**Observations & Interviews:** While the documented policy is comprehensive and aligned with local law and client COC requirements, and the grievance committee meeting minutes show regular review of general issues, a procedural inconsistency was identified. Interviews with 7 workers in the cutting and finishing sections revealed a repeated, though not universal, reluctance to formally report instances of verbal harassment or intimidation from line supervisors, primarily related to production targets. These workers confirmed awareness of the policy and the union representative but expressed a perception that such reports would be informally discouraged as "wasting the committee's time with small matters," despite finding the supervisor's language demeaning. The grievance log shows only 2 harassment-related entries in the past year, both resolved, which appears disproportionately low given the interview feedback and the factory's size (1,200 employees).
**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** This indicates a potential gap between the formal mechanism's availability and its practical, perceived accessibility for all forms of harassment, potentially undermining the otherwise robust system. Management must reinforce, through renewed training and communication, a culture where all harassment grievances are actively solicited and treated with uniform seriousness, without perceived prioritization. The union should be engaged as a partner in this communication initiative.
**Severity: Minor**

Factory 028 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** CL-2023-028-01F
**Compliance Area:** PROHIBITION OF CHILD LABOR (Code: CL.01)
**Background & Context:** Factory 028 has a documented history of non-compliance in the area of young worker documentation, having been placed under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP Ref: Q4-2022-07) following the previous audit for inadequate age verification procedures. This follow-up audit included a focused review of hiring records and production floor checks specifically to verify the effectiveness of implemented CAP measures.
**Observations & Interviews:** During the floor walk-through on 23 October 2023, the audit team observed one individual on the finishing line whose appearance prompted a document review. While the provided national ID card was valid, cross-referencing with the worker's signed employment application form revealed a discrepancy in the recorded date of birth, indicating an age of 17. Subsequent confidential interviews were conducted with 4 workers from the same production unit, one of whom corroborated that the individual in question had previously mentioned attending secondary school during the last academic year. Management was unable to produce the mandatory proof of age document for this worker prior to the date of hire, as required by the CAP.
**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** This constitutes a failure to maintain an effective age verification system, representing a breach of both the client's Code of Conduct and the previously agreed CAP. The factory must immediately remove the young worker from production duties, provide evidence of lawful remediation, and submit a revised procedural document for verification.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-028-2024-01 | Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work** Following the corrective actions mandated from the previous audit (CAP-028-2023, issued 15 November 2023), a focused review of forced labor indicators was conducted. During document verification on 12 March 2024, it was observed that the factory continues to retain original employee identification documents (national ID cards) for 147 out of 312 production operatives in the sewing section, citing a "company policy for safekeeping" as noted in Section 4.2 of the internal handbook. This policy was reiterated by two line supervisors during separate interviews. Furthermore, confidential worker interviews held on 13 March 2024 with eight randomly selected operators from the same section confirmed that they had repeatedly requested the return of their documents without success, with one worker stating, "I cannot leave because they have my papers," a sentiment echoed by three others. The retention of personal identity documents is a violation of the standard's Clause 7.1 on freely chosen employment, which the factory had previously committed to rectify, and constitutes a significant restriction on workers' freedom of movement. **Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** A-OSH-2023-028-04
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Background & Context:** Factory 028 is currently operating under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) following the non-conformities identified in the audit of 12 October 2022. As part of the ongoing monitoring, this inspection focused on verifying the implementation of corrective actions related to fire safety, specifically the clearance of emergency exits and adherence to the established evacuation plan. A walk-through of all production floors and warehouse areas was conducted on 15 November 2023, accompanied by interviews with 7 production workers from different departments.

**Inspector Observations & Evidence:** During the floor inspection, auditors observed a persistent and significant failure to maintain clear egress paths. On the second-floor stitching section, Main Aisle 'B' (designated as a secondary fire exit route on the posted floor plan) was found to be approximately 70% obstructed for a continuous length of 8 meters by two pallets of WIP (work-in-progress) garments and a dismantled sewing machine frame. This obstruction was present for the duration of the 2-hour inspection and was confirmed to be a regular storage area by two workers interviewed. This condition directly contradicts section 4.2 of the factory's own approved CAP, which stipulated "all emergency egress routes will be kept clear of obstructions at all times," and violates international safety standard IFC EHS 4.6.

**Worker Interview Notes:** Workers interviewed demonstrated awareness of the location of the nearest exit but expressed concern about accessibility, with one cutter noting, "We have to move those boxes sometimes to get through if the main aisle is crowded, but the supervisors tell us to put them back there." This indicates a systemic tolerance of the violation by line management.

**Follow-up & Conclusion:** Despite previous commitments, the factory management has failed to implement and enforce a fundamental life safety procedure. This recurring critical violation presents an immediate and severe risk to worker safety in the event of a fire or other emergency requiring rapid evacuation. The finding is escalated due to its recurrence under a CAP.
**Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: AF-028-2023-07-WB-002**
**Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits (Code: WB.1.1, WB.3.2)**
**Background & Context:** During the unannounced audit conducted on October 26-27, 2023, a focused review of wage records was undertaken due to the factory's documented history of non-compliance in this area, specifically the prior issuance of CAP-028-2023-01 requiring remediation of overtime calculation errors. The review period covered payroll from July through September 2023.
**Observations & Interviews:** Inspection of time cards and payroll registers for September revealed a systemic discrepancy between recorded overtime hours and paid overtime hours for 127 production line workers. The payroll calculation was based on a fixed monthly "attendance day" count of 26 days, rather than the actual hours worked as per individual time cards, resulting in underpayment for overtime worked on Sundays and holidays. Interviews with 14 randomly selected workers from three departments confirmed they had not received payment for these specific overtime periods, with several providing corroborating pay slip copies. The management representative acknowledged the practice, citing a "simplified accounting method."
**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** This finding constitutes a repeat violation of the agreed corrective action plan and local labor law stipulating payment for all hours worked. The practice led to a systemic underpayment of wages for a significant portion of the workforce during the review period.
**Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-028-2023-047 **| Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (SA8000: 8.2/8.3) **| Date of Observation:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the Corrective Action Plan (CAP) issued after the Q2 2023 audit (Ref: 028-2023-012), which cited systemic failures in grievance mechanisms and supervisory conduct, this inspection focused on verifying the implementation of revised policies and awareness training conducted in August 2023. **Observations & Interviews:** While documentation of the training was presented, unannounced interviews with 17 production line workers across three departments revealed ongoing issues. Multiple workers in the cutting section (4 of 6 interviewed) described a persistent environment of verbal abuse and intimidation by line supervisor [Name Redacted], citing incidents as recent as October 24th where workers were subjected to derogatory comments regarding their speed and threatened with punitive assignment changes for minor errors. The factory's grievance log showed no entries from the cutting section in the last 90 days, and workers expressed a continued fear of retaliation for reporting, indicating a failure in the effectiveness of the revised procedures. **Conclusion:** The evidence demonstrates that the corrective actions have not been internalized or enforced at the supervisory level, constituting a repeated violation of the commitment to a workplace free from harassment and abuse. **Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-028-2023-09-FOA-001**

During the review of Freedom of Association and Collective Bargaining compliance, conducted on September 12-13, 2023, it was noted that the factory management has failed to fully implement the corrective actions outlined in CAR-028-022-007 from the previous audit dated March 2023. While a formal Worker Representative Committee (WRC) exists on paper, investigator observations and confidential interviews with 14 workers from various departments revealed a systemic deficiency. The elected worker representatives confirmed in interview notes that they have not received any training on their rights or functions, and management has not convened a single meeting with the full WRC in the past six months to discuss collective bargaining agreements or working conditions, despite repeated requests. This constitutes a failure to establish a genuine and functioning mechanism for collective bargaining, as required by the corrective action plan and international labor standards. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref:** HR-028-2023-04  
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources  
**Background:** Factory 028, operating under a corrective action plan (CAP) initiated following the prior audit of 14 March 2023 (Report # AU-028-2023-01), was subject to a follow-up verification of HR documentation and contracting practices.  
**Observations & Interviews:** A review on 27 November 2023 of personnel files for a sample of 34 workers hired post-CAP implementation revealed that 19 files (approximately 56%) contained employment contracts lacking one or more legally mandated clauses, specifically those pertaining to detailed disciplinary procedures and precise overtime compensation calculations as per national labor law. Furthermore, interviews conducted with 12 of these affected workers confirmed they had not received a copy of their signed contract for personal retention, with several stating they were only shown the document briefly at the time of hiring. This represents a failure to fully implement the agreed CAP item HR.03, which mandated the revision and proper issuance of compliant contracts to all employees by 30 September 2023.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: CAP-F028-2023-04-F01 (Follow-up to CAP-F028-2022-09-R01)**
During this follow-up inspection conducted on October 26-27, 2023, to verify implementation of the corrective action plan stemming from prior non-conformities, the audit team continued to identify systemic discrepancies in the management of working hours and overtime, an area previously cited for excessive overtime. Review of payroll records for September 2023 and timecard sampling for the first three weeks of October indicated a pattern where, in several production departments, daily working hours routinely exceeded the local legal and code standard of 12 hours, with instances of 13.5 hours recorded. Interviews with a cross-section of 17 production workers confirmed this pattern was not isolated, with multiple workers stating such extended shifts occurred 3-4 times per week during peak periods, and that while overtime premium was paid, genuine voluntary consent was questionable due to production pressure. This represents a failure to implement effective operational controls to ensure legal working hour limits are respected, as required per the corrective action plan commitment. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref:** INT-2023-028-F06 **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Ref. Code HR-04) **Date of On-site Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Context:** This facility remains under the Corrective Action Plan (CAP ID: Q3-2023-45) stemming from the previous audit cycle, which included a non-conformance for discriminatory hiring practices. The current review focused on monitoring implemented CAP actions and assessing ongoing adherence to the established non-discrimination policy.

**Observations & Evidence:** While management provided updated policy documentation, interviews conducted with 17 production workers across three departments on October 26 revealed a persistent pattern of inequity. Six female workers in the cutting section separately reported being systematically passed over for operator promotion opportunities in favor of male counterparts with less seniority, with two interviewees citing a supervisor's verbal assertion that "machine operation is not suitable for women." Furthermore, a review of promotion records for the past 12 months showed that of 14 internal promotions to operator level, only 2 were female, despite a nearly equal gender distribution in eligible entry-level positions.

**Conclusion:** The evidence indicates that the corrective actions from the prior audit have been insufficient to eradicate discriminatory practices, specifically gender-based discrimination in advancement opportunities, which constitutes a violation of local law and the client's code of conduct. **Severity: Moderate.** A root cause analysis and revised CAP are required to address this systemic issue.


============================================================
Factory 029 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: AF-029-2023-08-WB-02**

**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (WB) - Payment of Wages

**Background & Observation:** During the inspection conducted on October 26, 2023, a review of wage records for the pay period ending September 30, 2023, revealed a recurring pattern of discrepancies between the documented piece rates and the actual wages disbursed to a significant portion of the workforce. The factory operates primarily with a piece-rate system, which was found to be poorly communicated and inconsistently applied. In the sewing section, for example, the posted rate for Operation #447 (sleeve setting) was listed as 0.45 RMB per piece, yet payroll records for 37 of 44 workers sampled showed an effective rate of only 0.38 RMB per piece for this operation.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews with twelve (12) production workers, all of whom are migrant workers with limited local literacy, confirmed this systemic underpayment. Workers stated they were not provided with clear, itemized pay slips and were simply told the total amount due each month, making verification impossible. Several workers interviewed on October 27 noted they had questioned the amounts but were dismissed by line supervisors, with one worker (Interview #WB-14) quoted as saying, "We are told to be happy we have work and that the calculation is too complicated for us to understand."

**Conclusion & Follow-Up Required:** This constitutes a failure to remunerate workers for all work performed in accordance with clearly communicated rates and a violation of local minimum wage regulations when calculated on an hourly basis. The lack of transparent pay stubs for a vulnerable, migrant workforce exacerbates the violation. The factory management must immediately recalculate wages for the audited period, provide back pay with detailed statements, and implement a transparent, verifiable wage calculation and communication system.
**Severity: Critical**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-029-2023-04**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Section 2, ILO Forced Labour Convention, 1930 (No. 29))**
**Fieldwork Dates: October 23-26, 2023**

Upon review of employment practices, it was identified that the factory's remote rural location, coupled with its workforce composition of predominantly migrant workers with limited local ties or access to legal resources, creates an elevated risk profile for forced labor indicators. During worker interviews conducted on October 24th (14 workers from 3 departments), a recurring pattern emerged regarding the retention of original identity documents. Management confirmed the policy of holding workers' national ID cards and/or household registration documents for "safekeeping" for the duration of their contract, ostensibly to prevent loss. Three interviewed workers expressed explicit concern that retrieving these documents prior to contract completion would be difficult and could result in termination or forfeiture of owed wages, effectively restricting their freedom of movement and ability to resign. This practice of unlawfully withholding identity documents to coerce continued employment constitutes a violation of the workers' right to freely leave employment and presents a significant forced labor risk.
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: 029-CON-04-2024**
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources (Employment Terms)
**Background & Observation:** During the document review conducted on 23 April 2024, it was noted that while the factory maintains signed employment contracts for its 287 production workers on file, the contractual terms presented a significant discrepancy. The contracts, issued in a standard template, were written exclusively in the national language, which is not the primary spoken or written language of the majority migrant workforce residing at the facility. Subsequent worker interviews with 12 randomly selected employees from the sewing and finishing departments revealed that none had received a copy of their signed contract for personal retention, and only two could vaguely articulate key terms such as their base wage calculation or probation period duration. This lack of comprehensible documentation and provision to the worker, particularly in a rural setting with limited independent access to legal resources, constitutes a failure to ensure mutual understanding of terms and conditions of employment as required by international labor standards and local law.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan must include issuance of bilingual contracts (in both national language and workers' primary language), provision of a copy to each employee, and documented training sessions to explain key terms, with re-audit verification required within 90 days.


============================================================
Factory 030 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FAC-030-24-FOA-001**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FACB)**
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

While the factory maintains a formal policy on non-discrimination against union members and has a documented procedure for handling worker grievances, interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, with a stratified sample of 37 production workers from three departments revealed a systemic gap in practical implementation. Specifically, eight workers from the sewing section (approximately 22% of interviewees on this topic) stated they were unaware of the existence or purpose of the elected Worker Representative Committee, believing management appointments were still in effect, despite committee elections having been documented as occurring in Q1 2023. This represents a failure in effective communication and worker education regarding their legitimate right to organize, as per the facility's own policy and international standards (ILO Convention 87). Furthermore, review of committee meeting minutes from the last six months showed a predominance of welfare and facility issues discussed, with no documented instances of collective bargaining on terms and conditions of employment, suggesting the committee's role is perceived as consultative rather than representative. Management is requested to provide a corrective action plan to address this awareness deficit and to facilitate genuine bargaining mechanisms. Severity: Minor.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Reference:** OSH-2023-030-F03 | **Date of Inspection:** 17 October 2023 | **Area:** Stitching & Finishing Sections

**Background & Observation:** During the walkthrough of the production floor on the aforementioned date, the auditor noted general good housekeeping and clear emergency egress pathways, consistent with the factory's established profile and ISO 45001 certification. However, a focused inspection of the fabric cutting section revealed an inconsistency in the implementation of machine guarding protocols. Specifically, on two of the eight operational straight-knife cutting machines (Serial #s C-45 and C-48), the factory's own procedural requirement for the use of a permanent, fixed guard over the lower blade drive mechanism was not being followed; instead, temporary wire mesh guards had been inadequately fastened, leaving a potential pinch point hazard accessible. Interviews conducted with three operators from this section confirmed this was not an isolated incident, with one worker stating the temporary guards had been in place for "a few weeks" since the original guards were damaged. While the factory's documented Lockout/Tagout (LOTO) program was verified as comprehensive, this observed deviation from required guarding standards presents a direct physical hazard to operators. **Severity: Moderate**

**Follow-up Note:** The Compliance Officer was notified on-site and has initiated a corrective action plan to rectify the guarding on all cutting machines; verification of completion is required within 30 days.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-030-02**  
**Area:** Forced Labor  
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023  

During the review of payroll records for the period September 1 – October 15, 2023, and subsequent confidential worker interviews conducted on-site, it was noted that the factory maintains generally robust controls, including clear contracts and the visible posting of worker rights. However, in interviews with 8 of 32 production line workers selected at random, 3 workers from the finishing section disclosed that their original identification documents (national ID cards) were retained by the factory's security office at the time of hiring, purportedly for "verification and safekeeping," and had not been returned. These workers expressed concern that requesting the return of their documents might jeopardize their employment. This practice, while not applied to all workers and seemingly inconsistent, constitutes a retention of personal documents which can restrict workers’ freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment. The factory's compliance officer was unaware of this localized practice, which contradicts the factory's stated policies and its otherwise strong track record. **Severity: Moderate** - Corrective action required to ensure all personal documents are returned immediately and a clear, communicated policy is implemented to prohibit this practice.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding: DF-030-2024-007 | Compliance Area: Discrimination | Reference: SA8000:2014 Section 3, Client Code of Conduct Section 8.1**

During the audit conducted on February 14, 2024, a review of personnel records, policies, and worker interviews revealed a procedural inconsistency regarding recruitment and promotion, despite the factory's overall strong governance framework. While the management team presented a formal non-discrimination policy aligned with ISO 26000 guidance, inspector observations within the cutting and packing departments indicated a notable demographic disparity in supervisory roles. Interviews with 17 workers from various production lines, including 9 female and 8 male operators, yielded four separate but unsubstantiated anecdotal accounts from female employees suggesting a perceived preference for male internal candidates for floor-level promotions, though no formal complaints were filed. The dedicated compliance officer acknowledged this perception and confirmed that while hiring records from the past 12 months show no evidence of discriminatory hiring, the factory’s internal job posting system for lead roles is not consistently documented or communicated to all shifts, potentially creating an inequitable access to opportunity. As this constitutes a failure in the implementation of an effective management system process to ensure and demonstrate non-discrimination in all aspects of employment, a minor non-conformance is issued. **Severity: Minor**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-030-2023-08**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1)**
**Date of Review: October 24-26, 2023**

A review of time and attendance records for the period of September 1, 2023, through October 20, 2023, alongside confidential interviews conducted with 17 production workers across three departments on October 25, revealed a systemic deviation from both client Code of Conduct standards and national legislation regarding weekly working hour limits. While the factory maintains exemplary payroll documentation confirming correct overtime premium payment, inspector observations and worker testimony identified a consistent pattern wherein approximately 65% of the sampled workforce in the cutting and sewing sections exceeded the 60-hour maximum work week during peak production periods in late September. This was corroborated by electronic time clock data, which showed multiple instances of 72-hour work weeks, though all overtime appeared to be voluntary and duly compensated. The finding indicates a failure of managerial oversight and production planning, despite the presence of a dedicated compliance officer, to adequately control hours in response to fluctuating order volumes. The factory is required to submit a corrective action plan detailing revised production scheduling and capacity planning protocols to prevent recurrence.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 031 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: small family-owned factory with 50-100 workers, limited HR capacity, first time being audited
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-03-2024-031
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 6, Client Code of Conduct Section 4.2)

**Background & Observation:** Factory management provided time and wage records for the audit period covering March 2024, which indicated a consistent pattern of a 60-hour workweek, including 20 hours of overtime, for production staff. Initial document review showed payroll calculations for these hours. However, during physical verification on 10 April 2024, inspector observations and unannounced floor walks at 8:00 PM revealed approximately 35 sewing operators actively working. Cross-referencing this with the provided sign-in sheets, which showed a 6:00 PM end time for the same day, identified a clear discrepancy between recorded and actual working hours.

**Worker Interviews:** Confidential interviews conducted with 7 workers from the sewing section on 11 April 2024 confirmed that, to meet shipping deadlines for order #P04892, they had regularly been required to work until 8:30 PM from Monday to Saturday for the three weeks preceding the audit, with one additional Sunday worked. These hours were not accurately reflected in the official timecards, which were systematically altered to reflect compliance with the 60-hour maximum, though workers stated they were paid correctly for all hours worked based on handwritten production slips. This practice was attributed by workers to management's desire to "pass the audit" while maintaining production output given their limited administrative staff.

**Finding:** The factory is in violation of both local legal limits and client Code of Conduct requirements regarding maximum working hours. The systematic under-recording of working hours, despite apparent correct compensation, constitutes a deliberate falsification of records designed to conceal non-compliant practices. This breach of ethical and transparent record-keeping is a serious violation of audit integrity.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-Up Note:** Corrective action must address both the cessation of excessive hours and the implementation of a transparent, verifiable time-keeping system subject to supervisory review.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Harassment & Abuse (Audit Code: HA)**
**Background & Context:** The audit of Factory 031, a first-time auditee, included a review of policies and procedures related to the prevention of harassment and abuse, with particular attention given to the operational environment and management culture given the factory's small, family-owned structure and self-acknowledged limited formal HR function. Inspection activities took place on 22-24 October 2023.
**Observations & Interviews:** While a basic code of conduct was posted, no dedicated policy or procedure on harassment, abuse, or grievance mechanisms specific to these issues was identified. During confidential interviews conducted with 12 workers (approximately 15% of the workforce) across various departments, a recurring theme emerged regarding the conduct of one floor supervisor. Three separate workers, when asked if they felt safe from verbal abuse, cited instances where the same supervisor used derogatory language and shouted at operators for production errors. One worker interview note stated, "He calls us stupid and lazy if a bundle is wrong, which happens often because the instructions change. We are afraid to complain because he is the owner's nephew." No records of any training on harassment prevention for management or workers were available for review.
**Finding:** The factory has failed to establish and implement adequate policies and procedures to prevent harassment and abuse in the workplace. This constitutes a violation of the requirement for a safe and respectful work environment, compounded by the lack of a functional grievance mechanism and evidence of recurring verbal abuse by a member of supervisory staff.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan (CAP) must include development and implementation of a formal anti-harassment policy, establishment of a safe reporting channel, and mandatory training for all management and staff, with specific attention to supervisory conduct.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: FAC-031-24-EP-003**

In the area of Emergency Preparedness, the factory's planning and procedural documentation were found to be insufficient for ensuring the safe and rapid egress of all personnel in the event of an emergency. During the physical inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, it was observed that the primary assembly point, designated in a handwritten site plan in the manager's office, is located in a secured rear courtyard accessible only via a single 1.2-meter-wide gate, which was itself partially obstructed by stored raw materials, reducing the clear width to approximately 0.8 meters. Interviews with a cross-section of 8 production workers on the same date revealed a general lack of awareness of emergency procedures; 7 of the 8 workers interviewed stated they had never participated in an emergency drill and could not correctly identify the location of the assembly point, with several indicating they would likely exit via the main entrance regardless of the nature of the emergency. This represents a significant failure in both infrastructure planning and worker training. Follow-up note: Management acknowledges this gap, citing limited administrative resources, and has verbally committed to revising the emergency plan and conducting a drill within the next quarter. **Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-031-2023-01**
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Element 2, ILO Forced Labour Convention, 1930 (No. 29))
**Background & Context:** During the document review for the period covering January to October 2023, it was noted that Factory 031 utilizes a temporary worker service provider, "Swift Labor Solutions," for approximately 30% of its sewing line workforce. Initial review of service contracts indicated standard terms; however, subsequent worker interviews revealed concerning practices.
**Observations & Interviews:** On the audit date of 17 October 2023, interviews were conducted with 8 temporary workers from the aforementioned agency, facilitated in private without management presence. A consistent narrative emerged from 6 of the 8 workers interviewed, who stated that their original identification documents (National ID cards) were retained by the labor agency's representative upon hiring. They further reported being told the documents would only be returned upon completion of a mandatory 12-month service period with Factory 031, with the threat of forfeiting all unpaid wages if they resigned prematurely. This constitutes a clear restriction of movement and coercion through deprivation of personal property.
**Analysis:** The retention of personal identity documents by a labor provider, coupled with punitive financial threats for early departure, effectively removes workers' ability to freely terminate employment. This practice is a fundamental indicator of forced labor, as it eliminates voluntary employment through psychological coercion and potential debt bondage (wage forfeiture). The factory management, while claiming unawareness of this specific practice, failed in its due diligence to ensure its labor provider adhered to fundamental labor standards.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note:** Immediate corrective action required prior to any approval or certification. All identity documents must be returned to workers without condition, and the labor provider agreement must be terminated or radically amended with verification.

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area 1.02: Prohibition of Child Labor**
During the review of personnel files for 30 production workers on 12 March 2024, auditor noted that 5 files (Ref: PF-017, PF-021, PF-033, PF-044, PF-050) contained copies of national identity cards indicating the workers' ages as 16 and 17 years. Physical inspection of the production floor at approximately 10:45 AM revealed two individuals, subsequently identified from the aforementioned files, operating heavy-duty band knife cutting machines (Operation Code: C-03), which are explicitly classified as hazardous machinery under local ordinance and our client's prohibited equipment list for minor workers. In subsequent confidential interviews with 12 workers from the cutting department, 3 individuals corroborated that the minors in question regularly perform this high-risk operation. The factory management, when presented with this evidence, acknowledged a lack of formal age verification and risk assessment procedures, attributing this to their limited HR function and familial hiring practices. This constitutes a violation of both national law and client Code of Conduct section 1.2. **Severity: Critical** Immediate cessation of this work assignment and re-deployment to permissible tasks is required, with formal documentation to be submitted for verification.

[Wages & Benefits]
During the review of payroll records for the period of January to March 2024, it was observed that the factory operates on a primarily piece-rate system. Although the payroll summaries indicated that all workers received amounts exceeding the local minimum wage, a detailed reconciliation of production records, timesheets, and pay slips for 10 sampled workers revealed inconsistencies. Specifically, in interviews conducted on 2024-04-15 with 7 production line workers, 4 confirmed that their pay slips (Ref: WC-031-04) do not itemize or separately calculate the legal overtime premium for hours worked beyond the standard 48-hour week, with overtime paid at the same piece-rate as regular hours. This constitutes a failure to comply with the legally mandated overtime rate of at least 1.5 times the regular rate of pay. The management, while cooperative, demonstrated a lack of formal understanding of this requirement, attributing the practice to longstanding industry custom. A follow-up note has been made to request a detailed breakdown of the overtime premium calculation methodology. Severity: Moderate.
