Factory 032 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Child Labor]
During the announced audit conducted on 16-18 October 2023, a review of personnel files for the 89 production workers employed in the cutting and sewing sections revealed a discrepancy concerning one individual, Mr. A. Rahman (Employee ID# CL-032-1147), whose documented date of birth in factory records indicated he was 18 years of age. However, subsequent cross-verification during worker interviews, specifically with interview #WR-032-021, the individual in question disclosed an age of 17 years and confirmed he had been working on the assembly line for five months, a period which commenced when he was 16. This finding represents a breach of Section 7.1 of the Company's Code of Conduct and local labor law, and is of particular concern given that Factory 032 is currently operating under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP-032-09) stemming from previous non-compliances in recruitment verification procedures. The immediate cessation of work for the minor was mandated, and the factory management was instructed to implement enhanced age verification protocols, including the cross-checking of school leaving certificates and national ID cards, with documentation to be submitted for review within seven business days. Severity: Critical

[Discrimination]
Audit finding documented under reference DS-032-04 within the compliance area of Discrimination (Code: 3.4). A review of factory personnel records from January to March 2024, coupled with confidential worker interviews conducted on 17 April 2024 with 14 employees from various production lines, revealed a pattern of discriminatory hiring and assignment practices. It was observed that all 32 workers in the higher-skilled and better-paid stitching section were male, while the entire contingent of 28 workers in the lower-paid finishing and packing sections were female. Interview notes from multiple female workers indicated they had applied for stitching positions but were told by the hiring supervisor that "those machines are only for men" and that "women are more careful for packing work." This constitutes a clear policy of gender-based discrimination in employment and assignment, a repeat issue noted in the previous CAP. The factory is in violation of local labor law and the applicable standard of conduct. Severity: Moderate.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-03-2024 (CAP Follow-up)**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1)**
**Background:** As noted in the CAP stemming from the Q3 2023 audit (Ref: 2023-09-AUD), Factory 032 was cited for systemic overtime violations exceeding legal and code limits, requiring implementation of a revised production planning and timekeeping system. This follow-up inspection aimed to verify the efficacy of those corrective actions.

**Observations & Interviews:** Review of time and attendance records for the period of March 1-15, 2024, indicated a pattern of continued non-compliance. Production records for the cutting and sewing departments showed orders scheduled that inherently required excessive overtime to meet deadlines. Cross-referencing with payroll data revealed that 67% of production line workers exceeded the 60-hour workweek limit during this period, with a sample of 12 workers averaging 74 hours. During confidential interviews conducted on March 28, 2024, with 8 workers from these departments, 7 confirmed working until 10:00 PM or later for 6-7 consecutive days, noting that while formal requests for time off were sometimes recorded, operational pressure made refusal untenable. The mandatory weekly rest day was not consistently provided, as corroborated by gate access logs showing Sunday entries for key departments.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to demonstrate sustainable correction of previous violations. Working hour controls remain ineffective, and management continues to utilize excessive, mandatory overtime as a standard operational practice, in direct violation of local law and the agreed CAP. This represents a persistent, widespread failure of management systems.
**Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-032-24-HA-003**
**Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (CCLS Section 7.1)**
**Background & Context:** During the follow-up audit conducted on 12 March 2024, particular attention was given to the implementation of the Corrective Action Plan (CAP Ref: 23-Q4-102) stemming from the previous non-conformance (NC-23-087) related to inadequate management systems for preventing workplace harassment. The factory's stated policy, as documented in the revised Employee Handbook v.2.1, explicitly prohibits all forms of verbal, physical, and psychological abuse.

**Observations & Interviews:** While documentation was found to be in order, confidential worker interviews conducted with 14 operators from the cutting and sewing departments on 13 March 2024 revealed a persistent disconnect between formal policy and shop-floor realities. Three separate interviewees (Worker IDs #87, #112, and #203) provided consistent, anecdotal accounts of Section Supervisor Mr. [Name Redacted] using derogatory language and public humiliation as a method of productivity control, with one specific incident cited occurring on approximately 28 February 2024. The workers expressed a palpable fear of retaliation, noting the absence of a truly anonymous or trusted reporting mechanism, which was corroborated by the auditor's review of the "Grievance Log" which showed zero harassment-related entries for the past eight months despite these verbal claims.

**Conclusion:** The evidence indicates a failure to effectively implement and enforce the harassment policy, allowing an environment where verbal abuse and fear of retaliation undermines the established procedures. This constitutes a recurrence of the systemic management failure previously identified.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 033 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness (Audit Code: EP.01-EP.08)**
**Finding:** During the inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, it was observed that the factory's emergency preparedness protocols exhibit significant deficiencies, particularly concerning the night shift operations. While primary exit routes for the day shift were found to be nominally clear, an inspection of the western storage annex, which houses fabric inventory and is occupied by eight packing staff during night operations, revealed that the designated secondary emergency exit (Exit C, as per site plan F033-02A) was found chained and padlocked. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews on October 24, with 3 out of 5 night shift workers interviewed stating they had never participated in a fire drill and were unaware of an alternate exit from that section. The management representative indicated the lock was for security purposes due to the rural location and migrant worker population, demonstrating a fundamental failure to prioritize life safety over asset protection. A follow-up note is required to verify the immediate removal of the lock and the implementation of a supervised drill for all shifts.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Code: SC-033-04**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SC.3 - Non-Discrimination & Harassment)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** The audit team's review of Factory 033's hiring records and organizational profile indicates a workforce comprising approximately 87% migrant workers from distant provinces, a common demographic for factories in this rural economic zone where local labor pools are limited. The inspection included a review of hiring, promotion, and payroll records from Q3 2023, alongside confidential worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023.
**Observations & Evidence:** During the review of promotion and training records for the past 18 months, it was noted that of the 23 supervisory and line-leader positions filled, only 2 were occupied by migrant workers, despite migrants constituting the overwhelming majority of the operational workforce. Furthermore, during confidential interviews with 14 migrant workers from the sewing and finishing departments, 9 individuals reported a perceived systematic exclusion from skill development opportunities and advancement, with several noting that verbal communications from certain middle managers suggested a preference for "local stability" over "temporary help," with one worker quoting a manager as stating, "you people are here today and gone tomorrow, so we invest in those who will stay." The factory's published non-discrimination policy, while present, was found to be generic and not actively communicated or reinforced in local dialects.
**Conclusion & Follow-Up Note:** The evidence indicates a pattern of discriminatory practice in advancement and training opportunities based on worker origin and migrant status, which contravenes the audit standard SC.3 and creates a two-tiered workforce. This systemic issue undermines worker morale and represents a significant ethical and legal risk. Management must revise and implement a corrective action plan to ensure equitable access to promotion and training, with clear, measurable objectives for migrant worker inclusion.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
During the site inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team observed systemic deficiencies in the factory's management of hazardous energy within the cutting and sewing sections. Specifically, the lockout/tagout (LOTO) procedures for industrial sewing machines and cutting equipment, as required by code ISO 45001:2018 and referenced in audit clause OSH-07, were found to be either non-existent or grossly inadequate, with multiple machines observed under maintenance without isolation of power sources. Worker interviews with 7 of the 23 maintenance staff, all migrant workers from distant provinces, revealed a consistent lack of training on safe lockout procedures, with several stating they were instructed to perform minor repairs while machines were electrically live to minimize downtime. This constitutes a failure to provide a safe system of work and exposes workers to a high risk of amputations or electrocution. **Severity: Critical** A follow-up verification must be scheduled within 30 days to review corrected procedures and training records.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: F033-2023-08-C-HR-002**
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources (Employment Contracts & Personnel Files)**
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**

Based on a review of personnel records conducted on the aforementioned date, coupled with confidential worker interviews held with 17 production line operators selected at random, a systemic failure in the proper issuance and management of legally compliant employment contracts was documented. The inspector observed that of the 87 personnel files reviewed, only 23 contained signed contracts that met minimum legal standards for content, with the remainder either missing entirely, utilizing an outdated company template lacking key clauses on wage calculation and working hours, or bearing signatures that appeared to be uniformly applied, indicating potential forgery. Worker interviews corroborated this, with 14 of the 17 interviewees stating they had never received a personal copy of a signed contract, and several migrant workers expressed confusion regarding their specific terms of employment, noting they had only verbally agreed to a monthly salary. This practice leaves workers, particularly the vulnerable migrant population prevalent at this site, without clear, written recourse regarding their terms and conditions, constituting a significant failure in due diligence and transparency. A follow-up request to management for immediate rectification and a revised contract template has been issued.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: CL-033-2024-01**
**Compliance Area: CHILD LABOR - Prohibition & Young Workers (SA8000:2014 Section 3 / Client Code of Conduct Section 5.1)**
**Background & Verification Methodology:** During the announced audit conducted on 12-14 March 2024, the verification of child labor prevention protocols included a review of personnel files for 67 production workers (representing approximately 40% of the workforce), inspection of working areas across all production floors, and confidential interviews with 14 randomly selected operators. The factory's rural location and its reliance on migrant labor necessitate heightened due diligence in this area, given the acknowledged risk of documentary fraud in regions with limited access to formal administrative services.
**Observations & Non-Conformity:** While the personnel files reviewed contained identity documents indicating all workers were above the minimum age, a discrepancy was identified during worker interviews. One worker, interviewed on the second shift of 13 March 2024, was observed performing auxiliary thread-cutting tasks in Section B and, when questioned confidentially, stated her age as 15 years. This disclosure was consistent with her apparent physical maturity. Cross-referencing this individual's provided details led to her personnel file, which contained a Local National ID card showing an age of 18. The document presented obvious signs of alteration upon closer examination, a fact not previously identified by factory management.
**Conclusion & Follow-Up Action Required:** The factory's age verification procedure, which relies solely on document submission without robust validation mechanisms, has proven ineffective in preventing the employment of a person under the legal minimum age. Management was immediately notified and the minor was removed from productive work pending further action. The factory is required to submit a corrective action plan detailing enhanced age verification procedures, including document authenticity checks and the implementation of a management-level grievance channel for age concerns, by 28 March 2024.
**Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: SC-08-033-2024 / Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (SA8000:2014 §3.2)**
During the on-site inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2024, the audit team identified systemic procedural failures in the prevention and remediation of workplace harassment. Background context notes the factory's remote rural location and a predominantly migrant workforce, which heightens vulnerability and creates dependency on management structures. While a formal anti-harassment policy was documented, interviews with 17 production operators, selected across three departments, revealed a consistent pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by two identified line supervisors, including public humiliation for missing production targets and the use of derogatory language based on workers' regional origins. Management acknowledged awareness of "personality conflicts" but provided no evidence of investigative procedures or corrective actions taken, indicating a failure in both implementation and the provision of accessible grievance mechanisms. The absence of a functioning, confidential reporting channel, coupled with the workers' expressed fear of retaliation, constitutes a breach of the requirement for a safe and respectful workplace.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan must include supervisor re-training, establishment of a trusted grievance committee with worker representation, and verified confidential worker interviews to be conducted within 90 days.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, a review of time and attendance records for the period of September 2023 revealed systematic discrepancies indicative of non-compliant working hour practices. Inspector observations and payroll analysis (Ref: WH-033-01) identified that production line workers consistently worked seven-day weeks during peak periods, with interviewed workers (8 of 12 interviewed) confirming verbal instructions from line supervisors to under-report hours. Specifically, worker testimonies noted regular shifts of 12 hours, yet corresponding records showed only 10-hour days, thereby obscuring both the excessive daily hours and the unlawful seventh workday. This practice, coupled with the factory's remote location and the vulnerable migrant worker profile, creates a high risk of worker exploitation and fatigue. The management acknowledged the pressure to meet delivery deadlines but failed to provide a robust timekeeping system to prevent falsification. **Severity: Critical**. A corrective action plan must address systemic record-keeping failures and implement verified rest days.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-033-02: Compliance Area - Forced Labor & Involuntary Work**
During the on-site inspection conducted on 17-18 October 2023, a review of payroll records for the preceding six months and confidential interviews with 22 of the 287 production workers, predominantly migrant workers from distant provinces, revealed systemic practices indicative of coercion. It was documented that management routinely withholds workers' original identity documents and residency permits under the pretext of "safekeeping," with 19 of the interviewed workers confirming they do not have personal possession of these papers, creating a significant barrier to freedom of movement. Furthermore, interview notes from Worker #11 and #15 detailed that they were required to pay recruitment fees equivalent to three months' base salary, a debt leveraged to prevent resignation, with repayment enforced through excessive and unexplained payroll deductions noted in the sample audit of January 2023 payroll files. This combination of document retention, debt bondage, and the factory's isolated rural location, which limits workers' access to alternative employment or legal recourse, constitutes a severe infringement of ILO Core Conventions 29 and 105. A follow-up review of the dormitory registers also indicated a pattern of unexplained deductions for sub-standard accommodation, further tying workers to the facility. **Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WAG-033/24-08
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Payment of Wages & Overtime)
**Date of Inspection:** 23-24 October 2023
**Background & Observations:** Given the factory's rural location and workforce composition of predominantly migrant workers, particular attention was paid to wage payment systems and record-keeping. A review of payroll records for September 2023, along with corresponding time cards and bank transfer statements, revealed inconsistencies. Specifically, for the pay period ending 15 September, the overtime hours recorded for 34 workers in the cutting department were not reflected in the corresponding wage payments, with a calculated average shortfall of 12.3 hours per worker for that period.
**Worker Interviews:** During confidential interviews conducted on 24 October, 7 out of 10 workers from the cutting department stated they had not received correct overtime compensation for the previous month and were unsure of the precise overtime rate, citing reliance on a complex piece-rate system they did not fully understand. Management acknowledged a "clerical error" in payroll processing for that specific department and period.
**Conclusion:** This constitutes a failure to pay wages for all hours worked in accordance with contractual and legal requirements, as per audit code WAG-1.2. The finding is systemic due to the number of workers affected and the confirmed record-keeping failure. A follow-up verification of corrected payments will be required within 30 days.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FA-033-2023-087 | Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: FOA.01)** 

Following a review of provided documentation and conducted interviews on 14-16 November 2023, it was found that while the factory management has not formally prohibited the formation of worker associations, the practical environment severely impedes this right. Background context notes the workforce is predominantly composed of migrant workers residing in company-provided dormitories with limited independent mobility, a factor exacerbating their vulnerability. During separate, confidential interviews with 22 workers across various production departments, 18 individuals expressed unawareness of any existing worker representative committee or trade union, with several stating a belief that initiating such organization would lead to managerial disapproval or non-renewal of their temporary contracts. This sentiment was indirectly corroborated by the absence of any posted notices, collective bargaining agreements, or meeting minutes regarding worker-elected representation, and the Human Resources Manager could not produce evidence of any active, democratically elected worker body. The combination of a geographically isolated workforce with limited access to external legal or organizational support and the prevailing fear of reprisal creates a de facto restriction on freedom of association. **Severity: Moderate**. Follow-up required: management must develop and implement a clear, communicated policy with non-retaliation assurances and facilitate democratic election of worker representatives, with proof of procedure and training to be submitted within 90 days.


============================================================
Factory 034 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref. WH-034-2023-08**
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (Section 7.1 of the Supplier Code of Conduct).

**Background & Context:** The audit at Factory 034 was conducted on October 26-27, 2023. Given the factory's profile being located in a rural industrial zone and its reliance on a predominantly migrant workforce, particular attention was paid to working hour practices and overtime management, as these are commonly problematic areas in such operational environments.

**Observations & Document Review:** A review of attendance records and production reports for the three-month period of July through September 2023 revealed systematic and excessive overtime. Production peaks, particularly in the weeks of August 14 and September 11, show workers on the main sewing lines consistently exceeding 85 hours of work per week. Attendance punch cards, though manually maintained, corroborate this data, indicating regular 13-hour shifts spread over seven consecutive days, with no 24-hour rest period observed in any seven-day cycle during the reviewed months.

**Worker Interviews:** Confidential interviews were held with 14 production line workers selected at random from the dormitories. Their testimonies uniformly confirmed the observed patterns, with several workers stating that refusal to work the mandated overtime, communicated verbally by line supervisors, would result in punitive measures including reduced allocation of work in subsequent days and threats of dismissal. Notably, eight of the fourteen interviewed workers stated they had not received any premium pay for these extensive overtime hours, with their payslips reflecting only the regular hourly rate.

**Conclusion:** The factory is in violation of both the legal limits for working hours and the Code of Conduct’s requirements for a 24-hour rest period and premium overtime compensation. The practices are systemic, management-driven, and exploit the vulnerable position of the migrant workforce. **Severity: Critical.** Corrective Action Plan required within 7 business days, with a follow-up verification audit to be scheduled within 90 days to review implementation of revised timekeeping and payroll systems.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-034-2023-08**
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (CCS Section 7.1)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Context & Observations:** During the document review and subsequent worker interviews, the audit team identified systemic vulnerabilities related to worker freedom of movement and coercion. The factory's rural location and workforce composition (approximately 92% migrant workers from distant provinces) create a heightened risk profile. It was observed that the factory management retains all original employee identity documents and work permits for "safe-keeping" pursuant to a stated policy reviewed by auditors, which management asserts is for the workers' own benefit and to facilitate local police registration requirements.

**Worker Interviews:** In confidential interviews conducted on October 26 with 14 production line workers selected at random from the dormitory roster, 11 workers confirmed their original national ID cards and/or household registration documents were held by the factory's administration office. Eight of these workers expressed a clear desire to retrieve their documents but feared termination or penalty if they insisted. Three workers interviewed separately indicated they had been required to pay a "deposit" of 1,000 RMB upon hiring, which was documented as a "training fee," with the understanding it would be forfeited if they resigned before completing a 24-month service period. This practice was corroborated through a review of a sample of employment contracts and intake forms.

**Conclusion:** The combination of unlawful document retention, which effectively restricts workers' ability to seek alternative employment or leave the isolated area, coupled with the imposition of a financial deposit constituting a debt burden levied to compel continued employment, creates a situation of involuntary work. These practices are in direct violation of the benchmark code and local labor law, indicating a failure of management systems to ensure basic worker freedoms.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note:** A formal corrective action plan (CAP) must address immediate document return, reimbursement of all deposits, and revision of all relevant hiring and HR policies. Legal remediation must be verified within 30 days.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: AF-034-WB-2023-008**
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (WB)
**Date(s) of Investigation:** October 23-26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** The audit scope included a review of wage records for the period of August-September 2023 and worker interviews. It was noted that the factory employs a significant population of migrant workers (estimated at 85% of the workforce) on short-term contracts, a factor which necessitates heightened diligence in wage payment procedures. During the document review, inspectors identified a systemic discrepancy between the hours recorded on the factory's manually maintained attendance ledgers and the considerably lower hours subsequently transcribed into the official payroll system, particularly for the stitching and finishing departments.

**Worker Interview Summary:** Confidential interviews were conducted with 22 workers across all production departments. 17 of these workers (approximately 77% of interviewees) provided consistent testimony that their received wages for the last two pay periods fell short of expected amounts, citing unpaid overtime as the primary concern. Specifically, multiple workers from Line 4 and Line 7 stated that overtime work performed on 12 separate days in September, each ranging from 1.5 to 3 hours, was not compensated at the legally mandated premium rate, but was instead either paid at the regular rate or omitted entirely from their pay slips. This practice constitutes a failure to remunerate workers for all hours worked and a violation of statutory overtime calculations.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Note:** Based on the corroborative evidence from document review and worker testimony, the factory is in violation of applicable wage laws for failing to pay for all hours worked and for failing to apply the correct overtime premium. This is a repeated finding from the previous audit (see CAR-034-2022-015). The prevalence and financial impact on a vulnerable workforce necessitate a significant corrective action plan. **Severity: Critical.**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference CL-034-2023-02**

During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 23-24 October 2023, a potential child labor violation was identified. While no individuals appearing underage were observed on the production floor during the walk-through, the procedural review revealed systemic deficiencies in the age verification process. The factory's HR files for 12 recently hired seasonal workers, recruited during the peak period in August 2023, contained only national ID card copies; there was no evidence of a secondary age verification check, such as cross-referencing with household registries or school-leaving certificates, a critical control point given the demographic of the predominantly migrant workforce. Furthermore, interviews with four workers from the packing section revealed one individual (Worker ID# 7812) who, when discussing their work history, stated they had "been at the factory for three years since leaving home," a timeline which, if correlated with the provided ID birthdate, would indicate they were 15 years old at the time of initial hiring. The factory management was unable to produce robust retrospective verification for this employee's hiring date. This represents a failure in the ongoing due diligence process to identify and remediate past instances of underage labor. **Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SAF-2023-034-08  
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)  
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023  
**Background & Context:** The audit included a review of OSH management systems, physical inspections of the production and storage areas, and confidential worker interviews. The factory's location in a rural industrial zone and its workforce composition, predominantly comprising migrant workers with limited awareness of local legal recourse, were noted as contextual factors for this assessment.  

**Observations & Evidence:** During the walk-through of the cutting and sewing sections on October 26, the audit team observed a systematic failure in the implementation of machine guarding. Specifically, seven out of twelve band saw machines in the cutting room were found to be operating without the required blade guards, exposing workers to direct contact with moving blades. This condition was previously identified in the internal checklist from May 2023 but remained uncorrected. Furthermore, in confidential interviews conducted with 14 production workers, 9 confirmed that they had not received any hands-on training regarding lockout/tagout procedures or specific machine safety hazards, relying instead on informal peer guidance. The factory’s training records for the past year showed only generic orientation sessions with no machine-specific certifications.  

**Conclusion & Follow-up Requirement:** This constitutes a breach of section 7.4 of the OSH code and the factory’s own stated procedures, creating an immediate and high-probability risk of serious physical injury. The root cause is attributed to inadequate supervisory oversight and a lack of dedicated safety maintenance resources. The corrective action plan must include the immediate safeguarding of all identified machinery, verified by photographic evidence, and the implementation of a validated training program for all affected operators, to be submitted for review by November 30, 2023.  
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-034-07-2023**
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources (Employment Agreements & Personnel Files)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

During the document review and worker interviews, it was observed that the factory's practices regarding employment contracts do not meet the requisite standard for providing clear, written terms of employment as mandated by local labor law and client code of conduct. A review of personnel files for a randomized sample of 30 production workers, conducted on October 26, revealed that only 40% contained a signed, individualized employment contract. Subsequent interviews with 15 workers from the cutting and sewing departments confirmed this systemic issue, with 12 interviewees stating they had never been presented with a formal contract to sign, and instead relied solely on verbal agreements regarding their wages and terms. This failure to provide formalized contracts exposes the predominantly migrant workforce to significant risk regarding the enforcement of their terms of employment, leaving them vulnerable to misunderstandings and potential exploitation due to their limited access to legal resources in this rural locality. **Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: EP-034-2024-02**
**COMPLIANCE AREA: Emergency Preparedness (Egress & Drills)**
**AUDIT DATE(S): October 22-23, 2024**

**BACKGROUND & OBSERVATIONS:** The factory operates from a three-story reinforced concrete structure. During the physical inspection of the second-floor sewing section, which houses approximately 180 workers per shift, it was noted that two of the four designated primary exit doors were obstructed. One was locked with a deadbolt ("for stock security," per a line supervisor), and the other was partially blocked by a rack of WIP garments, reducing the clear egress width to 0.6 meters, below the 1.1-meter standard as referenced in code FFP-7.1. Interview notes from discussions with 14 randomly selected workers on October 23 indicated a unanimous lack of participation in a full building evacuation drill within the last 12 months; their understanding of assembly point locations was inconsistent, with most citing the main parking area despite the posted emergency plan directing personnel to a field 150 meters to the west. Management provided a log showing quarterly fire extinguisher checks but could not produce verified records of coordinated emergency drills involving all personnel.

**FOLLOW-UP & CONCLUSION:** The combination of impeded egress routes, inadequate drill frequency, and insufficient worker training on emergency procedures creates a significant risk to life safety, particularly given the high occupant load and the migrant worker population's potential unfamiliarity with the premises. The factory is required to submit a corrective action plan addressing these systemic failures within 14 days.
**Severity: Critical**


============================================================
Factory 035 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: EP-035-2024-002**

**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Fire & General Evacuation)

**Background & Observations:** The audit team conducted a review of the factory's emergency preparedness protocols on 14 March 2024, noting the generally high standard of housekeeping and clear, unobstructed main aisles. A documented emergency response plan is in place and posted in common areas. However, during the physical inspection of the second-floor finishing department, it was observed that one of two designated secondary evacuation routes, specifically the stairwell access door marked "Exit B," was found to be functionally obstructed. While not fully locked, the door's panic bar mechanism was noted to be stiff and required significant force (approximately 15kg of pressure as tested by the lead auditor) to activate, potentially causing delay and crowd compression during an urgent evacuation. This condition was verified to be a chronic issue, as noted in a previous internal safety committee memo from January 2024 (Ref: SC/Memo/24-01), for which a corrective work order had been issued but not yet completed.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews with 8 workers from the affected department confirmed awareness of evacuation procedures and assembly points, a positive indicator of routine drills. However, three of the interviewed workers spontaneously cited difficulties with the Exit B door, with one stating, "We know it sticks, so we all just plan to use the other stairs if the alarm goes off." This indicates a procedural workaround that undermines the designed redundancy of the evacuation plan.

**Conclusion:** The failure to maintain a designated emergency exit in immediate and full working order constitutes a violation of section 7.3 of the approved Code of Conduct and relevant local fire safety regulations. While the factory's overall emergency preparedness framework is robust and the primary exits remain fully functional, this specific deficiency creates a measurable increase in risk for a portion of the workforce. The finding is classified as Moderate, as the obstruction was not absolute and alternative egress was available, but the failure to address a known deficiency is of concern.

**Severity: Moderate**

**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Request (CAR) EP-035-002 issued; requires demonstration of repair and a supervised drill utilizing the corrected exit route within 30 days.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref:** OS&H-035-2024-008 **| Date of Inspection:** 17 April 2024 **| Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OS&H) - Emergency Preparedness

**Context & Observations:** During the routine inspection of the facility's emergency preparedness protocols, the audit team noted a generally high standard of housekeeping and clearly marked primary egress routes. However, a detailed walkthrough of the cutting and spreading section on the second floor, conducted on the aforementioned date, revealed that two of the four required emergency exits were partially obstructed. Specifically, Exit Door B-02 was blocked by a mobile fabric rack storing approximately 15 rolls of denim, reducing the clear width to 0.6 meters, while the secondary access to the external stairwell (Exit B-04) was impeded by several unsecured cardboard bales awaiting disposal. These conditions were confirmed via photographic evidence and physical measurement. Interviews with a cross-section of 12 workers from the affected department indicated awareness of the exit locations but confirmed the obstructions were a chronic issue, with three workers noting the rack had been stationed there for "at least two weeks," a statement corroborated by the factory's own internal safety committee meeting minutes from 02 April 2024 which flagged the issue for rectification. Despite the presence of a strong grievance mechanism and generally positive industrial relations, the failure to implement timely corrective actions for known egress obstructions constitutes a failure to uphold Section 7.3 of the Client's Code of Conduct and represents a significant immediate risk to life safety in the event of a fire or other emergency requiring rapid evacuation.

**Severity: Critical**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WH-2023-035-02
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1, B.1.3)
**Date of Discovery:** 17 October 2023

**Background & Observation:** While the factory maintains detailed and accurate weekly timecards for all 1,247 employees, a reconciliation of payroll records for the audit period of September 1-30, 2023, against these timecards revealed a systematic discrepancy. Inspector observations and subsequent cross-verification identified that production line workers in the cutting and sewing departments (approximately 309 individuals) consistently exceeded the 60-hour maximum work week, as stipulated in the client's Code of Conduct, during three separate weeks within the month. Specifically, the weeks ending September 8, 15, and 22 showed an average of 62.5 hours worked for this worker subgroup. It is noted that all overtime was voluntary and compensated at the correct premium rate, and the factory's strong grievance mechanism was not utilized by workers regarding this issue, indicating possible awareness and acceptance of the practice.

**Worker Interviews:** Confidential interviews conducted with 14 workers from the affected departments on October 17 confirmed the excessive hours, with several stating the extra time was necessary to meet shipping deadlines for a major order. All interviewed workers affirmed they had consented to the overtime.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** Despite the presence of excellent worker-management relations and a functional union, which was consulted and acknowledged this finding, the factory has failed to implement adequate operational controls to ensure adherence to weekly maximum hour limits. Management is requested to submit a corrective action plan detailing revised production planning protocols by November 10, 2023.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, the audit team identified a discrepancy in the personnel file for employee ID #035-2287 (Mr. A. Rahman), hired on 15 August 2023, where the provided National ID number, upon cross-verification with the issued certificate, indicated a date of birth corresponding to an age of 14 years at the time of hiring. Subsequent confidential interviews with three workers from the finishing section, including one union steward, corroborated that the individual in question is commonly known to be the younger brother of a senior operator and is believed to be underage, with one interviewee noting he "should be in school." While the factory's established grievance mechanism and generally sound worker-management relations were noted, and management immediately suspended the individual pending verification upon being presented with these findings, the presence of a worker under the legal minimum age constitutes a breach of CLS standard 1.1. Follow-up action requires the factory to provide valid age verification documentation, complete a root-cause analysis of the recruitment process failure, and implement immediate remediation for the juvenile in accordance with national law.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-035-24-008 **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Section 7.1 of the Supplier Code of Conduct). **Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023. During a review of human resources procedures and worker interviews, the audit team identified a procedural inconsistency with the factory's otherwise strong record in industrial relations. While management demonstrated a clear policy of non-discrimination and the active union provides a robust channel for collective issues, the hiring and assignment process for the specialized 'Thread Checking' department shows a potential bias. Interview notes from 8 out of 12 workers in that department, conducted on October 25, revealed that all current operatives are male, with multiple interviewees stating that supervisors have informally expressed a preference for male workers in these roles due to a perceived "better aptitude for the detailed visual focus required," a notion not supported by any objective skills assessment. This has resulted in a de facto gender-based barrier to entry for this specific job classification, contrary to equal opportunity principles. The factory management was cooperative in acknowledging this discrepancy and has committed to a revised, skills-based hiring protocol. **Severity: Minor.** Follow-up required within 90 days to review revised recruitment records for the Thread Checking department.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: CHR-035-24-008**
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources**
**Audit Dates: October 22-24, 2024**

A review of personnel files and employment contracts was conducted for a representative sample of 35 workers across various departments and employment tenures, selected from a total workforce of 1,242. While the factory maintains a generally robust HR administration system and the documented worker-management relations are positive, inspectors observed a procedural inconsistency in the issuance of signed contracts. It was noted that for 7 out of the 35 files reviewed (approximately 20% of the sample), the worker's signed copy of the employment contract was not provided to the worker at the time of hiring, but was instead retained in the personnel file; interviews with three of these workers confirmed they had received only an unsigned copy for their records at onboarding. Although all contracts were ultimately signed by both parties and contained all legally mandated clauses, this practice represents a deviation from the requirement for workers to possess their own executed copy, potentially diminishing transparency. The HR manager attributed this to an administrative oversight during a period of high recruitment in early Q3 2024 and has committed to rectifying the process.
**Severity: Minor**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
**Finding Reference: WB-2023-035-02**

A review of wage records for the period covering March through May 2023, alongside worker interviews conducted on 15 May 2023, revealed a procedural inconsistency in the calculation and payment of legally mandated annual leave. While the factory correctly provides and records annual leave entitlement, the calculation for the leave payment itself is based on the basic wage rate, as confirmed in interviews with 7 of 22 production line workers and verified in payroll records for sampled employees (e.g., Employee IDs #4512, #6783). This practice does not conform to Section 12, Article 45 of the national Labour Law, which stipulates that annual leave pay must be calculated based on the worker's average earnings, inclusive of all regular allowances. The factory's active union representative, during a management interview on 16 May, acknowledged this discrepancy and indicated it had been raised informally but not yet formally resolved through the collective bargaining agreement. Follow-up note: The HR department has been notified to develop a corrected calculation methodology.
**Severity: Minor**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref:** FO-035-24-008
**Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Audit Code: ILO C087 & C098)
**Background & Observation:** The audit scope included a review of the factory's industrial relations framework, with particular attention to the functionality of the Collective Bargaining Agreement (CBA) effective from January 2024 and the operations of the registered union "Unity Textile Workers' Union" (UTWU). During the document review on 12 November 2024, it was verified that the current CBA was duly signed and posted in Bengali on all departmental notice boards. Furthermore, observation of the monthly Joint Consultation Committee meeting on 14 November 2024, attended by 7 elected worker representatives and 4 management members, demonstrated a formalized and interactive dialogue process.
**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted with 14 workers across various production lines on 13-14 November 2024, including 5 union executive committee members, confirmed that union dues are collected on-premises without interference and that union representatives are granted reasonable time and facility access to perform their duties. Several workers spontaneously cited the recent successful negotiation of an inflation-adjusted food allowance as evidence of the bargaining process.
**Conclusion:** The mechanisms for freedom of association and collective bargaining are not only formally established but are actively and effectively utilized, with no evidence of anti-union discrimination or interference found. Management provides demonstrable support for the union's legitimate activities.
**Status: Compliant.**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference: FA-035-24-018 | Compliance Area: HAR-01 (Harassment & Abuse) | Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

During the evaluation of management systems and worker interview processes, the audit team conducted a documented review of the factory's grievance mechanism logs (Grievance Register entries #147 through #162, dated Q3 2023) and held confidential interviews with 32 workers across various production departments. While the established formal procedures and the active union presence were noted as significant strengths, contributing to a generally positive industrial relations climate, interviews revealed a persistent, lower-level cultural issue. Specifically, 7 of the 32 workers interviewed (approximately 22%) indicated, without prompting, a recurring pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by two identified line supervisors in the cutting section, involving the use of derogatory language and public humiliation to enforce production targets, which has not been formally reported through the grievance channel due to fears of indirect retaliation. This indicates a gap between the formally documented policy and its consistent application at the supervisory level, requiring targeted management review and corrective training. **Severity: Moderate**

Factory 036 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Reference:** SA-036-2023-08, Section 5.2 (Equal Opportunity)
**Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** The audit team reviewed the factory's hiring, promotion, and disciplinary policies during the transitional period following the ownership change in Q2 2023. While the documented policies were found to be nominally compliant with local law and client SCoC requirements, implementation at the operational level was inconsistent. **Observations & Interviews:** A review of promotion and team leader assignment records from June to October 2023 indicated a disproportionate assignment of male workers to higher-skilled operator and supervisory roles within the sewing department, despite a workforce that is approximately 68% female. During confidential interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, 7 out of 15 female workers from two separate production lines stated that they were actively discouraged from applying for mechanic helper positions, with two workers quoting a line supervisor as saying, "that job is too technical and heavy for women; you are better with the machines." This sentiment was not echoed in management interviews, indicating a disconnect between formal policy and floor-level practice. **Conclusion & Follow-Up:** This constitutes a violation of the client's Code of Conduct regarding non-discrimination in employment opportunities. The factory is required to provide documented, non-discriminatory criteria for all advancement opportunities and conduct immediate anti-discrimination training for all supervisory staff. Severity: Moderate.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: WB-036-24-008**
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Payment of Wages)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the recent change in ownership and ongoing management transition, this inspection focused on verifying the accurate calculation and timely payment of wages for all workers, particularly for the September 2023 pay period. A review of payroll records for 30 randomly selected workers across 3 production departments was conducted, supplemented by confidential worker interviews.

**Observations & Interviews:** The payroll register and individual payslips for September showed a consistent pattern of miscalculation for piece-rate workers exceeding their production targets. The factory's newly implemented payroll software was found to be incorrectly applying the higher piece-rate bonus, calculating it only on the units exceeding the target rather than on all units produced once the target was met, as stipulated in the individual employment contracts reviewed. This administrative error, attributed to the software configuration during the transition, was confirmed through a line-by-line reconciliation for 8 of the 30 sample files. During confidential interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, 7 out of 12 piece-rate workers from the sewing section spontaneously reported receiving "less bonus than expected" for September, though they could not specify the exact discrepancy, aligning with the documented systemic error. The preliminary financial assessment indicates an average underpayment of approximately 4.5% of total earnings for affected piece-rate personnel.

**Follow-Up Note:** Management was alerted to this discrepancy on-site and acknowledged the software configuration issue. A full review of all piece-rate worker payments for September and October 2023 is required, followed by immediate remediation for all affected workers with back-pay plus any legally mandated interest. A corrected software configuration must be verified prior to the November payroll run.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2023-036-04 **| Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Machinery & Personal Protective Equipment) **| Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

Following the recent change in ownership and management transition noted in the factory profile, the audit team conducted a detailed inspection of the cutting and sewing sections, with particular attention to machinery safety protocols. It was observed that 17 out of 32 industrial sewing machines inspected had ineffective or missing needle guards, and the safety interlocks on two fabric cutting machines in Section B had been deliberately bypassed with tape, a condition acknowledged by the line supervisor as a "temporary measure" to prevent production delays. During worker interviews conducted on October 27, 2023, 5 of the 12 operators interviewed confirmed they had not received formal training on the correct use of machine guards under the new management, and expressed concern over the increased risk of laceration and entanglement. This constitutes a failure to ensure engineering controls are maintained and workers are adequately protected from foreseeable mechanical hazards, as required by section 7.4 of the Client's Code of Conduct and local occupational safety regulations. The management has been advised to implement immediate corrective actions and provide documented retraining.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 037 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
During the review of human resources documentation conducted on August 12, 2023, it was found that while permanent employees have compliant labor contracts on file, the factory's utilization of temporary dispatch workers from three separate agencies lacked the requisite formal tripartite agreements clearly outlining the responsibilities of the factory, agency, and worker, as stipulated in the client's Code of Conduct (Ref: COC-HR-008). Interviews with 12 agency workers across two product lines confirmed this gap, with several workers stating they had only signed a document with the agency and had received no formal contract from Factory 037 itself, leaving ambiguity regarding workplace grievance procedures. A subsequent request for these tripartite agreements yielded only bilateral contracts between the factory and the agencies, omitting worker signatures and specific terms of engagement, which presents a significant risk in managing labor obligations for this contingent workforce, which numbered approximately 85 individuals at the time of the audit. The factory management acknowledged the oversight and committed to rectifying this with the labor agencies within 30 days. Severity: Moderate.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-037-24-008 | Compliance Area: FOA & CB (SA8000:2014 Section 4, ILO Conventions 87 & 98) | Audit Date: October 26, 2023**

During the document review and subsequent worker interviews, it was identified that while the factory has a legally registered enterprise-level trade union, its practical functionality and representation of worker interests appear severely constrained. Management provided minutes from the last union committee meeting, dated eight months prior on February 15, 2023; however, in confidential interviews held on-site, 14 out of 18 randomly selected workers from various production departments stated they were unaware of the union’s existence or its elected representatives, and no collective bargaining agreement was available for review. Furthermore, inspection of the posted notices in the main canteen and production floors revealed no information pertaining to union activities or leadership, and the designated union office was found to be repurposed as a storage room for sewing machine spare parts. This systemic lack of operational trade union presence and the absence of good faith bargaining mechanisms effectively deny workers the right to freedom of association and collective bargaining as per international standards. **Severity: Moderate.** *Follow-up Required: Factory management must demonstrate concrete, verifiable steps to reactivate the union structure, ensure democratic elections, provide a dedicated functional space, and commence good faith bargaining within a corrective action plan timeline.*

Factory 038 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Code: EHS-EP-038-2024-003**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date of On-site Inspection: 17 October 2024**

While a review of the documented emergency response plan and training records indicates a generally robust programmatic approach, consistent with the facility's established profile and ISO 14001 certification, a discrepancy between procedure and practical execution was identified during the unannounced evacuation drill conducted at 14:30 hrs. The evacuation of the second-floor cutting section, housing approximately 87 workers, was observed to be delayed by 4 minutes and 15 seconds beyond the factory's internal target time, due to two of the four designated secondary exits being obstructed by temporary storage of WIP (work-in-progress) bundles and a non-functional emergency light fixture in the adjacent corridor, as corroborated during interviews with 5 operators from that section. Although the primary routes remained clear and the alarm was audible, this failure to maintain all means of egress unimpeded and properly illuminated represents a deviation from section 7.3 of the approved emergency plan and international best practice for life safety. The compliance officer has been advised to initiate immediate corrective action and provide evidence of clearance.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FL-2023-038-02
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor / Coercive Labor Practices (SA8000:2014 Section 1, Client Code of Conduct Section 5.1)
**Background & Context:** Factory 038 maintains a robust social compliance management system, as evidenced by its ISO 9001 certification and longstanding positive audit history. The facility employs a full-time, trained Compliance Officer, and management demonstrated a proactive attitude toward continual improvement during the opening meeting. The audit included a review of policies, payroll records for the period of January to March 2023, and worker interviews conducted on 23-24 April 2023.
**Observations & Evidence:** During a documentation review, it was identified that the factory's "Worker Discipline Policy" (Doc. Ref. HR-007, Rev. 3), while comprehensive, retains a clause permitting the deduction of wages for disciplinary infractions related to quality errors, specifically citing "negligent workmanship leading to production of B-grade goods." This policy was confirmed as active by the Human Resources manager. Interviews with 14 production line workers, conducted privately, revealed that while no such deductions had been implemented within the last 12 months, three workers expressed uncertainty and concern that their pay could be reduced for making mistakes, indicating a perceived financial coercion.
**Analysis & Conclusion:** The retention of a policy clause allowing for punitive wage deductions, even if not recently enacted, creates a climate of financial coercion and constitutes a clear violation of the "No forced labor" principle, which prohibits any form of monetary penalty as a disciplinary measure. This policy directly contravenes both international standards and client requirements, as it could compel work through the threat of a financial penalty.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Required:** The factory must revise the Worker Discipline Policy to remove all clauses permitting punitive wage deductions and provide documented training to all workers and management on the revised, compliant policy by 31 May 2023.

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: CL-2023-038-02**
During the document review and worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, the audit team identified a potential gap in the verification of age documentation for one (1) individual. While the factory's strong procedural framework and ISO-certified systems were confirmed, an interview with Worker ID# 4172 (employed in the finishing section) revealed that his original national ID card was retained by a prior recruitment agent in his hometown. The factory's personnel file for this worker contained only a notarized photocopy, which, although appearing genuine, does not meet the strict "original document verification" requirement stipulated in the company's own Code of Conduct (Section 4.1) and local law for conclusive age confirmation. The Compliance Officer was notified immediately and commenced a re-verification process with the relevant authorities. This is considered an isolated procedural lapse rather than evidence of intentional employment of underage labor, but it represents a failure in the initial hiring control.
**Severity: Minor**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Area 3.01: Non-Discrimination & Equal Opportunity (SA8000:2014, Sec. 3; ILO Conventions 100 & 111; Factory Code of Conduct, Sec. 5.2)**
**Background & Scope:** During the audit conducted on 17-18 October 2023, the area of non-discrimination was reviewed through a combination of document examination (including hiring, promotion, and disciplinary records from Q1-Q3 2023), management interviews, and confidential worker consultations. A total of 32 workers from various departments and employment tenures were interviewed in private sessions facilitated by a native-language interpreter.

**Observations & Evidence:** While the factory’s formal policies are comprehensive and prominently displayed, and management demonstrated a clear understanding of principles, interviews with production line workers revealed a persistent, albeit informal, practice of assignment bias. Specifically, 7 of 15 interviewed sewing operators from the “Basic Line” reported that lighter-color or more complex garment assemblies are routinely assigned to younger female workers (typically under age 30), while older female workers (aged 45 and above) are consistently allocated heavier materials (e.g., denim, canvas) and simpler stitching operations. Management confirmed this operational pattern exists, justifying it as “efficiency-based team structuring,” but could not produce any objective skills-assessment records to substantiate that the assignments were based solely on merit or capability rather than age. This creates a two-tier system where older workers have less opportunity to earn higher piece-rate incentives linked to more complex work.

**Conclusion:** This constitutes a violation of the principle of equal opportunity in employment and assignment, as it results in inequitable treatment based on age, which is a protected category. The finding is classified as Minor due to the absence of any discriminatory intent in formal policy, the lack of impact on hiring or base wage, and the factory’s otherwise strong compliance culture; however, it requires corrective action to align practice with policy.
**Severity: Minor**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA038-2023-09-28 / FO-02**

**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: B.7.1)**

While the factory maintains a generally positive industrial relations environment and a written policy affirming workers' rights to freedom of association, the audit team identified a procedural inconsistency during document review and worker interviews conducted on September 26-27, 2023. Review of the 'Worker Representative Election' records from the most recent election in Q1 2023 showed that the process, though formally documented, was facilitated entirely by management without the presence of a neutral third-party observer, which represents a deviation from best practice guidelines for ensuring procedural integrity. Interviews with 14 production workers across three departments revealed that four workers (approximately 28.5%) were either unaware of the election's occurrence or unsure of the current representatives' identities, indicating a lapse in effective communication. The factory's dedicated compliance officer acknowledged this gap and has proposed a corrective action plan to revise the election protocol and implement enhanced communication channels, to be reviewed at the next management committee meeting. **Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-038-2023-07-F02**  
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (SA8000:2014 Section 7 / Buyer Code of Conduct Section 5.1)  

**Background & Context:** Factory 038 maintains a documented policy prohibiting harassment and abuse, overseen by a dedicated compliance officer and supported by historically strong audit records. During the onsite inspection on October 26, 2023, the audit team reviewed the grievance mechanism logs and training records, which indicated procedural compliance. However, during confidential worker interviews conducted with 37 employees from various departments on October 27, 2023, a recurring theme emerged regarding supervisory conduct in the finishing section.  

**Observations & Interview Notes:** Multiple workers (specifically 5 of 12 interviewed from the finishing department) described instances wherein line supervisor Mr. [Name Redacted] routinely uses derogatory language and personal insults, referring to workers as "too slow" or "useless," particularly during peak production periods. These verbal reprimands, reported to have escalated in frequency over the past three months, are delivered publicly on the production floor, creating an environment of humiliation and fear. While no physical abuse was alleged, and the factory’s formal grievance channel exists, interviewed workers expressed reluctance to report due to concerns over retaliation and a belief that such "shouting" is an accepted managerial method to meet targets. The compliance officer confirmed awareness of "general morale issues" in that department but cited no formal grievances filed, indicating a potential gap in the effectiveness of the remedy system.  

**Conclusion & Follow-up Requirement:** The evidence demonstrates a failure to fully uphold the requirement for a workplace free from harassment, specifically verbal abuse and humiliation, which constitutes a breach of both the factory’s policy and international standards. The factory is required to conduct a formal investigation into the allegations, implement corrective actions including supervisor retraining and reinforced non-retaliation assurances, and provide evidence of such to the auditing body within 30 days.  
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Area/Code:** WOH-01 (Working Hours & Overtime Compliance)
**Date of Review:** October 26, 2023
**Reference Documents Reviewed:** Attendance records for September 2023, wage slips for August & September 2023, factory working hour policy (version 3.1), posted production schedules.

**Finding Narrative:**
During the review of September 2023 attendance records for a sample of 127 production workers across three production lines, it was identified that while daily working hours were generally within legal limits, the factory failed to consistently provide the mandatory one consecutive day of rest in a seven-day period. Specifically, for the pay period ending September 16, approximately 43% of the sampled workforce worked for 13 consecutive days without a rest day, which was corroborated during confidential interviews with 8 workers from the cutting and sewing departments. Although the factory maintains ISO-certified procedures and the dedicated compliance officer demonstrated a strong understanding of the requirements, the internal monitoring system did not flag this patterned violation of weekly rest provisions. Management stated this was due to an unforeseen rush order, but acknowledged the procedural lapse in seeking the necessary overtime exemptions and ensuring compensatory time off. This constitutes a systemic failure in the application of working hour controls.

**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 039 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: WH-OT-03, CAP Item #7 from Q4-2023 Audit)
**Background & Context:** Factory 039 has a documented history of non-compliance regarding excessive working hours and failure to accurately compensate overtime, for which a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) was implemented following the audit conducted on 12 November 2023. The current audit specifically reviewed adherence to the mandated 60-hour maximum work week, proper overtime authorization, and accurate payroll records for the period of January to March 2024.
**Observations & Interviews:** A detailed review of time cards and payroll records for March 2024 revealed consistent seven-day work weeks for the entire cutting department (approx. 47 workers), with weekly hours averaging 78 hours. Worker interviews conducted on 15 April 2024 with seven employees from the sewing section corroborated this pattern, with multiple interviewees stating they had worked for 13 consecutive days in late February to meet a shipping deadline and expressed uncertainty about the overtime rate calculation on their pay slips. The required one-day-in-seven rest was not consistently provided.
**Finding:** Despite being under a corrective action plan, Factory 039 has systematically failed to implement controls to manage working hours, resulting in a severe and repeated violation of both local law and client Code of Conduct standards regarding weekly hour limits and rest days. The systemic nature of this finding indicates a failure of managerial oversight and the previous CAP's ineffectiveness.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note:** This finding triggers an immediate review of the CAP's effectiveness and requires submission of a revised, time-bound remediation plan from factory management within 48 hours, focusing on production planning and timekeeping system overhaul.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: DISC-039-2023-02**

Following up on the previous corrective action plan (CAP Ref: 2022-Q4-12) which addressed discriminatory hiring practices, this inspection on October 26, 2023, included a review of promotion and task assignment records from the preceding six months, alongside confidential interviews with 28 production line workers. Inspector observations noted a clear demographic stratification within the sewing department, where all 12 line leaders and quality checkers were from the same regional ethnicity as the factory ownership and management. Worker interview notes from 7 employees identified as being from minority groups revealed consistent narratives regarding being passed over for supervisory roles despite seniority, with one worker (Interview #22) stating, "We are told we are not suited for leadership here." Cross-referenced with internal job postings and skills matrices, this pattern indicates a systemic barrier to advancement for certain ethnic groups, constituting a failure to implement the non-discrimination policy effectively. This is a repeat violation from the prior CAP.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** CAP-039-F/24-08-HA
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (Code: HA.1.2, HA.3.1)
**Background & Context:** Factory 039 is operating under an active Corrective Action Plan (CAP-039-01/2024) following the Q4 2023 audit, which identified systemic failures in management systems for worker protection. This follow-up assessment specifically reviewed the implementation of revised policies and grievance mechanisms related to harassment and abuse.
**Observations & Interviews:** During the site inspection on 12 March 2024, the posted "Zero Tolerance Harassment" policy was found to be only in English, not in the native language of the production workforce. Interviews with 17 workers from the sewing section, conducted privately, revealed that 5 workers (approximately 29%) reported experiencing or witnessing verbal abuse from line supervisors, including public shouting and derogatory remarks regarding speed, during the period of 15-29 February 2024. While a new grievance box was installed, worker interviews indicated a persistent fear of reprisal, with none of the interviewed workers having used the mechanism. The designated harassment committee, per policy, had not met in the last 60 days, and meeting minutes were not available for review.
**Analysis:** The factory has failed to demonstrate effective implementation of its corrective actions. The absence of accessible policies, credible grievance mechanisms, and active committee oversight, coupled with continued credible reports of verbal abuse, constitutes a breakdown of required management systems.
**Follow-Up Required:** Management must provide evidence of disciplinary action for the reported incidents, reconvene the committee with worker representatives, and demonstrate effective, confidential grievance functionality. A root-cause analysis of supervisory behavior is required.
**Severity: Moderate**
