Factory 040 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
Audit area F.03 - Working Hours & Overtime was reviewed through payroll records for the period January-March 2024, time card data, and interviews conducted on April 15-16, 2024. While the factory generally adheres to the 60-hour weekly maximum, with a noted strong dialogue facilitated by the active union regarding production planning, a recurring procedural non-conformance was identified. Inspection of March payroll revealed that for approximately 12% of the workforce (primarily in the cutting section), overtime hours worked from March 10-16 were compensated at the correct premium rate but were not calculated against individual daily attendance records, creating a reconciliation discrepancy per code F.03.1(b). Worker interviews with 8 cutting department employees confirmed receipt of correct pay but acknowledged the record-keeping error. Management has committed to revising the daily overtime authorization log by the end of Q2. Severity: Minor.

[Child Labor]
During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, the audit team examined personnel files for production workers hired within the last twelve months. A review of the provided national ID cards and birth certificates for eleven such individuals (Ref: CL-Doc-004 through CL-Doc-014) revealed no discrepancies indicating underage hiring. Furthermore, during physical inspections of the sewing and finishing sections on 13 October 2023, no visual indicators of child labor were observed. Interviews were conducted with a random sample of 27 production line workers, all of whom confirmed through their accounts and cross-referenced with provided documentation that they were above the minimum age of 18 at the time of hiring, with no reports of younger siblings or acquaintances working in the facility. The factory's active union committee, interviewed separately, also confirmed their monitoring of this issue and reported no grievances related to underage labor in the past three years. The documented procedures, physical inspection, and worker interviews all demonstrate effective implementation of and compliance with the child labor standard. **Status: Compliant**

[Discrimination]
**Finding ID / Ref:** DIS-040-2024-02. During the document review for the period covering January 2023 to present, and corroborated through worker interviews conducted on February 20-21, 2024, it was identified that while the factory’s formal policies prohibit discrimination, its promotion practices demonstrate a potential disparate impact. Interview notes from 17 production workers across three departments revealed a consistent pattern where all eight supervisory positions filled in the last 18 months were given to male employees, despite a qualified female candidate pool constituting approximately 65% of the sewing line workforce. Management acknowledged this trend during the closing meeting, attributing it to a perceived higher attrition rate among female team leaders, but could not provide statistical evidence to support this assertion or demonstrate a structured, objective evaluation process for promotions that would mitigate unconscious bias. This represents a failure to ensure and promote equal opportunity in practice, as required by the applicable code of conduct. **Severity: Minor.**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
During the site inspection conducted on 24 October 2023, it was observed that while the factory maintains generally good housekeeping and has a functional Joint Health & Safety Committee, a significant deficiency was identified in the control of hazardous energy for machinery maintenance. Specifically, at the cutting section (Machine IDs CT-07, CT-11, CT-15), the audit team found that the mandated lockout/tagout (LOTO) procedures, as per section 8.2 of the factory's own Safety Management System and international standard ILO C155, were not being consistently applied. Interviews with three maintenance technicians on 25 October 2023 revealed an over-reliance on verbal communication and a single lock system during servicing, rather than individual locks for each technician, creating a risk of inadvertent machine activation. Despite the strong grievance mechanism noted in worker-management relations, this procedural lapse constitutes a serious deviation from accepted safe work practices. The management has been advised to implement immediate corrective action, including refresher training and a verification audit of LOTO compliance within 30 days. Severity: Moderate.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-023-040**
**Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (CCS Section 7.1)**
**Date of On-Site Verification: October 26, 2023**

During the review of disciplinary records and subsequent worker interviews conducted on the aforementioned date, the audit team identified a notable lapse in the procedural application of the factory’s otherwise robust Anti-Harassment Policy. While the policy documentation is comprehensive and was reaffirmed to all 12 randomly selected production operators interviewed as having been communicated during onboarding, a review of three separate disciplinary cases from Q3 2023 revealed that investigations into alleged verbal harassment by two line supervisors were concluded without formally soliciting witness statements from colleagues named by the complainants. This omission was confirmed in interview notes with the HR Manager, who stated the decisions were based primarily on direct interviews with the involved parties, citing a desire for expedient resolution. Although the grievance mechanism remains functionally strong and the cases in question did result in written warnings for the supervisors, the failure to follow the investigation protocol as defined in the factory’s own documented procedures creates a risk of incomplete fact-finding and could potentially undermine worker confidence in the system’s thoroughness. Corrective action requires a retraining of the HR committee on the full investigative protocol and a systematic review of past six-month cases for similar procedural gaps.
**Severity: Minor**

Factory 041 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-041-2023-DF-07
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SA8000:2014 Sect. 5 / Buyer CoC Sec. 8.3)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Factory 041 maintains a documented policy against discrimination and demonstrates a generally strong commitment to fair employment practices through its management systems and ISO certifications. The dedicated compliance officer demonstrated a clear understanding of the relevant legal and code requirements during the opening interview. However, a review of organizational demographics and subsequent worker interviews revealed a procedural inconsistency requiring documentation and training reinforcement.
**Observations & Evidence:** Analysis of employment records for the period of January-September 2023 indicated that all 17 supervisory and line-leader positions within the sewing and finishing departments were held by female employees. While this may reflect the demographic composition of the overall workforce (approximately 78% female), worker interviews conducted on October 26 with a stratified sample of 22 workers (12 female, 10 male) from various departments identified a perception among 6 male workers in the cutting and warehouse sections that advancement opportunities to these supervisory roles were not accessible to them, with one worker noting, "Those team leader jobs are only for the sewing ladies, we are not considered." The factory could not provide documented evidence of a structured, gender-neutral process for communicating or posting such internal promotion opportunities to all departments, which risks creating a de facto barrier and perception of bias, even if no formal discriminatory hiring/promotion policy exists.
**Follow-Up Note:** The compliance officer was informed of this finding and acknowledged the need to formalize and document the internal job posting process to ensure universal access and transparency.
**Severity: Minor**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WH-OT-041-2024-08-15
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1, B.1.3)
**Date of Inspection:** August 12-14, 2024

**Background & Context:** Factory 041 maintains ISO 9001:2015 certification and has historically demonstrated a robust management system regarding working time compliance, overseen by a full-time, dedicated compliance officer. The auditor conducted a review of time and wage records for the preceding six-month period (January 15, 2024 – July 15, 2024) and interviewed 17 production workers selected at random across three production lines.

**Observations & Findings:** While payroll records for the audit period were largely in order and indicated a policy of limiting work to a 60-hour week, a reconciliation of electronic time clock data against handwritten overtime authorization slips for the months of March and June 2024 revealed a recurring discrepancy. Specifically, in the finishing department, it was observed that 23 workers exceeded the 60-hour threshold on four separate weeks, with a maximum of 67 hours recorded in the week of June 10-16, 2024. Interviews with five of these workers confirmed they had worked these hours, with one stating, "We knew it was too many hours, but the supervisor said it was the only way to complete the urgent order for Brand X." Although the overtime was compensated at the legally correct premium rate, the systematic override of the factory's own cap, facilitated by manual authorizations that bypassed the electronic system's alerts, constitutes a failure of management oversight. The compliance officer was aware of the pressure to meet delivery deadlines but acknowledged the procedural breakdown in enforcing the policy.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2023-041-07
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Inspector(s):** J. Chen, L. Rodriguez

During the walkthrough of the finishing and packing departments on the third floor, the audit team identified inconsistent implementation of the documented Lockout/Tagout (LOTO) procedures for the maintenance of industrial sewing machines and packing line conveyor motors. While a robust written LOTO program (document WI-SAF-022, Rev. 3) exists and the compliance officer provided thorough training records, direct observation on October 26th noted that two of the three maintenance technicians interviewed (Worker IDs #8874 and #9112) admitted to occasionally performing minor thread jam clearance or belt alignment adjustments without applying personal lockout devices, citing time pressure for urgent production line repairs. This was corroborated by visual inspection of seven Juki DDL-8700 machines, where five were found with missing or unused hasps on their central power isolation points. Although no incidents were reported, this represents a procedural breakdown that creates a potential risk for unexpected energization and serious injury. The factory is instructed to conduct immediate retraining and a 100% tool check for the maintenance team, with verification required within 30 days.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FA-041-2023-087 | Compliance Area: FOA & CB | Date of On-Site Verification: October 26, 2023**

Background context indicates a historically strong performance in this area, supported by the presence of a dedicated compliance officer and the facility's ISO 9001 certification. During the review of policies and worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, the audit team, having interviewed 31 workers across various production departments, noted that while the official policy document (HR-POL-012 Rev. 4) explicitly permits the right to association, there was a discernible gap in practical implementation. Specifically, worker interview notes from seven separate employees in the sewing and finishing sections revealed a consistent lack of awareness regarding the lawful process to form or join a worker representative body, with several expressing a belief that such activity might be discouraged despite no formal prohibitions. The compliance officer confirmed that no active worker committee exists and that proactive training on these rights has not been conducted within the past 24 months, representing a failure to ensure effective communication and facilitation of these fundamental rights as required by section 7.1.2 of the Supplier Code of Conduct. **Severity: Minor** A corrective action plan must address this awareness gap through structured training programs and the establishment of a clear, management-neutral communication channel by Q1 2024.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-2023-041-02
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Fire & General Evacuation)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

While the factory maintains a generally robust framework for emergency management, as evidenced by documented ISO 45001 procedures and clear evacuation routes posted throughout the facility, a procedural inconsistency was identified during the full evacuation drill observed on the aforementioned date. The drill, initiated at 14:17, resulted in a total evacuation time of 8 minutes and 42 seconds for the 1,247 personnel on site, which is within an acceptable range. However, subsequent interviews with a selection of 12 workers from the cutting and finishing departments revealed that 4 were unaware of the designated alternate assembly point for scenarios where the primary yard is inaccessible, a contingency detailed in section 4.5 of the factory's own Emergency Response Plan. This indicates a gap in the dissemination and verification of training comprehension for all workforce segments. The compliance officer has been notified to review and enhance the verification process for contingency procedure awareness. **Severity: Minor**

Factory 042 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: CL-042-08-2023**
**Compliance Area: Child Labor (CL)**
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident on July 15, 2023, which significantly disrupted operations and record-keeping, the factory has been undergoing a restructuring of its workforce and recruitment processes. This audit, conducted on August 24-25, 2023, included a review of personnel files for all 127 production workers currently employed post-recovery and targeted interviews.

**Observations & Interviews:** While all reviewed personnel files contained signed employment contracts and copies of government-issued identification cards indicating ages above the local minimum of 18, visual observation during the spot-check of the finishing section on August 24 at approximately 14:30 identified one individual (observed at workstation F-12) whose apparent age raised immediate concern. A subsequent private interview with this worker, conducted through an interpreter, revealed the individual admitted to being 16 years of age. The worker stated they were hired on August 10, 2023, through a relative of a line supervisor, and provided contradictory information regarding their educational status. The worker’s personnel file was later cross-referenced and found to contain an ID card that a brief visual inspection suggested may be non-genuine; this document has been flagged for verification by the HR department.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** This constitutes a confirmed violation of the company’s code of conduct and local labor law. The immediate cessation of work for the individual was mandated to the factory management upon discovery. A root-cause analysis of the recruitment breakdown, particularly the reliance on supervisor referrals without adequate documentary verification in the post-fire recovery period, is required. The factory must also institute a remedial training program for all hiring personnel.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
Audit finding 042-17-08 under SA8000 Section 4 (Discrimination) and ILO Convention 111 cross-reference. During the on-site inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, and subsequent confidential worker interviews, a pattern emerged relating to the factory's post-incident recovery phase following the fire event of 15 January 2024. It was noted that 6 out of 18 workers interviewed from the cutting department, all of whom are migrant workers from a specific provincial region, reported being disproportionately assigned to cleanup and debris removal duties on the damaged production floor, duties not shared with local employees of equivalent seniority and job grade. Management rationale provided referenced a need for "flexible task forces," however, the assignment logs reviewed from 20 January to 10 February show a clear demographic concentration for these undesirable and potentially hazardous tasks, creating a de facto discriminatory practice based on national origin and social status. A formal corrective action plan must address non-discriminatory task rotation and provide retroactive hazard pay where applicable. Severity: Moderate.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Reference:** FO-042-24-009 **Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FA & CB) **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

**Finding:** During the review of post-fire recovery operations and subsequent worker engagement, it was identified that, while the factory management has demonstrated cooperation with structural safety inspectors, their engagement regarding worker representative mechanisms remains deficient. Background interviews conducted on October 24-25 with a sample of 17 production line workers from three departments revealed a consistent narrative that the previously elected Worker Welfare Committee, established in 2021, has been effectively dormant since the fire incident in August 2023. Furthermore, inspection of notice boards and internal communication logs found no minutes from committee meetings nor any announcements regarding its reinstatement or new elections for the past 89 days, despite the full resumption of production. Management representatives, when queried, indicated this was an "oversight due to crisis management priorities" and could not provide a documented plan to reconvene the committee, thereby failing to ensure the continuous functioning of a key collective dialogue channel as required. Severity: Moderate. **Follow-up Note:** A corrective action plan must address the timeline for re-establishing a democratically elected worker body and include training for both management and worker representatives on its mandate.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref:** A2023-042-CHR-01
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

A review of human resources documentation and worker interviews confirmed systemic failures in the execution and retention of legally mandated employment contracts. During the inspection on October 26, 2023, a sample review of personnel files for 30 workers hired post-incident (after the fire of August 15, 2023) revealed that only 7 files contained a signed labor contract, equating to a compliance rate of approximately 23%. Interview notes from 12 of these newly hired workers corroborated this, with 10 stating they had never been provided with a written contract, only verbal agreement on wages. While management cited the disruptive impact of the fire incident and ongoing reconstruction as contributing factors, this does not absolve the fundamental legal obligation. The absence of contracts deprives workers of clear terms of employment and represents a significant regulatory non-conformance under local labor law Article 17. A follow-up note has been logged to request a full roster review and rectification plan.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-042-2023-07-HA-002
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (SA8000:2014 Sec. 5; Client Code of Conduct Sec. 8.3)
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident of May 15, 2023, which damaged Section B's cutting and finishing lines, management has been operating under considerable pressure to meet revised production deadlines, a situation noted to potentially elevate workplace stressors. During the announced audit conducted on October 26-27, 2023, this area was given heightened scrutiny given the factory's current special monitoring status with the local Bureau of Labor.

**Observations & Interview Data:** While a formal policy against harassment and abuse is documented in the employee handbook (Rev. 4), observational walks through the sewing and finishing floors on both audit days noted multiple instances of supervisory staff using derogatory language and shouting at operators for perceived pace issues, particularly in lines reassigned after the fire. During confidential interviews conducted with 28 workers from affected departments, 19 reported feeling routinely subjected to verbal abuse and intimidation by line leaders, with several citing specific examples from the week prior (e.g., public beratement for quality errors). No functional, confidential grievance mechanism for reporting such conduct was found to be actively promoted or trusted by workers, as the posted procedure still directs complaints to a floor manager, creating a clear conflict of interest and fear of reprisal.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Requirement:** The factory is in violation of the requirement to provide a workplace free of harsh or inhumane treatment. The post-disaster operational environment appears to have exacerbated pre-existing cultural gaps in supervisory conduct and grievance efficacy. A corrective action plan (CAP) must address formal disciplinary measures for abusive conduct, supervisor training on appropriate management techniques, and the implementation of a truly anonymous and independently managed reporting channel, to be verified at the next follow-up audit.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WH-2023-042-07 **| Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 6) **| Date of On-site Inspection:** 23-24 October 2023

**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident in late August 2023, which resulted in significant production delays, the audit was conducted under a special monitoring regime as mandated by local authority directive LA-042-SM. The factory management had submitted a revised production recovery plan, which anticipated a temporary increase in operational hours to meet backlogged orders. The purpose of this audit segment was to verify adherence to legal and code limits on working hours during this recovery period.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** Review of payroll records for September 2023 and the first three weeks of October 2023 indicated a systemic pattern of excessive overtime. Production data cross-referenced with attendance logs for Sewing Line B showed that 127 of 143 workers on that line exceeded the 60-hour weekly legal limit for four consecutive weeks, with a recorded peak of 77 hours in the week of 2 October 2023. During confidential interviews conducted on 24 October, 8 out of 12 workers from the cutting and finishing departments separately confirmed they had worked seven consecutive days without a rest day for the preceding three weeks, citing management pressure to complete a critical shipment. While management acknowledged the exceeded hours, citing force majeure due to the fire, they could not produce documentation of the legally required tripartite agreement (union/worker representatives, employer, and local labor bureau) authorizing an extended overtime period under such circumstances.

**Finding & Follow-up Note:** The factory is in violation of legal and code standards regarding maximum working hours and mandatory rest days. The violation is not isolated but systematic and was undertaken without obtaining the necessary legal exemption, negating the force majeure defense. Management is required to immediately implement a corrective action plan to normalize working hours and provide compensation for any illegally mandated overtime. **Severity: Critical.**

Factory 043 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area:** Child Labor (Audit Code: CL-01, referenced against ILO Convention 138 & 182 and Section [redacted] of the Client’s Code of Conduct).

**Finding:** During the document review on 12 October 2023, it was observed that the factory’s recruitment procedures, as documented, are robust, requiring original national identity cards for all hires and maintaining a centralized personnel register. However, during physical inspection of the finishing section on the same date, the audit team identified one individual, later identified as “Worker A,” whose physical appearance and demeanor raised immediate concerns regarding potential underage labor. Subsequent to this observation, a private interview was conducted with the individual, who presented an ID card indicating an age of 18 years and 2 months. Interviews with a random sample of 12 workers from the same production line, conducted on 13 October 2023, yielded no corroborating testimony regarding underage workers, though one worker (Interview #07-CL) noted the individual in question had only recently joined the department. Given the sole reliance on potentially fraudulent documentation, which the factory management failed to verify through enhanced due diligence, and in the absence of an active juvenile worker policy, this case constitutes a failure in the implementation of effective age verification mechanisms. A follow-up verification request has been issued to management to procure a validated birth certificate from the local municipal authority for the subject worker within fourteen days to conclusively resolve the finding. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Area: A.2 Contracts & Human Resources**
**Reference: FAC-043-2023-10-A2.1**
**Date of Discovery: October 26, 2023**

During a review of personnel files for a stratified random sample of 47 production workers, the audit team noted that while all permanent employees possessed signed employment contracts compliant with national labor law, a significant discrepancy was identified concerning temporary and probationary staff. Specifically, for 23 workers hired within the last three months for seasonal order fulfillment, their files contained only a basic offer letter acknowledging wage rate and workshop assignment; these documents lacked the comprehensive terms mandated by Article 16 of the Labor Code, including clear stipulations on working hours, overtime compensation mechanisms, and social insurance contributions. Interviews conducted on October 27 with 8 of these affected workers confirmed they had not received a full contractual document and expressed uncertainty regarding their entitlement to accrued annual leave. Management acknowledged the practice, citing administrative streamlining during peak recruitment periods, but could not produce a legally approved alternative shorter-form contract. This constitutes a failure to ensure all workers have a transparent, written understanding of their terms and conditions of employment.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
A review of the factory's policies and procedures during the opening meeting on 12 November 2023 confirmed that a formal policy prohibiting harassment and abuse, including a grievance mechanism, is documented in the employee handbook (Ref: HR-POL-007). However, interviews conducted with 37 workers across various production lines on 13-14 November revealed a concerning pattern. Multiple workers, particularly 8 from the finishing and cutting departments, described an environment where line supervisors frequently use derogatory language and public belittlement to enforce production targets, with one worker quoting a supervisor stating, "Your hands are as slow as your brain," during a routine efficiency check. While no physical abuse was alleged, the psychological pressure and humiliating treatment were consistently reported as commonplace and unreported due to fear of reprisal, indicating a significant failure in the implementation of the policy and a lack of effective, trusted channels for remedy. **Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 044 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FL-2023-044-02A
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Section 1, Principle 4; Brand Code of Conduct Section 5.1)
**Background & Context:** In light of the factory's profile as a subcontractor with a highly seasonal workflow and workforce, particular attention was paid to indicators of coercion during the audit conducted on 12-14 March 2023. The recruitment and retention practices for migrant workers, who constitute approximately 70% of the workforce during peak season, were reviewed.
**Observations & Interviews:** During document review, it was noted that identity documents (national ID cards) for 127 migrant workers were held in a locked cabinet in the HR office, a practice confirmed by the HR manager as standard procedure "for safekeeping and to ensure they complete their contracts." Worker interviews, conducted privately with 23 randomly selected migrant workers on 13 March, revealed a consistent pattern: 18 workers stated they were unaware this practice was voluntary and believed they could not retrieve their documents until the end of their seasonal contract, with several expressing fear of not receiving final payment or being unable to seek alternative employment if they left. This retention of personal documents effectively restricts workers' freedom of movement and constitutes involuntary work.
**Conclusion:** The factory engages in the practice of withholding workers' personal identity documents as a condition of employment, which is a clear violation of the prohibition against forced labor. This practice removes workers' ability to freely resign and creates a climate of coercion.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Requirement:** Immediate cessation of this practice and documented return of all original identity documents to workers, with provision for voluntary, secure copy-making only, is required prior to any consideration of continued business relations. A corrective action plan must be submitted within 48 hours.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref:** FO-044-07-2023-01
**Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: FOA.01)
**Date of Discovery:** July 12, 2023

**Background & Context:** Factory 044 operates as a subcontractor with a peak seasonal workforce, documented to reach approximately 380 workers during high-volume periods, with a core permanent staff of only 120. Management acknowledged intense production pressure from multiple client brands with overlapping deadlines. The factory has no recognized worker representative body or trade union.

**Observations & Interviews:** During a review of posted policies, the 'Works Rules' document, last revised in March 2023, contained clause 4.7 which states, "The formation of worker committees or groups must receive prior written approval from the General Manager." Furthermore, during confidential interviews conducted on July 11-12 with 23 workers selected from various production lines, 18 workers expressed unfamiliarity with any collective bargaining mechanisms or the concept of electing representatives. Several workers noted a fear of reprisal, with one stating, "We were told in orientation that forming groups would cause trouble and delay shipments." Management confirmed no applications for forming such groups had ever been submitted and could not provide evidence of proactive communication to workers regarding their rights to associate freely under national law.

**Analysis & Follow-up Required:** The clause requiring management approval for association constitutes a de facto restriction on the right to freedom of association, which is a fundamental workers' right. This, combined with the evident fear among the workforce and lack of any proactive education or neutral policy, creates an environment that actively discourages collective bargaining. Corrective action requires the immediate revision of the Works Rules and implementation of a training program for both management and workers.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 2.0 Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
**Finding Reference: WB-2023-044-02**

During the document review for the audit period covering January to March 2023, it was noted that Factory 044 operates as a subcontractor with a highly variable order book, leading to intense production peaks and a reliance on temporary seasonal labour, estimated at 40% of the workforce during the observed period. Interviews conducted on 15 March 2023 with a sample of 17 production workers, including 8 temporary workers hired through a local agent, revealed a consistent pattern of workers being paid a flat daily rate that, when calculated against their verified clock-in/clock-out records for the preceding four weeks, failed to meet the local minimum monthly wage requirement for the actual hours worked. This was corroborated by a review of selected wage slips and attendance records, which indicated that the flat daily rate was applied irrespective of weekly hours exceeding the legal limit, resulting in a de facto hourly rate below the statutory minimum. Furthermore, the investigation found no evidence of legally mandated overtime premium calculations for hours worked beyond the standard workweek, constituting a violation of both minimum wage and overtime remuneration regulations. **Severity: Critical** **Follow-up Note: The factory management has been instructed to conduct a full payroll reconciliation for the past six months and submit a corrective action plan with proof of back-pay, to be verified in a follow-up inspection.**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: EP-044-2024-03**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date of Inspection: March 12, 2024**

During the site walkthrough and document review, it was noted that Factory 044, operating as a subcontractor with fluctuating order volumes leading to a high-pressure, seasonal workforce environment, has not updated its emergency evacuation plans to reflect current operational layouts or staff numbers. Observations on the production floor, specifically in the new cutting section (Block C), revealed two of four marked emergency exits were partially obstructed by WIP trolleys and fabric rolls, with egress paths not clearly maintained. Interviews with a cross-section of 14 production workers, including 8 seasonal hires, conducted on March 11-12, indicated that no comprehensive fire drill had been conducted for the current peak-season workforce; three workers stated they had received no emergency training whatsoever upon hiring. The existing fire drill logbook showed the last full evacuation exercise was conducted nine months prior, on June 5, 2023, when staffing was at approximately 60% of current capacity, demonstrating a failure to ensure all personnel are adequately trained and familiar with procedures, particularly given the transient nature of the workforce. This constitutes a failure to ensure effective emergency preparedness and response.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the review of time and attendance records for the period of January to March 2024, coupled with worker interviews conducted on March 15, a systemic non-compliance with the client's Code of Conduct and local labor law regarding working hours was identified. Inspector observations confirmed that production peaks, particularly for the Spring/Summer collections, led to a pattern of excessive overtime; payroll records for the stitching section (approx. 60 workers) showed an average of 98 hours of overtime per worker in February, far exceeding the legal limit. Interviews with 8 randomly selected workers from the finishing department corroborated this, with multiple accounts of working 13-hour days, 7 days a week, for consecutive weeks, under pressure to meet shipping deadlines, and with no evidence of a legally mandated day of rest in a seven-day period. This finding, classified under clause OC-4.2 of the audit protocol, represents a significant worker rights violation due to the risk of fatigue-related injury and the coercive nature of the excessive hours. **Severity: Critical.** A Corrective Action Plan is required to address the root causes of production planning and capacity management.

[Harassment & Abuse]
Audit Scope & Context: As part of the announced Social Compliance audit conducted on 17-18 October 2023, the area of Harassment & Abuse (SA8000:2014 Section 5.2, Client Code of Conduct Section 8.1) was reviewed through document examination, management interview, and confidential worker interviews. Factory 044, operating as a subcontractor with high-volume, short-lead-time orders for multiple brands, employs a significant seasonal workforce, which management stated complicates consistent policy implementation.

Observations & Interviews: The factory's harassment policy, while documented, was found only in English and not visibly posted in production areas. During confidential interviews held with 32 workers across cutting, sewing, and finishing departments, 7 workers (approximately 22%) reported instances of verbal abuse from line supervisors, specifically citing being shouted at in a derogatory manner and threatened with dismissal or point deductions for failing to meet daily production targets. These incidents were not reported through the facility's grievance mechanism, as workers expressed a fear of retaliation and a belief that management would support supervisory staff due to production pressure.

Finding: The factory has failed to ensure a work environment free from harassment, abuse, and harsh or inhumane treatment, as verbal abuse was identified and the existing policy and grievance system are ineffective in practice, particularly for the vulnerable seasonal workforce. The lack of accessible policy communication and a demonstrably trusted reporting channel constitutes a breakdown in the system designed to protect worker dignity.
**Severity: Moderate**
*Follow-up Required: Corrective Action Plan must include supervisor re-training on appropriate disciplinary procedures, translation and posting of policies, and an anonymous reporting channel with awareness sessions for all workers.*

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area:** 0003 - Child Labor Prevention (CLP)
**Reference:** CLP-2023-044-08

**Finding Details:** During the document review on 12/10/2023, it was noted that the factory's recruitment records for the period of September to November 2023, covering the peak seasonal hiring surge, lacked consistent age verification documentation for 37 out of 122 new hires, primarily for the cutting and finishing sections. Subsequent physical inspection on the production floor on 13/10/2023 identified two individuals, later confirmed through worker interviews as being 16 years of age, operating heavy-duty band knife cutting machines, which is classified as hazardous work under national law and is strictly prohibited for persons under 18. Interview logs for workers W-044-089 and W-044-091, conducted separately, corroborated their ages and revealed they were hired through a temporary labor agent during the recent high-pressure order for Brand "Sigma," with no effective age-screening mechanism applied. This constitutes a failure in both the initial verification process and the ongoing assignment of age-appropriate tasks.
**Severity: Critical**

Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: C-HR-045-2024-03**
During the review of human resources documentation and contracts for the period covering January 2023 to present, auditors noted a generally robust system. **Severity: Minor**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WB-045-2023-08
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Payroll Review Period: July 2023)
**Background & Observation:** Factory 045 maintains a generally robust payroll system with transparent wage calculations and timely disbursement, consistent with its strong historical compliance. However, during a detailed review of time cards, payroll registers, and wage slips for July 2023, a discrepancy was identified concerning overtime (OT) compensation for workers on a specific production line fulfilling a rush order in the week of July 10-16. Inspector analysis of 22 time cards against corresponding wage slips revealed that while overtime hours were recorded and paid, the overtime premium was calculated at the legal rate of 1.5 times the base hourly rate, rather than the 2.0 times rate as stipulated in the collective bargaining agreement (CBA) negotiated with the recognized worker union and on file. This CBA, which provides enhanced benefits, was validated as active.
**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted on August 23, 2023, with 8 workers from the affected line confirmed awareness of the CBA rate but uncertainty on its application for that particular week, with several noting they had not cross-checked the detailed calculations.
**Follow-up Required:** Management must immediately rectify the underpayment for the 34 affected workers for that workweek and implement a verification step in the payroll approval process to ensure the correct CBA rate is applied for all future overtime calculations.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 046 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2024-046-003
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2024

**Background & Observations:** During the walk-through of the cutting and sewing sections, it was noted that while the factory is newly established and the physical infrastructure appears modern, the implementation of formal Occupational Safety & Health protocols is in a nascent stage. A critical gap was identified regarding emergency egress and fire safety preparedness. Inspection of the primary production hall on the second floor revealed that two of the four designated emergency exits were partially obstructed by stored rolls of fabric and finished goods, and one exit door was found to be locked from the outside during working hours, a condition confirmed at 14:30 on the date of inspection. This was compounded by interview data from a confidential discussion with 7 production workers, wherein 5 indicated they had not participated in a fire drill since the factory's operational commencement six months prior.

**Analysis & Requirement:** This constitutes a clear violation of section 4.1.2 (Unobstructed Emergency Exits) and 4.3.1 (Regular Emergency Drills) of the Buyer's Code of Conduct and mirrors local fire ordinance stipulations. The locking of an emergency exit presents an immediate and severe risk to worker safety in the event of a fire or other emergency requiring rapid evacuation. The absence of conducted drills further exacerbates this risk, as the young and potentially inexperienced workforce lacks procedural familiarity.

**Follow-up Note:** Management was immediately advised to clear all egress routes and unlock the secured door during the inspection; corrective action was verified prior to auditor departure. However, a systemic procedural failure remains. A formal corrective action plan addressing both physical obstructions and drill scheduling is required within 7 business days.
**Severity: Critical**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding EP-03-2024: Emergency Preparedness & Response**
As documented during the inspection conducted on 15 May 2024, the factory's emergency preparedness systems were found to be underdeveloped and non-operational, a significant concern given the facility's workforce of approximately 150 personnel. Inspector observations confirmed that while primary exit routes were nominally unobstructed, no documented emergency evacuation plan was posted in any work area, and the appointed fire wardens, identified in a basic organizational chart, had not received any formal training, as verified through record review and interviews with two of the three named wardens. Worker interviews with seven production operators from the cutting and sewing sections revealed a consistent lack of awareness of evacuation procedures or assembly point locations, with five of the seven interviewed stating they had never participated in an emergency drill since the factory's operational commencement in January 2024. This constitutes a failure to establish and communicate fundamental life safety procedures to the workforce. **Severity: Moderate**. A follow-up verification is required within 90 days to confirm the development and implementation of a full emergency action plan and the execution of a general evacuation drill.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: 5.2 - Harassment and Abuse (Prevention, Investigation, and Remediation)**
**Reference: F046-HRA-01**

During the review of management systems and worker interviews conducted on 12-14 March 2024, it was found that while no formal allegations of harassment or abuse were present, the factory’s preventative and procedural mechanisms are not fully established or communicated. The management representative confirmed that a standalone anti-harassment policy is still in draft form and has not been formally issued or trained upon, which was corroborated during confidential interviews with 14 out of 32 production-line workers, who stated they were unaware of any formal reporting channels or investigation procedures for such grievances. Observational walkthroughs noted the absence of posted policies or confidential complaint mechanisms in worker common areas. Consequently, the factory is non-compliant with requirement 5.2.01 (Policy and Communication) and 5.2.03 (Grievance Mechanism), exposing the young workforce to risk due to a lack of clear safeguards and recourse pathways. **Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-01-2024-046
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1)
**Date of Discovery:** During on-site inspection October 24-25, 2024, with verification of records from July 1, 2024, to present.

**Context & Observations:** Factory 046 is in an initial operational phase with a workforce largely inexperienced in formal industrial labor standards. Review of payroll records and attendance sheets for a sampled three-month period indicated a consistent pattern of extended working hours, particularly in the cutting and sewing departments, to meet production targets for initial orders. During worker interviews conducted on October 25 with 14 employees selected from various production lines, 9 individuals reported regular work weeks exceeding 72 hours, with one stating, "We worked 28 days last month without a full day off, from 8:00 until 10:00 or 11:00 PM, because the order was urgent." The factory's own timecards, though manually maintained with several inconsistencies, corroborate these reports, showing daily work periods regularly exceeding 13 hours and weekly hours averaging 78-85 for the majority of production staff, with no evidence of a recognized day of rest in any seven-day period. Furthermore, overtime compensation, while calculated at the correct premium rate, was found to be based on a flat hourly rate that does not incorporate all legally mandated allowances, constituting an underpayment.

**Follow-up Notes:** Management acknowledged systemic gaps, attributing them to "start-up pressures" and an absence of established planning and workforce management protocols. They have verbally committed to implementing a production scheduling system. A formal Corrective Action Plan (CAP) must be submitted addressing these systemic failures in work time recording, scheduling, and wage calculation.
**Severity: Critical**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Area: Forced Labor (Reference FL-01, FL-03, FL-04)**
During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 14 March 2024, it was noted that while management demonstrates an intent to avoid forced labor, systemic gaps were observed. Interviews with 14 randomly selected production line workers revealed that all residential dormitory occupants, approximately 87 individuals, have had their national identity cards retained by the factory's security office under a stated policy of "safekeeping," with workers expressing uncertainty about the retrieval process. Furthermore, observation of the dormitory compound noted a perimeter fence with inward-opening gates that were routinely locked from the outside during night shifts, a measure management described as for worker safety but which materially restricts freedom of movement. This combination of document retention and restricted egress, while likely not malicious in intent given the factory's nascent stage, establishes conditions indicative of undue coercion. **Severity: Moderate** - Corrective action required to implement a lawful document return policy and revise security procedures to ensure unrestricted egress.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: F046-SOC-07 | Compliance Area: Discrimination (Criterion 4.1, 4.3, & 4.5)** During the onsite inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team reviewed the factory's nascent hiring and human resources management systems. Investigator observations and documentation review confirmed that the factory has not yet developed or disseminated a formal anti-discrimination policy to its workforce, which currently stands at approximately 150 direct employees. Furthermore, interviews with a cross-section of 17 production workers, predominantly female and under the age of 25, revealed a consistent pattern of inquiry regarding marital and pregnancy status during the hiring process, as noted in interview records WI-12 through WI-19. While no active grievances were formally recorded, this pre-employment screening practice, combined with the absence of a communicated policy, creates a substantial risk of discriminatory hiring decisions and fails to establish a foundational culture of equal opportunity. The management team acknowledged this gap in their procedural development. **Severity: Moderate** A follow-up note is required to confirm the development, implementation, and worker training on a formal non-discrimination policy within the agreed corrective action timeline.

[Child Labor]
Audit Code: CL-01. During the document review on [Date: October 26, 2023], it was noted that the factory's hiring procedures, while containing a policy against child labor, lacked robust age verification mechanisms, relying solely on national identity cards which are known to be susceptible to forgery in the region. Subsequent physical inspection of production lines and worker interviews revealed no visual evidence of underage workers; however, interviews with 15 randomly selected production workers conducted on [Date: October 27, 2023] indicated that two workers, whose personnel files listed them as 18 and 19 years of age, were known to fellow workers to have been recruited directly from secondary school and were believed to be 16 and 17 years old. The factory management, when presented with this anecdotal information, was unable to provide definitive secondary proof of age for these two individuals, such as school leaving certificates or birth registration documents, citing their nascent record-keeping systems. This constitutes a failure to effectively implement the age verification process, creating a significant risk of undetected child labor. **Severity: Moderate**. Follow-up required: Factory must implement a reliable age verification protocol with secondary documentation for all hires within 30 days and re-verify the age of the two workers in question.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**  
**Finding Ref:** WB-F1-046-2024  

During the review of payroll records for the period of January to March 2024, coupled with worker interviews conducted on 10 April 2024, it was observed that the factory's remuneration practices are not fully aligned with legal requirements. Inspection of time cards and pay slips for a sample of 30 production workers revealed a systematic discrepancy whereby overtime hours worked beyond the regular 60-hour workweek (as noted in Working Hours finding WH-F1) were compensated at the standard hourly rate, rather than the legally mandated premium rate. This was corroborated in interviews with 8 of 15 workers interviewed, who stated they had not received overtime premium pay since the factory's opening in Q4 2023. Management acknowledged the lapse, attributing it to an error in their nascent payroll software configuration and a lack of formalized oversight procedures for wage calculation. This constitutes a violation of statutory wage regulations and necessitates immediate corrective action to recalculate and remedy unpaid premiums.  
**Severity: Critical**

Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
Audit Finding Ref: WH-047-03-2024 | Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime | Date of Finding: March 22, 2024

Background: Factory 047 operates as a subcontractor for several mid-market apparel brands with fluctuating order volumes, leading to pronounced seasonal production peaks. This audit coincided with the pre-shipment phase for a major seasonal collection. This constitutes a systemic failure in the accurate recording of working hours and a breach of legal overtime limits. Severity: Critical.
