Factory 048 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WG-048-03
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (C.01 - Payment of Wages)
**Background & Observation:** During the review of payroll records for the period covering March 2024, it was noted that the factory, due to its nascent stage of operational development, has not yet fully implemented a systematic overtime tracking and compensation mechanism separate from its base wage structure. Inspector observations on 14 April 2024 revealed that while time cards were physically maintained, the payroll calculation methodology did not clearly delineate regular hours from overtime hours for the purposes of applying the legally mandated premium rate. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 7 production line workers, who indicated that their payslips, though provided, listed only a total sum for "wages" without a breakdown of hours or overtime premiums, creating confusion and preventing verification of correct payment.
**Analysis:** The failure to calculate and pay overtime at the statutory premium rate constitutes a violation of local labor law. Although the payroll records indicate that workers were paid for all hours worked, the absence of a transparent calculation for overtime premiums means compliance cannot be verified and underpayment is likely occurring. The factory management acknowledged this gap in their systems and attributed it to the ongoing implementation of their HR software platform.
**Follow-up Note:** Management has committed to revising their payroll protocol by the next pay cycle; however, retrospective correction for previous pay periods must be addressed. Severity: Moderate

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: FAC-048-EP-003**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date(s) of Review: October 26, 2023**

During the site inspection conducted on the above date, it was noted that while the factory has installed a basic fire alarm system and designated assembly points, the overall emergency preparedness framework lacks the necessary rigor and demonstrated effectiveness required for a facility of this scale. Inspector observations confirmed that of the twelve emergency exits mapped on the provided floor plan, three were partially obstructed by stored raw materials and two had signage that was unclear or missing. Furthermore, worker interviews with a sample of 17 production staff from the cutting and sewing sections revealed a concerning lack of awareness; fourteen workers could not accurately describe the location of their primary assembly point, and none had participated in a full facility evacuation drill since operations commenced approximately eight months ago. The absence of documented drill procedures, training records, and a robust worker instruction program constitutes a significant failure in the duty of care. **Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Contracts & Human Resources - Finding Ref: FAC048-CHR-02**

During the document review conducted on-site on 12 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's HR systems remain under development. A sample review of personnel files for 30 workers, selected across multiple production departments, revealed that while appointment letters were present, only 10 contained fully executed employment contracts stipulating essential terms such as wage calculation, working hours, and probationary periods as required by national labor law. In subsequent confidential interviews with 15 workers, 9 confirmed they had signed a document upon hiring but were not provided a copy for their records and expressed uncertainty regarding several contract terms. This deficiency indicates a systemic gap in formalizing the employment relationship and ensuring worker understanding of their terms and conditions, posing a risk of future disputes. The management representative acknowledged the oversight, attributing it to rapid onboarding and nascent procedural controls. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan must address the retroactive issuance of compliant contracts and the implementation of a verified distribution protocol.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-048-02
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1)
**Date of Review:** October 26-27, 2023

**Background & Observations:** The facility, being newly established with an inexperienced management team, has implemented a basic manual timekeeping system utilizing paper-based sign-in/sign-out registers at the factory gate. A review of production records for the peak month of September 2023, cross-referenced with sampling of the aforementioned gate registers for the weeks of September 11-17 and September 18-24, indicated consistent production outputs that would necessitate operational periods exceeding the local legal limit of 60 hours per week. Specifically, the registers showed multiple entries for the cutting and sewing departments where daily in/out times recorded exceeded 12 hours, with no consistent weekly rest day evident for a significant portion of the workforce.

**Worker Interviews:** Confidential interviews conducted with 14 workers from various production lines on October 27 confirmed these observations, with 11 workers stating their regular work schedule was from 8:00 to 21:00, six days per week, amounting to a 78-hour workweek. These workers further stated that while overtime was at times verbally communicated as voluntary, operational pressures and production quotas created a coercive environment where refusal was not a practical option. No interviewed worker could produce or recall receiving any detailed overtime payslips breaking down premium overtime rates, with several noting their remuneration was a fixed monthly amount regardless of hours exceeding the statutory norm.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Required:** The factory is in violation of both maximum working hour limits and the requirement for a mandatory day of rest, compounded by inadequate record-keeping that fails to accurately capture overtime hours and corresponding premium payments. This systemic failure constitutes a significant breach of the applicable labor standards and the audited code of conduct. Management must immediately implement a compliant timekeeping system, revise production planning to adhere to legal limits, and provide retroactive compensation for unpaid overtime premiums.
**Severity: Critical**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FO-048-22-01**

During the review of the factory's implementation of its Freedom of Association and Collective Bargaining policy, conducted on 12-14 November 2024, it was noted that while management has a written policy in place, practical application and worker awareness remain deficient. In interviews with 27 randomly selected production workers, 22 were unaware of the policy's existence and could not correctly identify any worker representative, stating they had never been informed of their right to freely associate. Furthermore, management acknowledged in a discussion that no election process for worker representatives has been initiated since the factory's opening eight months ago, citing the operational focus on establishing production as a primary reason for the delay. This constitutes a failure to actively promote and facilitate the practical exercise of these fundamental rights, creating an environment where collective bargaining cannot meaningfully occur.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area 3.0 – Child Labor Prevention**
During the document review on 12 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's recruitment procedures, while verbally confirmed by HR personnel, lacked a standardized, verifiable age verification process. Subsequent physical inspection of production lines and worker interviews conducted on 13 October 2023 revealed no visual evidence of underage workers; however, interviews with 7 randomly selected employees from the cutting and sewing sections indicated potential gaps. One worker, when asked for age verification documentation, could only produce a local identification card of questionable authenticity, which appeared altered, though the individual's apparent age was consistent with adulthood. The factory management, upon being presented with this observation, acknowledged the weakness in their current system, attributing it to their nascent operational status and high turnover within their young workforce. A formal follow-up request (Ref: CL-048-1023) has been issued for the factory to provide, within 30 days, a revised procedural document for age verification, inclusive of secondary ID checks and a robust age declaration form, to mitigate this systemic risk.
**Severity: Minor**

[Forced Labor]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work**

A review of payroll and personnel records for the period covering January to March 2024, coupled with confidential worker interviews conducted on April 10, 2024, revealed systemic issues regarding the retention of worker identity documents and the imposition of unlawful deposits. It was observed during the document verification process (Ref: DL-048-04) that the factory's HR department holds the original national identity cards for approximately 85% of the workforce, ostensibly for "safekeeping and registration purposes." This practice was corroborated during separate interviews with 7 out of 12 production line workers, who stated they were required to surrender their IDs upon hiring and have not been able to retrieve them, with management citing an unwritten policy that documents will be returned only upon resignation. Furthermore, interview notes from Worker #9 (transcript on file) indicate that a "training deposit" of 500 RMB was deducted from her first month's wages, a condition not detailed in her signed employment contract but presented as mandatory to secure her position. These practices collectively restrict workers' freedom of movement and constitute financial coercion, which are clear indicators of forced labor under audit protocol SA8000:2014, Section 3. The factory's nascent compliance systems have failed to identify or remediate these high-risk practices.
**Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Finding Ref:** HR-08-048 / **Audit Area:** Discrimination (Hiring, Employment Terms, & Workplace Treatment) / **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

This assessment reviewed policies and practices against the standards required under the applicable code of conduct, with a specific focus on equal opportunity. The factory has not yet formalized a written anti-discrimination policy or disseminated such principles to the management team, which is a noted gap given the facility's nascent stage of development. During confidential interviews conducted with 17 production workers on the audit date, 5 female workers from the sewing section reported that during the hiring process approximately three months prior, they were questioned by the hiring supervisor about their marital status and future family planning intentions, with the implication that hiring might be contingent upon their answers; no male applicants interviewed reported being subject to similar inquiries. While no direct evidence of hiring denial based on these grounds was substantiated, the practice of posing selectively applied discriminatory questions creates a significant risk of biased employment decisions and a non-inclusive workplace environment, necessitating immediate corrective action to establish and enforce clear, equitable hiring protocols. **Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-048-2024-07-F03**
**Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (Code: HR-04)**
**Background & Context:** During the routine social accountability audit conducted on July 17-18, 2024, particular attention was given to management systems and worker understanding regarding protections against harassment and abuse, given the factory's recent establishment and predominantly young workforce demographic, with an estimated 75% of operatives under the age of 30. **Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** While a generic anti-harassment clause was present within the employee handbook, no dedicated, accessible policy document was visibly posted in worker areas or provided during onboarding, as confirmed in interviews with 8 newly hired workers from the sewing section. Furthermore, interviews with 12 workers across various departments revealed a consistent lack of awareness of any formal, confidential grievance mechanism specifically for reporting harassment or abusive conduct; three separate interviewees expressed unprompted concerns about "verbal pressure" and "shouting" by line supervisors to meet production targets, though no specific incidents of physical abuse were alleged. The management team acknowledged that formal training on harassment prevention and the intended grievance procedure has not yet been implemented, stating these systems are "under development."
**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to establish, communicate, and implement an effective management system to prevent harassment and abuse, leaving workers, particularly a vulnerable young workforce, without clear protections or confidential reporting channels, which creates a significant risk of violations occurring undetected and unaddressed.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan (CAP) required within 30 days, detailing policy development, posting, training rollout schedule, and establishment of a confidential reporting procedure.


============================================================
Factory 049 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory in rural area with limited access to legal support, mostly migrant workers
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: DISC-049-2024-02**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Equal Opportunity & Treatment)
**Background & Context:** The audit scope for this area included a review of hiring, promotion, and disciplinary policies, as well as observations and interviews conducted on the factory floor and in dormitories. Given the factory's remote location and workforce composition of approximately 85% migrant workers, particular attention was paid to potential differential treatment based on regional origin or ethnic background.
**Observations & Evidence:** During worker interviews conducted on 23-24 October 2024, 7 out of 12 interviewees from outside the province indicated that line leader assignments and opportunities for machine operator training are predominantly given to local workers from the surrounding county. Review of training records for the past year showed that of 28 workers promoted to machine operator, only 4 were migrant workers, despite migrants constituting the majority of the general workforce. Furthermore, the factory's "Rules and Regulations" document, reviewed on 23 October, lacks any policy statement prohibiting discrimination in hiring or promotion.
**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** The evidence indicates a systemic pattern of preferential treatment based on worker origin, which limits the advancement opportunities for migrant workers. This constitutes a violation of the requirement for equal opportunity. A corrective action plan must address both policy development and implementation to ensure fair access to training and promotion.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-049-24 / HR-03
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (CCP Section 7.1)
**Background & Context:** The audit scope for this area included a review of the factory's policies, disciplinary procedures, and grievance mechanisms, alongside confidential worker interviews conducted on-site between October 22-24, 2024. Given the factory's profile employing a predominantly migrant workforce in a remote location, particular attention was paid to the accessibility and effectiveness of existing reporting channels and management's awareness of their obligations.
**Observations & Interviews:** While a "Zero Tolerance for Harassment" poster was observed in the main canteen, the associated policy document (HR-POL-004) lacks definitions and examples of abusive behavior, and was found to be available only in English, a language not spoken by the majority of the production line workers. During confidential interviews with 14 workers from the sewing and finishing departments, 3 workers independently reported instances of verbal abuse and intimidation by line supervisors, including the use of demeaning language and threats of dismissal for failing to meet high production targets. These workers expressed a belief that reporting such incidents through the designated grievance box, which is located in clear view of management offices, would lead to retaliation and possible termination, and they were unaware of any alternative, anonymous reporting procedure.
**Conclusion & Follow-up Note:** The combination of an inadequately communicated policy, a non-confidential grievance mechanism, and credible worker testimony indicating a fear of reprisal demonstrates a failure to ensure a workplace free from harassment and abuse. Management must revise and communicate the policy in appropriate languages, establish a trusted and anonymous reporting channel, and conduct mandatory training for all supervisory staff. **Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (Audit Code: FL-08 / Ref: 049-FL-20241027)
**Context & Observations:** During the documentation review and physical inspection conducted on 26-27 October 2024, it was noted that the factory utilizes a dormitory system for approximately 85% of its workforce, predominantly migrant workers from distant provinces. A review of the 'Factory Rules and Regulations' document (Version 3.1) revealed a stated policy permitting the deduction of 'training costs' from worker wages, a practice requiring further scrutiny for potential debt bondage indicators. Inspector observations on the shop floor and in the dormitory area noted the presence of several posted notices warning workers that "early resignation will result in severe penalties," though the specific nature of these penalties was not defined.
**Worker Interviews:** Interviews with 14 randomly selected production workers from 3 departments revealed a pattern of concerning practices. Three workers (Interview Refs: W-INT-049-07, W-INT-049-11, W-INT-049-13) provided consistent testimony that their original identification documents (National ID cards) were retained by the factory's security office upon arrival, under the guise of "safekeeping and registration." All three stated they had made repeated but unsuccessful requests for the return of these documents, which are required for freedom of movement, access to banking services, and seeking alternative employment. This practice effectively restricts their ability to voluntarily terminate employment.
**Finding:** The confiscation and retention of workers' original personal identity documents by the factory constitutes a severe restriction of movement and a fundamental violation of the principle of voluntary work. This practice, combined with policies threatening financial penalties for resignation, creates a climate of coercion and indicates a strong indicator of forced labor as defined by ILO Core Convention 29 and the audit protocol's FL-08 criteria. The factory's rural location and the workers' status as migrants with limited access to legal resources exacerbate their vulnerability to this practice.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note:** Immediate remediation required, including the unconditional return of all original identity documents to workers and a revision of all policies implying financial penalty for resignation. A surprise follow-up verification visit is recommended within 30 days to confirm document return.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
**Finding Ref:** 049-WB-2023-008

**Background & Observation:** During the review of payroll records for the period of March 1-31, 2023, and through cross-referencing with production data and time cards, a systemic discrepancy was identified concerning the calculation of overtime remuneration. The factory utilizes a piece-rate system for base wage calculation, which was found to be set at or slightly above the regional minimum wage. However, the method for calculating the overtime premium, as mandated by national labor law Article 45, was not correctly applied. Specifically, for the 137 production line workers, overtime hours were compensated at a rate of 1.5 times the worker's effective piece-rate earnings for that week, rather than 1.5 times the hourly rate derived from the minimum wage guarantee, as required. This calculation error was confirmed in a sample of 22 payroll files reviewed.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted on April 12, 2023, with a randomized selection of 14 workers from various departments confirmed a widespread lack of understanding regarding the precise breakdown of their overtime pay. While most acknowledged receiving additional pay for hours beyond the standard 48, none could verify the correct legal rate. Several migrant workers expressed reluctance to inquire for fear of reprisal, noting the remote location and limited access to independent legal counsel.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to ensure payment for overtime work at the statutory premium rate, resulting in underpayment to a significant portion of the workforce. This constitutes a violation of both local law and section 7.3 of the Brand's Code of Conduct. A follow-up review will require a detailed reconciliation for the past six months to quantify the total back wage liability and a revised payroll procedure.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the review of time and attendance records for the period of March 1-31, 2024, auditors observed a systemic pattern of excessive working hours, inconsistent with both local legal limits and brand standards. Inspector observations of production handover logs and gate security shift records, corroborated by payroll data, indicated that a significant portion of the production line workforce, approximately 60% of the 342 employees, routinely worked seven days per week during peak production. This was further substantiated during confidential worker interviews conducted on April 10, 2024, with 14 employees from the sewing section (Ref: INT-WH-049-04), who confirmed regular work weeks of 78-84 hours, with overtime hours often recorded under a separate, non-compliant "task rate" system that obscured true compensation. The factory's remote location and the predominantly migrant workforce composition present heightened risks for worker exploitation and limited recourse. Given the deliberate nature of the record-keeping inaccuracies and the severe exceedance of legal working hour limits, this finding constitutes a critical violation of the applicable labor code and brand Code of Conduct. **Severity: Critical** A corrective action plan (CAP) must address immediate cessation of the seven-day work week, accurate payment for all overtime, and management system revisions.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref:** HR-049-03-2024
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources (Employment Agreements)
**Date of Discovery:** March 12, 2024

**Background & Observations:** A review of personnel files for a sample of 30 production workers, selected at random, indicated a systemic failure to provide legally compliant employment contracts. While basic offer letters were on file, inspector observations confirmed that none of the reviewed documents contained the specific terms of employment as required by national labor law, such as detailed job description, precise wage calculation methods, or a clear specification of working hours. This is of particular concern given the factory profile, which indicates a predominantly migrant workforce in a rural location with limited access to legal counsel, increasing their vulnerability.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted on March 12, 2024 with 8 workers from the sample group corroborated the document review. Six of the eight workers interviewed stated they had never signed a formal contract, only an "entry form," and expressed uncertainty regarding their exact entitlements to overtime pay and leave accruals. Two workers provided handwritten, non-standard agreements lacking any factory seal or authorized signature.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Note:** The factory has failed to establish a fundamental safeguard for the employment relationship. The absence of lawful contracts denies workers evidence of their terms and conditions, which is a critical failure in due diligence and a violation of the audit protocol's baseline requirements for documented transparency. A corrective action plan must address the creation and execution of compliant contracts for all employees.
**Severity: Critical**


============================================================
Factory 050 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: PROH-4: Harassment & Abuse (Zero Tolerance)**
**Reference: Audit Protocol v.7.1, Section 3.8**

Following up on Corrective Action Plan item CAP-050-22-03 from the previous audit, which mandated formal management training on anti-harassment policies and the establishment of a confidential grievance mechanism, the audit team conducted a review of the updated policy documentation and interviewed a total of 37 production workers across various departments on March 12th and 13th, 2024. While documentation was found to be technically compliant, multiple worker interviews revealed a persistent cultural issue; three separate workers in the finishing department, interviewed privately, described a pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by line supervisor Mr. Chen, including the use of derogatory language and public humiliation for missing production targets, with one worker providing a specific incident from February 28th. Although a grievance box is now physically present, all interviewed workers expressed a tangible fear of retaliation for using it, stating that reports are not truly anonymous as management "will know who complained." This constitutes a failure to effectively implement the corrective action and provide a workplace free from harassment and abuse.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FL-2023-050-02
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (Code: FL.1, FL.3)
**Background & Context:** During the unannounced audit conducted on 15-17 October 2023, particular attention was paid to forced labor indicators due to Factory 050's documented history of non-compliance and ongoing corrective action plan (CAP) from the Q2 2023 audit. The review focused on freedom of movement, retention of identity documents, and contractual transparency.
**Observations & Interview Notes:** While direct withholding of passports was not observed, inspector review of personnel files for 32 migrant workers (specifically from batch hiring dated 12 August 2023) revealed that all had submitted their original national identity cards to the factory's security office under a stated "safekeeping policy." During confidential interviews with 7 of these workers, 5 confirmed they had requested the return of their documents for personal banking matters but were told by the HR officer that the cards could only be retrieved upon resignation, effectively preventing their freedom of movement and access to essential services. This practice was confirmed as standard procedure in a follow-up discussion with the HR Manager on 16 October.
**Analysis:** The involuntary retention of personal identity documents constitutes a coercive practice that restricts workers' ability to leave their employment and is a clear violation of the Forced Labor protocol. This finding indicates a failure to implement the previously agreed CAP item FL.1.1.
**Severity: Critical**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding EP-2023-050-04: Emergency Preparedness and Response**
Following up on the previous Corrective Action Plan (CAP Ref: Q4-2022/07) which cited deficiencies in emergency drill documentation, this inspection on 12 October 2023 identified ongoing systemic failures within the emergency preparedness program. During a review of drill records, the last full factory evacuation drill was conducted on 15 March 2023, exceeding the required six-month frequency stipulated by the Factory Safety Protocol FSP-101. Subsequent inspector observations on the third-floor sewing section revealed two of four designated primary exit routes partially obstructed by WIP rolling racks and fabric bundles, with approximate obstructions extending 1.5 meters into the egress path. Interviews with 7 production workers from this section confirmed they had not participated in any site-specific drill or refresher training within the current calendar year, with several expressing uncertainty regarding the location of their designated assembly point. This persistent non-compliance, coupled with the physical impediments to egress, represents a significant regression from the previously agreed CAP and poses a direct risk to worker safety in the event of a genuine emergency. Severity: Moderate.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SOC-08-DIS-2024-050-03
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SOC-08)
**Background & Context:** Factory 050 is currently operating under a corrective action plan (CAP Ref: 2023-Q4-112) following multiple non-compliances identified in the previous social audit dated 15 November 2023. The CAP included mandatory anti-discrimination training for all management and HR personnel, which was reportedly completed on 10 January 2024. This follow-up audit included a review of hiring, promotion, and disciplinary records from 1 December 2023 to the date of the audit, alongside worker interviews.
**Observations & Evidence:** During a review of payroll records and production line assignments on 22 April 2024, the audit team identified a pattern wherein 15 out of 17 workers in the higher-skilled (and higher-paid) embroidery section were male, while the adjacent packing section was staffed exclusively by 24 female workers. Interviews with 9 workers (4 male, 5 female) revealed consistent testimony that female operators are informally discouraged from applying for machine operator vacancies in the embroidery and cutting departments. One female worker with 4 years of tenure (Worker ID: 050-447) stated, "I asked my line supervisor three times about training for the embroidery machines, but he said those jobs are not for women because the machines are heavy and require technical thinking." This statement was corroborated by two other female interviewees. No documented justification based on bona fide occupational requirements was available to explain this gendered segregation.
**Conclusion:** The evidence indicates a systemic practice of gender-based discrimination in job assignment and access to promotion, violating the factory's own non-discrimination policy and the standards outlined in the CAP. This finding represents a failure to implement effective corrective actions from the previous audit.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2024-050-03
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Machine Guarding & Emergency Egress)
**Background & Context:** Factory 050 has a documented history of non-conformances related to unsafe work practices and inadequate machine guarding, with Corrective Action Plan (CAP) items OSH-2023-12 and OSH-2023-15 from the previous audit dated 15 November 2023 still open and under monitoring. The current audit, conducted on 24 April 2024, included a physical inspection of the 2nd-floor sewing production area and interviews with 7 machine operators.

**Observations & Evidence:** During the walk-through, the audit team observed that 11 out of 34 inspected industrial lockstitch machines (Juki model DLN-5410N) remained without functional needle guards, despite this being a specific requirement of the prior CAP. Furthermore, multiple aisles leading to the designated emergency exits, notably Exit B and Exit C, were found to be partially obstructed by WIP (work-in-progress) trolleys and fabric rolls, reducing the clear width to approximately 70cm, which is below the minimum 1.1-meter requirement as per the facility's own evacuation plan. Worker interviews corroborated these observations, with 5 of the 7 operators stating the missing guards were a common concern and that aisle blockage was a persistent, daily issue.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to implement the agreed-upon corrective actions within the stipulated timeframe, resulting in a continued and unacceptable risk of serious laceration injuries and a demonstrable impediment to safe emergency evacuation. This constitutes a failure to meet the fundamental requirements of the occupational safety and health program.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FA-050-24-008 **Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: HR.06)

**Background & Context:** Factory 050 has been under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) since the Q3 2023 audit, which identified a Minor violation (HR.06.02) for failing to display legally required posters regarding worker rights to association in a prominent location. The CAP required corrective action by 15 December 2023.

**Observations & Interviews:** During the site inspection on 22 February 2024, the required posters were now observed to be displayed in the main canteen, constituting initial compliance with the prior finding. However, during confidential interviews held with 12 production workers from various departments, a recurring issue was identified. Four workers from the stitching section separately reported that during departmental meetings in January 2024, a line supervisor made discouraging remarks regarding union membership, stating it was "unnecessary trouble" and that "the factory management handles our concerns directly." This was corroborated by a separate interview with one of the sewing line team leaders. No evidence was provided of disciplinary action against the supervisor for these statements, nor of any management communication to reinforce the policy of non-interference.

**Conclusion:** While the previous technical violation has been remediated, the worker interviews indicate a persistent cultural and procedural failure to actively protect and promote the principle of freedom of association, as required by section HR.06.01 of the Code of Conduct. Management has not demonstrated sufficient due diligence to ensure middle management and supervisory staff understand and uphold this policy, creating a chilling effect on worker organizing efforts.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
During the announced audit conducted on 12-15 March 2024, under audit code CA-24-050-01, a review of personnel files and production floor observations identified a compliance discrepancy within the juvenile worker program. Inspector review of file #WRK-2023-089 for operative "A. Rahman" (DOB: 15 July 2007), hired on 10 February 2024 for the finishing section, revealed the individual was 16 years of age at hire. While the juvenile worker medical certificate and parental consent were on file, subsequent cross-referencing with timecard data (TCRD-2024-02-14) and interview notes from two other finishing department workers confirmed that this minor worker was regularly scheduled for and worked 48-hour weeks, exceeding the maximum 36 hours per week permitted for juvenile workers as stipulated in the corrective action plan (CAP-F050-2023-12, item 7B) and national law. This constitutes a repeat violation of juvenile working hour protections. Severity: Moderate.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding HR-03-2024 (Reference CAP-F050-2023, Follow-up Item #7):** During the review of employment contracts and human resources documentation, conducted on March 14-15, 2024, it was observed that the factory's implementation of its corrective action plan regarding contract transparency remains incomplete. While permanent staff possessed signed contracts, a sample of 112 temporary workers hired within the last quarter revealed that 27 individuals (approximately 24%) had either not received a personal copy of their executed contract or were provided with a document lacking the company's authorized signature, as verified through cross-referencing with provided payroll records. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews (12 workers interviewed), where 3 workers stated they had "only seen the contract briefly at hiring." This practice contravenes local labor law Article 24 and the brand's Code of Conduct, section 3.1, on the provision of clear, written terms of employment, and represents a failure to fully close the previous non-conformance. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-2023-050-02 / **Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime / **Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Context:** Factory 050 is under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) following the Q3 2022 audit, which identified systemic violations of overtime regulations, including failure to provide mandatory weekly rest. The CAP required implementation of a revised production planning system and verified overtime consent procedures. **Observations & Interviews:** Review of payroll records for September 2023 and time cards for the week of October 16-22, 2023, indicates a persistent pattern of non-compliance. In the reviewed week, 72% of the production floor (approx. 310 workers) worked seven consecutive days, failing to receive the legally mandated 24-hour weekly rest period. Interviews with a sample of 17 workers from the cutting and sewing departments confirmed this pattern is routine, with several workers stating they had worked 13 consecutive days prior to the audit. While overtime premium pay appears correctly calculated, verbal consent was obtained under duress, with workers reporting management暗示 that refusal would impact future assignment allocation. **Conclusion:** The factory has failed to implement sustainable corrections from the previous CAP, demonstrating a repeat violation of weekly rest requirements and coercive practices in obtaining overtime consent. **Severity: Moderate** (elevated from previous Minor due to repeat, systemic nature and failure of CAP). **Follow-up Note:** This finding is linked to CAP items WH-2022-050-01A and 01B; require root cause analysis of production planning failures for revised CAP.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WAG-2023-050-F03
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (C-A.1.1 Regular Wage Payments)
**Background & Context:** Factory 050 is operating under an active Corrective Action Plan (CAP Ref: 2022-Q4-112) following a previous audit which identified systemic discrepancies in wage calculation and overtime remuneration. The current audit, conducted on 23-24 October 2023, included a detailed review of wage records for the period September 2023 to 23 October 2023 and confidential interviews with 17 production line workers selected at random across three departments.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** A reconciliation of time cards against payroll registers for the pay period ending 15 October 2023 revealed a pattern where overtime hours worked on Sundays and public holidays were calculated at the standard hourly rate, rather than the legally mandated premium rate. This was corroborated during worker interviews, with 9 of the 17 interviewees specifically noting their holiday overtime pay was "the same as normal day pay." Examination of the factory's payroll policy documentation confirmed this incorrect calculation methodology was still being applied in practice, constituting a failure to implement the corrective actions outlined in the prior CAP.

**Follow-up Notes:** This is a repeat violation, indicating a breakdown in the implementation of managerial controls and a lack of effective worker communication regarding wage adjustments. The finding represents a violation of local labor law Article [XX] and the fundamental principle of payment for all hours worked at the correct statutory rate.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 051 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref:** EP-2023-051-02 **Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness **Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** The facility, currently undergoing a management transition following its acquisition in Q3 2023, was assessed for its state of readiness regarding fire and emergency evacuation procedures. This evaluation is considered of heightened importance given the recent organizational changes and the reported workforce of approximately 320 persons on-site during the audit.
**Observations & Evidence:** Inspector walk-through of the production floors (Cutting, Sewing, and Finishing) on October 26 revealed that several primary and alternate emergency exit routes were partially obstructed by temporary storage of WIP (Work-in-Progress) and unused fabric rolls, a condition which contravenes clear egress requirements. Furthermore, a review of the emergency preparedness documentation found the existing Fire Emergency and Evacuation Plan to bear the previous ownership's company name and dated contact information, and had not been formally reviewed or updated post-acquisition. During structured interviews conducted with 14 workers across two production shifts, 9 workers indicated uncertainty regarding the location of their designated assembly point, with several citing that the most recent full facility evacuation drill occurred over 14 months ago, prior to the change in ownership.
**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** The combination of obstructed egress paths, outdated and non-relevant emergency plans, and insufficient worker training/drill frequency constitutes a significant failure in ensuring adequate emergency preparedness. The new management must prioritize the immediate clearance of all exit routes, the development and communication of a revised emergency plan, and the scheduling of a facility-wide drill.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
During the document review conducted on 17 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's personnel files for 15 production line workers hired in the period of August-September 2023, following the ownership transition, contained photocopies of National Identity Cards indicating dates of birth that would make them 15 years of age. Subsequent physical verification and confidential worker interviews with 3 of these individuals on the audit date confirmed their ages as 14 and 15, with one interviewee stating they were hired "because the new managers needed people quickly to meet the order." This constitutes a clear violation of local labor law Article 12 and the applicable code of conduct section CL.01, which strictly prohibits the employment of persons under 16. Corrective action requires the immediate removal of all underage workers from production duties, provision of financial support for their education, and the implementation of a robust age verification procedure. Severity: Critical.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-051-24-008**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: CBA.01)**
**Date of Assessment: March 12, 2024**

Following the recent change in ownership and management transition noted in the opening conference, the audit team conducted a review of policies and procedures related to worker representation. While the new management has posted a policy statement affirming workers' rights to freedom of association in principle, inspections on March 12th revealed that the previously established Worker Welfare Committee, elected in Q4 2023 under prior ownership, has been effectively suspended. Interviews with 17 production line workers across three departments confirmed that all committee activities ceased in January 2024 following the ownership transfer, with no new election process initiated or communicated. Furthermore, review of meeting minutes and discussion with two former committee members indicated that no good faith efforts to bargain collectively have been undertaken since the transition, despite the committee's prior submission of a collective bargaining proposal regarding wage adjustments in December 2023. The cessation of the sole recognized representative body, without a lawful or procedurally clear alternative, constitutes a significant impediment to the exercise of this fundamental right.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note: Corrective Action Plan must include a clear timeline for the reinstatement or re-election of a representative body and a formal response to the pending bargaining proposal.**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-03: Forced Labor and Coercion**  
With reference to the new ownership transition finalized in Q4 2023, the audit team, during the onsite inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, identified systemic inconsistencies in the management of worker identification documents. It was observed through payroll cross-verification and subsequent confidential interviews with 23 production line workers that the practice of retaining original government-issued identification cards (specifically national ID cards and household registration documents) under the pretext of "safekeeping for new employee processing" was uniformly applied to the entire cohort of 87 workers hired since the ownership change. Worker interview notes from 14 March indicate multiple accounts of management refusal to return these documents upon request, with one worker (Interview Ref: WI-09) stating, "I asked for my ID back last month to open a bank account, but my supervisor said it is factory policy and I would not get my attendance bonus if I kept asking." This practice of unlawful document retention, which effectively restricts workers' freedom of movement and ability to resign, constitutes a coercive labor practice under forced labor indicators FL-01 and FL-03 of the benchmarked code of conduct. **Severity: Critical.** A corrective action plan must address the immediate return of all original personal documents and the implementation of a compliant policy, with verification to be completed within 7 calendar days.

[Discrimination]
Audit Area 4.0 – Discrimination (Ref: SAI 8000 Sec. 4 / Client Code HR-03). The audit scope included a review of hiring, promotion, and disciplinary policies, and interviews conducted on March 12 and 13, 2024. While the formal, newly implemented policy documentation was found to be compliant, observations and worker interviews revealed a concerning pattern in practice. Specifically, of the 28 workers interviewed from the cutting and finishing departments, 17 reported a clear preference in assignment of coveted overtime hours and lighter-duty tasks to workers of the local ethnic majority, with several workers from minority groups stating they had not been offered overtime in over three months despite volunteering. This was corroborated by a review of the overtime allocation logs for the past quarter (Jan-Mar 2024), which showed a statistically disproportionate allocation. This de facto discrimination, reportedly stemming from line-level supervisory practices under the previous ownership, has persisted into the transition period and constitutes a violation of the standard. The new management must implement controls to ensure equitable application of opportunity. Severity: Moderate.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-03-2024-051**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 6, ILS Standard)**

Background context indicates the facility is undergoing a management transition following its acquisition in Q4 2023. A review of payroll records for the sample period of March 1-31, 2024, alongside daily time cards and production data, revealed a systemic pattern of excessive overtime. Inspector observations confirmed a six-day workweek was in operation, and worker interviews conducted on April 17, 2024, with 14 randomly selected employees from the sewing section, indicated consistent work hours from 08:00 to 21:00, including three hours of mandatory overtime, with only a one-hour meal break. This results in a 12-hour daily working period. The interviewed workers' statements were corroborated by the reviewed time records, which showed an average of 78 hours per week for the sample group, far exceeding the legal limit of 60 hours (regular plus overtime). Management acknowledged awareness of the issue, attributing it to aggressive production targets set during the ownership transition, but had not implemented an effective corrective action plan. This constitutes a violation of both local labor law and the applicable code of conduct regarding maximum working hours and mandatory overtime.
**Severity: Critical**

Factory 052 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area:** 4.2.1 Harassment & Abuse (Audit Code: HR-HA-2023)
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident of 15 March 2023 and the factory's subsequent placement under special monitoring, this audit includes a heightened review of management systems and worker well-being. Particular attention was given to the potential for increased supervisory pressure due to recovery-related production delays.
**Observations & Interviews:** During unannounced floor walks on 12 October 2023, auditors observed several interactions where line supervisors used derogatory language, including terms like "useless" and "slow donkey," directed at workers who had fallen behind target. Subsequent confidential interviews with 17 production workers from Sewing Lines 3 and 5 confirmed a pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation, with 14 workers reporting such incidents had increased in frequency since production resumed in May. Multiple workers stated they feared reporting this behavior due to concerns over job security during the factory's fragile recovery period.
**Finding:** The factory has failed to maintain an effective policy and procedure to prevent harassment and abuse, as evidenced by the documented culture of verbal abuse and the absence of a trusted, functional grievance mechanism, which is exacerbated by the current post-crisis operational environment. This constitutes a breach of the Responsible Sourcing Code, section 4.2.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** Management must immediately conduct refresher training for all supervisory staff on respectful communication and the non-retaliation policy, and re-communicate the grievance procedure to all employees by 30 November 2023.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: FL-052-03**
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (SA8000:2014 Section 1 / ILO Convention 29)
**Background & Context:** Following the severe fire incident on 15 March 2024, which resulted in the temporary closure of the western sewing wing, the audit focus included a heightened review of worker freedom of movement and voluntary employment, recognizing the potential for exploitative practices during recovery and rebuild phases under special monitoring. Inspector observations on 28 April 2024 noted that management had retained the passports and national identity cards of 37 migrant workers (primarily from Batch S-24/01), citing a new post-fire "security protocol" to secure documents in the main office safe. During confidential interviews with 8 of these affected workers, 6 confirmed they had repeatedly requested the return of their documents for personal banking and remittance purposes since early April but were refused by HR personnel, who stated the documents were being held "for their own safety and to ensure factory continuity." This retention of personal documents against the workers' will effectively restricts their freedom of movement and constitutes a means of coercion, as they are unable to leave their employment without forfeiting their essential identity papers. The factory has failed to demonstrate that this practice is required by law or that workers have provided informed, voluntary consent for such retention.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** Corrective Action Plan must include the immediate, unconditional return of all original identity documents to workers, with the option for voluntary, documented safe-keeping agreements, and training for HR on permissible document handling.

[Discrimination]
**Compliance Area: 3.6 - Discrimination (Employment & Occupation)**
**Ref: AUD-052-24-FO-018**

Following the resumption of partial production after the fire incident of 15 January 2024, and during the period of special oversight by district authorities, the audit team reviewed hiring records for Q1 2024 and conducted confidential interviews with 27 workers across various production lines on 12 April 2024. It was observed that while the factory’s written policy is compliant, the practical implementation shows a deviation. Specifically, the post-fire rehiring process appears to have disproportionately excluded workers from certain demographic groups; interview notes from five former employees (Worker IDs 4017, 4122, 4355, 4388, 4401) indicate they were not recalled despite expressing availability, with three of those interviewed alleging that floor supervisors made preferential recall decisions based on regional origin and marital status. Management was unable to produce documented, objective criteria for the recall selection process when requested, citing the chaotic circumstances post-incident. This constitutes a failure to ensure equal opportunity in employment practices. **Severity: Moderate**. A follow-up review of the recall procedure and potential remediation for affected workers is required within 60 days.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding EP-024-052**
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Post-Incident Recovery & Ongoing Protocols)
**Background & Context:** Factory 052, which is under special administrative monitoring per Local Ordinance 7-2024 following a significant fire incident on 12 March 2024, was reviewed for the reinstatement and efficacy of its emergency response systems. The audit focused on the transitional period between the restoration of physical infrastructure and the full operational readiness of personnel and procedures.

**Observations & Evidence:** During the site inspection conducted on 15 May 2024, it was noted that while primary firefighting equipment has been replaced and evacuation routes are visibly marked, the documented emergency drills mandated under the factory's own corrective action plan have not been fully executed. Specifically, the required full-scale evacuation drill for all shifts, scheduled for completion by 30 April 2024, has been postponed twice, with management citing production pressures to meet revised delivery deadlines. Interviews with 7 production workers from the cutting and sewing departments revealed inconsistent understanding of revised assembly point locations, with 4 of the 7 workers providing incorrect or uncertain information when queried.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** This constitutes a failure to maintain a state of operational readiness and demonstrates a lapse in the execution of the agreed-upon corrective action timeline. The absence of a validated, post-incident drill undermines the theoretical preparedness of the new protocols and leaves the workforce inadequately trained for a potential emergency, a particular concern given the facility's recent history. A follow-up verification audit is required within 30 days to witness a successful full facility drill.
**Severity: Moderate**
