Factory 062 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area: C.3 Contracts & Human Resources - Employment Contracts & Personnel Files**
**Audit Reference: A-2023-062-14C**

**Finding Detail:** During the document review on 14 November 2023, the audit team sampled personnel files for 35 workers from the current seasonal production peak, representing approximately 15% of the onsite workforce. It was observed that 28 of the sampled files (80%) contained only a basic, non-brand-specific factory registration form, lacking key legally mandated terms such as detailed job description, precise wage calculation method, working hours, and social insurance registration particulars. Subsequent confidential interviews conducted with 12 production line workers on 15 November 2023 corroborated this systemic deficiency; 10 interviewed workers stated they had never signed a formal labor contract, with several seasonal workers noting they were given only an oral agreement regarding a piece-rate pay scale. This practice fails to meet the requirements of local labor law Article 10 and constitutes a significant risk to workers' understanding of their terms and conditions of employment, particularly within a high-turnover, seasonal operational model. The factory management acknowledged the gap, attributing it to the rapid onboarding of temporary staff under high-pressure order deadlines.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-062-24-HA-003 | Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (HA) | Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

During the review of management systems and subsequent confidential worker interviews, the audit team identified systemic issues in the workplace environment pertaining to disrespectful conduct and verbal abuse. Background context notes the factory operates as a subcontractor under significant production pressure with a workforce comprising approximately 60% temporary seasonal employees, a factor management cited as contributing to communication challenges. In interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, with 28 workers selected from the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments, 9 workers (32%) independently reported instances of line supervisors using derogatory language, shouting, and making threatening remarks regarding job security, particularly during peak production periods and when quality errors were discovered. These reports were consistent across multiple production lines and were corroborated by direct observation by the lead auditor on October 26 of a supervisor aggressively berating an operator for a sewing error in a manner likely to constitute verbal abuse. The factory's policy on harassment, while documented, lacks specific implementation procedures and effective, confidential grievance mechanisms, leading to under-reporting and a perceived culture of impunity for such behavior. Management acknowledged awareness of "high-pressure communication" but had not conducted specific training for supervisory staff on appropriate disciplinary techniques and abuse prevention.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)
**Finding Ref:** WB-062-2024-03

**Background & Observation:** During the onsite inspection conducted on March 12-14, 2024, the audit team reviewed payroll records for the period of January 1 to February 29, 2024, for a sample of 32 production workers from the seasonal workforce. Initial documentation indicated basic wage rates met the regional minimum wage standard. However, a detailed reconciliation of time and attendance records against payment slips revealed systematic discrepancies for workers engaged in overtime.

**Worker Interviews & Documentation Review:** Interviews with 14 assembly line operators confirmed that production peaks in January and February necessitated frequent extended shifts, with workers reporting an average of 72-78 hours per week. While payroll records reflected these hours, the calculation for overtime compensation applied only the basic minimum wage rate, failing to incorporate the legally mandated piece-rate earnings into the overtime premium calculation, as required by local labor law Article 24, Section 3. This resulted in a systematic underpayment of overtime wages.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Note:** The factory is not in compliance with statutory requirements for the correct calculation of overtime remuneration. A recalculation for the sample group indicates an average underpayment of approximately 12.3% on overtime wages for the period reviewed. Management stated the practice was due to a payroll system configuration error and misinterpretation of the piece-rate inclusion rule. A full retrospective calculation for all affected workers is required.
**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 063 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**AUDIT CODE: WOH-2024-063-07 | COMPLIANCE AREA: WORKING HOURS & OVERTIME | AUDIT DATE: October 23-24, 2024**

While the factory maintains a generally robust timekeeping system with biometric clock-in/out records and has a documented policy aligning with local legal standards (a maximum 60-hour workweek including overtime), a discrepancy between policy and practice was identified. Inspection of payroll records for the sample period of September 1-30, 2024, for three production lines (A, C, and F) revealed that 127 of 412 sampled workers exceeded the 60-hour threshold in at least one week, with a maximum recorded week of 67 hours. Interviews conducted with 14 randomly selected operators from those lines corroborated this, with several noting frequent, though not always formally recorded, "off-the-clock" work to meet pressing shipping deadlines, estimated at 1-2 hours per shift in the audit week. Although the overtime was compensated at the correct premium rate and the instances were not found to be chronic for all workers, the systemic failure to prevent weekly hour limits constitutes a breach of both local law and the Brand’s Code of Conduct. The factory management was cooperative in reviewing these findings and has committed to revising production planning. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan (CAP) must address systemic production scheduling and timekeeping controls to prevent recurrence.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: EP-2023-063-04 | Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness | Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**

Background context and previous audit history indicated a generally robust emergency management program, consistent with the facility's ISO 45001 certification and the oversight of the dedicated compliance officer. However, during a full, unannounced evacuation drill conducted at 14:30 hours on the date of inspection, inspector observations and subsequent worker interviews revealed a significant procedural lapse. While the evacuation time of 7 minutes 22 seconds for the 1,247 personnel on-site was within acceptable parameters, interviews with 18 workers from the cutting and finishing departments on the third floor indicated they were not audibly alerted by the central alarm system and instead initiated egress based on visual cues from colleagues, as the local alarm strobes functioned but the audible alarms in that zone were inaudible over operational machinery noise. This constitutes a failure of the early warning system in a defined section of the facility, potentially delaying evacuation initiation during a real, visibility-obscured emergency. Follow-up notes confirm the Compliance Officer was notified immediately and has initiated a work order with maintenance to test and rectify the alarm system zoning. Severity: Moderate.

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference:** CL-063-2023-04
**Compliance Area:** Child Labor & Young Workers (ILO C138, C182; Client Code of Conduct Section 3.1)

**Background & Observation:** Factory 063 maintains a formally documented and communicated policy prohibiting child labor, which is posted in common areas. During the document review conducted on 16 October 2023, the Human Resources files for 127 production workers hired within the last 12 months were examined. All personnel files contained copies of government-issued identification cards or birth certificates, with no discrepancies noted. The recruitment process, as described by the HR Manager, includes a verification step against the provided documentation. Furthermore, interviews conducted with a randomized selection of 18 production line workers from various departments on 17 October 2023 confirmed an understanding of the minimum age policy, with no reports or indications of underage workers present on site. The factory's longstanding ISO certification and dedicated compliance oversight appear to provide a robust procedural framework for prevention.

**Status: Compliant.** No evidence of child labor was found during this audit cycle.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FA-063-24-008 **Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FACB)

**Background & Observation:** During the onsite inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, the audit team reviewed the factory's trade union registration documents and collective bargaining agreement (CBA), which were found to be in order and up-to-date. Furthermore, the dedicated compliance officer provided a comprehensive overview of the established Worker Representative Committee (WRC), whose elected members' terms were duly posted on all main notice boards. However, during confidential worker interviews held on 13 March 2024, a discrepancy was noted: of 32 production workers interviewed from various departments, 9 workers (approximately 28%) expressed unawareness of the WRC's most recent election process held in January 2024, and 7 workers were uncertain of the committee's specific role in grievance escalation, indicating a potential lapse in effective communication and worker education regarding this crucial mechanism.

**Conclusion & Follow-Up:** While no active suppression of association rights was observed and the formal structures are compliant, the findings suggest insufficient ongoing efforts to ensure all workforce tiers are fully informed and engaged with the existing representative structures. This represents a gap in the implementation of the factory's own procedures. The factory management is required to submit a corrective action plan detailing enhanced communication strategies and training for the WRC to effectively fulfill its mandate for all employees.

**Severity: Minor**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: OSH-2023-063-F07**
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health (Machine Guarding & Hazard Communication)**
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**

During the comprehensive facility walk-through conducted on the aforementioned date, the audit team observed generally high standards of housekeeping and adherence to procedural safety protocols, consistent with the factory's established profile and ISO 45001 certification. However, a focused inspection of the cutting and spreading department revealed three industrial sewing machines (Asset IDs: SM-087, SM-088, SM-089) where the installed needle guards had been deliberately removed or bypassed, exposing workers to potential puncture and laceration hazards. Interviews with two operators assigned to these workstations confirmed this was a persistent condition, undertaken to facilitate the handling of thicker fabric layers, and indicated a lack of recent supervisory intervention. This constitutes a failure to maintain essential point-of-operation safeguarding as per section 7.3 of the factory's own Safety Manual and international machinery safety standards ILO Convention 155. The dedicated compliance officer was notified immediately, and a corrective action request (CAR-063-041) was issued. **Severity: Moderate**

Factory 064 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 4.0 Wages & Benefits (Reference: AUD-STD-4.1, 4.3, 4.5)**
**Background & Observations:** The facility operates as a subcontractor with a highly variable order book, leading to pronounced peaks in production demand, particularly noted during the audit period covering Q4 2023 and Q1 2024. A review of payroll records for the week of 12 March 2024, selected due to its representation of a high-volume production period, revealed a workforce of 347 direct employees, with a significant portion (approximately 60%) hired on temporary contracts. While minimum wage compliance was formally met, analysis of timescards against wage slips for 15 randomly selected workers from the sewing and finishing departments identified consistent discrepancies.
**Worker Interviews & Findings:** Interviews conducted on 14-15 March 2024 with 22 production line workers confirmed systemic issues. Twelve (12) workers independently reported that during peak weeks, their recorded overtime hours were routinely capped at 36 hours per month in the system, despite actual overtime worked often exceeding 60 hours, to avoid triggering the higher legal overtime premium rate for hours beyond this threshold. This practice resulted in underpayment of legally mandated overtime wages. Furthermore, the stipulated attendance bonus was withheld from 8 interviewed workers for the month of February 2024 due to taking a single day of sick leave, a policy not communicated in their employment contracts.
**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to remunerate all work performed at the correct statutory rate, constituting a violation of wage payment regulations. The practice of capping overtime records to avoid higher premium payments is a deliberate, systemic failure affecting a substantial portion of the workforce.
**Severity: Critical**

[Child Labor]
**COMPLIANCE AREA: 01.03 - CHILD LABOR & YOUNG WORKERS**

During the documentation review conducted on 24/10/2023, it was noted that the factory's stated hiring policy, as per their Employee Handbook Revision 3, aligns with local law and international standards, prohibiting the employment of individuals below the age of 18. However, subsequent physical verification of the production floor on the aforementioned date revealed the presence of several workers whose appearance suggested they might be below the legal working age. These individuals were observed operating overlook stitching machines in Section B and performing trimming duties in the finishing section. As a standard procedure, the audit team requested personnel files for these six specific workers for age verification. The factory management was only able to provide four of the requested files; the remaining two files, for workers identified as Sanjay K. and Priya M., were stated to be "under processing" with the contractor agency. Interviews conducted privately with these two individuals on 25/10/2023 revealed conflicting information regarding their ages and educational background, with both stating they had left school only two years prior, which, if accurate, would place them at approximately 16 years of age. The lack of verifiable, government-issued proof of age documentation for these workers constitutes a failure to effectively implement the factory's own policy and raises a reasonable suspicion of underage labor, requiring immediate corrective action. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding 2024-06-15 / REF: AFC-064-28 / Area: Contracts & Human Resources**

Upon review of personnel files and subsequent worker interviews conducted on June 13, 2024, it was established that the factory's practices regarding employment contracts are not uniformly applied or consistently compliant with local labor law. Inspector observations from a sample of 32 personnel files (drawn from a workforce of approximately 450) revealed that 18 files contained only a basic, non-detailed registration form, lacking legally mandated clauses on wage calculation, working hours, and termination procedures. Interviews with 14 production line workers, including 9 hired within the last 2 months for peak seasonal orders, corroborated this discrepancy; 10 interviewees stated they had signed only a "factory rules" document upon hiring and had not received a copy of any signed contract. This failure to provide legally binding contracts to a significant portion of the workforce, particularly the temporary seasonal employees, exposes them to undue risk and denies them clear terms of employment. A follow-up note is required to mandate a full file review and the issuance of compliant contracts to all employees. Severity: Moderate.

Factory 065 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Code: F-SS-EP). **Date of Inspection:** 17 October 2023. **Background/Context:** With the recent change in ownership and management transition noted during the opening meeting, a focused verification of emergency protocols was conducted to ensure continuity of critical safety systems. **Observations:** During a physical inspection of the second-floor cutting department (Zone B), it was found that the primary designated emergency exit route was obstructed by three trolleys of cut work-in-progress fabric and two portable bundling machines, rendering the exit pathway impassable for approximately 65% of its width. Furthermore, the emergency lighting unit adjacent to this exit was non-functional upon manual testing. **Worker Interviews:** Interviews with 7 production workers from this department on the date of inspection revealed that the monthly emergency evacuation drill, previously scheduled for the first Tuesday of each month, had not been conducted for the past three months due to "new management schedules." Three workers stated they were unaware of the current assembly point location following recent changes to the factory yard layout. **Conclusion:** The obstruction of a primary exit, coupled with the failure to conduct regular drills and communicate essential procedures during the management transition, constitutes a significant failure in ensuring effective egress and worker readiness. A follow-up review of the Emergency Preparedness and Response procedure (Doc. #EPR-065-01) is required to ensure it reflects current conditions and management accountability. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB-03)
**Finding Reference:** 065-WB-2024-001

During the onsite inspection conducted on 12-14 November 2024, a review of wage records for the period of July-October 2024, alongside confidential worker interviews with 32 production line employees, revealed systemic discrepancies in overtime remuneration. It was observed that while the base wage met the local minimum wage standard, the overtime rate applied consistently across the reviewed pay slips was calculated at 1.3 times the hourly base rate, rather than the legally mandated 1.5 times for work performed on rest days. This practice was confirmed through cross-referencing time cards and payroll ledgers for a sample of 15 workers from the cutting and sewing departments. Management attributed this discrepancy to an ongoing transition to a new payroll software system under the new ownership. This constitutes a violation of national labor law Article [XX] and places the factory in breach of contractual client requirements on wage compliance. A follow-up note requires the corrected calculation methodology to be applied retroactively and for proof of remedial payments to be submitted for verification. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: CHR-065-04 | Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources | Date of Finding: October 26, 2023**
During the review of personnel files and worker interviews, it was noted that due to the recent change in ownership and transitional management, a significant discrepancy exists between corporate policy and on-ground implementation regarding employment contracts. Specifically, while a new standardized contract template was introduced in August 2023, inspector observations of a sample of 32 personnel files for workers hired post-transition revealed that 19 files contained only a signed offer letter, lacking the final, legally binding employment contract stipulated by the factory's own HR manual. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 8 of the affected workers on October 25, who confirmed they had not received a finalized contract copy for their records, stating they were told "the new system was still being set up." This failure to issue executed contracts within the probationary period denies workers formal clarity on terms of employment, though management has provided evidence of intent to comply. **Severity: Moderate**. Follow-up required: Factory management must complete the retroactive issuance and execution of contracts for all affected workers and demonstrate a verified, systematic process for all new hires.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: FAC-065-2023-SOC-04 (Discrimination)**
Based on a review of hiring and promotion records conducted on 14-15 November 2023, and corroborated by confidential worker interviews with 32 employees across 4 production departments, the audit team identified systemic disparities in employment conditions tied to gender and regional origin. Inspector observations and payroll analysis confirmed that all 18 line-supervisor positions are occupied by male employees from the same provincial origin as the new ownership group, despite a workforce that is approximately 68% female. Furthermore, interview notes from 9 female operators with over 5 years of tenure revealed repeated expressions of being passed over for advancement in favor of less experienced male counterparts since the management transition, with one worker stating, "They only promote their townsmen now." This constitutes a violation of the client’s Code of Conduct, Section 5.1, on equal opportunity. **Severity: Moderate**. Follow-up required: management must provide a written corrective action plan addressing non-discriminatory hiring and promotion policies.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref:** A-OSH-065-04 **| Area:** Occupational Safety & Health **| Date of Inspection:** 2023-10-26
**Background & Context:** The audit was conducted during a period of acknowledged transition following the change in ownership in Q3 2023. The new management team, while demonstrating intent to improve systems, has not yet fully implemented comprehensive safety protocols across all production departments. This finding pertains specifically to the woven cutting section, which employs approximately 45 workers per shift.
**Observations & Evidence:** During the physical inspection of the cutting room conducted on October 26, the auditor observed that three out of five operational band saw machines (asset tags CT-07, CT-09, CT-11) were missing their manufacturer-installed blade guards, leaving the cutting blades fully exposed during operation. Furthermore, the required "Machine Safety Operating Procedures" placards were not posted at these workstations. During worker interviews, four of the seven cutting section workers spoken to confirmed that the guards had been removed for "ease of thread changes" several weeks prior and had not been replaced; one worker indicated a near-miss incident on or around October 12 involving loose clothing. The factory's own preventative maintenance log showed no record of guard inspections or repairs for these units in the last 90 days.
**Analysis & Follow-up Requirement:** This constitutes a failure to provide and maintain necessary engineering controls to eliminate a known amputation hazard, violating both the factory's own stated safety policy (Section 4.1) and the fundamental requirements of internationally recognized safety standards. The condition was brought to the immediate attention of the Production Manager and the newly appointed Safety Officer on-site. A corrective action plan detailing the immediate cessation of use for non-compliant machinery, sourcing of replacement guards, and retraining of all cutting section staff is required within 48 hours.
**Severity: Critical**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-065-24-008 | Compliance Area: FOA & CB (B.1.1, B.2.3) | Date of Finding: 17 October 2023**
During the review of policies and worker interviews conducted on 16-17 October 2023, it was noted that, following the change in ownership and ongoing management transition, there is no currently active worker representative structure or functioning collective bargaining agreement. Interviews with 32 production workers across three departments revealed a consistent lack of awareness of any existing worker committee, with several workers expressing uncertainty about the procedure to form one and a perceived reluctance from new mid-level supervisors to discuss such matters. While the new management has provided a generic policy document stating respect for freedom of association, dated August 2023, no concrete steps have been taken to re-establish representative channels, and the previous CBA was allowed to lapse upon the transfer of ownership. This constitutes a failure to actively promote and facilitate the means for collective bargaining as required.
**Severity: Moderate** | **Follow-up:** Management must develop and communicate a clear, time-bound action plan for the democratic election of worker representatives by Q1 2024.

Factory 066 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: SG-ST-066-24-014 | Compliance Area: HR & Social Compliance - Discrimination (Category: Equal Opportunity)**

During the audit conducted on 12-14 March 2024, a review of hiring and promotion practices was undertaken in the context of the factory's recent change in ownership and ongoing management transition. Background interviews with 17 production workers, alongside a detailed examination of personnel files for all 28 hires since the ownership transfer on 15 January 2024, revealed a concerning pattern. Inspector observations and worker interview notes (Log: WI-066-09 through WI-066-13) indicate a clear hiring preference for male workers for all positions involving machinery operation or packing, with the new management citing a belief in their greater physical stamina and perceived technical aptitude. Of the 28 new hires, 26 are male, and the two female hires were exclusively placed in lower-paid sewing thread trimming roles. This constitutes a systemic bias based on gender in recruitment and initial placement, contravening both international standards and local labor law provisions on equal opportunity. The management team, while cooperative, demonstrated a lack of awareness of these requirements, indicating a need for immediate policy implementation and training. **Severity: Moderate** - A corrective action plan must be submitted within 14 days, with a follow-up verification scheduled for Q3 2024 to review revised hiring procedures and training records.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Compliance Area 7: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Audit Code: FOACB-04)**

Following the recent change in ownership and management transition noted in the factory profile, auditors conducted a review of policies and worker representative structures on 12-15 March 2024. While a Worker Representative Committee (WRC) exists on paper, established under the prior ownership, interviews with 27 production workers across three departments revealed a unanimous lack of awareness of the current WRC members or any election process held in the last 12 months. Management confirmed, in a meeting dated 14 March, that they had "temporarily suspended" the committee to "reconstitute it under new management directives," but provided no written timeline or procedure for this reinstatement, effectively leaving no functioning mechanism for collective worker representation. This constitutes a failure to uphold the principle of freedom of association, as workers have been denied an established channel for dialogue without a lawful alternative being provided in a timely manner, a situation exacerbated by the transitional period. **Severity: Moderate**. Follow-up required: Management must provide a clear, written action plan with dates for the democratic election of a new WRC.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding REF: CHR-066-03 | Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources | Date of Inspection: 17 October 2023**

Background context indicates that Factory 066 underwent a change in ownership in Q3 2023, resulting in a transitional management structure. During the review of personnel files for a randomized sample of 47 production workers hired post-transition, it was observed that 31 files (approximately 66%) contained only a basic job application form and lacked a signed, individualized employment contract stipulating essential terms as required by local law and the applicable code of conduct, including clear details on wages, working hours, and job description. Worker interviews conducted on 18 October 2023 with 12 of the affected employees confirmed this absence; notes from worker #0883 state, "I was told to start work immediately and sign only the attendance register. I have not seen a contract." This failure to provide legally binding employment contracts to a significant portion of the workforce constitutes a violation of the fundamental right to clear terms of employment. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up review is required within 90 days to verify the issuance of compliant contracts to all employees.

Factory 067 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-067-03**
**Compliance Area:** Forced Labor & Involuntary Labor (SMETA 4 Pillar - Element 1)
**Date of Discovery:** 23 October 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the partial production halt due to the fire incident in Wing B on 15 August 2023, management stated that recovery operations were prioritizing the reinstatement of core sewing lines. During the document review for payroll and attendance records for the period September to October 2023, inconsistencies were noted between stated working hours and overtime payments for a specific subset of workers involved in cleanup and machinery relocation. This prompted a targeted interview session.
**Observations & Worker Testimony:** On 23 October 2023, auditors conducted private interviews with 7 workers from the cleanup and maintenance team. Four of these workers independently reported that their national identity cards had been retained by line supervisors following the fire, under the pretext of "processing emergency compensation," and had not been returned. These workers stated they felt compelled to accept mandatory overtime, including working on their designated weekly rest day, for fear of not recovering their documents or facing dismissal during the fragile recovery period. Management confirmed the practice of document retention for "safekeeping" but could not produce a voluntary, written consent from the affected workers as per requirement FL.01.04.
**Conclusion:** The retention of personal identity documents, combined with worker testimony indicating a perceived coercion to work excessive hours under threat of penalty during a period of known economic vulnerability, constitutes a violation of the Forced Labor principle, as it restricts the workers' freedom of movement and ability to voluntarily terminate employment.
**Severity: Critical**
**Follow-up Note:** This finding has been immediately escalated to the Special Monitoring Authority liaison, and a corrective action plan (CAP) requiring the immediate, documented return of all personal documents and a revised overtime policy is mandated within 48 hours.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB-04, WB-07)
**Reference:** Interview Log WB-INT-067-2024-03, Payroll Sample FY24-Q1

Following the significant disruption caused by the fire incident on 15 January 2024 and the subsequent factory closure for remediation until 12 February 2024, this audit focused on wage continuity and calculation during the recovery period. Inspector observations of payroll records for February and March 2024, cross-referenced with time cards and bank transfer statements, revealed a systemic discrepancy wherein 127 production line workers were paid only for days actively worked post-reopening, failing to receive the legally mandated severance and wage payments for the period of forced closure, as confirmed in interviews with 14 workers from three departments on 28 March 2024. This constitutes a failure to guarantee wages during a suspension of work for reasons not attributable to the worker, as per national labor law Article 62. Management acknowledged the lapse, attributing it to cash flow difficulties during rebuilding, and has submitted a corrective action plan (CAP-067-041) for retroactive payment, pending approval from the special monitoring authority. Severity: Critical.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding FO.2023.067-12A (Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining)**

Following the facility's resumption of partial operations post-incident, inspectors conducted a review of policies and interviewed a cross-section of 14 production line workers on 24 October 2023. While management has demonstrated commendable efforts in physical reconstruction, a significant procedural gap was identified regarding employee representative mechanisms. It was observed that the former Worker Representative Committee, active prior to the fire of August 2023, has not been reconvened, and no interim election process has been initiated or communicated to the workforce. Worker interviews (Ref: WI-067-08 through WI-067-14) consistently noted a lack of any formal channel through which to collectively address post-incident concerns, including relocation to temporary production zones and revised shift patterns, with three interviewees expressing apprehension about raising such issues individually. In the absence of a democratically elected body, the principle of collective bargaining is effectively suspended, which is of heightened concern during this recovery period. **Severity: Moderate** – requiring the establishment of an interim representative structure within 30 days, with a follow-up verification scheduled for Q1 2024.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2023-067-F02
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Emergency Preparedness & Fire Safety)
**Audit Dates:** October 24-26, 2023

**Finding:**
Following the major fire incident of August 12, 2023, which resulted in the factory's current special monitoring status, the audit team notes significant reconstruction efforts in the affected western warehouse section. However, during inspection of the operational sewing floors on October 25, it was observed that the primary and alternate escape routes for the northeast cutting department, which hosts approximately 120 workers, were found to be partially obstructed by temporary storage of inbound fabric rolls and WIP trolleys, reducing the clear egress width to less than 0.8 meters at points, contravening the mandated minimum of 1.1 meters per local fire code FSC-101.7. Interviews with three workers from that department confirmed on a non-attributable basis that this has been a recurring condition since production resumed in September, and they expressed concern over the congestion during shift-change drills. While the factory management has demonstrated intent through the submission of a corrective action plan for structural repairs to the authorities, the ongoing obstruction of life safety pathways presents an immediate and unacceptable risk, necessitating prompt rectification. 
**Severity: Critical**

Factory 068 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Harassment & Abuse]
During the on-site assessment conducted from May 12-14, 2024, particular attention was given to the implementation of the corrective action plan (CAP Ref: CAP-068-11-2023) item requiring enhanced management training on prevention of harassment and abuse, following prior non-conformances in this area. Investigator observations and interviews with a stratified sample of 37 production line workers revealed a persistent gap; while a new grievance procedure (Policy Doc: HR-7.2) was documented, six separate interview subjects (Interview IDs: W-22, W-23, W-27, W-29, W-31, W-34) reported instances of verbal abuse and intimidation by supervisory staff, specifically citing the use of derogatory language and threats of disciplinary action for not meeting unofficial production targets. These workers indicated a reluctance to utilize the formal grievance channels due to fears of retaliation, a concern not adequately mitigated by the factory's announced non-retaliation policy. Consequently, the facility has failed to demonstrate effective operationalization of its corrective actions to ensure a workplace free from harassment and abuse. Severity: Moderate.

[Forced Labor]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: FL-2023-068-02**
**COMPLIANCE AREA: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (CAC: FL.01, FL.03)**
**Background & Context:** During the announced audit conducted on October 26-27, 2023, a specific review of employment practices was undertaken due to Factory 068's documented history of compliance issues under the ongoing corrective action plan (CAP Ref: 2022-Q4-045), which included previous findings related to excessive overtime and retention of worker documents. The inspection focused on verification of worker freedom of movement and voluntary employment. **Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** A review of personnel files for 30 randomly selected production line workers revealed that 17 employees (approximately 57% of the sample) had their original national identity cards held by factory management, filed in a locked cabinet in the HR office, a practice confirmed by the HR manager as "standard for safekeeping and guarantee." During confidential interviews held on October 27 with 12 workers from the sewing department, 3 workers separately reported that they had requested the return of their documents within the last six months but were refused, with management stating the documents were necessary "until the contract ends" and that leaving beforehand would result in forfeiture of two months' wages. This practice of withholding personal documents restricts workers' ability to voluntarily terminate employment and constitutes a retention of personal property as a form of coercion. **Follow-up Note:** This finding represents a failure to implement the required corrective actions from the previous CAP and is a clear violation of the fundamental principle of voluntary work. Severity: Critical.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: 068-WH-03-2024**
**COMPLIANCE AREA: WORKING HOURS & OVERTIME (Code: B.1.1, B.1.3)**
**BACKGROUND/CONTEXT:** Factory 068 remains under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) from the Q4 2023 audit, with specific requirements to implement verifiable controls for accurate working hour and overtime recording and compensation. During this follow-up inspection conducted on March 12-14, 2024, a focused review was performed to assess the efficacy of implemented measures.

**OBSERVATIONS & EVIDENCE:** While the factory has introduced a new biometric timekeeping system, a reconciliation of timeclock data against production records and wage slips for January and February 2024 revealed persistent discrepancies. Specifically, for the pay period ending February 15, review of data for 23 workers in the cutting and sewing departments indicated an average of 14.2 hours of unrecorded and uncompensated overtime per worker. This was corroborated by handwritten supervisor production logs which noted work completed outside of the officially recorded shift times. Furthermore, the official records submitted for review indicated consistent 60-hour workweeks, just at the maximum limit, but failed to capture the extended hours evidenced elsewhere.

**WORKER INTERVIEWS:** Confidential interviews were conducted with 14 workers across key departments on March 13. Eight (8) workers confirmed they routinely work beyond their recorded shift times, typically 1.5 to 2 hours per day, to meet production targets and that this additional time is not reflected on their timecards or paid at the legally mandated overtime premium. Several workers expressed concern that refusing this extra work would negatively affect their future assignment opportunities.

**CONCLUSION:** The factory has failed to demonstrate sustained compliance with the CAP requirements and is in violation of local labor law and code standards regarding accurate recording of working hours and payment for all overtime worked. The systemic nature of the under-recording, which leads to wage violations, constitutes a repeated and fundamental breach.
**Severity: Critical**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
During the follow-up audit conducted on October 26, 2023, to verify the corrective actions from the previous CAP (Ref: AUD068-2023-Q3, Finding OSH-04), inspectors noted a failure to fully implement the required machine guarding improvements. While the factory had installed guards on the primary cutting machines as previously cited, subsequent observation of the sewing section revealed that 12 out of 15 overlook machines remained unguarded at the needle and chain looper areas, posing a laceration and amputation hazard. This was corroborated during interviews with five sewing operators, who confirmed the guards had been removed by line supervisors to increase production speed and had not been reinstalled. The recurrence of this specific machine guarding issue, despite its prior identification and the agreed-upon corrective action plan, demonstrates a systemic failure in the factory's safety management and adherence to its CAP obligations. **Severity: Moderate.**

Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: CHR-069-24-008**
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Background & Context:** Following the previous audit (Report #AU-069-23-Q4) which identified systemic non-compliance in employment documentation, Factory 069 was placed under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) with a key requirement to ensure 100% issuance of legally compliant employment contracts to all direct and contract workers by 31 March 2024.
**Observations & Interviews:** A document review on 14 May 2024 of a randomized sample of 35 personnel files from the current workforce of 1,200 revealed 9 files (approximately 26% of the sample) contained either outdated contracts, contracts lacking mandatory clauses on working hours and grievance procedures, or no signed contract at all. Interviews conducted with 12 workers from the cutting and finishing departments on the same date corroborated this; three workers (Worker IDs #4572, #4981, #5113) stated they had never received a copy of their signed contract, while two others (Worker IDs #4320, #4405) reported their contracts did not reflect their actual wage rate after the recent adjustment. This finding indicates a failure to implement the agreed CAP and represents a regression from previous commitments.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-OT-069-2023-02
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 6)
During the onsite inspection conducted from October 23-26, 2023, a review of time and attendance records for the third quarter of 2023 revealed a persistent pattern of non-compliance with both the legal limits and the buyer's Code of Conduct, specifically concerning excessive overtime. A reconciliation of payroll records with production data for the sample month of September indicated that approximately 65% of the production workforce consistently exceeded the 60-hour workweek limit, with several instances recorded of workweeks extending to 78 hours during peak periods. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews, where 8 out of 12 production operators from Lines 3 and 5 stated they had not voluntarily consented to the excessive overtime and expressed fatigue, noting management pressure to meet targets. This finding represents a repeat violation from the previous CAP and demonstrates a failure to implement effective operational planning to manage workflow. **Severity: Critical**

Factory 070 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Compliance Area: 4.0 Emergency Preparedness (Reference: SA8000/9.2, BSCI PSC 11)**
**Finding:** During the inspection conducted on 12 November 2023, it was observed that while the factory has installed a basic complement of fire extinguishers and illuminated exit signs, the overall emergency preparedness framework is underdeveloped and inconsistently implemented. The emergency evacuation plan, reviewed by the auditor, is a generic template not site-specific to the layout of Factory 070 and is posted only in the management office, not on production floors. Worker interviews with 7 randomly selected production operators on the second floor confirmed that no comprehensive evacuation drill has been conducted since the factory's opening six months ago; their understanding of assembly points was vague and contradictory. Furthermore, measurement of the main aisle on the cutting section floor found it to be 0.8 meters wide, falling below the required 1.1-meter minimum clearance, which constitutes an impediment to egress. The factory management acknowledges these gaps, attributing them to the nascent stage of their operational systems, and has committed to developing a corrective action plan.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-070-04 | **Date of Inspection:** 2023-10-26 | **Area:** Production Floor - Cutting Section

During the walkthrough inspection on the aforementioned date, it was observed that while the factory has provided basic personal protective equipment (PPE), its implementation and control systems are insufficient. Specifically, in the high-risk cutting section, audit sampling of 12 operators revealed that 8 were not utilizing the cut-resistant gloves issued to them, citing discomfort and lack of dexterity. Worker interviews conducted with 5 employees in this department on 26/10/2023 further confirmed a lack of formal training on the specific hazards of the machinery and the correct use of PPE, with one interviewee stating they had "never been shown how to use them properly." This represents a failure in both the enforcement of safe work procedures and the effectiveness of training, exposing workers to a significant and immediate risk of lacerations or amputations. **Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
Audit Finding Ref: DISC-23-070-01 | Compliance Area: Discrimination (including hiring, promotion, compensation, and workplace treatment) | Date of Discovery: October 26, 2023

Background & Observations: During the review of human resources documentation and subsequent worker interviews, it was noted that Factory 070's management team, due to its nascent stage of development, has not yet formulated or disseminated a formal non-discrimination policy. This procedural gap was evident in the observations of departmental segregation on the production floor, where all twelve (12) operators observed on the automated cutting machines were male, while the entire finishing and packaging section, comprising approximately thirty-five (35) workers, was staffed exclusively by female employees. Worker Interviews: Interviews conducted with a sample of 18 workers on October 26, 2023, revealed a consistent narrative; four (4) female workers in the finishing section stated they were not considered for higher-paying machine operator roles upon hiring, with one interviewee (Worker ID# 047) quoting a line supervisor as saying, "this machine work is not suitable for women." Furthermore, the HR manager acknowledged a preference for hiring younger female workers for visual quality inspection roles, citing "better eyesight and patience," which constitutes discriminatory hiring criteria. Follow-up Required: The factory must immediately draft and implement a clear non-discrimination policy, provide training to all hiring and managerial staff, and review current placement practices to ensure equitable opportunity. Severity: Moderate.

Factory 071 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the review of attendance records for the period covering March 1-31, 2024, auditors noted a recurring pattern of excessive overtime in the Cutting and Finishing departments. While the factory maintains a formal policy limiting work to 60 hours per week, a sample check of payroll for 15 randomly selected workers from these departments revealed that 9 individuals exceeded this limit during the peak production week of March 11-17, with one worker recorded for 72 hours. Interviews conducted with 7 workers from these departments confirmed consistent management pressure to meet shipping deadlines, with verbal instructions to under-report hours being alleged by three interviewees (Ref: INT-071-22 to INT-071-24), though this could not be corroborated with physical evidence. This constitutes a breach of both the factory's own documented policy and the client's Code of Conduct regarding working hour limits. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up review of corrective actions, including revised production planning and supervisor training, is required within 90 days.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-071-02**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor (Including Fees and Coercion)**
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

During the review of payroll records and subsequent confidential worker interviews, a procedural inconsistency was identified concerning the management of worker identification documents. While factory policy, as confirmed by the dedicated compliance officer, strictly prohibits the retention of original worker passports or national ID cards, investigator observations on the production floor and in the dormitory areas noted several workers who verbally indicated a perceived obligation to surrender their original documents to line supervisors for "safekeeping" upon hiring, contrary to established protocol. Follow-up interviews with a sample of 17 workers from the stitching department, conducted on October 27, revealed that 5 workers (approximately 29% of the sample) had voluntarily provided their original documents to supervisory staff under the belief it was a mandatory condition for employment, though all confirmed they could request and receive them back without direct penalty; however, this practice creates an environment of potential psychological coercion and represents a deviation from the factory's own documented control system designed to prevent forced labor indicators. The factory management was immediately responsive to this finding, initiating a corrective action plan to re-communicate the policy and conduct a document return verification audit by November 10, 2023.
**Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: SA-071-24-017** | **Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (SA8000 / Client CoC Section 7.1) | **Date of On-site Verification:** October 26, 2024

While the factory maintains a generally professional environment, evidenced by its ISO 37001 certification and a dedicated Compliance Officer, Mr. Chen, interviews conducted with 32 production line workers across three departments on the audit date revealed a persistent pattern of concerning verbal conduct. Specifically, five separate workers in the cutting and sewing sections, under condition of confidentiality, reported that two designated line supervisors frequently use derogatory language and public shaming tactics, such as yelling comments like "you work like a donkey" or "your hands are useless," to criticize work speed or quality, creating an atmosphere of intimidation. This was corroborated during a discreet observation period in Sewing Section B, where an auditor witnessed a supervisor using a loud and demeaning tone while correcting an operator. Although a formal grievance procedure exists, the interviewed workers expressed a unanimous fear of retaliation, with three stating they would not use the hotline for such issues, indicating a breakdown in the effective implementation of the factory's own "Zero Tolerance Harassment" policy. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan is required to address this cultural and supervisory gap, including refresher training for all supervisory staff and a re-communication campaign to reinforce the non-retaliation policy, with follow-up verification to be conducted within 90 days.

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WB-2024-071-03
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Payroll & Recordkeeping)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2024

A review of wage and hour records for the period of September 1-30, 2024, alongside worker interviews conducted on October 26, revealed a recurring discrepancy in the compensation for piece-rate workers exceeding the standard 60-hour workweek. While the factory correctly calculated and paid the statutory overtime premium for hours worked beyond 60, it was identified that the regular rate of pay used for these overtime calculations was based solely on the piece-rate earnings for the specific week in question, rather than being averaged over the entire workweek as required. This methodology, confirmed in an interview with the payroll supervisor, results in a minor underpayment of overtime wages when a worker's productivity (and thus piece-rate earnings) fluctuates significantly from week to week. Interviews with 8 piece-rate workers from the sewing section indicated an awareness of overtime pay but no specific knowledge of the calculation basis. The factory is required to recalculate overtime for the audit period using the correct weighted average method and implement a corrected payroll procedure. **Severity: Minor**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Reference:** EP-2024-071-03
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Fire & General Evacuation)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2024

Following a review of documentation and a physical inspection of the facility, it was noted that the factory maintains a comprehensive written emergency preparedness plan, last updated in June 2024, and demonstrates a strong institutional commitment to safety protocols, as evidenced by its ISO certification and the presence of a dedicated compliance officer. However, during unannounced evacuation drills conducted for both the first shift (08:30 hrs) and the second shift (16:30 hrs) on the date of inspection, observational data and post-drill interviews with 12 randomly selected workers from the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments revealed a significant procedural discrepancy. While the assembly point at the western parking lot is clearly marked and sufficient for the full complement of 1,200 personnel, approximately 30% of interviewed workers from the second-floor sewing module reported utilizing a nearer, unauthorized assembly area in the adjacent loading bay, which is not designated in the plan and would obstruct emergency vehicle access. This indicates a breakdown in communication and drill efficacy for a specific segment of the workforce, despite otherwise robust systems. The compliance officer has been advised to review and reinforce evacuation route training, particularly for the second-floor departments, and to submit a corrective action plan for this inconsistency.
**Severity: Minor**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: OSH-2024-071-03**
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health**
**Date of Inspection: 15 October 2024**

The audit team conducted a review of the factory's occupational safety and health management system, including a physical inspection of production floors A and B, the chemical storage area, and the boiler room, alongside interviews with 12 production workers. While the factory maintains a generally high standard, with clear documentation of its ISO 45001 certification and weekly safety committee meetings noted in the logbook, a notable deviation was identified regarding emergency egress. Specifically, in the western section of cutting room B, a secondary emergency exit door (marked EXIT 4B on the evacuation plan) was found to be partially obstructed. Inspection on the aforementioned date revealed that a mobile rack containing approximately 25 bales of fabric had been positioned within the 1-meter marked clearance zone, reducing the effective clear width of the egress path to 0.7 meters, which is below the 0.9-meter minimum standard as stipulated in section 7.10.2 of the client's Code of Conduct and relevant national fire regulations. During worker interviews, three of five employees assigned to that section stated the rack had been in that location "for a few days" to facilitate ongoing work. This constitutes a failure to maintain unimpeded means of escape at all times. A follow-up note has been logged for the dedicated compliance officer to provide corrective and preventive action evidence, including staff re-training on egress integrity, by the agreed corrective action deadline.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: CHR-071-24-003 | Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources** | **Audit Date(s):** October 23-24, 2024

**Background & Observation:** While the factory maintains a generally robust human resources management system, a procedural inconsistency was identified during the document review of employment contracts for a sample of 42 production workers hired between Q2 and Q4 2024. All contracts were present on file and contained the requisite clauses regarding wage, working hours, and job description. However, the audit team noted that for 17 out of the 42 sample files, the signed contract copy provided to the worker, as verified during interviews conducted on October 24, bore a date of issuance that was an average of 8 calendar days later than the worker's verified first date of employment, constituting a post-facto contracting practice. This was corroborated in separate interviews with 5 of the affected workers, who confirmed they began work without having received the formal contract document, though they acknowledged receiving it subsequently. While management, including the dedicated compliance officer, expressed that this was an administrative oversight during peak hiring periods and no terms were retroactively altered, the failure to provide the legally binding contract prior to the commencement of employment represents a breach of established protocol and local labor law requirements regarding timely provision of employment terms.

**Severity: Minor**

**Follow-up Required:** The compliance officer is to implement and document a verification step within the onboarding checklist to ensure contract signing precedes the first working day, with a full file review to be completed by December 15, 2024.

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: DF-071-24-008**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (including hiring, promotion, remuneration, and access to training)
**Background & Context:** Factory 071 maintains a formal Anti-Discrimination Policy dated January 2023, which is posted in common areas and included in the employee handbook. The policy explicitly prohibits discrimination based on gender, ethnicity, religion, age, and marital or pregnancy status. A review of hiring records from the past twelve months shows a diverse workforce in terms of gender and ethnicity at the operator level.
**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** During interviews conducted on March 14-15, 2024, with 32 workers across various production departments, a pattern emerged regarding advancement opportunities. Four separate workers, all female and with between 5-8 years of tenure at the factory, stated during confidential interviews that they had been passed over for supervisory positions in the cutting and finishing departments in favor of male colleagues with less seniority. When asked, they indicated they were not provided with a clear rationale for these decisions. A review of training records for the advanced machine maintenance program offered in Q4 2023 showed that of the 12 employees selected, 11 were male, despite there being qualified female candidates in the relevant departments.
**Analysis & Follow-up Required:** While no overt discrimination was observed in basic hiring, the evidence suggests a potential systemic bias in promotion and access to advanced skill development, potentially limiting career progression for female employees. This represents a deviation from the factory's own policy and international labor standards. The compliance officer is required to conduct a formal review of the promotion and training nomination criteria and processes, and provide documented evidence of corrective actions, including consideration of the interviewed workers for future openings.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
Audit Scope: Section 3.2 - Child Labor Prevention (Audit Code: CLP-2024-071). During the review of personnel files for 120 production workers on 15 May 2024, a discrepancy was noted in the file for worker ID #3472, whose provided national ID card indicated a date of birth calculating to 17 years and 11 months at the time of hiring on 12 February 2024. While this places the worker above the minimum age of 16 as per local law and brand standards, the factory's own documented procedure (HRM-POL-009, Rev.3) explicitly requires the HR department to conduct a mandatory verification interview and obtain additional secondary documentation for any applicant under 18 years of age prior to hiring. The dedicated compliance officer confirmed that this specific procedural step was not followed for this hire, representing a lapse in the factory's internal due diligence controls designed to mitigate child labor risk. The worker was interviewed separately and their physical appearance and provided educational certificates were consistent with the stated age, confirming no actual underage labor was present. However, the failure to adhere to the documented under-18 verification protocol constitutes a procedural non-conformance requiring corrective action. Severity: Minor.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FAC071-24-FOA-003**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**
**Date of Assessment: 17 October 2023**

While the factory maintains a formally documented policy affirming workers' rights to freedom of association and collective bargaining, and the management demonstrated awareness of these principles during interviews, verification activities identified a procedural shortfall. During a review of onboarding records for 35 workers hired in Q3 2023, it was noted that the mandatory training module on workers' rights, including FOAA provisions, had not been administered to this cohort due to an oversight in scheduling following a peak recruitment period. Furthermore, in confidential interviews conducted on 18 October 2023 with twelve (12) production operators from three departments, eight (8) were unable to correctly identify the location of the factory's elected Worker Representative or the procedure for contacting them, indicating a need for improved communication and awareness. The factory's otherwise strong framework, including the presence of a duly elected representative and no evidence of anti-union discrimination, is noted. However, the failure to ensure all new hires receive essential rights training constitutes a breach of section 7.2 of the applicable Code of Conduct. **Severity: Minor**

Factory 072 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: CL-2023-072-01**

Following the significant disruption caused by the fire incident in the warehouse sector on 15 May 2023, which precipitated a period of operational instability and led to the factory's current status under special monitoring by the local Labor Department, the audit team conducted a focused review of workforce composition. During unannounced floor walks on 17 October 2023, inspectors observed an individual, later identified as "Subject A", performing auxiliary tasks in the finishing section whose physical appearance and demeanor raised immediate concerns regarding potential underage labor. Subsequent interviews with 12 production line workers, conducted privately, yielded conflicting testimonies; while most stated the individual was a family member of a senior cutter, one worker informally suggested the individual was 15 years old and helping temporarily due to post-fire staffing shortages. A review of personnel files for all 87 production staff was requested; however, management could not produce any identity verification or age documentation for Subject A, citing the loss of some records in the fire and the individual's "probationary" status. This failure to maintain and provide verifiable proof of age for every worker constitutes a breach of the fundamental protocols for age verification, notwithstanding the extenuating circumstances of the recent incident.
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: SAC-2023-072-F01 (Contracts & Human Resources)**
During the review of personnel files and worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, it was noted that the factory's ongoing recovery and re-hiring process following the fire incident of August 15 has led to significant procedural gaps in contract administration. Of the 37 personnel files reviewed for workers hired or re-hired post-incident, 29 were found to lack a signed employment contract, constituting a 78% non-compliance rate for the sampled group. Worker interviews with 12 of these individuals confirmed they had undertaken work duties without having received or executed a formal contract, leaving terms of employment, including wage rates and working hours, undocumented and verbally communicated. This practice fails to meet the requirements of local labor law and the Responsible Sourcing Program's benchmark on transparent employment relationships, particularly concerning for a facility under special monitoring. **Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-OT-072-2024-008 | Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Local Labor Law & Client Code of Conduct Section 7.1)**

Following the fire incident on 15 March 2024 and the subsequent production disruption, the audit team reviewed working hour records for the recovery period from 1 April to 30 April 2024. While management stated that all overtime was voluntary and compensated at the correct premium to meet urgent orders, inspection of April 2024 punch cards and payroll records for a sample of 47 production line workers revealed a systemic pattern of excessive hours. Specifically, 34 workers were found to have exceeded the maximum 60-hour workweek as stipulated by local law on three consecutive weeks, with several individual workweeks recorded at 72 hours. Worker interviews conducted on 28 April with 12 employees from the stitching section corroborated this, with interviewees noting management pressure to accept all overtime shifts to "help the factory recover," though they confirmed payment was received. This constitutes a violation of both legal limits and the client's Code of Conduct. Given the repetitive and willful nature of the breach during a period of known heightened scrutiny, the finding is elevated. **Severity: Critical** A corrective action plan must address systemic scheduling controls and management pressure. Follow-up verification will be required within 30 days.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: OSH-072-24**
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health**
**Date of Inspection: 14 October 2024**

Following the fire incident of August 2024, which resulted in the factory being placed under special monitoring, this audit focused heavily on the reinstatement of core fire safety and emergency protocols. During a walkthrough of the main production floor on the aforementioned date, auditors observed that while primary emergency exits were unobstructed, the designated secondary escape route in the western cutting section remained officially demarcated but was partially blocked by three bales of incoming fabric awaiting processing, reducing the clear egress width to approximately 0.7 meters, which falls below the mandated minimum. Interviews conducted with 7 production workers from that section revealed inconsistent understanding of revised evacuation assembly points, with four workers referencing the old pre-fire muster location. Although management has demonstrated a proactive attitude in restoring major systems, this combination of a physical impediment to a defined escape route and inadequate retraining on updated procedures constitutes a significant failure in maintaining assured means of egress. The local authority's monitoring directive explicitly requires unimpeded evacuation routes and documented worker training on post-incident plans.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: 3.2 Harassment & Abuse (Audit Code: SA-8000, Element 4.2)**
**Finding Reference: FA-072-24-087**

Following the fire incident of 15 March 2024 and the subsequent period of operational recovery and special monitoring by local authorities, particular attention was paid to management-worker interactions and the overall psychosocial environment. During unannounced floor walks conducted on 23-24 October 2024, auditors observed numerous instances of supervisory staff, particularly on Line 7 (sewing section), using derogatory language and shouting at operators to increase productivity amidst heightened pressure to meet backlogged orders. In confidential interviews held with 27 production workers, 12 individuals (approximately 44% of those interviewed) independently reported a pattern of verbal intimidation and humiliation by two identified line supervisors, citing examples where workers were threatened with dismissal or assignment to undesirable tasks for minor errors. Management confirmed awareness of "heightened tensions" but had not implemented any targeted training or refresher sessions on the company's Policy on Respect in the Workplace since the resumption of full operations. While the post-disaster context is acknowledged, the failure to maintain effective controls to prevent abusive behavior constitutes a breakdown in established management systems.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-072-2023-08-D01**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SA8000 / Section 7, Buyer Code of Conduct Section 5.4)
**Date of Assessment:** October 26-27, 2023
**Context:** Following the fire incident of August 2023, which resulted in a partial shutdown of the sewing section, the factory management has been engaged in a phased re-hiring and retraining process under the oversight of local labor authorities. The audit focus included a review of hiring practices and workforce composition during this recovery period. Inspector observations during the facility tour and document review of the 127 new hires post-incident indicated a pronounced demographic shift towards male workers in the sewing department, which historically had been predominantly female. Subsequent confidential interviews with 19 workers, including 8 from the new hire cohort, revealed a consistent narrative from 7 female interviewees that hiring supervisors had made verbal inquiries regarding marital status, pregnancy plans, and family obligations during recruitment, with several reporting statements to the effect of "we need workers who can focus on recovery and not take leave." Management representatives, when questioned, cited the physical demands of the recovery environment and initial cleanup as justification for a perceived need for "more resilient labor," an explanation not supported by documented risk assessments or the actual light-duty nature of the retraining work observed. This represents a clear deviation from established policies prohibiting discrimination in hiring.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-072-04**  
**Compliance Area: Forced Labour (ILO Core Convention 29)**  
**Date of Assessment: 17 October 2023**  

Following the fire incident on 15 August 2023, which resulted in significant damage to Dormitory Block B, the audit team reviewed current living and working arrangements for the 127 affected production line workers. During physical inspection on 17 October, it was observed that management had instituted a mandatory overtime policy, verbally communicated to line supervisors, requiring all displaced workers to work seven-day weeks until production backlog is cleared, with non-compliance resulting in deduction of the temporary housing allowance provided post-fire. Interviews conducted with 23 workers from the affected group confirmed universal awareness of this policy, with several stating they feared dismissal and loss of accommodation if they refused weekend work. This policy, which was not documented in any amended workplace agreement, constitutes a severe restriction of workers’ freedom to terminate employment or decline overtime, particularly given their vulnerable circumstances following the disaster. Management acknowledged the policy was in place but cited "recovery necessities" and pressure from buyers to meet delayed shipment deadlines.  

**Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
**Finding Reference: WB-072-2024-01**

Following the fire incident on March 15, 2024, and the subsequent partial operational shutdown, the audit focused on payroll records from the recovery period of April 2024. A review of time cards and wage slips for 37 production workers, cross-referenced with workshop attendance logs, revealed a systematic discrepancy. It was observed that for the pay period April 8-21, all workers engaged in cleaning and salvage operations on the damaged 'C' wing were paid a flat daily rate of 95 RMB, irrespective of hours worked. Interviews with five affected workers confirmed they routinely worked 11-hour days during this two-week period; however, the flat rate paid falls significantly below the legal minimum wage calculation for the hours documented, and no overtime premiums were applied for hours worked beyond the standard 8-hour day, constituting a dual violation of local minimum wage and overtime regulations. This practice appears to have been implemented as an ad-hoc measure for non-standard tasks post-incident but was not formally documented or communicated as a temporary variance.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-072-24-008**  
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**  
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2024**  

Following the fire incident of August 2024 and pursuant to the factory's current status under special monitoring by local authorities, this assessment focused on reviewing the facility's adherence to established worker representation protocols. During document review and interviews conducted with 17 production workers on October 26, it was confirmed that the previously recognized workers' committee was formally dissolved by management in the immediate aftermath of the fire, citing restructuring needs, and has not been reconstituted. Management acknowledged this dissolution but stated intent to hold new elections "once operations normalize," failing to provide a written timeline or plan for reinstatement, thereby effectively suspending collective bargaining mechanisms for over two months. Worker interviews further revealed apprehension among several employees regarding potential repercussions for raising group grievances, with three interviewees expressing specific concern that management now requires all complaints to be channeled exclusively through the newly appointed (and management-selected) post-fire safety liaison, effectively bypassing any independent worker representation. This constitutes a failure to maintain previously established industrial relations frameworks without demonstrable cause or a lawful alternative.  
**Severity: Moderate**
