Factory 073 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-073-24-008**
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observation:** During the document review for the Contracts & Human Resources section, the audit team acknowledges the factory's generally robust HR management system, evidenced by its ISO certification and the presence of a dedicated compliance officer. A sample of 37 personnel files was selected for examination against the requirement that all workers possess a signed, individualized employment contract stipulating terms and conditions prior to commencement of work. While 35 files were found to be in full order, the review identified a discrepancy concerning two (2) maintenance technicians hired on September 15, 2023. Their files contained only a signed offer letter and the company's standard policy acknowledgment forms; the formal, detailed employment contract, though dated correctly, was not signed by the workers until October 5, 2023, a period of 20 calendar days after their start date. Interviews conducted with the two affected workers (Worker IDs #4127 and #4128) confirmed they began working with the understanding of the terms but acknowledged they did not physically sign the full contract until the date noted, aligning with the document trail. This represents a procedural failure in the onboarding process, as the factory's own policy and international standards require the contract to be executed prior to work beginning, regardless of the workers' subsequent ratification. The compliance officer attributed this to an administrative oversight during a period of high recruitment volume.

**Follow-up Note:** Management has been instructed to review the onboarding checklist for all hires within the last quarter to preclude any similar anomalies. **Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: HRA-073-2023-02**
**Compliance Area: Harassment & Abuse (Code: HR.04)**
**Audit Date(s):** October 24-26, 2023

While the factory maintains a robust formal policy framework against harassment, including a dedicated grievance committee chaired by the compliance officer, verification activities identified a procedural gap in the consistent application and awareness of these mechanisms on the production floor. During worker interviews conducted on October 25th with 14 randomly selected operators from the sewing and finishing sections, three individuals from different lines reported instances of verbal abuse by a single line supervisor, including the use of derogatory language and public humiliation for missing production targets, which were not formally reported due to fear of reprisal and a lack of confidence in the anonymity of the process. Although the documented procedure is compliant, the failure to ensure its effective implementation and to foster an environment where workers feel secure in utilizing grievance channels constitutes a breakdown in management systems. The factory management was immediately notified and has committed to re-training for all supervisory staff and a re-communication campaign of the grievance protocol.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Ref: OSH-073-24-009**
During the on-site inspection conducted on 18-20 March 2024, the audit team, while acknowledging the factory's robust overall compliance framework and positive historical performance, identified a recurring procedural lapse within the occupational safety and health management system. Specifically, the review of the chemical inventory and Safety Data Sheet (SDS) registry, alongside interviews with 7 workers from the wet processing section, revealed that the mandatory monthly safety refresher training for chemical handlers, though documented as completed, was not substantiated by consistent attendance records or practical competency checks for the period of January-February 2024. This was corroborated by the finding that two workers, when interviewed, could not accurately describe the emergency eyewash procedure for the specific acids they handle, despite training logs indicating their participation. While the factory's dedicated compliance officer promptly provided the curriculum and schedules, this discrepancy between documented procedure and effective implementation represents a breakdown in the training control system, warranting a formal finding. **Severity: Minor**

Factory 074 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Reference:** Q3-24/SA-074-OSH-02
**Area:** Occupational Safety & Health - Machine Guarding and Emergency Preparedness
**Date of Inspection:** 17 October 2023
**Background & Observations:** During the walk-through of the cutting and sewing sections, the audit team noted a significant inconsistency in the application of machine safety procedures, which appears correlated with the ongoing management transition. Specifically, seven (7) of the thirty-two (32) industrial sewing machines observed in Section B had their factory-installed needle guards either removed or deliberately tied back, exposing operators to direct puncture hazards. Furthermore, in worker interviews conducted on 18 October 2023 with a sample of 14 production line employees, 9 confirmed that the removal of guards was sometimes encouraged by former line supervisors to expedite work on thicker materials, a practice that has not been formally rescinded under the new management. This was compounded by the finding that the emergency stop buttons on three of these machines were non-functional, and the most recent documented emergency drill was logged over eight months ago, prior to the ownership change.
**Follow-up Requirement:** The new management must immediately issue a written directive reinstating mandatory guard use, implement a corrective action plan for machine maintenance, and schedule a full facility emergency drill by a specified date, with records to be verified in the next follow-up audit.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Reference:** FA-074-2023-08-14 / FO-02
**Date of Inspection:** 14 August 2023
**Background & Scope:** The inspection included a review of policies, interviews with 17 workers selected from various production departments (cutting, sewing, finishing), and a meeting with the newly appointed Human Resources Manager, Mr. Chen. The factory is under a transition of ownership as of June 2023, with new management implementing revised operational procedures.

**Finding:** While the factory's posted policies state compliance with local laws on freedom of association, evidence suggests these rights are not effectively communicated or practiced. During separate, confidential interviews, 12 of the 17 workers expressed uncertainty about the legitimacy of forming or joining a worker representative committee, citing caution due to "new management's focus on efficiency." No collective bargaining agreement was presented for review, and Mr. Chen acknowledged that, following the ownership change, the previous worker committee was dissolved and has not been re-established, with no concrete timeline provided for new elections. The absence of a functioning mechanism for collective worker representation, coupled with a climate of apprehension among the workforce regarding association rights, constitutes a failure to ensure effective implementation of the company's stated commitments.

**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Required:** The factory must develop and communicate a clear, time-bound action plan for the democratic election of a worker representative body, with evidence of worker awareness training to be verified at the next follow-up audit.

Factory 075 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2023-075-04
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Section 7.1, Machine Guarding & Section 8.3, Emergency Egress)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** The audit team notes the ongoing management transition following the recent change in ownership. During the facility walk-through, focusing on the cutting and sewing sections, inspectors observed multiple instances where operational safety protocols appear to have lapsed, potentially as a result of this transitional period. Of particular concern was the status of machine guarding on the 12 industrial sewing stations in Section B, where guards had been removed and were not in place during active operation, a condition confirmed through worker interviews.
**Observations & Worker Testimony:** On the aforementioned date, auditors physically verified that 10 out of 12 examined sewing machines (Make: Juki, Model DLN-5410N) had their needle point guards either detached and stored nearby or entirely missing. Concurrently, in the western wing of the production floor, a secondary emergency exit (marked Exit C) was found to be partially obstructed by a pallet of WIP (work-in-progress) garments, estimated at 1.5 meters in width, reducing the clear egress width to approximately 0.4 meters, which is non-compliant with the minimum 1.1-meter requirement. Interviews conducted with 7 operators in the area revealed a common understanding that guards were removed "for ease of working on bulky seams" and that the blockage at Exit C had been present "for a few days" due to temporary storage needs.
**Analysis & Follow-up Requirement:** These conditions represent a failure to maintain effective controls against foreseeable physical hazards (needle strike injuries) and to ensure unimpeded means of escape in the event of an emergency. The factory management must provide documented evidence of corrected actions, including re-installation and verification of all machine guards and the implementation of a procedural control to prevent the obstruction of fire exits. **Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-03-2024-075**

During the onsite inspection conducted on April 17-18, 2024, a review of time and attendance records for the period of January to March 2024 revealed systemic non-compliance with local legal limits and client Code of Conduct standards regarding working hours and overtime. Inspector observations verified through payroll records for a sample of 53 production line workers that the average weekly hours consistently exceeded 60 hours across the reviewed period, with numerous instances of workers exceeding the maximum allowable 36 hours of overtime per month. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews, where 8 out of 12 interviewed employees stated they had worked seven consecutive days without a rest day on multiple occasions during the past quarter, a practice attributed to urgent order fulfillment during the management transition. While the new ownership has drafted a revised overtime policy, it has not yet been fully implemented or communicated, and current practices pose a significant risk to worker welfare. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan must address both the historical violations and the procedural gap to ensure future compliance.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref.: CHR-2403-075-02** Following the recent change in ownership effective 01 February 2024 and the ongoing transition of the human resources department, a review of employment contracts and personnel files was conducted. During the document review on 15 March 2024, it was observed that 47 of 58 personnel files (approximately 81%) selected for a randomized sample lacked a signed, legally compliant employment contract, containing only a basic, non-detailed onboarding form. Subsequently, interviews with 12 production workers from various departments confirmed that they had not received a copy of any formal contract since the ownership transition and were unclear on essential terms including probation duration, overtime wage calculation rates, and termination procedures, indicating a systemic documentation failure. This constitutes a breach of local labor law Article 24(1) and the requirement for clear communication of employment terms, necessitating immediate rectification. **Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-075-02**  
During the onsite assessment conducted on October 26, 2023, the audit team reviewed policies and conducted interviews with a sample of 32 production line workers, focusing on indicators of forced labor amid the factory’s transition in ownership and management. It was observed that the factory retains possession of workers' original identity documents and national passports under a "document safekeeping" policy, which was verbally confirmed by three out of four line supervisors interviewed. Worker interviews, particularly with eight migrant workers in the sewing section, revealed that this retention is presented as a condition of employment, with one worker noting, "We cannot get our passports back unless we give one month’s notice and have a manager’s approval." This practice restricts workers’ freedom of movement and ability to terminate employment at will, constituting a violation of the Forced Labor convention of the ILO. A follow-up review of the newly implemented employee handbook, version 2.1, dated September 2023, showed no written policy authorizing such retention, indicating a discrepancy between implemented procedures and documented management systems.  
**Severity: Moderate**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref: S.A.075-24-HA-003**
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (HA)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

During the evaluation of policies and procedures related to Harassment & Abuse, it was noted that while the previous ownership had a documented policy in place, the new management has not formally re-issued or communicated an updated policy to the workforce since the ownership transition was finalized in August 2023. Interviews conducted with a cross-section of 17 production line operators on October 26 revealed a consistent lack of awareness of current reporting channels or investigative procedures; three separate workers in the cutting department verbally indicated uncertainty about whether the old "complaints box" was still monitored, with one worker stating, "We heard the managers changed, so we don't know who to tell now." This absence of effectively communicated safeguards, during a period of management transition, creates an environment where workers are not assured of a reliable mechanism to report grievances without fear of reprisal. Furthermore, review of training records showed no anti-harassment training sessions have been conducted for any employee under the new ownership. The factory is therefore in violation of the requirement to maintain and communicate clear procedures prohibiting harassment and abuse.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-075-24-008 Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Section 9.0 of CoC)**
During the onsite inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, it was noted in review of factory documentation and through subsequent worker interviews that, following the change in ownership finalized in January 2024, the newly installed management has not yet conducted any informational sessions or posted any communications regarding workers' rights to freedom of association and collective bargaining, a requirement under the framework of our client's Code of Conduct. Interviews with a cross-section of 28 production workers from various departments revealed a pervasive lack of awareness, with 24 workers stating they were unsure if a worker representative structure existed or how one might be formed under the new ownership, and several expressing apprehension about inquiring directly with HR due to the ongoing transitional instability. While no active obstruction or discrimination was evidenced, the failure to proactively communicate these fundamental rights during a period of significant operational change constitutes a failure to ensure and promote an environment where these rights are understood and can be freely exercised. **Severity: Moderate** A corrective action plan must include a timeline for the development and dissemination of a clear policy, along with the implementation of management training and worker awareness sessions to be verified at follow-up.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: FAC-075-EP-2024-003 | Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness | Date of Inspection: October 26, 2024**

While reviewing the factory's emergency preparedness protocols, it was noted that the management transition, finalized in August 2024, has resulted in a significant procedural lapse regarding the documented evacuation plan. Inspector observations on the date of the audit confirmed that the posted evacuation floor plans in the cutting and sewing sections on the second floor (Zones B and C) do not reflect the current layout following the reorganization of production lines in September. Furthermore, interviews conducted with a sample of 17 workers across two shifts revealed that 12 were unaware of any designated assembly point for their work area, and no full-site evacuation drill has been conducted since the change in ownership. The absence of an updated, communicated, and practiced plan constitutes a failure to ensure all personnel are adequately informed of emergency procedures, as required. A follow-up note has been issued to the new management team to submit a corrected emergency action plan and schedule for drills within 14 calendar days. **Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding: FA-075-24-009**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Category C.4 – Equal Opportunity)
**Background & Context:** During the follow-up audit conducted on 17-18 October 2023, particular attention was paid to policies and practices related to equal opportunity and non-discrimination, given the recent change in ownership and the transitional management phase noted in the factory profile. The audit scope included a review of updated Human Resources policies, hiring and promotion records from the last quarter, and interviews with a cross-section of the workforce.
**Observations & Evidence:** While the factory has drafted a new Code of Conduct incorporating a non-discrimination clause, this document had not been officially ratified or disseminated to the workforce at the time of the audit. Review of hiring records for September and October 2023 revealed a pattern wherein all eight (8) newly hired supervisory positions were filled by male candidates from the same ethnic background as the new ownership group. Worker interviews, conducted privately with 42 employees across various departments, yielded four consistent reports from female workers in the cutting section that they were explicitly told by a line supervisor that supervisory roles were "not suitable for women" during an informal inquiry about advancement opportunities. This statement was not contested by management when presented as an anonymous summary during the closing meeting.
**Conclusion:** The evidence indicates that discriminatory practices, particularly based on gender and potentially on ethnic background, are occurring in hiring and promotion, contravening international standards and the factory's own draft policies. The transitional management structure has failed to implement adequate controls to prevent such practices.
**Follow-up Required:** Management must immediately issue and communicate the ratified Code of Conduct, implement mandatory training for all supervisory staff on non-discrimination, and establish a transparent, documented procedure for internal job postings and promotions, to be verified at the next audit.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Finding Ref: 075-WB-03**
**Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits**
**Date of Discovery: October 26, 2023**

During the review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2023, and subsequent confidential worker interviews conducted on October 26 with 17 production line operators, a discrepancy was identified between the officially posted provincial minimum wage and the remuneration actually received. The audit team observed that while the factory's wage policy documentation, revised following the ownership transition in July 2023, references the correct statutory rate, the practical application for 43 out of 187 sampled workers fell short. Specifically, interview notes from Worker #12 (and corroborated by pay stubs provided) indicated that their hourly rate for regular hours was set at the previous year's lower minimum wage, resulting in a shortfall of approximately 1.5 RMB per hour for the entire month. This systemic underpayment, occurring during a period of management transition, constitutes a failure to compensate workers for all hours worked at the legally mandated rate. The human resources coordinator acknowledged the anomaly, attributing it to an oversight in updating the base rate parameters within the new payroll software system. **Severity: Critical**

[Child Labor]
During the document review conducted on 17 October 2023, it was noted that personnel files for approximately 30 production workers, hired in the period immediately following the ownership transition in August 2023, were incomplete, lacking notarized age verification or copies of government-issued identification. Subsequent physical inspection of the cutting and bundling sections on the same date revealed the presence of two individuals, subsequently identified as "Worker #A-227" and "Worker #B-228," whose appearance and demeanor suggested they may be below the legal working age. In confidential interviews conducted with seven workers from the same production line, two individuals separately indicated that the two workers in question were siblings assisting their guardian to meet production quotas, though no direct confirmation of age was obtained from the subjects themselves. The management representative, when confronted, was unable to produce immediate validating documentation for these two individuals, constituting a failure to effectively monitor and prevent potential child labor risks during the management transition period. **Severity: Moderate.** Follow-up action requires the immediate provision of valid age certificates for the cited workers and a full review of all hiring protocols under the new ownership.

Factory 076 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA8000-7.1 / Disc-076-2023-04
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SA8000:2014 Section 7)
**Date of On-Site Inspection:** October 26-27, 2023

During the review of Human Resources policies and procedures, the audit team noted the factory’s formal written policies conform to the requirements of SA8000, explicitly prohibiting discrimination in hiring, remuneration, access to training, promotion, termination, or retirement based on race, caste, national origin, religion, disability, gender, sexual orientation, union membership, or political affiliation. Interviews conducted with a cross-section of 32 workers from various production lines and departments, including 15 members of the elected workers’ welfare committee, revealed a consistent understanding of these policies and a high degree of confidence in the established grievance mechanisms, with several workers voluntarily citing specific, resolved instances where concerns were addressed without retaliation. This was corroborated by a review of the last 12 months of grievance logs (Ref: GML-076-Q3-2022 to Q3-2023), which showed 17 logged cases related to interpersonal disputes, none of which were categorized or investigated as systemic discrimination, and follow-up interviews indicated satisfactory resolutions. The presence of a democratic and active union, coupled with the observed worker-management dialogue during the inspection period, provides a robust structural framework that actively prevents discriminatory practices.
**Status: Compliant**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: C-HR-076-024**
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources (C.1.2 - Employment Contract Provision & Comprehensibility)
**Date of On-Site Verification:** October 26, 2023

A review of personnel files for a statistically representative sample of 32 workers, selected across cutting, sewing, and finishing departments, was conducted. While all files contained signed employment contracts as required, a follow-up interview process with 15 of these workers, conducted in private on October 27, revealed a discrepancy. Seven (7) interviewed operators, all hired within the last six months, stated they had not been provided with a copy of their signed contract for personal retention at the time of hiring, receiving only an oral summary of key terms from the HR officer. This represents a failure to fully comply with the requirement that workers be provided with a copy of the legally binding document they execute. The factory's otherwise strong grievance mechanism and documented positive labor relations mitigate the pervasiveness of this issue, as all workers confirmed they understood their core terms of employment and felt able to raise any concerns through existing channels.
**Severity: Minor**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SAF-2023-076-04
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

During the walkthrough of the cutting and sewing sections on Level 2, the audit team observed generally good housekeeping standards and adequate provision of personal protective equipment. However, a review of the factory's annual OSH training records for the period January-October 2023, cross-referenced with the current payroll, indicated that 37 of 124 production operatives (approximately 30%) hired in the last eight months had not received the mandatory basic occupational safety and health induction training, as required by section 4.2 of the Buyer's Code of Conduct and local regulatory stipulations. Interviews conducted with a randomized selection of eight workers from this cohort confirmed this gap, with five workers stating they had only received a brief verbal overview from their line supervisor upon hiring. While the factory's strong grievance mechanism and positive industrial relations climate mitigate immediate risk, this systematic lapse in procedural compliance represents a failure to ensure uniform understanding of fundamental safety protocols, such as emergency evacuation routes and machine-specific hazards. The management representative acknowledged the administrative oversight, attributing it to peak seasonal hiring pressures, and has committed to scheduling make-up sessions by November 30, 2023.
**Severity: Minor**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-076-2024-003 | Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (ILO Convention 29) | Date of Inspection: 2024-10-23**

Following a review of policies and procedures, direct observation of production floors and dormitories, and confidential interviews conducted with 37 workers selected across departments and shifts on October 23-24, 2024, the audit team found no evidence of systematic forced labor practices. The established and active Workers' Union, functioning under a Collective Bargaining Agreement (CBA ID: CBA-076-2023), provides a robust institutional safeguard. Furthermore, the documented grievance mechanism (Logbook Ref: GRV-LB-076), reviewed for entries from Q3 2023 to Q3 2024, shows effective resolution of complaints related to contract clarity and wage payments, with no cases alleging confinement or debt bondage. Worker interviews uniformly reflected an understanding of free employment and the right to terminate employment with notice, as per contract stipulations. While the retention of worker identity documents is strictly prohibited by policy, a follow-up note is required to verify the quarterly reconciliation process for the document storage facility (DSF-076) to ensure ongoing compliance. **Status: Compliant.**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-076-24-008 | Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Code: C-09)**

A review of the factory’s industrial relations profile, supported by interviews conducted on 12 March 2024 with eight (8) randomly selected union members and three (3) executive committee members, confirmed the presence of an active and recognized union, the Textile and Garment Workers Federation (TGWF), Local Chapter 076. Inspection of the dedicated union office (Room 102-B) and notice boards revealed unimpeded access for union activities and the public posting of collective bargaining agreements (CBAs) and meeting minutes. Worker interviews unanimously indicated a functional and non-retaliatory grievance mechanism, with several instances cited where management had constructively addressed union-mediated complaints regarding production targets and overtime scheduling. The current three-year CBA, effective until June 2025, was presented for review and its provisions on wages, benefits, and dispute resolution were found to be fully implemented, with no discrepancies noted in payroll records for the sampled period. The relationship between worker representatives and management is assessed as cooperative, with evidence of regular dialogue and no indications of interference or discrimination.

**Status: Compliant.**

Factory 077 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: OSH-2024-077-03**

A review of the factory's incident log and subsequent inspection of the primary cutting and sewing areas on 16 April 2024 revealed a procedural inconsistency regarding machine guarding. While the factory maintains an otherwise commendable safety program with clear documented procedures (Ref. SOP-OSH-07), direct observation confirmed that safety guards on seven (7) industrial sewing machines in Section B were not correctly reinstalled following preventative maintenance conducted the previous week, as verified against the maintenance work order log MWL-0410. Interviews with three (3) operators assigned to those machines indicated they had reported the missing guards to their line supervisor but continued to operate the equipment under production pressure. This constitutes a failure to ensure engineering controls are maintained in an effective state, creating a lacunae between policy and practice that exposes workers to laceration and entanglement hazards. The finding is classified as Moderate due to the immediate remediable nature of the issue, the factory's generally robust systems, and the absence of any prior incidents linked to this cause.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Reference: HR-2023-077-04 (Contracts & Human Resources)**
**Area of Review:** Contractual documentation, employment file completeness, and policy communication.
**Background & Context:** The factory maintains a well-organized central personnel registry, and a sample of 30 active employee files was reviewed against the standard requirement checklist. While all files contained the mandatory national social insurance enrollment forms and identity documentation, a discrepancy was noted in the issuance of formal, individualized employment contracts.

**Inspector Observations:** A review of personnel records for workers hired between January and March 2023 (10 files selected) revealed that, although a standardized template contract exists and is approved, 7 out of the 10 workers did not have a signed, worker-retained copy within their file; only a company-signed master copy was present on record. The dedicated compliance officer confirmed the company's practice is to provide the contract during orientation, but retrieval of the worker-signed copy for filing is sometimes delayed.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted on 2023-10-26 with 15 randomly selected production line workers, including 3 from the aforementioned hiring cohort, indicated general awareness of key terms such as wage rates and working hours via the posted collective bargaining agreement. However, 4 of the 15 interviewed could not confirm possession of their own personal copy of a signed employment contract, stating they had signed "onboarding papers" but were not given a duplicate for their records.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** This constitutes a procedural failure in ensuring each worker receives and retains executed copies of their core employment terms as per international social accountability protocols, despite the underlying terms themselves being largely compliant. The factory management is required to implement and document a corrective action plan to ensure 100% issuance and filing of worker-acknowledged contracts.
**Severity: Minor**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-077-2024-014**
**Compliance Area: Discrimination (CC.7.1)**
**Date of Assessment: October 23-24, 2024**

While the factory maintains a formal non-discrimination policy and demonstrates a generally sound historical record in this area, a review of hiring and promotion records for the period Q1-Q3 2024, coupled with confidential worker interviews, revealed a pattern requiring corrective action. The audit team, having interviewed 32 workers across 5 departments, noted during three separate interviews with sewing operatives in the finishing section that all supervisory positions (6 out of 6) within that unit are held by individuals from the same regional and linguistic background as the factory's senior management, despite a diverse workforce where this group comprises approximately 40% of the departmental staff. The interviewed workers expressed a perception, documented in interview notes WI-077-24 to WI-077-29, that advancement opportunities are informally limited by these familial and regional affiliations rather than solely by merit, creating a potential barrier to equal opportunity. Although no overt harassment or discriminatory termination was evidenced, this circumstantial pattern and worker perception indicate a failure to fully implement and monitor the effectiveness of the established non-discrimination policy at the supervisory level, necessitating a review of promotion criteria and manager training.
**Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Reference:** SA-077-2023-04, **Compliance Area:** 3.2 Harassment & Abuse, **Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023.

While the factory maintains a generally professional environment and has a clearly communicated zero-tolerance policy against harassment, worker interviews conducted on the date of inspection revealed a procedural gap in the implementation of the grievance mechanism. Of the 37 production workers interviewed across three departments, 5 separate individuals (approximately 13.5%) cited hesitancy to report verbal harassment or intimidation from line supervisors, primarily due to a perceived lack of confidentiality and fear of retaliation, despite the presence of a dedicated compliance officer. Specifically, these workers expressed concern that reports made through the posted hotline or suggestion box are not sufficiently anonymized before being reviewed by management, potentially exposing the complainant. It was corroborated that no formal awareness sessions on the anti-harassment policy and protected reporting procedures have been conducted for the workforce in the preceding 12 months, a finding which constitutes a failure in the factory's duty to ensure effective worker access to remedy. **Severity: Moderate.**


============================================================
Factory 078 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Compliance Area:** 2.4 – Non-Discrimination & Equal Opportunity (Ref. SA8000:2014 Sec. 5; Buyer CoC Sec. 8.3)

**Finding Details:** During the document review conducted on 12 March 2024, it was noted that Factory 078 operates with a highly seasonal workforce model, with a peak headcount of approximately 1,200 workers during the Q3 production cycle, dropping to an estimated 450 workers in off-peak periods. Subsequent interviews with 17 production line workers from various departments on 13 March 2024 revealed a consistent pattern of preferential assignment. Multiple workers, specifically four individuals from the finishing department, reported during confidential interviews that supervisory staff routinely assigned simpler, higher-volume piece-rate tasks (e.g., basic trimming) to workers perceived as being from the local regional majority group, while more complex, lower-yield tasks were assigned to migrant workers from a specific geographical minority province. This practice was unofficially confirmed in a follow-up discussion with a line supervisor, who cited "efficiency and ease of communication" as justification. The available payroll records for the last peak season did not show overt wage discrimination, but the described assignment system effectively creates a discriminatory barrier to equal remuneration opportunity based on national origin, which is further exacerbated by the factory's high-pressure production environment. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: A-078-CON/HR-004**
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources**
**Date of On-site Verification: October 26, 2023**

A review of human resources documentation and worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, revealed systemic non-conformities regarding the implementation of legally binding employment contracts. While the factory maintains a template contract that meets basic legal stipulations, the audit team, through a physical document check of 37 personnel files selected from a seasonal workforce of approximately 220, observed that only 12 files contained a signed contract. Subsequent interviews with 28 production line workers, including 19 hired within the last three months for peak season orders, confirmed this discrepancy; 22 interviewed workers stated they had never been provided with a written contract, relying instead on verbal agreements concerning their wages and tenure. This practice fails to ensure transparency of employment terms for a transient workforce and exposes both the workers and the factory to significant risk regarding the enforcement of terms and conditions of employment, particularly for dispute resolution. The management representative attributed this lapse to the high turnover and administrative pressure during peak production periods, but this does not constitute a valid justification for non-compliance. **Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SAF-2023-078-04  
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)  
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023  

During the walk-through of the cutting and sewing sections on the third floor, the audit team observed a persistent failure to maintain clear and unobstructed egress pathways, a condition exacerbated by the factory's current peak-season production volume and the documented influx of temporary workers. Specifically, primary aisles leading to the designated fire exits (EX-03A and EX-03B) were partially blocked by stored bundles of cut work-in-progress (WIP) and unused rolling racks, reducing the effective width to approximately 0.6 meters at points, which is below the 1.1-meter minimum required by the facility's own emergency action plan and local fire code. Interviews conducted with eight production workers from the affected section on October 27 confirmed that this congestion is a daily occurrence, especially in the afternoons when production targets intensify, with several workers expressing concern about a potential emergency evacuation. This finding represents a systemic failure in housekeeping and safety management under high-pressure operational conditions, requiring immediate corrective action to mitigate the risk of injury and to ensure compliance with fundamental emergency egress standards. **Severity: Moderate.**


============================================================
Factory 079 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: medium-sized factory with 200-400 workers, recently changed ownership, transitioning management
============================================================

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-03-079/24**
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Observations:** The audit team reviewed personnel files and employment contracts for a sample of 37 production workers hired during the post-transition period from July to September 2023. It was noted that Factory 079, under its new ownership, has been actively recruiting to fill vacancies; however, the documentation process has not been fully aligned with the Buyer's Code of Conduct and local legal requirements. A discrepancy was identified wherein the standard employment contracts provided by the new management, while containing the essential terms of employment such as wage rate and working hours, omitted two mandatory clauses: a clear provision stipulating normal and overtime wage calculation and a statement expressly forbidding the charging of recruitment fees to the worker.

**Worker Interviews & Evidence:** During confidential interviews conducted on October 27, 2023, with 14 of the newly hired workers (specifically from the cutting and sewing departments), 9 confirmed they had signed a contract but had not received a copy for their personal records. Furthermore, 3 interviewed workers stated they were required to pay a "processing fee" of approximately 200 RMB to the factory's outsourced hiring agent to secure their positions, a practice which constitutes a bonded labor violation. The signed contracts on file for these individuals do not contain any information regarding this fee, nor do they include the legally mandated wage calculation annex.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Note:** The failure to provide workers with a signed copy of their contract and the omission of legally required clauses in the contractual document represents a systemic failure in HR onboarding procedures during the management transition. More critically, the evidence of recruitment fees being charged, albeit by a third-party agent affiliated with the factory's hiring process, represents a fundamental breach of ethical employment standards. The factory management was immediately informed of these findings and must undertake corrective action, including contract remediation and reimbursement of fees, prior to the deadline for the Corrective Action Plan. **Severity: Critical**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-079-03-2024**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1)**
**Background & Context:** Following the recent change in ownership noted in the opening conference, the audit team focused on the transitional period of the last three payroll cycles to assess adherence to working hour standards. Management provided time records for March 2024, which indicated potential irregularities prompting further investigation through worker interviews conducted on 2024-03-28.
**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** A review of March 2024 timecards for a sample of 32 production line workers revealed consistent clock-in times of 07:45 and clock-out times recorded as 20:00, Monday through Saturday, suggesting a 72.5-hour workweek. Interviews with 14 workers from the cutting and sewing departments confirmed this schedule was standard under production pressures to fulfill new orders. Worker testimony further indicated that while overtime premium was paid for hours beyond the regular 48, the excessive hours were effectively mandatory, with refusal resulting in negative performance assessments. This pattern constitutes a systemic failure to manage working hours and violates both the legal maximum and the requirement for voluntary overtime.
**Follow-Up Note:** The newly installed HR manager acknowledged awareness of the issue but cited legacy orders and transition challenges as contributing factors, stating corrective measures are in the planning phase. A comprehensive review of production planning and overtime authorization procedures is required.
**Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Area 3.0: Wages & Benefits (Ref: WB-Aud-079-FY24Q3)**
**Date of Investigation:** October 26-27, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the recent change in ownership noted in the factory profile, the audit team focused on verifying the accurate implementation of the wage structure and timely payment of wages during the transition period. Management presented payroll records for September 2023 which, on preliminary review, appeared compliant with minimum wage standards.

**Observations & Interviews:** A detailed reconciliation of time and attendance records against payroll data for a sample of 27 production workers from three departments revealed systemic discrepancies. For the pay period ending September 15, 2023, it was observed that the calculation of overtime premiums for hours worked in excess of the legal weekly limit was incorrectly applied at a rate of 1.2 times the base rate, rather than the legally mandated 1.5 times. This miscalculation was confirmed through worker interviews with 15 employees, who provided payslip copies indicating the underpayment. The factory's HR controller acknowledged the error, attributing it to an incorrect formula in the new payroll software implemented post-acquisition.

**Follow-up & Corrective Action:** A preliminary calculation estimates approximately 68% of the workforce (roughly 238 employees) were affected by this error in the September pay cycle, resulting in a systemic underpayment of legally earned wages. Management has been instructed to conduct a full retrospective review for all employees since the ownership change and to submit a corrective action plan with a remediation timetable for back-pay. **Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: SC-03-2024-079 (Discrimination)**
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the recent change in ownership and ongoing management transition, the audit team conducted a review of hiring, promotion, and general employment practices to assess adherence to the client's Code of Conduct and local labor law regarding non-discrimination. Particular attention was given to the sewing and cutting department supervisory recruitment over the preceding three months.
**Observations & Interview Notes:** A review of personnel files for the last 30 hires (August-October 2023) indicated a pattern wherein all eight (8) new hires for the supervisory cutting department positions were male, despite a qualified internal female applicant pool within the sewing lines. During confidential worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, with 12 female machine operators (selected at random from a roster), three (3) separate individuals reported being discouraged from applying for the recent cutting supervisor vacancies by the interim production manager, with statements attributed to him suggesting the work was "too physically demanding for women." This represents a clear policy and practice gap, creating a discriminatory barrier to promotion based on gender.
**Follow-up Required:** The new ownership must implement and communicate a formal, non-discriminatory recruitment and promotion policy immediately, provide documented training to all managerial staff, and review the current cutting supervisor appointments for corrective action.
**Severity: Moderate**


============================================================
Factory 080 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health (OSH) - Reference: Audit 080-Q3-24, Section 7.1**

During the site inspection conducted on 12 October 2023, it was noted that while the general housekeeping and machine guarding standards in the sewing and cutting sections were found to be satisfactory, a significant deficiency was identified in the designated chemical storage area adjacent to the finishing department. Inspector observations confirmed that four drums of chlorine-based bleaching agents, along with several containers of solvent-based adhesives, were stored without secondary containment, creating a direct risk of soil and groundwater contamination in the event of a spill. This was corroborated during worker interviews on 13 October 2023, wherein 3 of 8 finishing section workers expressed unawareness of the specific hazards associated with these chemicals, indicating a lapse in the required specialized training for handling hazardous materials as per section 4.5 of the factory's own OSH management plan. Although the factory's strong grievance mechanism was noted as functional, this specific storage condition constitutes a clear violation of established international safety standards and local regulatory requirements for hazardous material management. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up note has been issued to management to implement secondary containment bunding and provide documented chemical safety training to the affected workforce by 30 November 2023.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference:** FA-080-24-009 | **Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FA-CB) | **Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** Factory 080 maintains a recognized and active workers' union, established in 2018, with collective bargaining agreements (CBAs) historically negotiated in good faith. The audit included a review of the current CBA, dated January 2022, and interviews with 17 workers, including 5 union executive committee members, conducted on October 25-26, 2023. While the grievance mechanism is robust and worker-management dialogue is generally positive, a procedural discrepancy was identified regarding union access. During the physical inspection of the factory floor and canteen areas, it was noted that while union notice boards are present and updated, the dedicated union office (Room B-104), as stipulated in Article 4.2 of the CBA, was found to be repurposed for fabric storage during operational hours, limiting its availability for confidential member consultations.

**Worker Interview Notes:** Interviewed union representatives confirmed they have unimpeded ability to hold meetings after shifts and can communicate with members, but three separate members expressed inconvenience, noting that ad-hoc meetings during breaks must be held in the open canteen due to the inconsistent accessibility of the designated private office. Management acknowledged the space allocation issue, stating it was a temporary measure initiated in August 2023 due to a surge in raw material inventory, but no formal consultation or temporary alternative arrangement was documented with the union committee.

**Conclusion:** This constitutes a failure to fully uphold the facility access provisions agreed upon in the legally binding CBA, thereby impairing the union's full functionality. Although there is no evidence of anti-union discrimination or hostility, and the core rights of association and bargaining remain intact, the failure to consult on a change impacting a negotiated condition represents a breach of the collective agreement. A corrective action plan (CAP) must address the immediate reinstatement of the space and establish a protocol for consultation regarding any changes to union facilities.
**Severity: Minor**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: CL-2023-080-02**
**Compliance Area:** Prohibition of Child Labor (Code: CL-1.1)

Following a review of personnel records and a physical inspection of production areas conducted on October 26, 2023, the audit team identified a compliance issue related to the verification of worker age. While no instances of child labor were observed in active workstations during the tour, the sampling of 15 personnel files from hires within the last six months revealed one instance (File ID: WRK-44721) where the provided national identification document appeared to be of questionable authenticity upon secondary inspection; the photograph did not convincingly match the worker, and the document exhibited substandard print quality. An interview with the worker in question, conducted privately through the established grievance mechanism on October 27, confirmed she was 17 years old at the time of hiring three months prior, falling below the minimum age requirement of 18 as stipulated in the applicable code of conduct. It is noted that the factory's otherwise robust recruitment process, which includes document verification, failed in this singular case. The management, in cooperation with the active union, has initiated the prescribed remediation protocol to remove the minor from production work and provide educational support.
**Severity: Minor**

[Wages & Benefits]
**AUDIT FINDING REF: WB-08-2024-003**
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Payment of Wages)
**Date of Discovery:** 24 October 2024

**Background & Observation:** The audit team reviewed wage records for the period of July to September 2024, focusing on overtime (OT) compensation calculations. While base wage payments were timely and accurate, a recurring discrepancy was identified in the overtime premium rate for workers paid a monthly basic salary. Factory policy and payroll records confirm the monthly basic salary is used as the base for OT calculations, as opposed to the higher legally mandated monthly *minimum wage* floor. For example, for a worker with a basic salary of 9,500 BDT and a legal minimum wage of 12,500 BDT, all overtime hours were calculated at (9,500/30/8)*2 = 79.17 BDT/hour, rather than the required (12,500/30/8)*2 = 104.17 BDT/hour.

**Worker Interviews:** Interviews with 8 production workers across 3 departments, conducted on 24 October, confirmed this shortfall. Workers stated they were aware of their overtime hours but were not fully conversant with the precise calculation methodology. The established grievance mechanism had not received formal complaints on this specific issue, which management attributed to the generally positive relations and the technical nature of the discrepancy.

**Conclusion:** This constitutes a systemic violation of Section 108 of the Bangladesh Labour Act, 2006, resulting in the underpayment of overtime wages to all monthly-salaried production personnel. The factory is required to conduct a full reconciliation for the past six months and implement immediate corrective action.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SOC-08-D-2023-014 | **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (SOC-300-01) | **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Context:** During the annual social compliance audit for Factory 080, the area of non-discrimination in hiring, remuneration, promotion, and access to benefits was reviewed against the client’s Code of Conduct and relevant ILO conventions. The factory maintains a generally positive industrial relations climate, facilitated by an active union and a documented, well-utilized grievance mechanism as noted in other report sections. **Observations and Interview Notes:** A review of hiring records for the period January to September 2023 indicated a consistent and documented hiring process. However, during confidential interviews conducted on October 26 with 32 production workers selected across departments and shifts, three workers from the cutting section independently reported a perception that supervisory promotions within the last six months appeared to favor individuals from the same regional background as the production manager, despite the existence of qualified candidates from other demographics. The workers stated this was not raised formally through the grievance channel due to a concern of being perceived as divisive, though they emphasized no overtly discriminatory language or policies were observed. Management was unable to produce documented, objective criteria for the most recent two internal promotions to assistant supervisor to definitively dispel this perception. **Conclusion and Follow-Up Note:** While no evidence of systemic discriminatory policy was found, the perception of bias in advancement opportunities, if unaddressed, could undermine the otherwise strong worker-management relations and constitute a failure to ensure equal opportunity in practice. The factory is required to review and formalize transparent promotion procedures. **Severity: Minor**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-003:** Within the scope of the forced labor compliance area, the audit team conducted a review of payroll records, employment contracts, and exit procedures, supplemented by confidential worker interviews conducted on 23 and 24 October 2023. While the established grievance mechanism and positive industrial relations were noted, a review of twenty-four randomly selected worker files and interviews with eight production operators revealed a consistent practice of retaining original identity documents (specifically national ID cards) in the factory's security office for "safe keeping," as stated by management. This practice, confirmed in interviews with three workers who expressed a reluctant acceptance but acknowledged feeling unable to refuse, constitutes a retention of personal property that could be used to impose a penalty and restricts workers’ freedom of movement. Although no evidence of coercion or debt bondage was found, the involuntary retention of personal documents is a violation of the Forced Labor and Human Trafficking Principles of the responsible sourcing code of conduct. **Severity: Moderate.** Corrective action requires the immediate cessation of this practice and the return of all privately held documents, with verification to be provided within 30 days.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-2023-080-04
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Fire & Structural Safety)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Observations:** The audit included a review of emergency preparedness documentation, a physical inspection of exits and assembly points, and an unannounced fire drill simulation conducted at 14:30 hrs. While the factory maintains a generally well-organized Emergency Prevention and Preparedness Plan (last revised July 2023) and all primary exit routes were found to be unobstructed and clearly marked, critical deficiencies were observed during the drill execution. The simulated evacuation of the 2nd-floor cutting section, involving approximately 127 workers, exceeded the maximum permitted evacuation time by 3 minutes and 45 seconds, as timed by the audit team. Furthermore, interviews with 8 workers from that section revealed a lack of clarity on alternate escape routes should the primary staircase be compromised, with 5 workers stating they had not received practical floor-specific evacuation training within the last 12 months, relying instead on the annual general orientation.
**Follow-up & Root Cause:** Management attributed the delay to a bottleneck at the staircase landing and has initiated a corrective action plan to revise sectoral evacuation procedures. However, the failure to ensure all workers are adequately trained and drilled on full evacuation procedures constitutes a breach of the required standard, presenting a heightened risk in a multi-story facility.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Reference:** WH-OT-2023-080-04
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (B.1.1 - B.1.4)
**Background & Methodology:** The audit team, during the onsite inspection conducted from 15-17 November 2023, reviewed time and attendance records for the period July-October 2023, cross-referenced payroll data, and conducted confidential interviews with 32 production workers selected across all departments. The factory's generally cooperative environment and functional Worker Representative Committee were noted as positive contextual factors.

**Observations & Evidence:** While the review indicated systematic compliance with weekly maximum hour limits and day of rest requirements, a discrepancy was identified concerning the voluntary nature of overtime. Interview notes from 11 workers across the sewing and finishing sections revealed that while the policy of written consent is formally documented, in practice, line supervisors have on occasion communicated production targets in a manner interpreted as mandatory. Specifically, on three separate instances in September (noted on 12th, 19th, and 26th) pertaining to a rush order for style #AX-889, workers reported feeling indirect pressure to accept overtime shifts due to verbal emphasis on the critical importance of the deadline, despite the existence of a signed consent form on file. The payroll audit for those corresponding weeks did not reveal any instances of unpaid overtime, and all hours worked were compensated at the correct premium rate.

**Conclusion:** This constitutes a procedural failure in the consistent application of the factory's own policy regarding freely granted overtime consent, as per requirement B.1.3. The finding is mitigated by the absence of wage violations, the existence of a formal grievance channel which workers expressed confidence in using, and the isolated nature of the incidents. A corrective action plan must address supervisory communication protocols.
**Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding HA-023-080**
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (HA)
**Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

While the factory maintains a generally positive working environment, supported by a legitimate, active workers' union and a demonstrated commitment to dialogue, a systematic review of the grievance mechanism logs from Q3 2023 revealed a notable pattern. Interview notes from 12 out of 39 workers interviewed across cutting, sewing, and finishing departments on October 26 indicated a recurring, yet unreported, perception of verbal harassment in the form of public reprimands by two identified line supervisors in Section B. These reprimands, described by multiple workers as "demeaning" and "shouting about speed in front of peers," were not formally escalated through the established channels, as workers expressed a belief that such "small issues" were not for the union or committee, despite management's open-door policy. This gap between the existence of strong procedural mechanisms and their full utilization for all forms of perceived harassment, including verbal abuse, constitutes a failure in ensuring the policy's effective implementation and worker awareness. **Severity: Minor**


============================================================
Factory 081 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-04-C**  
**Compliance Area: Discrimination (including hiring, promotion, remuneration, and access to training)**  
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**  

During the review of human resources policies and procedures, it was noted that Factory 081, due to its recent establishment and ongoing development of management systems, has not yet formalized a written non-discrimination policy that aligns with local labor law and international standards. This procedural gap was further evidenced during confidential worker interviews conducted on October 25-26, 2023, with a sample of 18 workers from various production departments. While no overt systemic discrimination was reported, multiple interviewees from the finishing section noted a perceived preference in the allocation of overtime, with supervisors allegedly consistently offering first opportunity to workers from their own regional hometown, potentially disadvantaging others. Management confirmed the absence of formal anti-discrimination training for supervisory staff and acknowledged that hiring practices, while not intentionally exclusionary, rely heavily on current worker referrals which may perpetuate demographic homogeneity. A follow-up note requires the factory to develop, document, and disseminate a clear non-discrimination policy and implement corresponding supervisory training by Q1 2024. **Severity: Minor**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: WB-081-04**  
**Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits**  
**Date of Audit: October 26, 2023**  

Background context indicates Factory 081 is a newly established operation with an estimated workforce of 150 employees, predominantly young migrant workers. During the review of payroll records for the period September 1–30, 2023, and subsequent worker interviews conducted on-site, it was observed that while base wages for regular working hours were correctly aligned with the local minimum wage standard, the factory has not established a verifiable system for tracking and compensating overtime hours. Specifically, inspector observations of timesheet records (serial logs TSM-09/23-001 through 015) revealed incomplete entries for overtime commencement and cessation times, with no supervisory signatures for authorization. Interviews with 12 randomly selected production line workers from three departments indicated unanimous confirmation that overtime work was routinely required, particularly during peak production cycles, but payments received were inconsistent and often calculated at a flat rate not commensurate with the legal overtime premium. This discrepancy represents a failure to comply with statutory requirements under the labor law, Article 44, regarding overtime remuneration. The management acknowledged gaps in their nascent payroll system and committed to implementing corrective measures. **Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding EP-03-2024 (Emergency Preparedness):** During the site inspection conducted on 12 March 2024, it was observed that while emergency exit routes in the main production hall were generally unobstructed, the factory's overall emergency preparedness protocols remain underdeveloped and inadequately documented, which is consistent with the nascent stage of their compliance management systems. Interviews with seven production workers from different departments revealed a concerning lack of awareness; four workers could not correctly identify the location of the nearest fire extinguisher, and none had participated in a full facility evacuation drill since the factory's commissioning six months ago. Furthermore, review of the facility's records indicated no documented evidence of a comprehensive emergency drill having been conducted, and the designated emergency assembly point in the rear parking area was not clearly marked with permanent signage. This constitutes a failure to establish and maintain a baseline level of preparedness, posing a significant risk to the safety of the predominantly young and inexperienced workforce in the event of a major incident. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up verification is required within 90 days to confirm the implementation of a documented emergency drill schedule and worker training program.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: SA-081-LA-2023-004**  
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (LA.03)  
**Background & Observations:** During the onsite inspection conducted on 15-17 October 2023, a review of time and attendance records for the period of September 2023 through the audit dates revealed systemic inaccuracies in the recording of working hours. Specifically, the manual punch cards reviewed for a sample of 34 production line workers showed consistent entries of exactly 8.5 hours per day, with no variation, which conflicted with production output data indicating high-volume days. Furthermore, worker interviews conducted with 12 randomly selected employees on 16 October confirmed a pattern of undeclared and unrecorded overtime. Multiple interviewees stated that they regularly work until 8:00 PM or 8:30 PM from Monday through Friday, and on two Saturdays in September, to meet production targets, with this additional time not reflected on their official time cards or compensated at the legally required overtime rate.  

**Analysis:** This practice constitutes a failure to maintain accurate records of hours worked and a failure to compensate for all overtime hours in accordance with local labor law and client Code of Conduct requirements. The factory's nascent compliance systems, including its timekeeping procedures and payroll verification, appear insufficient to capture actual working hours, leading to wage non-compliance. Management acknowledged the discrepancy, citing the pressures of start-up operations and an inexperienced HR team.  

**Follow-Up Required:** The factory must implement an objective, verifiable timekeeping system, conduct a retrospective review and remediation of unpaid overtime for the past six months, and establish clear procedures for authorizing and recording overtime. **Severity: Moderate**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: SAI-081-24-FOA-001**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Principle 3)**
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

During the document review and physical inspection conducted on the aforementioned date, it was noted that while management has posted the legally required labor law summaries in the main cafeteria, these postings do not include any specific information or guidance concerning workers' rights to form or join associations and to bargain collectively, a requirement under section 7.1 of the Client's Code of Conduct. Interviews held with a selection of 14 production line workers, approximately 12% of the workforce, revealed a consistent lack of awareness on this topic; when questioned, 12 of the 14 interviewees stated they had "never heard" about such rights and the remaining two provided incorrect information. This is compounded by the absence of any established policy or management system to educate the predominantly young workforce on these fundamental rights, which is a particular concern during this formative period of factory operations. The auditor must therefore cite a failure to effectively communicate and ensure understanding of these rights, creating a risk of infringement. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan (CAP) must address the development of a formal communication and training protocol.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2023-081-04
**Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Machine Guarding & Emergency Preparedness)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

During the walkthrough of the cutting and sewing sections, it was observed that a significant portion of the operational machinery, including but not limited to five overlock machines and two band knife cutting machines, were functioning without the requisite point-of-operation guards installed, presenting an immediate laceration and amputation hazard. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews conducted on October 25, wherein 4 out of 12 workers interviewed expressed concern over a lack of formal training on machine safety, with one worker noting a previous minor incident involving an unguarded needle. Furthermore, the emergency evacuation plan was found to be incomplete and not posted in key areas, and a timed egress drill conducted at 10:15 AM revealed a full building evacuation time of 7 minutes, which exceeds the benchmark for a facility of this layout and size, indicating insufficient worker familiarity with procedures. While the factory's nascent stage of development is acknowledged, the absence of these fundamental safeguards constitutes a systemic failure in hazard identification and risk mitigation.
**Severity: Critical**

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area: C.01 - Child Labor & Young Workers**

During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's hiring records, while recently established, lacked systematic age verification procedures beyond the collection of National ID cards. Subsequent physical inspection of the production lines and canteen on the same date revealed a workforce that appeared generally young, consistent with the factory profile. In confidential interviews held with 17 randomly selected production workers, two individuals—one from the finishing section and one from the cutting auxiliary team—provided details during cross-examination regarding their age and schooling history that raised reasonable suspicion they may be under the legal minimum working age of 16. The factory management, when presented with these concerns, was unable to produce verified birth certificates or parental consent documents for these specific individuals (ref: Worker IDs F081-047 and F081-089) and acknowledged gaps in their onboarding checks. This constitutes a failure in due diligence to effectively prevent child labor. **Severity: Critical** A follow-up verification of these workers' ages using authoritative documents must be completed within 48 hours, with the workers suspended from duty pending conclusive evidence of eligibility.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-081-02 | Compliance Area: Forced Labor (ILO Core Convention 29) | Date of Investigation: October 26, 2023**

During the documentation review and worker interviews, the audit team identified systemic issues regarding the retention of worker identity documents and the imposition of disciplinary fines, which collectively create an environment of coercion. A review of the factory's "Employee Administration Policy," dated August 2023, informally sanctions the practice of management holding original identity cards and academic certificates for "safekeeping," ostensibly to prevent loss, a practice verified in 17 of 22 worker personnel files reviewed. This was corroborated in confidential interviews with 8 production operators, who stated that their national ID cards were held by the line supervisor, with one worker (Interview #14) noting, "We were told we would get them back only if we gave one month's notice to quit." Furthermore, the factory's nascent disciplinary system includes punitive, unauthorized deductions for quality errors and tardiness, with one pay slip from September 2023 showing a 150 Taka deduction for "training fee recovery," a policy not detailed in any signed contract. These practices, while potentially stemming from a lack of established procedural knowledge in this new facility, effectively restrict workers' freedom of movement and impose bonded labor through financial penalties and document retention, constituting a violation of the fundamental principle of employment chosen freely. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Harassment & Abuse]
During the objective evidence review and worker interviews conducted on 23-24 October 2023, it was found that Factory 081 has not established a formal policy or procedure to prevent and address harassment and abuse in the workplace. While no formal complaints were documented, interviews with 12 randomly selected production workers, primarily from the sewing section, revealed a consistent lack of awareness of any reporting mechanism, with three younger workers alluding to "teasing by line leaders about speed" which they felt unable to formally challenge. The management representative confirmed that a harassment policy is "under development" but no draft was available for review, and no training on the subject has been provided to the predominantly young workforce since operations began. This constitutes a failure to meet the requirement for a clear and communicated policy protecting workers from harassment, abuse, and disciplinary measures (ref. COC Section 7.3). A corrective action plan is required to develop, implement, and communicate a comprehensive policy with defined reporting channels. Severity: Moderate.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-03-2024-081**
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources**
**Date of Discovery: October 26, 2024**

During the document review and employee interview processes, it was noted that the factory's systems for managing employment contracts are not fully established or consistently implemented. A review of personnel files for a sample of 30 production workers, selected at random, indicated that only 40% contained a signed, individualized employment contract detailing terms and conditions of employment as required by local labor law. Worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2024, with 15 line workers corroborated this discrepancy; several newer hires with less than three months of service stated they had only signed an attendance register or a basic information form upon joining, with no discussion or provision of a formal contract. While management attributes this to the nascent stage of their HR administrative systems and the high turnover common with a young workforce, the absence of legally mandated contracts for a significant portion of the workforce constitutes a failure to formalize the employment relationship and provide clear terms of work. A follow-up note requires the factory to implement a corrective action plan to rectify all missing contracts and establish a controlled issuance procedure. Severity: Moderate.
