Factory 082 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-082-24-FO-015 **| Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Criterion 7.1 & 7.3 of the Responsible Sourcing Initiative (RSI) Code) **| Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident of August 2023 and the factory's subsequent placement under enhanced supervision by the District Labor Office, this audit included a focused review of hiring and workforce management practices to assess equitable treatment during the recovery period. **Observations & Interviews:** Documentary review of the last three months' payroll and hiring registers (July-September 2023) indicated a hiring freeze for positions above helper/cleaner level. During worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2023, three (3) out of fourteen (14) female sewing operators from Line 5 separately alleged that the two (2) supervisory positions created for the new product line in September were filled by male candidates from outside the factory, despite internal female applicants with over five years of tenure possessing the requisite skills. The hiring manager, when questioned, stated the decision was based on a "need for fresh perspectives to manage post-fire restructuring," but could not produce documented, objective criteria used to disqualify the internal female candidates. This presents evidence of a potential systemic bias in promotional opportunities. **Severity: Moderate** **| Follow-up Note:** The factory management is required to review the contested hiring decision against non-discriminatory criteria and provide a corrective action plan for equitable promotion procedures by Q1 2024.

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-03: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work**

Following the fire incident of 15 January 2024, which resulted in the temporary shutdown of the west wing sewing section, the audit team reviewed policies and worker conditions related to potential coercion. During interviews conducted on 28 February 2024 with 14 production workers from the affected section, three workers (IDs W-082-311, W-082-298, and W-082-305) disclosed that management had informed them their February wages would be withheld to contribute to the factory’s recovery costs unless they voluntarily worked on their weekly rest day to meet a critical order deadline. This was corroborated by a review of the posted overtime roster for 17 February, which showed these individuals scheduled without prior consensual agreement, creating a situation of undue pressure and perceived penalty. While no physical confinement was observed, this constitutes a clear indicator of forced labor under the audit protocol's definition, as work was obtained through the threat of a financial penalty. A follow-up with the Senior Production Manager confirmed this directive was given verbally to expedite recovery, demonstrating a lack of procedural controls. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2023-082-04
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Fire Safety & Emergency Preparedness)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observations:** Subsequent to the fire incident of August 12, 2023, and the factory's placement under special monitoring by the local Fire Service Department, this audit focused on verifying the restoration and operational status of critical fire safety systems. While the main production building has been structurally repaired and cleared for occupancy, inspector observations on the date of the audit identified that the newly installed wet riser system and fire hose reels on the second and third floors, though physically present, remain non-operational. System pressure gauges registered at zero, and a functional test initiated at valve station 3B at 14:30 hrs confirmed no water flow. Interview notes from discussions with four workers from the cutting and sewing sections revealed that the mandatory monthly emergency evacuation drills, which were reportedly reinstated in September, have not been conducted for the month of October, with two workers stating they were unaware of the updated assembly point for their department.

**Follow-Up & Conclusion:** This constitutes a failure to maintain essential life safety equipment in a state of operational readiness, directly contravening section 4.1.2 of the National Fire Code and creating a significant immediate risk to personnel, particularly in a facility with a recent history of fire. The finding is exacerbated by the lapse in drill frequency, compromising procedural preparedness. Management stated that a final inspection and pressure test by the municipal authorities is pending, but no temporary compensatory measures (e.g., additional temporary fire watches or accessible alternative extinguishing equipment) were observed to be implemented. Severity: Critical.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-082-24-019 | Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FOACB)**

Following the fire incident of 15 March 2024 and the subsequent placement of the facility under special monitoring by the District Labor Department, the audit team conducted a review of FOACB mechanisms. During inspections on 10 May 2024, it was noted that while the factory's stated policy allows for worker representation, the de facto Worker Welfare Committee (WWC) has not convened since the fire, and its elected members confirmed in 4 out of 7 confidential interviews that management has not engaged with them regarding the reinstatement of displaced workers or changes to working conditions post-incident. This constitutes a failure to maintain meaningful dialogue with worker representatives, a condition exacerbated by the current recovery period. Management acknowledged this lapse, citing operational disruption, but failed to provide a corrective action plan for re-establishing the committee's functions, thereby contravening section 7.1 of the Supplier Code of Conduct. **Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: EP-082-04-2024**
In the context of the factory's ongoing recovery from a significant fire incident on 12 February 2024 and its current status under special monitoring by district authorities, the audit team conducted a thorough review of emergency preparedness protocols on 18 April 2024. Observations revealed that, while primary exit routes were clear, the newly installed fire alarm system in the rebuilt cutting section (Block C) had not been integrated into a facility-wide drill scenario, and inspection tags indicated the last full system test was dated 15 March 2024. Interviews with 7 production workers from that section confirmed they had received no instruction on the new alarm's distinct audible signal, with one worker noting, "We heard it go off by mistake last week, but we were told by the supervisor to keep working." This constitutes a failure to ensure all personnel are trained in and familiar with emergency signals and procedures, particularly critical given the facility's recent history. The lack of documented drill incorporating the new safety infrastructure represents a significant lapse in maintaining preparedness. **Severity: Moderate** A follow-up verification is required within 30 days to witness a full evacuation drill utilizing all current alarm systems.

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: CL-082-2023-04**
**Compliance Area: CH-1 (Minimum Age & Juvenile Workers)**
**Date of On-site Verification: October 26, 2023**

Following the fire incident of August 15, 2023, which resulted in a partial shutdown of production Lines 4 and 5, and subsequent placement of the facility under special monitoring by the local Labor Department, the audit team conducted a targeted review of hiring and age verification procedures. While management presented a newly implemented "Enhanced Onboarding Protocol," document cross-checking of 32 personnel files for workers hired in the post-recovery period (September-October 2023) revealed inconsistencies; specifically, two files (Employee IDs #4017 and #4032) contained national ID cards that exhibited potential discrepancies in physical appearance versus the document photo and listed birth dates calculating to 15 years of age. Interviews conducted with 14 workers on the refurbished Line 4 on October 26 confirmed that one individual (corresponding to ID #4017) was observed performing finishing tasks and, when interviewed separately without management present, stated she was "helping her aunt" and had been attending school until the recent semester. This constitutes a failure to maintain an effective age verification system, a critical requirement under both local law and the Responsible Sourcing Initiative (RSI) Code of Conduct, particularly concerning for a facility under heightened scrutiny. **Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SC-2023-082-14A | **Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (SC.3) | **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Context:** In light of the factory's ongoing recovery from the fire incident of August 2023 and its subsequent placement under special monitoring, particular attention was paid to management-worker interactions and the overall psychosocial work environment. **Observations & Interviews:** During unannounced floor walks on October 26, auditors observed a supervisory style characterized by elevated vocal reprimands and pervasive pressure, which several workers subsequently described as "constant shouting" to meet accelerated production targets for fire-related order reclaims. Interviews with 14 workers from the sewing section revealed three specific, corroborated accounts from the period of October 10-24, wherein Line Supervisor #7 (Mr. Chen) was reported to have used derogatory language targeting workers' speed and intelligence, including a specific incident on October 18 where a worker was subjected to a public belittlement in front of approximately 15 peers for a sewing error. While no physical abuse was alleged, the pattern of verbal abuse and humiliation creates a climate of intimidation and fear, which is exacerbated by the current high-pressure operational context. The factory's grievance mechanism, while documented, was cited by interviewed workers as ineffective in this specific area due to fears of retaliation from line-level management. **Follow-up Required:** Management must provide immediate documented training for all supervisory staff on acceptable disciplinary procedures and the non-retaliation policy, and demonstrate a corrective action plan to address this culturally embedded behavior. Severity: Moderate.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-082-04**
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 6, Local Labor Law Article 41)
**Background & Context:** Given the factory's status under special monitoring following the fire incident on 15 March 2024 and its subsequent recovery production phase, particular attention was paid to working hour practices to assess pressures on the workforce. Review covered payroll records for April 2024, timecard data, and conducted confidential worker interviews.
**Observations & Evidence:** While daily working hours were largely within legal limits, inspection of production floor timecards for the week of 22-28 April revealed systematic overtime exceeding the monthly legal cap of 36 hours, with a recorded high of 58 hours for the cutting department. Interviews with 7 workers from the sewing section confirmed that, although voluntary, this pattern has been consistent for the past three weeks to meet revised shipment deadlines for lost orders. Management acknowledged the breach, attributing it to catch-up production schedules approved under a provisional operational permit from the district authority.
**Conclusion & Follow-up:** A formal corrective action plan (CAP) must be submitted to address systemic scheduling, demonstrating how production planning will align with legal overtime limits without reliance on excessive hours, to be verified at the next special monitoring visit scheduled by the Labor Department. Severity: Moderate


============================================================
Factory 083 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with active union, good worker-management relations, strong grievance mechanism
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: CL-083-2023-02**

During the review of personnel files for production employees hired within the last twelve months, a discrepancy was identified in the documentation for one worker (Employee ID: P-4412) assigned to the finishing section. While the provided national ID card indicated an age of 18, cross-referencing with the factory's own mandatory educational certificate records—a proactive measure implemented by the joint worker-management committee in Q1 2022—revealed the individual’s date of birth to be 17 years and 4 months at the time of hiring on 15 March 2023. Interviews conducted on 24 October 2023 with the individual and three of their line colleagues confirmed the younger age, though all stated the hiring was initiated by the worker presenting the apparently falsified document out of economic necessity, and there was no indication of forced labor. The factory’s union representative was immediately consulted and the situation was rectified prior to the audit close-out, with the individual provided a formal severance and enrolled in a remedial education program with partial wage compensation, as per the jointly agreed-upon remediation protocol. Given the immediate remediation, the robust due diligence systems in place that uncovered the issue, and the absence of systemic failure or intent, this is classified as an isolated procedural breach.
**Severity: Minor**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2024-083-007 | **Area of Inspection:** Main Sewing Floor & Chemical Storage | **Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Background & Observation:** While the factory maintains generally good housekeeping standards and a documented safety committee that includes union representatives, as noted in the opening meeting, a physical inspection of the primary sewing floor revealed a systemic issue regarding emergency egress. Specifically, aisle widths in three of the five production rows were measured and found to be consistently between 60-68 centimeters, falling below the minimum required width of 90 centimeters as stipulated in the factory’s own Safety Policy (Section 4.2) and international best practice (ILO code C120). This congestion was primarily due to the unauthorized introduction of temporary storage carts for work-in-progress materials. During interviews conducted on October 26 with 7 production workers from different lines, 5 confirmed that the narrow aisles have been a persistent issue for "several months," causing difficulty during material handling and expressing concern that it would impede a swift evacuation. Although the grievance mechanism is functional and worker-management relations are strong, this specific hazard had not been formally reported through that channel. The factory must rectify this obstruction to ensure unimpeded access to all emergency exits and firefighting equipment.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WH-OT-083-04-2023
**Compliance Area:** Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 7)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26-27, 2023

**Background & Observation:** The audit team acknowledges the generally positive industrial relations climate at the facility, facilitated by an active union and functional grievance mechanism. A review of production records for the three-month period of July-September 2023 indicated several peak periods, particularly in late August, where order fulfillment required significant overtime. During the review of October 2023 timecards and payroll records for a sample of 47 workers across the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments, it was observed that, while daily and weekly hour limits were respected, the facility failed to obtain individual written consent for overtime work as mandated by local law and client Code of Conduct, relying instead on a prior blanket consent clause within the collective bargaining agreement and verbal team briefings. Interviews conducted with 15 workers on October 27 confirmed their awareness of overtime schedules via supervisors and union representatives, and all stated overtime was paid correctly at the legal premium rate, but none could recall providing specific written authorization for the overtime worked in the audited period.

**Conclusion:** This constitutes a procedural failure in the implementation of overtime regulations. While the cooperative worker-management relationship and the existence of the CBA provision mitigate the risk of forced overtime, the absence of the specific, individual consent documentation remains a deviation from required standards. The factory management is advised to revise its overtime authorization procedure to incorporate individual worker confirmation, to be implemented by the next audit cycle.
**Severity: Minor**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: FL-2023-083-02 | Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work (CPSA Section 7.1, 7.3) | Audit Date: October 26-27, 2023**

Following a review of payroll records for the period of September 1 to October 15, 2023, and subsequent interviews conducted on-site, it was observed that the factory's policy regarding the return of worker identification documents, specifically national ID cards, upon hire presents a potential risk of involuntary restriction of movement. While management stated the practice is for "safekeeping and verification purposes" and documents are returned upon request, interviews with 7 out of 32 randomly selected workers in the finishing department indicated a perceived pressure to leave documents with the security office, with two workers expressing uncertainty about the procedure to retrieve them. It is noted that the presence of an active union and a functional grievance mechanism, as confirmed through separate interview sessions, provides a credible avenue for redress, and no cases of actual document withholding to compel work were substantiated. Nevertheless, the policy and its implementation create an environment where coercion could occur, constituting a violation of the "No Deposits" principle under forced labor standards. **Severity: Minor**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: FAC-083-2023-Q4-EP-02**

**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (Fire & General Evacuation)

**Finding:** During the inspection conducted on November 15, 2023, it was observed that the factory's emergency preparedness protocols, while structurally sound, demonstrated a procedural deficiency during the scheduled full evacuation drill. The drill, which involved approximately 1,247 personnel from all shifts, was initiated at 10:35 AM. Inspector observations and subsequent interviews with 23 workers from various departments, including cutting, sewing, and finishing, confirmed that while the alarm was audible and evacuation routes were unobstructed, the assembly process at the designated muster points was disorganized and prolonged, taking approximately 14 minutes to achieve a full headcount, exceeding the internal target of 9 minutes. This delay was attributed to insufficiently trained floor wardens, who were unable to efficiently direct workers to correct zones and conduct rapid accountability checks, a point corroborated in worker interviews where three individuals stated they were unsure of their specific muster point subgroup. Although the worker-management committee, noted as active and collaborative in other areas, has a scheduled meeting to review drill outcomes, the current gap in drill execution effectiveness presents a moderate risk to life safety in a genuine emergency. **Severity: Moderate.** Follow-up required: Verification of updated warden training and a re-drill to be evidenced within 90 days.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-083-24-009 | Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (CBA Section 7)**

During the audit conducted on 12-14 March 2024, the inspection team reviewed the factory's union registration documents (Certificate #TU-4521, issued 2019) and collective bargaining agreement (CBA effective Jan 2023-Dec 2025). Interviews were held with 7 members of the elected union executive committee and 23 randomly selected union members from the sewing, cutting, and finishing departments. All interviewees confirmed the union operates without management interference, with the Chairperson noting monthly bipartite meetings are held consistently, the latest on 28 February 2024, to discuss production and welfare issues. Furthermore, the grievance mechanism log (Register GHR-083) showed 47 cases filed in the last six months, with 38 resolved through direct union-management dialogue and 9 pending but within the agreed resolution timeline. The CBA is displayed in full on notice boards in the canteen, and wage slips for February 2024 were cross-checked and confirmed to correctly reflect the agreed overtime premiums and annual increment as per the CBA clauses 4.2 and 4.5. **Status: Compliant.**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference:** A-2023-083-07 | **Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Criterion 4.1, 4.2, 4.3) | **Date of Inspection:** 17-19 October 2023

**Background & Observation:** While the overall industrial relations climate at the facility is positive, with a functional Workers’ Union and a demonstrated grievance mechanism, a focused review of hiring, promotion, and disciplinary records for the past twelve months revealed a pattern requiring attention. Specifically, the audit team examined the composition of line leader and quality controller positions, which are considered entry-level supervisory roles. It was observed that of the 28 individuals promoted into these positions from the production floor during the review period, only 3 were female, despite females constituting approximately 68% of the total direct production workforce. This discrepancy was raised with the HR Manager during the document review on 18 October.

**Interviews & Notes:** In confidential interviews with 14 workers (8 female, 6 male) from three different production departments, multiple female workers expressed a perception that these promotional opportunities were not equally accessible. One worker with 5 years of tenure noted, "We hear about the openings, but the helpers who get chosen are always the young men the supervisors already talk to." The union representatives confirmed they had received informal concerns on this matter but had not yet escalated it through the formal grievance channel, considering it a "soft" issue.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** The documented statistical disparity, coupled with the subjective perceptions gathered, indicates a *de facto* gender-based discrimination in promotional practices for lower-level supervisory positions. This occurs despite the presence of a non-discrimination policy. The factory management has been advised to conduct a formal review of its promotion criteria and selection panel processes to identify and eliminate unintended bias. A corrective action plan is required to address this systemic issue.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Reference:** HR-083-24-007
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Context & Observation:** A review of personnel files for a randomized sample of 30 production workers, representing approximately 8% of the direct workforce, was conducted. The factory maintains a well-organized, centralized records room. While the majority of documents, including identity verification and proof of age, were found to be in order, a discrepancy was noted regarding the issuance of formal employment contracts.

**Detail of Finding:** According to local labor law Article 23(1), a written contract must be provided to an employee within 30 days of commencement of work. The file review revealed that for 4 out of the 30 workers sampled (constituting 13.3% of the sample group), the signed employment contracts were dated between 32 and 45 days after their recorded hire dates, as evidenced by timesheet and payroll records. Interviews with three of these affected workers, conducted on October 27, confirmed they began work without a formal contract and received the document later, though they affirmed they were aware of their basic terms of employment verbally upon hiring. The factory's otherwise strong industrial relations and functional grievance mechanism, including active union oversight, likely mitigated more severe impacts from this administrative lapse.

**Follow-Up Required:** Management must review all personnel files to ensure 100% compliance with the statutory contracting timeline and implement a procedural control to prevent recurrence. Evidence of corrective action is required.
**Severity: Minor**

[Harassment & Abuse]
During the onsite inspection conducted on 12-14 March 2024, the audit team reviewed the factory's harassment prevention policy (HR-POL-007, rev. 3) and grievance mechanism records. While the established procedures are robust and the Workers' Union Committee confirmed the general effectiveness of the resolution process, interviews with 42 production line workers across all three shifts revealed three separate, documented instances from January 2024 where a line supervisor used verbally abusive language, including shouting and demeaning remarks, towards operators during peak production periods. These incidents were formally reported through the grievance hotline and were subsequently addressed by management with corrective actions, including mandatory re-training for the supervisor involved, as verified in case log GRV-2024-011 through GRV-2024-013. However, the recurrence of such behavior, albeit addressed post-reporting, indicates a gap in proactive prevention and supervisory conduct monitoring. Severity: Minor. A follow-up verification of the supervisor's conduct is required within 90 days.

Factory 084 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Discrimination]
During the review of personnel files and payroll records for Q4 2023 spanning from October to December, and reinforced through confidential employee interviews conducted on January 15-16, 2024, the audit team identified a systemic pattern concerning promotional opportunities within the stitching department. Of the 23 workers interviewed from this department, 17 (approximately 74%) reported a consistent company practice, verbally communicated by line supervisors, that operators wishing to be considered for promotion to Quality Checker or Supervisory roles must be willing to work permanent night shifts. This practice inherently disadvantages a significant portion of the female workforce, who comprised 89% of the stitching department at the time of the audit, as multiple interviewees cited familial and childcare responsibilities preventing them from accepting such permanent nocturnal assignments. While no discriminatory intent was alleged in hiring, this structural barrier to advancement constitutes a violation of the client’s Code of Conduct, section 4.2 on equal opportunity, and was acknowledged by the dedicated compliance officer in a follow-up discussion on January 17, 2024 (ref: Finding DF-084-02). Severity: Moderate.


============================================================
Factory 085 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-085-04-2024**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (Code: B.7.1)**
**Background & Context:** In light of the factory's status under special monitoring following the fire incident on 12 March 2024, particular scrutiny was applied to working hour practices to assess operational pressures during the recovery period. Review of payroll records for March and April 2024 indicated a pattern of extended production shifts ostensibly to meet backlogged orders. **Observations & Interviews:** During unannounced inspections on 23 and 24 April 2024, production floors remained operational until 22:15 and 22:30 respectively, exceeding the local legal limit. Interviews with 14 production workers from the cutting and sewing departments confirmed consistent, mandatory overtime throughout the audit period, with several workers reporting an average of 95 hours of work in the week of 15 April. While management cited the recovery effort as an extenuating circumstance, verification of timecards showed a systemic failure to obtain individual employee consent for overtime as required, and overtime premiums were calculated at a flat rate rather than the legally mandated differential. **Follow-Up Required:** Management must provide a revised production plan to the special monitoring authority that demonstrates a pathway to lawful working hours without reliance on excessive overtime.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: DISC-2024-085-02**

**Background & Context:** During the routine follow-up audit conducted on 23-24 April 2024, under the special monitoring protocol established after the Q1 fire incident, particular attention was paid to human resources and hiring practices to assess the factory's stability and equitable treatment of personnel during its recovery period. The audit scope included a review of hiring records from the preceding three months and interviews with a cross-section of the current workforce.

**Observations & Evidence:** A review of the 47 new hire records for production roles filed since 15 January 2024 revealed a consistent pattern wherein all 32 applicants successfully hired were aged between 18 and 25. Notably, 28 application forms from candidates over the age of 40, several with prior relevant garment experience noted, were on file but had been uniformly marked "Not Selected" with no further documented rationale. During confidential interviews with 19 workers, three separate individuals from the packaging department reported hearing supervisory staff stating a preference for "younger, faster hands" on the new assembly lines, with one interviewee quoting a line leader on March 12 as saying, "we are rebuilding modern, not for old methods."

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** This constitutes a violation of local labor law and applicable code of conduct provisions prohibiting discrimination on the basis of age. The pattern in documentation, corroborated by worker testimony, indicates a de facto hiring policy that disadvantages older workers, which is particularly concerning during a rebuilding phase that should focus on equitable opportunity. The HR manager was unable to provide a legitimate, non-discriminatory business justification for the observed hiring trend. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan must address policy communication, supervisor training, and a revised, objective hiring matrix.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-085-24-009 **Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (CCLA Section 7.1)
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident of 12 March 2024 and the subsequent disruption to production and management oversight, a specific review of workplace conduct protocols was warranted. The audit team, during the site visit on 18 April 2024, sought to evaluate the robustness of mechanisms to prevent harassment and abuse amidst the recovery period and increased workforce stress. Interviews were conducted with a cross-section of 17 production operators, 4 line supervisors, and the HR officer.
**Observations & Interviews:** While the formal policy against harassment is documented, worker interviews revealed a concerning pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by two first-line supervisors in the cutting department, identified as Supervisors A and B. Multiple workers (6 of 10 interviewed from this department) described being routinely shouted at, subjected to derogatory comments regarding their speed and intelligence, and threatened with punitive workload assignments or job loss for minor errors, particularly during the accelerated post-fire production catch-up periods. These allegations were consistent and detailed across separate interviews. The HR department had no active reporting or investigation for these allegations in the previous 90 days, indicating a breakdown in the grievance procedure.
**Follow-up Note:** Management must immediately investigate the specific allegations against the identified supervisors, reinforce zero-tolerance policy training for all managerial staff, and re-communicate the confidential reporting channels to all employees.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-2023-085-02**
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor (Freedom of Movement & Coercion)**
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident in Building C on October 15, 2023, and the subsequent special monitoring regime by the local Labour Department, this audit included a heightened focus on worker treatment and retention practices. During the document review and physical inspection conducted on November 28-29, 2023, auditors examined post-incident policies and payroll records for the 317 affected workers.
**Observations & Interviews:** While management stated that all workers were free to resign with standard notice, interviews with 14 workers from the fire-affected sewing lines revealed a consistent pattern of coercive practice. Twelve of the fourteen interviewed workers reported that their original identification documents (National ID cards), which had been stored in the factory's administrative office for "safekeeping" prior to the fire, had not been returned upon request. Workers stated that the HR department cited "ongoing fire investigation procedures" and "administrative delays" as reasons for withholding these documents, effectively preventing them from seeking alternative employment as they cannot legally enroll elsewhere without this government-issued identification. This practice was confirmed in a separate interview with the HR Manager, who acknowledged the policy but framed it as a temporary measure to "maintain workforce stability during recovery."
**Conclusion & Follow-up:** The withholding of personal identity documents constitutes a severe restriction on workers' freedom of movement and creates a significant power imbalance, indicative of coercion under forced labor protocols. This is a violation of Code Section FL.3.1. Management must immediately cease this practice and return all original documents to workers, providing certified copies for its own files if necessary. A corrective action plan must be submitted within 48 hours.
**Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref:** WB-085-03-24
**Compliance Area:** Wages & Benefits (Payment of Wages for Overtime)
**Date of Discovery:** March 21, 2024
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident in Building B reported on February 10, 2024, production has been consolidated into a single shift in the remaining facility, leading to significant operational pressures to meet deadlines. Our review focused on wage payments for the post-recovery period of February 15 to March 15, 2024.

**Observations & Interviews:** A detailed payroll examination for 127 production line workers revealed a systemic discrepancy between hours recorded on manually maintained overtime registers and the hours for which premium pay was calculated. For the period in question, overtime hours worked were consistently compensated at the standard hourly rate, in contravention of Section 12.4 of the national labor law, which mandates a premium of not less than 1.5 times the ordinary rate. Worker interviews conducted with 19 randomly selected employees on March 21 confirmed this practice uniformly, with several individuals noting their reluctance to raise the issue given the factory's fragile recovery status and fear of jeopardizing employment.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** The factory management acknowledged the discrepancy, attributing it to an administrative error within their rebuilt payroll system. However, the failure to pay legally mandated overtime rates constitutes a violation of core worker rights. The auditor notes that while the factory operates under special monitoring by local authorities, this monitoring appears focused on structural and fire safety compliance, not wage-related matters. A full reconciliation and remediation plan for back wages, plus interest, must be submitted.
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: HR-085-24-011 | Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources | Date of Finding: October 26, 2024**

Following the fire incident on August 15, 2024, which necessitated the temporary cessation of operations in Sewing Hall B and the subsequent special monitoring regime by the local Labour Bureau, the audit team focused on reviewing the contractual status of all workers reassigned to alternate production lines. During a document review on October 24, 2024, it was observed that while permanent staff retained their original contracts, the employment agreements for 37 temporary workers hired in the post-fire recovery period (from September 1, 2024, onward) were not available for inspection at the time of the audit. Interviews conducted with a sample of 8 of these 37 workers confirmed they had not received a signed copy of their employment contract, though they could verbally confirm their daily wage rate and had undergone basic safety orientation. This represents a failure to establish written terms of employment in accordance with local labor law Article 17 and undermines the factory's stated commitment to transparent labor practices during its recovery phase. The HR Manager attributed this lapse to administrative pressures during the restructuring of shifts. A follow-up note has been issued to management to produce all missing contracts for verification within seven days. **Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Ref: EP-085-24-003**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date of Audit Activities: October 26-27, 2024**

Given the factory's profile as an entity recovering from a significant fire incident (occurring approximately four months prior, in late June 2024) and currently under special monitoring by local authorities, a heightened level of scrutiny was applied to emergency preparedness protocols. During the site inspection on October 26, it was observed that while primary exit routes were clearly marked, the alternate fire escape stairwell at the north end of Cut & Sew Hall B was obstructed by approximately twelve bales of raw fabric and several dismantled sewing machine frames, severely impeding egress. Interviews conducted with 7 production workers from that section on October 27 revealed that the quarterly emergency drill scheduled for October had not been conducted, with workers stating the last practiced evacuation was "before the summer fire." Furthermore, review of the emergency drill logbook confirmed this lapse, showing the last documented drill was on May 15, 2024. This represents a significant regression and non-conformance against both local regulatory requirements and the factory's own corrective action plan submitted to authorities post-incident, particularly given their recent history. **Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
During the review of personnel files and production floor observations conducted on 24-25 October 2023, the audit team identified a significant lapse in the verification of workers' ages. While no individuals appearing to be underage were observed during the walkthrough, a subsequent document review revealed that four of the twenty-three workers interviewed (selected from the post-incident rehiring list) could not produce original age verification documents; they provided only local school-leaving certificates which are not considered legally valid for age proof under national law (refer to CL.01.04 verification protocol). This failure to establish a reliable age verification system, particularly during the expedited re-hiring process following the fire disruption, constitutes a breakdown in procedural controls designed to prevent child labor. Given the inherent risk and the factory's current status under special monitoring, this deficiency is elevated beyond a simple documentation error. **Severity: Moderate.** Follow-up requires the factory management to submit original government-issued identity documents for the four individuals in question to the audit team within seven business days for verification.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** OSH-2024-085-07 **| Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Section 9.1, Fire Safety & General Emergency Preparedness) **| Date of Inspection:** 22 October 2024

**Background & Context:** Following the significant fire incident reported on 15 August 2024, which resulted in a full production stoppage and subsequent placement under enhanced oversight by the District Labor Department, this audit included a focused verification of corrective and preventive actions related to fire safety. The factory management provided documentation of repairs and a new fire safety plan dated 10 September 2024.

**Observations & Evidence:** During a physical walkthrough of the cut-and-sew production halls on 22 October 2024, auditors confirmed the installation of new fire extinguishers and repaired electrical conduits. However, observation of the emergency egress routes revealed a persistent and systemic failure to maintain clear access. In the western wing, primary and secondary exit routes were partially obstructed by stored rolls of fabric and temporary stitching stations, reducing the clear width of the main corridor to approximately 0.8 meters at several points. Furthermore, during a scheduled fire drill at 11:00 hours on the audit date, the evacuation of the second-floor packaging section exceeded 4 minutes, with 5 of 12 workers interviewed stating they were unaware of the revised assembly point location since the factory layout changes post-fire. This was corroborated by a review of training records, which showed that only 30% of the workforce on the afternoon shift had received the updated evacuation drill briefing.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Requirement:** Despite management's efforts in hardware rectification, the critical life-safety protocols for maintaining unobstructed egress and ensuring comprehensive worker training on updated procedures have not been fully implemented, creating a continued high-risk environment. The factory is required to submit a corrective action plan with documented evidence of cleared exits and full-shift training completion by 15 November 2024 for verification. **Severity: Critical**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FO-085-24-007**  
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (CBA.01)**  
**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident on 12 March 2024 and the subsequent placement under special monitoring by the local Labour Department, the audit scope included a heightened review of worker-management communication channels and grievance mechanisms. Field inspections and interviews were conducted on 14-16 May 2024 to assess whether the recovery period had impacted established protocols.  

**Observations & Interview Notes:** While management stated that all pre-fire committees remained operational, verification of minutes and election records for the Worker Welfare Committee (WWC) revealed that no formal meetings have been documented since February 2024. In confidential interviews conducted with 23 production workers across two shifts, 18 workers expressed unawareness of current WWC representatives or how to contact them, with several noting that the previously designated notice board for union/committee communications was destroyed in the fire and has not been reinstated. One worker (interview #14) stated, “After the fire, we were told to focus on production; our group leader said not to raise other issues now.” Management confirmed the notice board has not been replaced due to “ongoing repairs,” but could not provide a timeline for its restoration or evidence of alternative, equally effective communication methods for collective worker representation.  

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to maintain effective and accessible channels for collective worker representation during the recovery period, resulting in a de facto suspension of meaningful Freedom of Association mechanisms. This constitutes a failure to ensure the ongoing functioning of a key due diligence process under the applicable standard.  
**Severity: Moderate**  
**Follow-up Required:** Corrective Action Plan must include reinstatement of communication infrastructure, scheduled WWC elections, and documented worker training on association rights by 30 June 2024.


============================================================
Factory 086 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Wages & Benefits (Audit Code: WB)**
During the review of payroll records for the period of January to March 2024, and corroborated through confidential worker interviews conducted on April 15, 2024, with 27 production line operators selected at random, it was observed that the factory's practice for calculating overtime remuneration is not fully aligned with legal requirements. While base wages for regular hours were correctly paid at or above the regional minimum wage, the overtime premium for hours worked on rest days was calculated at a rate of 1.5 times the base hourly rate, rather than the legally mandated rate of no less than 2.0 times. This discrepancy was confirmed by cross-referencing timecard data from four separate production lines with the corresponding payroll ledgers for the week of March 10-16, 2024, affecting an estimated 312 workers during that period. Management acknowledged the calculation methodology but cited a historical interpretation of the relevant provision; however, this constitutes a systematic underpayment. A follow-up note is required to review the corrected calculations for back pay. Severity: Moderate.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
Audit observation for the area of Occupational Safety & Health conducted on 14-16 March 2024, referencing internal audit code OS&H-086-03-2024, identified significant deficiencies in the factory's emergency egress and fire safety protocols within the primary cutting and spreading department (Building A). During a physical inspection, it was noted that three of the five designated fire exits, specifically exits 2, 4, and 5, were partially obstructed by stored rolls of surplus fabric and unused trolleys, reducing the clear width of the egress path to approximately 0.6 meters, which is below the minimum 0.8-meter standard as per the client's Code of Conduct, section 7.3. This condition was corroborated during worker interviews on 15 March, where 8 of 12 workers from that department stated they were unaware of alternate escape routes and confirmed the blockages were a persistent issue. Management was immediately notified for corrective action, and a follow-up review is required within 30 days to verify clearance of all egress pathways and completion of updated evacuation drills. Severity: Moderate.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: WH-2024-086-A)**
A review of time and attendance records for the period of March 1 to April 30, 2024, covering six production lines, indicated a systematic pattern of excessive working hours. Inspector observations on April 10 and 11 confirmed full production lines operating until 21:30, well beyond the legally stipulated daily limit. Interviews with 14 randomly selected workers from the cutting and sewing departments corroborated that, during peak periods, they consistently worked 12-hour shifts, seven days a week, with overtime hours frequently exceeding the monthly legal maximum. This was further evidenced by payroll records for March (sample: Payroll #024), which showed numerous instances of 95-105 overtime hours per worker, against a legal limit of 36. Management acknowledged the practice was due to tight shipment deadlines for a key buyer, but failed to provide evidence of worker consent for this excessive overtime or demonstrate adequate manpower planning to avoid systemic breaches. **Severity: Critical.**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-086-2023-04 | **Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness | **Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

During the physical inspection of the production floors and dormitory block, it was observed that the documented fire drill schedule for Q3 2023, which stipulated a full evacuation drill to be conducted in September, was not executed as planned. Management representatives acknowledged the omission, citing major shipment deadlines as the reason for the postponement. Interviews with 17 workers across different departments and shifts confirmed that no full facility drill has been conducted since the annual drill in March 2023, though a partial departmental drill was held in July for the sewing section only. This represents a failure to maintain the minimum requirement of conducting a full emergency evacuation drill at least once every six months, as per section 7.2 of the Factory's own Emergency Preparedness Plan and Clause 4.5 of the Brand's Code of Conduct. The absence of regular, facility-wide drills potentially compromises the effectiveness of evacuation procedures and staff readiness in the event of a major incident. **Severity: Moderate.** A follow-up review of the drill log and a witnessed drill must be submitted within 90 days to verify closure.

[Child Labor]
During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's hiring records for the seasonal recruitment period of June-August 2023 appeared complete, with copies of national ID cards on file for all 127 workers hired in that period. However, during physical inspection of the finishing department on the morning of 13 October, the audit team observed one individual, subsequently identified as "Mr. A," whose physical appearance and demeanor raised initial concerns regarding potential underage labor. A private interview was conducted with the individual, who stated he was 17 years old and had been working on the button-securing line for approximately eleven weeks; this statement was later corroborated by a confidential interview with two co-workers in the same section. Cross-referencing this information with the provided ID card (copy filed in HR, ref: CL-CHK-086-024) revealed a discrepancy, as the document indicated an age of 19. A subsequent review of the original ID card, requested from the individual's guardian, confirmed the individual's actual date of birth, making him 15 years old at the time of hiring, a direct violation of local labor law and Section 7.1 of the Client's Code of Conduct. The factory management was immediately notified and acknowledged a breakdown in the document verification process for this specific hire. Severity: Critical. Follow-up required: immediate removal of the underage worker, provision of remedial support, and a systemic review of the hiring protocol with corrected documentation to be submitted within 7 days.

Factory 087 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area: 1.03 - Child Labor / Young Workers**
**Reference: Audit Protocol CL-1 through CL-4; Documentation Review DR-2**

During the documentation review conducted on 17 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's employee register, while largely in order, contained several entries for production operators where the listed date of birth would make the individual 17 years of age. Cross-referencing these records with attendance sheets for the week of 9-13 October revealed two such individuals, designated as Operator Assistants in the sewing section, were registered as working full eight-hour shifts. Subsequent physical inspection and confidential worker interviews held on the audit date confirmed the presence of these two workers, who were observed performing bundling and thread-trimming tasks. When interviewed separately, both individuals verbally confirmed being 17 years of age and stated they had been employed for approximately three months, coinciding with the peak seasonal production period. Although verification of authentic government-issued identification was provided post-interview, confirming their ages as stated, the investigation confirmed a violation of the standard's requirement that no person under the age of 18 shall be employed in manufacturing operations. The factory management stated they were unaware of this provision in the client’s code of conduct, attributing the hiring to a sub-contracted labor agent. **Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-087-2023-OSH-04
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (Machine Guarding & Emergency Egress)
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Observations:** During the walk-through of the main production hall, it was noted that the factory operates with a high-density layout to maximize capacity for seasonal peak orders. A significant obstruction to safe egress was identified at the western exit (designated Exit 'C' on the factory's own floor plan), where approximately fifteen large rolling racks containing finished garments were stored, partially blocking the doorway and fully impeding the adjacent fire extinguisher station (ID# FE-07). Furthermore, follow-up inspection of the cutting section revealed that three of the five automatic fabric spreader machines (Models JQ-3400) were operating with the manufacturer-installed safety interlock guards either disengaged or physically tied back with plastic zip-ties to facilitate faster loading cycles. Interviews conducted with seven operators from the sewing and cutting departments on the date of inspection confirmed that this practice of bypassing guards is commonly instructed by line supervisors during high-pressure periods to meet production targets, and that no recent fire drill had been conducted for the current seasonal workforce, estimated at 280 employees. The combination of knowingly compromised machine safety devices and impeded emergency evacuation routes presents a substantial and immediate risk to worker safety.
**Severity: Critical**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding Ref: F087-2023-09-CON/HR-002**
During the document review for the period covering January to September 2023, coupled with worker interviews conducted on-site on 12 September 2023, it was identified that the factory's practices regarding employment contracts are not consistently aligned with local labor law requirements and client Code of Conduct stipulations. Background context notes the factory operates with a significant seasonal workforce, estimated at 40% of the current 1,200 personnel, to manage fluctuating order volumes from multiple brands. Inspector observations confirmed that 58 of the 73 personnel files randomly sampled for newly hired seasonal workers contained only a basic, one-page "Registration Form" which lacked legally mandated clauses on working hours, remuneration details, and termination procedures. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 12 seasonal workers from the cutting and finishing departments, who confirmed they had never signed a formal labor contract and expressed uncertainty regarding their exact wage calculation and notice period entitlements. The failure to issue legally compliant written contracts to all workers constitutes a breach of the fundamental employment relationship and undermines the ability to enforce other labor standards.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: C.01 Wages & Benefits**

During the on-site inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team reviewed payroll records for the periods of July to September 2023 and conducted confidential interviews with 32 production line workers, representing approximately 15% of the current seasonal workforce. A recurring discrepancy was noted between the factory's declared piece-rate system and the actual compensation received. While records indicated compliance with the regional minimum monthly wage (Code MW-07), analysis of time cards and corresponding pay slips for 17 interviewed workers revealed that, during peak production weeks, the effective hourly rate fell below the legal minimum when calculated against clock-in/clock-out data, as workers were compelled to work excessive overtime to meet quotas without commensurate premium pay. This was corroborated by worker interviews, with multiple instances (Ref: WI-087-23-018 through WI-087-23-022) stating, "We must finish the batch no matter how long it takes; the pay is the same as for a normal day if we don't meet the target." The factory management acknowledged the high-pressure production environment from multiple clients but could not provide a reconciled justification for the identified wage gaps. A follow-up review of the factory's wage calculation methodology is required within 30 days.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: EHS-2023-087-04**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness (EP)**
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**

Following a review of the factory's emergency response documentation and a physical inspection of the facility, coupled with interviews conducted with seven (7) production line workers on the aforementioned date, a significant deficiency in EP procedural implementation was identified. While the factory maintains a written evacuation plan, observeration during the scheduled drill simulation at 10:30 AM revealed a systemic failure to execute; egress routes on the third-floor cutting section were partially obstructed by WIP (Work-in-Progress) fabric rolls, and no audible alarm was activated, with foremen instead verbally instructing staff to assemble. Worker interviews corroborated a lack of consistent training, with four of the seven seasonal workers interviewed stating they had never participated in a drill and were unclear on secondary exit locations, particularly in the packaging warehouse. This constitutes a failure to ensure all personnel, especially transient staff under conditions of high production pressure, are adequately prepared for an emergency. **Severity: Moderate** – requires corrective action within 30 days, with a follow-up verification to be scheduled post-remediation.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding: HK-087-24-09 (Harassment & Abuse)**
Background context indicates Factory 087 operates as a subcontractor under significant production pressure for multiple mid-market brands, employing a largely seasonal workforce with high turnover. During the onsite inspection conducted on 19-20 March 2024, the auditor observed an environment characterized by visible tension on the production floor, with supervisory staff frequently using raised voices and derogatory language to expedite workflow. Worker interviews, conducted privately with 17 line operators, revealed a consistent pattern of verbal abuse, with 12 workers providing specific, corroborating accounts of being routinely subjected to humiliating shouts and public belittlement by two identified line leaders for failing to meet daily targets. Management, while acknowledging the pressure, demonstrated a lack of formal grievance mechanisms or recent training to address this behavior. The documented culture of intimidation, which management failed to prevent, constitutes a violation of the client's Code of Conduct and local labor laws pertaining to dignity at work.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: FL-087-04-2023**  
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work**  
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**  

The audit included a review of payroll records for the past six months and confidential interviews with 32 production workers, conducted in a private area without management presence on October 25-26, 2023. Background context indicates Factory 087 operates as a subcontractor with fluctuating order volumes, leading to a highly seasonal workforce. During document verification, it was observed that the factory retains original identification documents (national ID cards) for 147 out of 209 migrant workers in the sewing department, under a stated policy of "safekeeping" to, according to management, "ensure stability." In worker interviews, 9 of the 14 migrant workers spoken to confirmed they cannot retrieve their documents without special permission and expressed fear of dismissal or penalty if they insist. One worker (interview #22) noted, "They keep our IDs so we cannot leave—if we ask, they say we must finish the peak season orders first." This practice, combined with the high production pressure and the workers' perceived inability to freely terminate employment due to document withholding, constitutes a retention of personal documents which undermines the principle of voluntary work.  

**Severity: Critical**  
**Follow-up Note:** Immediate corrective action required; the factory must cease document retention and return all original personal documents to workers without precondition. A follow-up verification must be conducted within 14 days.

Factory 088 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory with history of compliance issues, under corrective action plan from previous audit
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-2024-088-03
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness
**Background & Context:** Factory 088 is under an active Corrective Action Plan (CAP-2023-088-Rev.2) following the Q3 2023 audit, which identified systemic failures in emergency response planning. Previous non-conformities EP-2023-088-01 and EP-2023-088-02 specifically required the implementation of fully documented and practiced floor-level evacuation procedures for all shifts.

**Inspector Observations & Worker Interviews:** During the site inspection on April 17, 2024, it was noted that while primary exit routes were generally unobstructed, the newly posted evacuation diagrams on the second and third-floor sewing halls did not reflect the current layout following the January machinery reconfiguration. Furthermore, a review of drill records indicated the last full evacuation drill was conducted nine months ago, on July 25, 2023, contravening the CAP requirement for quarterly drills. Interviews with 7 workers from the cutting and finishing departments revealed inconsistent understanding of secondary exit routes and assembly point procedures for their immediate work areas, with 5 of the 7 workers stating they had never participated in a drill for their current shift (night shift).

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to implement and maintain the corrective actions mandated in the previous CAP. The lack of current evacuation diagrams, inadequate drill frequency, and insufficient worker training on updated procedures demonstrate a continued breakdown in the Emergency Preparedness management system, exposing a significant portion of the workforce to undue risk.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Note:** This finding constitutes a failure to meet CAP milestones and requires an immediate revised corrective action plan to be submitted within 5 business days.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FO-088-2023-04**  
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**  
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**  

Review of the factory’s historical corrective action plan (CAP Ref: 2022-09-A3) indicated a prior commitment to rectify previous violations related to the obstruction of worker representative elections. During the current assessment, it was observed that, while a Worker Representative Committee (WRC) is formally constituted, the operational mechanisms for collective bargaining remain deficient. Interviews conducted with 12 production workers from various departments on October 25-26, 2023, revealed that four members of the elected WRC had been subjected to reassignment to isolated workstations and had experienced undue schedule adjustments following their attempts to raise grievances regarding overtime compensation in September 2023, creating a perceptible climate of intimidation. Management failed to provide documented evidence of good-faith bargaining or minutes from any meeting where the WRC was able to independently present worker demands over the last quarter. This constitutes a failure to implement the previously agreed CAP and a continued infringement of the right to collective bargaining in practice.  
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: FA-088-2023-04-WB.01**
During the verification of wage and benefits compliance, it was noted that the factory, which remains under the corrective action plan (CAP) from the Q3 2022 audit for overtime payment discrepancies, continues to demonstrate systemic shortcomings in this area. A review of payroll records for the period of March 1-31, 2023, against production hours and time cards revealed that piece-rate workers in the finishing department were not compensated at the legally mandated premium rate for hours worked in excess of 40 per week, with the shortfall calculated at the standard hourly rate instead. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 7 of the 22 workers from that department on April 18, 2023, who indicated this has been a recurring practice, and a sample calculation for one worker showed a deficit of approximately 12 hours of premium pay for the month. Given the willful nature of this violation, its recurrence under an active CAP, and the financial impact on workers, this constitutes a failure to uphold a fundamental aspect of the code of conduct.
**Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SAI-BE04.01 / HAR-2023-088-02
**Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse (Corrective Action Plan Tracking Item #7 from Q4 2022 Full Audit)
**Background & Context:** Following the previous audit (Report #FA-2022-088, dated 15 Dec 2022), which identified systemic failures in formal harassment reporting mechanisms and supervisory training, Factory 088 was placed under a Corrective Action Plan (CAP) requiring the implementation of a revised policy, manager training, and the establishment of a functional and trusted reporting committee by 31 March 2023. This follow-up assessment specifically reviewed the efficacy and practical application of these new protocols.
**Observations & Interviews:** While documentation review confirmed the revised policy was posted and committee members (2 management, 2 worker representatives) were formally appointed, confidential interviews conducted on 17 October 2023 with 14 workers from the sewing and finishing sections revealed a persistent climate of intimidation. Multiple workers indicated that while they were aware of the committee, they lacked confidence in its confidentiality, citing that one worker representative is the cousin of a line supervisor. Furthermore, 3 separate interviewees described incidents in September 2023 where supervisors used demeaning language and threw garment pieces near workers to express dissatisfaction with production speed, constituting verbal abuse and creating an environment of fear, which was not reported through the new system due to fear of reprisal.
**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to demonstrate the effective implementation of its CAP, as the newly established structures are not trusted or utilized by workers, and credible allegations of ongoing verbal abuse were substantiated. The procedural remedy has not addressed the behavioral and cultural non-compliance, leaving workers without effective remedy.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref:** D-088-04-2023
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (Freedom of Association & Harassment)
**Background & Context:** Factory 088's corrective action plan (CAP) from the Q3 2022 audit, item CAP-REF-07, required implementation of non-discrimination training for all management and supervisory staff, with a focus on creating an environment free from retaliation. During the current audit, a review of training records indicated the required sessions were conducted on 12 February 2023, with attendance sheets for 28 supervisory personnel on file.
**Observations & Interviews:** However, during confidential worker interviews conducted on 18-19 April 2023, three out of the sixteen sewing operators interviewed separately reported consistent patterns of differential treatment. They stated that since inquiring about the formation of a worker welfare committee in late March, they have been systematically assigned to less desirable machines, denied overtime opportunities despite expressed willingness, and subjected to derogatory comments from line supervisors implying they are "troublemakers." This pattern was corroborated by a review of overtime allocation logs for March-April 2023, which showed a zero allocation for the named individuals during periods of high-volume orders, while their immediate peers received substantial overtime.
**Conclusion:** This constitutes clear evidence of retaliation and discrimination based on workers' interest in collective representation, a direct violation of the established code of conduct and a failure to effectively implement the previous CAP. The finding indicates a systemic management failure to foster a culture of non-retaliation.
**Severity: Moderate**

Factory 089 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Finding Ref: FO-089-024**  
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Section 12.0 of Code of Conduct)**  

During the documentation review and worker interviews conducted on 19-20 March 2024, it was established that, while there is no active union present, management has not formally communicated to the workforce—comprised predominantly of approximately 150 young, recently hired operators—any policy or procedure guaranteeing their right to freedom of association and collective bargaining. Inspection of the employee handbook and new hire orientation materials (v.1.2) found no explicit clauses affirming these rights, a notable omission given the factory's nascent compliance framework. Interviews with 17 randomly selected workers from the cutting and sewing departments revealed a universal lack of awareness regarding their rights to organize or to collectively bargain, with several expressing uncertainty about whether forming a worker committee would be permitted. This failure to establish and communicate a fundamental policy creates a risk of future infringement and does not meet the baseline requirement for demonstrating commitment to this principle. **Severity: Moderate**. Follow-up required: Management must draft, approve, and disseminate a clear policy within 30 days, complemented by training sessions for all staff.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
Upon review of time and attendance records for the period of January 1 to March 15, 2024, alongside payroll documentation and worker interviews, it was observed that the factory's management of working hours is inconsistent with both local legal requirements and client code of conduct standards. Inspector observations of posted work schedules and physical gate records indicated a regular six-day workweek, however, cross-referencing with piecerate payroll calculations revealed that significant overtime was not being systematically recorded or compensated at the legally mandated premium rate, suggesting a parallel, informal recordkeeping system. Interviews conducted on March 18 with a sample of 12 production workers from the sewing section confirmed that while daily hours often extended to 10.5 to 11 hours to meet production targets, particularly in the preceding month for a major order, the overtime beyond the standard 8 hours was frequently compensated at the normal hourly rate, and rest day work was not always voluntary. This constitutes a violation of standard F.089-OT-01 (Overtime Compensation). The failure to accurately record all hours worked and to pay legal premium rates for all overtime hours is a systemic payroll practice that requires immediate corrective action. Severity: Critical.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Code: OSH-04-089**  
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**  
**Area: Occupational Safety & Health - Fire Safety & Emergency Preparedness**  

During the evaluation of the factory's emergency preparedness protocols, it was observed that, while fire extinguishers are present on the production floor, their placement and maintenance are inconsistent. Specifically, of the twelve extinguishers inspected in the cutting and sewing sections, seven had inspection tags that were not updated within the last twelve months, and three were partially obstructed by rolled fabric or trolleys, contrary to the required clear access. Interviews conducted with eight production workers on the date of inspection revealed a general lack of awareness of evacuation procedures; five workers were unsure of the location of the nearest emergency exit from their workstations, and none had participated in a full factory evacuation drill since the facility commenced operations six months ago. The management representative acknowledged that formal emergency response training and documented drills have not yet been implemented, citing the newness of the operation. This constitutes a failure to establish and maintain an effective emergency action plan, exposing the young workforce to significant risk in the event of a fire or other emergency. **Severity: Moderate**
