Factory 090 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: small family-owned factory with 50-100 workers, limited HR capacity, first time being audited
============================================================

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: OSH-2023-090-04**
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)**
**Date of Audit Inspection: October 26, 2023**

During the facility walkthrough and review of safety protocols, it was observed that while the factory has made a basic effort to provide a generally clean working environment, there are significant deficiencies in the formalization of its Occupational Safety and Health program. Notably, the factory does not have a designated, trained safety officer, and there is no documented OSH policy or written risk assessments for the operational hazards present, particularly for the cutting and pressing sections. Interview notes from 8 workers across different departments on October 26 indicated that emergency evacuation drills have never been conducted, and 5 of those interviewed were unclear on the location of the nearest fire extinguisher or assembly point. Furthermore, measurements taken in the packing area revealed that two of the three primary aisles were obstructed by WIP (work-in-progress) material, reducing the clear width to approximately 0.6 meters, which is below the 1-meter minimum required for safe egress as per the client’s code of conduct. The management representative acknowledged these gaps, attributing them to limited administrative capacity and a lack of prior audit experience. **Follow-up Required:** The factory must develop a comprehensive OSH management system, including appointed personnel, documented procedures, and regular worker training. Severity: Moderate

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the review of payroll records for the period of January through March 2024, coupled with timecard analysis and worker interviews conducted on 12 April 2024, it was identified that the factory's operational demands routinely exceed the statutory limits for working hours. Inspector observations confirmed that production line workers were consistently present for 12-hour shifts, six days per week, a schedule reflected in the reviewed time and attendance logs. Interviews with 8 out of 15 randomly selected production workers corroborated this pattern, with several noting this has been the standard schedule for peak season orders. While overtime premiums were correctly calculated and paid, the total weekly hours frequently reached 72, significantly breaching the maximum 60-hour limit as stipulated in section 7.2 of the Client Code of Conduct (Ref: COC-OVT-01). The management, demonstrating limited formal HR oversight, acknowledged the issue but cited fluctuating order volumes and a lack of skilled temporary labor as contributing factors, indicating a systemic reliance on excessive overtime. A follow-up note is required to review revised production planning and capacity management procedures. **Severity: Critical**.

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference:** EP-003-24
**Compliance Area:** Emergency Preparedness (A.7.2 Fire Safety & Emergency Procedures)
**Background & Inspection Context:** The audit scope included a review of the factory's emergency response plans, training records, and physical preparedness. As a first-time auditee with a workforce of approximately 75 personnel, initial documentation indicated a basic firefighting equipment inventory was present. On-site verification was conducted on March 12, 2024, including a review of records, a physical walkthrough of all production and storage areas, and confidential interviews with 8 workers from various departments.

**Observations & Evidence:** While several fire extinguishers were noted as present and serviced within the last 12 months, the factory's emergency preparedness program was found to be largely underdeveloped and undocumented. Inspector observation confirmed that exit routes, particularly in the woven storage section, were partially obstructed by semi-permanent inventory racks, reducing the clear egress width to approximately 28 inches in a key corridor. Furthermore, worker interviews revealed that no organized emergency drill (e.g., fire, earthquake) has been conducted in the past 24 months; 7 of 8 interviewed workers stated they were unaware of designated assembly points or had received any formal emergency procedure training. The single available evacuation floor plan posted in the office was outdated and did not reflect the current layout of the production floor.

**Conclusion & Follow-up Requirement:** The absence of a documented emergency action plan, combined with the lack of employee training and drills, and the obstruction of egress routes, demonstrates a systemic failure in establishing a minimum level of preparedness for worker safety in the event of an emergency. The factory management was verbally advised to immediately clear all exit pathways and must submit a corrective action plan addressing the development of procedures, training, and drill scheduling.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Reference:** HR-04-2023-090
**Compliance Area:** Contracts & Human Resources
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Finding:** The audit team reviewed the personnel files for a sample of 30 workers, representing approximately 40% of the workforce, and conducted confidential interviews with 12 employees on the production floor. It was observed that while the factory has a template employment contract, its implementation is inconsistent. Specifically, 9 out of the 30 files reviewed (30%) contained signed contracts; however, these documents were incomplete, lacking essential terms such as a clear job description, detailed wage calculation methodology, and specified working hours. Interview notes from workers, including Employee #7 and #11, indicated they had never received a copy of their signed contract for personal retention, confirming the procedural failure. This deficiency is attributed to the factory's limited HR capacity and represents a systemic failure to establish transparent and legally binding terms of employment, creating significant risk for labor disputes. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Ref: CL-2023-090-01**
**Compliance Area: Prohibition of Child Labor (CL)**
**Date of Investigation: October 26, 2023**

During the review of personnel files and payroll records for a sample of 30 production workers, it was noted that employee ID #047, presented as being 18 years of age, had a recorded date of birth in the factory's register which would indicate a current age of 17. Subsequent physical inspection of the cutting section on October 26, 2023, at approximately 14:30, identified the individual in question actively operating a manual cutting machine. A confidential interview conducted with the worker, in the presence of a worker representative, confirmed the date of birth from their national identity card, verifying their age as 17 years and 4 months. The factory management, when presented with this discrepancy, acknowledged a lapse in their age verification procedures during the hiring process, attributing it to an administrative oversight and their limited dedicated HR function. The employment of a person below the legal minimum age for work in a manufacturing environment constitutes a breach of the fundamental International Labour Organization Convention No. 138. **Severity: Critical**

[Discrimination]
During the document review conducted on 12 October 2023, it was noted that Factory 090's published recruitment policies, while containing a non-discrimination clause, lack explicit protections against discrimination based on gender, age, or ethnicity as required by local law and referenced standard SA8000 Clause 3.1. Interview notes from discussions with 7 production workers on 13 October revealed a consistent pattern, with three female workers over the age of 40 stating they were explicitly told they would not be considered for promoted operator roles, which were consistently filled by younger male workers, with one worker quoting a line supervisor as saying, "that job needs a young man's stamina." This is corroborated by payroll records showing no female workers in the two highest pay grades. The absence of a formal grievance mechanism (Finding HR-04) exacerbates this condition. Severity: Moderate. A corrective action plan must address policy revision, supervisor training, and a review of promotion and pay practices.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref:** FA-090-24-007 | **Compliance Area:** Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (CBA Clause 9.1-9.3) | **Date of On-site Inspection:** October 26, 2023
**Background & Context:** The facility, a first-time auditee, operates with a workforce of approximately 78 direct employees. Management stated during the opening meeting that no trade union is present and that all worker communications are handled informally through family relations and direct lines to ownership. Review of provided policy documentation revealed a cursory code of conduct statement on non-discrimination for union membership but no detailed procedures or training records on the right to freedom of association.
**Observations & Interviews:** During walk-through observations, no postings regarding worker rights to associate or contact information for relevant labor authorities were noted on any employee communication boards. In confidential interviews conducted with 14 workers from various departments on October 26, 2023, 9 workers expressed unawareness of their right to form or join a worker representative body, with several stating they feared "causing trouble" if they were to organize. While no active suppression was documented, the absence of proactive communication and enabling mechanisms has created a chilling effect. Management confirmation was received that no process exists for the formation of worker committees or for collective bargaining.
**Follow-up & Conclusion:** This constitutes a failure to actively uphold and facilitate the principles of freedom of association as required, stemming from limited formal HR structures. The factory must develop, document, and communicate a clear policy and implementation procedure, including management training and worker awareness sessions, to ensure an environment where association rights are understood and can be exercised without fear of reprisal.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: SOC-04 / Wages-2024-090-003**
**Compliance Area:** Wages, Benefits & Compensation
**Date of Review:** October 24-25, 2024

**Background & Observations:** In reviewing payroll records for the period covering September 1-30, 2024, and conducting cross-referenced interviews, it was noted that the factory operates on a predominantly piece-rate system for its 78 production operators. While time cards and production tallies were maintained, the audit team identified a discrepancy between the recorded hours of work and the compensation provided for those hours. Specifically, for the week of September 16-22, the reviewed payroll registers for a sample of 12 workers indicated that their total piece-rate earnings, when divided by their documented hours worked, resulted in an effective hourly rate falling below the regional statutory minimum wage of 25.00 CNY per hour. For example, one interviewed sewing operator, whose identity is documented in confidential interview file WI-090-11, reported working 62 hours that week for a gross pay of 1,426 CNY, yielding an average hourly rate of approximately 23.00 CNY.

**Worker Interviews:** During confidential interviews conducted on October 25 with 8 randomly selected production workers, 5 confirmed a lack of understanding regarding how their piece-rate pay was calculated in relation to the minimum hourly wage guarantee, expressing uncertainty about whether their total compensation always met the legal floor for all hours worked. This, combined with the payroll analysis, indicates a systemic failure in the wage calculation and review process, attributable to the limited HR capacity and lack of a formalized wage reconciliation procedure.

**Conclusion:** The factory is not in compliance with local labor law Article XX, which mandates that piece-rate workers must earn at least the equivalent of the minimum wage for all hours worked. This constitutes a violation of a fundamental worker right. A follow-up review of all payroll for the last six months is required to ascertain the full scope and financial remediation needed.
**Severity: Critical**


============================================================
Factory 091 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: well-established factory with strong compliance history, ISO certified, dedicated compliance officer
============================================================

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Area: 3.0 Forced Labor & Human Trafficking (Ref: FLP-08.2024)**
**Background & Scope:** The audit included a review of policies (including the Supplier Code of Conduct Acknowledgment), payroll records for the period of March 1 - April 30, 2024, and worker interviews conducted on April 25, 2024. Factory 091 maintains a documented policy prohibiting forced labor, and its ISO-compliant management systems were noted as generally robust. During the course of the worker interviews, which engaged 37 production line employees selected at random from the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments, a consistent procedural discrepancy was identified regarding worker mobility. **Observations & Evidence:** While all interviewed workers confirmed they retained possession of their personal identification documents and were free to resign, the audit team identified that the factory's "Resignation and Early Release Request Form (F-HR-09)" requires a mandatory 30-day notice period for all workers, without exception. This policy was verified in the 2024 Employee Handbook, Section 4.3. Furthermore, payroll reconciliation for March showed deductions from the final pay of four (4) employees who departed without providing this full notice, constituting a financial penalty for termination of employment. Worker interview notes (WI-14, WI-22) reflected an understanding that failure to work the full notice period "will result in lost wages." **Analysis:** This practice of imposing a compulsory notice period enforced by a financial penalty may unduly restrict workers' ability to leave employment voluntarily, which is inconsistent with the principles of freely chosen employment as defined by local law and international standards, despite the factory's otherwise strong compliance posture. The factory's dedicated compliance officer was informed of this finding at the closing meeting for immediate corrective action. **Severity: Minor**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding EP-2023-09A: Emergency Preparedness & Response Drill Documentation**
During the review of emergency preparedness documentation for the period of January to August 2023, it was noted that while the factory maintains a comprehensive written emergency action plan and has designated assembly areas, there is a discrepancy between the scheduled drill frequency and actual documented execution. The audit team, during the site inspection on September 12, 2023, observed well-marked exit routes and functional alarm systems. However, interviews with a cross-section of 14 production workers from the cutting and sewing departments revealed inconsistent recall of full evacuation procedures; five workers were unsure of the alternate assembly point for their section in the event the primary location was inaccessible. The documented drill log provided by the compliance officer shows only one full facility evacuation drill was conducted in the past eight months, falling short of the client’s Code of Conduct requirement for semi-annual drills. A follow-up with management indicated a drill was planned for Q3 but was postponed due to production deadlines. This constitutes a failure to ensure all workers are adequately trained and familiarized with emergency procedures through regular practice. **Severity: Moderate**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**AUDIT CODE: WH-2023-091-04**
**COMPLIANCE AREA: WORKING HOURS & OVERTIME**
**ASSESSMENT DATE(S): October 23-27, 2023**

While the factory maintains a formal overtime policy aligned with local law and was able to present approved overtime requests for the audit period, verification of time and attendance records for production line operators (Dept. 04, Sewing) for the week of October 16-20, 2023, revealed a systemic discrepancy between officially recorded hours and hours worked. Inspector observations confirmed operations commencing daily at 07:45 for line preparation, 15 minutes prior to the official 08:00 shift start, which was not captured on the digital clock-in system. This was corroborated in interviews with 7 of 15 randomly selected workers from the department, who stated this unpaid preparatory time is standard practice to meet daily production targets. Furthermore, cross-referencing payroll with attendance data indicated that overtime compensation, though paid at the correct rate, was calculated based on the 08:00 clock-in time, not the actual 07:45 start, resulting in a recurring underpayment of 1.25 hours of premium overtime pay per worker per week. This finding represents a breach of both the factory's own policy and legal requirements for accurate payment of all hours worked. **Severity: Moderate**. The Compliance Officer has been notified to conduct a root-cause analysis of the timekeeping system gap and implement corrective measures, including back-pay reconciliation.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Area:** Harassment & Abuse (Ref. SA8000:2014 Sec. 8.1 / Client Code: HR-04)
**Finding Reference:** HR-04-2024-091-01

Following a review of the factory's anti-harassment policy, which was found to be comprehensive and well-documented in line with ISO management system protocols, auditors conducted worker interviews on 14 March 2024. During confidential interviews with 37 production line operators across three departments, three workers from the cutting section separately reported instances of verbal abuse and intimidation by a line supervisor, including shouting, use of derogatory language, and threats of punitive production assignments for perceived slow work. While the factory's formal grievance mechanism is in place, these workers expressed a reluctance to utilize the system due to fears of retaliation, despite the presence of a dedicated compliance officer. This represents a breakdown in the effective implementation of the policy and creates an environment not free from harassment and abuse.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Reference: DISC-2024-091-03**

**Background & Context:** In accordance with standard audit protocol for social compliance, a review of the factory's non-discrimination policies, hiring practices, and workplace culture was conducted. Factory 091 maintains a formal policy against discrimination, which is posted in communal areas and forms part of its documented management system, aligned with its ISO certification. The audit scope for this area included document review, facility walk-through, and confidential worker interviews.

**Observations & Evidence:** During the document review conducted on 12 March 2024, it was noted that personnel files for the 127 production workers hired within the last 18 months showed a demographic composition of 98% female employees. Subsequent confidential interviews with 14 workers from various departments revealed a consistent narrative, with three separate workers from the cutting and finishing sections indicating they were aware of qualified male applicants who were not hired for production operator roles, with one worker quoting a line supervisor as stating, "we need women's hands for this delicate sewing work." Cross-referencing with the factory's own job application logs confirmed that 22 male applicants were recorded as "not suitable" for entry-level production positions during this period without further documented justification.

**Conclusion & Follow-up:** While the factory demonstrates robust procedural frameworks, the evidence indicates a de facto hiring preference based on gender for production line roles, which constitutes a violation of the standard's requirement for equal opportunity in employment. This finding is escalated due to its systemic nature, contradicting the factory's stated policies. The Compliance Officer has been notified to initiate corrective action, including a review of hiring criteria and mandatory training for HR and supervisory staff.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Reference:** OSH-2023-091-04
**Area:** Occupational Safety & Health
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

**Finding Details:** While the factory maintains a generally high standard of OSH management, evidenced by well-documented procedures and a recent internal inspection log reviewed from September 2023, a discrepancy was noted during the physical walk-through of the cutting section. The inspector observed that two of the five fabric cutting machines (Machine IDs C-07 and C-11) lacked the required fixed guarding to prevent operator contact with the blade mechanism during normal operation, a condition that was confirmed as not in accordance with the factory's own documented Lockout-Tagout and Machine Guarding Procedure, version 4.1. Interviews conducted with three operators assigned to that section revealed an awareness of the guarding policy but a stated reliance on "being careful," with one operator noting the guard on C-11 had been removed for adjustment "two weeks ago" and not reinstalled. Although no incidents were reported, this presents a clear lacuna in the enforcement of critical safety controls. The compliance officer was notified immediately, and a corrective action plan was requested.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding HR-04: Inconsistent Contractual Documentation for Seasonal Workers**
During the review of personnel files for the Q3 2024 seasonal hiring cohort (approximately 120 workers hired between 01-July-2024 and 15-August-2024), it was observed that while all permanent staff contracts were fully compliant, a sample review of 23 files for temporary/seasonal workers revealed 7 files (30%) where the signed employment contracts omitted the specific termination date as required by local labor law Article 12(2), listing only the indefinite project duration. Interviews with three affected workers from the cutting department on 14-November-2024 confirmed they understood their employment was temporary but were not provided with a definitive written end date. The factory's otherwise robust HR system failed to extend its rigorous controls to this subset of temporary labor, constituting a procedural inconsistency. **Severity: Minor**


============================================================
Factory 092 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Working Hours & Overtime]
During the review of attendance records and payroll data for the period of January 1 to March 31, 2024, it was observed that the factory's production schedule regularly exceeds statutory daily and weekly limits. Specifically, the records for 12 sampled workers in the sewing section showed an average of 72 hours worked per week across February, with consecutive workdays extending up to 28 days without a day of rest. Worker interviews conducted on April 10, 2024, with 7 randomly selected employees (Ref: WI-092-008 to WI-092-014) substantiated these records, with multiple interviewees confirming management expectations for mandatory overtime and a general lack of formalized overtime approval processes or adequate record-keeping for compensatory time-off, as required by local law and referenced under audit code WH.01.03. While management acknowledges these system gaps, citing the operational challenges of a new facility, the nature and frequency of the exceedances constitute a systemic failure. Severity: Critical. Corrective action required within 30 days, including a revised production planning protocol and verified worker training.

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Audit Finding A-04.03: Employment Contracts & Personnel Files**
During the verification of the Human Resources management system, it was noted that, while personnel files have been initiated for the current workforce of approximately 167 employees, a significant gap exists in the formalization of individual employment contracts. A review of a sample of 15 personnel files selected from sewing, cutting, and finishing departments on 12 March 2024 revealed that only 4 contained a signed, dual-copy employment contract outlining terms of employment, remuneration, and working hours as required by local law. Worker interviews conducted with 8 production operators on the same date corroborated this finding, with 6 interviewees indicating they had never been provided with nor signed a written contract, only receiving verbal terms upon hiring. The factory management acknowledged this systemic deficiency, attributing it to the rapid onboarding processes during the initial operational phase and a lack of established procedural controls. This absence of legally binding contracts exposes both workers and the factory to risk regarding the clarity and enforcement of employment terms. **Severity: Moderate.** A corrective action plan is required to rectify all existing files and implement a controlled onboarding procedure.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Ref: FO-092-01**  
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Section 7.1 of Code of Conduct)**  

During the onsite inspection conducted on 17-19 October 2023, it was noted that, while the factory has a stated policy of non-interference with worker rights to organize, there is no formalized mechanism or documented procedure to educate the predominantly young workforce (approximately 180 employees, with an average age of 23) on their rights under national law regarding association or collective bargaining. In confidential interviews held with 14 production workers across three departments, 12 indicated they were unaware of any legally permitted worker representative structures, and several expressed uncertainty about whether forming or joining a group would be tolerated by management, citing informal warnings from line supervisors against "creating groups that cause trouble." The factory’s internal communication boards and orientation materials reviewed contained no information on this topic. Management acknowledged that this element of the compliance system is still under development but could not provide a timeline for implementation. **Severity: Moderate** — due to the absence of proactive communication and training, creating a risk of suppression of these rights, though no active interference was substantiated. Corrective action required within 90 days, including development of a clear policy, training curriculum, and establishment of a democratically elected worker representative body.

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: 3.6 Harassment & Abuse (Audit Code: HR-08)**
**Background & Context:** During the evaluation of Factory 092's human resources systems, conducted on 12-14 March 2024, it was noted that as a newly established facility with a predominantly young workforce (estimated 70% under age 25), the foundational policies and procedural controls to prevent harassment and abuse in the workplace are still under development. **Observations & Interviews:** A review of the current employee handbook revealed only a single, generic clause prohibiting "misbehavior," with no definitions, examples, or a dedicated, confidential reporting procedure for grievances of this nature. In confidential interviews with 18 workers across various production departments, 5 individuals reported experiences of verbal abuse and intimidation by supervisory staff, citing instances of public humiliation and threats of dismissal for perceived underperformance; these accounts were consistent but could not be corroborated with documented reports, as no such reporting mechanism exists. **Analysis & Follow-up Action:** The absence of a formally communicated and accessible policy, combined with a culture where workers expressed fear of retaliation, creates an environment where harassment and abuse can occur unchecked, failing to meet the requirement for a safe and respectful workplace. The management team acknowledged this gap and has committed to developing a comprehensive policy by Q2 2024.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: EHS-EP-2024-092-03**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2024**

During the facility walkthrough and document review, it was noted that while the factory management has demonstrated initial intent by drafting a basic fire and evacuation plan, the systems for emergency preparedness remain largely undeveloped and unimplemented in practice. Inspector observations on the production floor (Building A, Cutting and Sewing Sections) confirmed that exit routes, while generally unobstructed, were not clearly marked with photoluminescent signage as required, and emergency lighting fixtures were absent in the two main aisles leading to the northern fire escape. Subsequent worker interviews conducted with a sample of 14 employees from various departments on the date of inspection revealed that only 3 had received any form of emergency response briefing, and none had participated in a full evacuation drill since the factory's commencement of operations eight months ago. This constitutes a failure to establish a minimum level of preparedness and worker training, leaving the predominantly young workforce unfamiliar with critical response procedures. **Severity: Moderate**. A follow-up review of the comprehensive emergency action plan and drill records is required within 90 days.

[Child Labor]
**Audit Finding Reference: CL-092-01**
**Compliance Area:** Child Labor Prevention & Young Worker Protection (Section 3.1 of the Supplier Code of Conduct)
**Date of Discovery:** October 26, 2023

During the document review and subsequent worker interviews, a discrepancy was identified concerning the verification of age for production staff. While the factory maintains personnel files for all 167 employees, the audit team’s inspection of a random sample of 30 files revealed that for 5 individuals, the copies of provided identification documents were of poor quality or partially obscured, making definitive confirmation of the date of birth difficult. Interviews conducted with 12 workers on the sewing line on October 26 indicated no visible presence of underage laborers; however, the inadequacy of the age verification documentation constitutes a failure in the due diligence process required to systematically preclude child labor. This represents a weakness in the factory’s newly established control systems. The management was cooperative and immediately initiated a corrective action to re-verify all employee identification documents, a process which is to be completed and evidenced by November 30, 2023.
**Severity: Minor**

[Discrimination]
**Audit Finding Ref: DIS-04-092**
**Compliance Area:** Discrimination (including recruitment, hiring, remuneration, promotion, training, termination, retirement)
**Background & Scope:** The audit included a review of HR policies and records from the period of January to March 2024, alongside management interviews and confidential worker discussions conducted on 14 March 2024. The factory's nascent stage of development is noted, with formal anti-discrimination policies still under development.
**Observations & Evidence:** While no overt systemic discrimination was identified in documented policies, confidential interviews with a cross-section of 18 production workers (approximately 12% of the workforce) revealed a recurring concern. Multiple female workers in the stitching section reported that supervisory positions and opportunities for machine mechanic training are consistently offered to male workers, even when female workers express equivalent interest and seniority. This was corroborated by a review of the current team leader roster, which shows only 2 out of 14 supervisory roles are held by female employees, despite a production line workforce that is approximately 70% female. Management acknowledged a historical preference for male candidates in technical and supervisory roles, citing perceived physical suitability and turnover rates, indicating a need for immediate corrective action and awareness training.
**Follow-Up Note:** The factory is required to develop and implement a formal non-discrimination policy with clear, equitable career path procedures, and provide evidence of such for the next surveillance audit.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference: OSH-092-04**
**Date of Inspection: 2023-10-26**
**Compliance Area: Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)**
**Background & Observations:** During the walkthrough of the production floor on the aforementioned date, it was noted that the factory, due to its recent establishment, is in the process of developing its formal safety management systems. In the cutting section, inspector observation confirmed the absence of functional machine guarding on three (3) of the five (5) operational band knife cutting machines, exposing operators to moving blades. Subsequent worker interviews conducted with a sample of 7 employees from the section on 2023-10-27 revealed a general lack of formal training on lockout/tagout procedures or specific hazard awareness for the equipment they operate daily. While the management representative indicated that personal protective equipment (PPE) is provided, the consistent use of cut-resistant gloves by the relevant operatives was not verified during the inspection period. This represents a failure to establish and maintain effective engineering and administrative controls for a recognized severe physical hazard, posing an immediate and serious risk of laceration or amputation.
**Severity: Critical**

[Forced Labor]
**Audit Finding FL-03: Forced Labor & Involuntary Work**
**Reference:** Section 3.1 of the Supplier Code of Conduct, ILO Forced Labour Convention (No. 29)
**Date(s) of Inspection:** October 26-27, 2023

During the review of payroll and attendance records for the period of September 1 to October 15, 2023, auditors identified a systematic practice concerning worker retention. It was observed that 17 of 42 production line workers interviewed (approximately 40% of the sample group) reported that their original government-issued identification documents (National ID cards) were held by factory management upon hiring, under the stated rationale of "safekeeping and processing." These documents were not returned upon request and workers expressed concern that leaving employment would result in forfeiture of these critical personal documents, effectively restricting their freedom of movement and ability to seek alternative employment. While management demonstrated a lack of awareness that this constitutes a retention of personal documents, a clear violation, the policy creates a climate of coercion and is deemed a severe infringement on worker liberty. **Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: W&B-003**
**Area: Wages & Benefits**
**Date of Discovery: October 26, 2023**

During the review of payroll records for the period of September 1-30, 2023, and subsequent interviews with 14 production workers selected at random, it was found that the factory is not correctly calculating overtime remuneration. The audit team verified that while time-and-attendance records accurately reflected hours worked beyond the standard 48-hour workweek, the overtime hours were compensated at the standard hourly rate, not at the legally mandated premium rate of 150% of the ordinary rate as per national labor law. This discrepancy was confirmed through examination of individual pay slips for three workers (Employee IDs #092-047, #092-089, #092-121) and corroborated during separate, confidential interviews. The management representative acknowledged this was an oversight due to the manual payroll system currently in use and a lack of established verification protocols for wage calculations. A follow-up note is required to review corrected payroll for October and the implementation of a validated calculation tool.
**Severity: Critical**


============================================================
Factory 093 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: factory recovering from recent fire incident, under special monitoring by local authorities
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: SA-2023-10-27-EP-002**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness**
**Date of Inspection: October 27, 2023**

**Background & Context:** Factory 093 is noted as being under special administrative monitoring following a significant fire incident on September 12, 2023, which resulted in a full production shutdown. This audit, conducted as a follow-up to the local authority's investigation, placed particular emphasis on evaluating the restoration and efficacy of emergency preparedness protocols. Observations were conducted across all production floors and warehouse areas, with interviews held with a cross-section of 12 workers from various departments and two members of the factory's internal safety committee.

**Observations & Findings:** Despite the recent incident, our inspection identified a significant lapse in the facility's updated emergency response planning. While newly installed fire suppression equipment was found to be in acceptable condition, the documented "Revised Emergency Evacuation Plan" dated October 5, 2023, has not been fully communicated or practiced. Crucially, four of the twelve workers interviewed (representing the cutting and pressing sections on the second floor) stated they had not received any refresher training on the new assembly points established post-fire. Furthermore, a scheduled emergency drill for the month of October, as mandated by the local monitoring authority, was postponed indefinitely due to production pressures, with no alternate date confirmed. This represents a failure to institutionalize corrective actions following a catastrophic event.

**Follow-Up Note:** The management representative acknowledged the oversight and cited the focus on physical reconstruction and production backlog as contributing factors, though these are not considered acceptable justifications for compromised worker safety protocols. Immediate rectification is required to align with both internal policy F-POL-007 and local regulatory stipulations.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
Following a review of documentation, physical inspection conducted on 12-14 March 2024, and interviews with 7 production operators, it was determined that while management has made commendable progress in reinstalling primary production machinery following the fire incident of January 2024 (Ref: Incident Log #24-011), critical gaps remain in the site's overall occupational health and safety framework. Specifically, auditor observation OSH-093-07 documented that the temporary electrical distribution panels installed to power the rebuilt sewing section lacked both requisite physical guarding to prevent accidental contact and clear labeling for emergency isolation, creating a significant electrocution and fire re-ignition hazard. This finding was corroborated during worker interviews, wherein two operators expressed concern over "exposed wires near the new machines." Despite the factory's status under special monitoring by local authorities, this constitutes a failure to provide a safe working environment as per section 4.1 of the factory's own Safety Policy and international standard ILO C155. A follow-up note has been issued to the Joint Health and Safety Committee (JHSC) for immediate rectification. **Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Finding Ref:** SA-SOC-093-07 | **Compliance Area:** Harassment & Abuse | **Date of Assessment:** October 26, 2023 | **Assessment Method:** Documentation Review, Managerial Interview, and Confidential Worker Interviews.

**Background & Context:** Following the fire incident of August 2023 and the factory's subsequent placement under special monitoring, this assessment aimed to evaluate the working environment and management systems, with particular attention to employee wellbeing and protection mechanisms during the recovery period. The factory management stated that a revised code of conduct, including anti-harassment policies, was disseminated to all personnel in September 2023.

**Observations & Interviews:** While documentation of the policy was present, confidential interviews conducted with 17 production line workers on October 26, 2023, revealed a significant and consistent pattern of verbal abuse and intimidation by two identified line supervisors in the sewing section. Multiple workers separately reported being subjected to frequent public derogatory remarks regarding their speed and competence, with one worker providing a specific account from October 24 where a supervisor threatened termination for any worker discussing wage concerns with peers, stating, "You are all replaceable, especially now." The factory's grievance committee, noted in documentation, was found to be non-operational post-fire, with no meeting minutes or logged cases since July 2023, leaving no functional mechanism for reporting or redress.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to provide a workplace free from harassment and abuse, and the absence of an active grievance procedure constitutes a systemic failure to uphold stated policies and protect worker rights, creating an environment of fear that is exacerbated by the post-crisis instability. This finding is elevated due to the punitive nature of the threats connected to wage discussions, which could constitute retaliation.
**Severity: Moderate** | **Follow-up Required:** Immediate implementation of operational grievance procedure and disciplinary action per policy; mandatory supervisor training to be verified within 30 days.

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Finding Reference: FA-093-FOA-001**
**Compliance Area: Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (FOCB)**
**Date of Assessment: October 26, 2023**

Following the fire incident of August 2023 and the subsequent placement of the facility under enhanced oversight by the local Labor Bureau, the audit team conducted a review of FOCB protocols, including document verification and confidential worker interviews. It was noted that, while the factory management has established a Worker Representative Committee (WRC) as per statutory requirements, the election process for the current term (2023-2024) was expedited and conducted on September 15, 2023, without the dissemination of prior meeting minutes or voter eligibility criteria to the general workforce. Interviews with 14 production line workers from various departments revealed a consistent lack of awareness regarding the WRC's specific members or mandate, with 11 of the 14 interviewees stating they were unaware of any election having taken place. The factory's stated commitment to social dialogue appears contradicted by this procedural opacity, which risks undermining the legitimate function of worker representation, particularly during a critical recovery period. Management is required to provide documented evidence of a transparent, communicated election procedure and undertake an awareness campaign to reaffirm worker rights under this clause.
**Severity: Minor**


============================================================
Factory 094 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: large export-oriented factory with 800+ workers, multiple product lines, experienced management team
============================================================

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Audit Area Ref: C.04 - Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining**
**Document Review & Interview Dates: October 24-26, 2023**

While factory management presented documentation, including a copy of the workplace rules (Section 7.2) affirming workers' right to associate freely and a signed declaration of non-interference, field observations and confidential worker interviews revealed substantive discrepancies. During interviews with 37 production workers across three product lines, 29 reported being unaware of any existing worker representative structure or election process, and 8 workers from the stitching section recounted being informally cautioned by line supervisors in early September that involvement with "outside unions" could negatively affect overtime allocation. Furthermore, a review of the notice boards in the main canteen and changing areas confirmed the absence of any posted information regarding freedom of association rights or contact details for the purported in-house worker committee, which management stated was formed in Q2 2023 but for which no election records or meeting minutes could be produced for verification. This constitutes a failure to effectively communicate and enforce policies, creating a chilling effect that undermines the principle of freely chosen representation.
**Severity: Moderate**
**Follow-up Required: Implementation of a corrective action plan to conduct bona fide elections under neutral oversight, followed by mandatory training for all management staff and visible, accessible worker communication of these rights.**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: 1.4. Harassment & Abuse (CCS Ref: HR-08)**
**Finding:** During the inspection conducted on 23-24 October 2023, the audit team reviewed the factory's policies and procedures, which were found to be formally documented and in alignment with local law and client COC requirements. However, a review of disciplinary records from Q1-Q3 2023 revealed three documented instances of verbal reprimands by line supervisors involving derogatory language directed at subordinates, which the factory classified under general "misconduct" rather than potential harassment. This was corroborated during confidential worker interviews, where 4 of 32 workers interviewed (approximately 12.5%) in the cutting and sewing departments indicated a consistent pattern of being subjected to shouting and humiliating comments by certain supervisory staff when production targets were missed, creating an environment of intimidation; these workers further stated they were unaware of a confidential mechanism to report such behavior without fear of retaliation. The factory's management, while expressing a commitment to a respectful workplace, had not conducted specific training for supervisory staff on identifying and preventing harassment and abuse, nor had it effectively communicated its grievance procedures to the workforce on the shop floor.
**Severity: Moderate.**

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SA-094-OSH-003
**Compliance Area:** Occupational Safety & Health (OSH)
**Date of Inspection:** 22 October 2023
**Background & Observations:** During the walk-through of the cutting and sewing sections on the third production floor, the audit team noted a systematic failure in the control of hazardous energy (lockout/tagout procedures) for the maintenance of industrial sewing machines and cutting machinery. Inspectors observed maintenance technicians performing routine servicing on five separate Juki industrial sewing machines without the application of any physical lockout devices on the machines' electrical disconnect points. The machines' power switches were merely turned to the "off" position, and no tagged warning signs were posted. This condition was verified during the review of the factory's maintenance logs for the week preceding the audit, which showed no documentation of lockout/tagout compliance for 17 recorded servicing events.
**Worker Interviews:** Interviews conducted with three maintenance technicians and two line supervisors on 23 October 2023 confirmed an ad-hoc and informal approach to the procedure. All three technicians stated they rely on verbal confirmation that a machine is off and do not utilize the factory's provided lockout kits, which were found stored in their original packaging in a supply closet. One technician remarked, "We know our machines; if the power is off, it is safe. Using the locks takes too much time during a busy production schedule."
**Analysis & Follow-up:** This constitutes a clear and substantive violation of the factory's own stated OSH policy, section 4.7, and international standard ILO Convention 155, Article 16, exposing workers to the risk of unexpected energization and serious injury. The finding is exacerbated by the fact that management was aware of the procedure yet failed to enforce it, indicating a systemic cultural gap in safety protocol adherence. A corrective action plan (CAP) must be developed and implemented immediately, with verification required within 30 days.
**Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
During the document review on 17 October 2023, it was noted that the factory's hiring records and personnel files for 12 workers in the cutting and finishing sections showed dates of birth indicating they were aged 16. Subsequent physical inspections and worker interviews conducted on 18 October 2023, however, revealed inconsistencies; specifically, interviews with 5 of these individuals (Worker IDs CL-094-087 through CL-094-091) strongly suggested, based on discussed life events and school graduation timelines, that their actual ages are likely below the minimum working age. This discrepancy was confirmed upon cross-referencing with the provided national ID cards, which were determined to be falsified documents not corresponding to official registry records. The management team acknowledged a lapse in their age verification procedures, citing rapid recruitment drives to meet seasonal demand. A corrective action plan must be submitted within 48 hours detailing immediate removal of the affected minors, provisions for their safe repatriation, and enhanced verification protocols. Severity: Critical.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Working Hours & Overtime (Ref: WH-OT-2024-094)**
During the review of payroll records for the period of March 1-31, 2024, and corroborated with timecard data and production reports, auditors identified a systemic pattern of excessive overtime within the stitching department. Production schedules for high-volume orders, particularly for style #ALPHA22, necessitated work hours far in excess of the 60-hour weekly limit stipulated in the client’s Code of Conduct. Interviews conducted on April 12 with a sample of 18 production operatives from lines 7 and 9 revealed consistent reports of working 78-84 hours during the peak weeks of March 10-16 and March 17-23, with incomplete records of rest day compensation. Management acknowledged the pressure to meet shipment deadlines but cited raw material delays as a contributing factor. This constitutes a repeated violation of both local law and client standards regarding maximum working hours. **Severity: Critical.** A corrective action plan must address systemic production planning and capacity management to prevent recurrence.


============================================================
Factory 095 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: subcontractor factory working for multiple brands, high production pressure, seasonal workforce
============================================================

[Discrimination]
**Compliance Area: Discrimination (Ref: SA8000 Sec. 5; Audit Code: DISC-04)**  
During the audit conducted on 17-19 October 2023, it was noted that Factory 095 operates with a highly seasonal workforce, with a current headcount of 427 against a peak season capacity of approximately 720. Background interviews with management revealed a stated policy of non-discrimination, however, review of hiring records from the preceding three months and worker interviews indicated a pattern of preferential hiring. Specifically, observations and interviews with 23 workers from the sewing section revealed that hiring for supervisory and quality control positions is informally channeled through existing team leaders, who predominantly recruit individuals from their own regional or linguistic background. This was corroborated by payroll analysis showing that 19 of 22 supervisory roles are occupied by individuals from a single province, while general assembly workers are sourced from diverse regions. This practice constitutes de facto discrimination in hiring and promotion on the basis of regional origin, creating a two-tier workforce and limiting advancement opportunities for a significant portion of the workforce. A follow-up note requires management to review and formalize recruitment channels with transparent criteria. **Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area: Contracts & Human Resources**
**Audit Code: CHR-03 | Reference: 095/2024/Q3-014**

During the review of personnel files and worker interviews conducted on October 26, 2024, inspectors noted systemic irregularities in the execution of employment contracts, which appear exacerbated by the factory's profile as a subcontractor with high seasonal demand. Of the 32 personnel files reviewed for workers hired within the last six months, 28 were found to contain contracts signed only by the employee, lacking the authorized signature and company chop of Factory 095 management, rendering them legally non-binding from the employer's side. This observation was corroborated during confidential interviews with 14 production line workers, 9 of whom stated they had never received a copy of their signed contract for personal retention, and 5 seasonal workers reported being instructed to sign a blank contract form during their rushed onboarding. The absence of properly executed and distributed contracts fails to fulfill the basic legal requirement of establishing clear terms of employment and presents a significant risk regarding the denial of agreed-upon wages and benefits, particularly for the transient workforce. **Severity: Moderate**

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area: CHILD LABOR (Audit Code: CL-001)**

A review of personnel records for the current production season, alongside targeted inspections of workstations, was conducted on 24 October 2023. During the floor walk at approximately 14:30, the audit team observed an individual, subsequently identified as "Worker A" in Section B (stitching), whose physical appearance and demeanor raised immediate concerns regarding possible underage labor. This was escalated for immediate document verification. Worker A was unable to produce any original identity documentation; the factory management presented a photocopied local ID card (ID #XXXXX) showing a date of birth that would indicate an age of 18. However, during confidential interviews held with 7 workers from the same production line, two separate interviewees disclosed that the individual in question is in fact the younger sibling of a regular operator and was brought in temporarily to assist in meeting a pressing shipment deadline, with one worker estimating the individual's age as "likely 15 or 16." The presented documentation is therefore deemed unreliable, and the factory has failed to establish an effective verification system to prevent the engagement of underage workers, a fundamental failure of due diligence given their subcontractor status and known use of seasonal labor. **Severity: Critical**

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Audit Finding Ref: WH-OT-095-03**
**Compliance Area: Working Hours & Overtime (BSCI Code: 6)**

Based on document review of time and attendance records for the period March 1-31, 2024, direct observation of production floors during unannounced spot checks on April 10 and 11, and confidential interviews conducted with 17 production operators from the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments, it was substantiated that the factory is not in compliance with local legal limits on working hours or the requirements of client Codes of Conduct. The review revealed a systemic pattern of excessive overtime, particularly on the lines producing orders for Brand J and Brand S, with records showing multiple workers exceeding the 60-hour workweek limit in all four reviewed weeks, and several instances of 13 consecutive workdays without a rest day. Worker interviews corroborated this, indicating management pressure to meet shipment deadlines results in mandatory, though often improperly recorded, overtime. It was noted that the factory's status as a subcontractor with a seasonal workforce exacerbates this issue during peak periods. A follow-up verification of payroll against attendance data is required to assess potential wage non-compliance related to these overtime hours.
**Severity: Critical**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Audit Finding Ref: WO95-2023-008**
**Compliance Area: Wages & Benefits (WB)**
**Audit Date(s):** October 23-26, 2023

A review of wage records for the period of September 2023, coupled with worker interviews conducted on October 24, 2023, has identified a systemic failure to compensate workers for all hours worked, in violation of local legal minimum wage ordinances and contractual code of conduct requirements (Ref. Section WB.1.1 and WB.2.3). Inspector observations of timecard data for 12 out of 30 randomly selected production line workers revealed a consistent pattern of clock-in times recorded at 07:45 for a stated shift start of 08:00, with workers confirming during confidential interviews that this 15-minute period is a mandatory daily team briefing and machine preparation time for which they receive no payment. Furthermore, interviews with 8 seasonal workers from the packing department indicated that they were paid a flat piece-rate during the peak season (August-September) that, when calculated against their verified working hours (often exceeding 66 hours per week), resulted in an effective hourly rate approximately 4% below the statutory minimum wage. The factory's payment system lacks the requisite transparency and fails to provide itemized pay slips, preventing workers from verifying the accuracy of overtime premium calculations. The management representative acknowledged the practice of the unpaid pre-shift period, citing "production pressure" as a rationale, but could not provide any legal justification for this wage deduction. **Severity: Moderate** A follow-up verification will be required within 90 days to confirm the issuance of retroactive pay for the unpaid working time and the implementation of a corrected wage calculation and payment procedure.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
**Audit Finding Reference:** SAF-2023-095-04
**Area:** Occupational Safety & Health
**Date of Inspection:** October 26, 2023

During the walk-through of the cutting and sewing sections on the third floor, the audit team observed significant congestion and obstruction of primary egress routes. Specifically, in the eastern wing, finished goods and raw material bundles were stored haphazardly, reducing the clear width of the main corridor to approximately 0.7 meters at multiple points, which is below the minimum 1.1-meter requirement per section 7.5 of the client's Fire Safety Protocol. This condition was corroborated during interviews with four (4) seasonal operators from Line 7, who expressed concerns about safe exit during an emergency, particularly during peak overtime hours when aisles are further congested. Although management cited high production pressure and temporary storage challenges due to concurrent orders for multiple brands, this constitutes a failure to maintain adequate means of egress. A review of the previous audit report (Ref: 095-AUD-2022-11) shows this is a repeat finding from the last inspection cycle, indicating ineffective corrective action. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining]
**Compliance Area: 7.0 – Freedom of Association & Collective Bargaining (Ref: SA8000:2014 Sec. 4, Audit Code: FOI-03)**

**Finding:** During the document review and worker interviews conducted on 11-12 March 2024, it was established that while there is no formal prohibition of worker organization in the company's policies, the operational environment and management practices effectively nullify the practical exercise of this right. Of the 37 production workers interviewed across both shifts, 29 confirmed awareness of the single elected Worker Representative, Mr. Chen (Line 5). However, subsequent interviews with Mr. Chen revealed he has not convened any meeting with workers in the past eight months due to management consistently denying his requests for meeting space and time, citing high-priority shipment deadlines (refer to interview notes WR-095-01). This, combined with the factory's profile as a subcontractor with a predominantly seasonal workforce (estimated at 60% during peak season), creates a climate of instability where workers are dissuaded from collective activity for fear of not having their contracts renewed. The management's failure to provide a functional mechanism for collective dialogue constitutes a violation of the workers' right to freedom of association.

**Severity: Moderate**

[Forced Labor]
**Compliance Area: Forced Labor (Audit Code: FL.01 - FL.04)**
**Finding:** During the onsite inspection conducted on 12-15 March 2024, audit procedures identified a systemic failure in the management of worker identity documents and freedom of movement, constituting a severe risk of forced labor. Background review of factory-provided dormitory logs noted a policy, verbally confirmed by the dormitory warden, requiring all resident workers (approximately 217 of the 340 total workforce) to surrender their national identity cards or passports to factory security "for safekeeping" upon occupancy, with retrieval only permitted upon termination of employment. Interviews with 14 randomly selected production operators from the seasonal cutting and finishing departments corroborated this practice; Worker ID#095-88 stated, "We get our cards back only if we quit, but then we lose our job and our bed. I needed my card for a bank matter last month, but they refused." This retention of personal documents under coercive circumstances, combined with the high production pressure and precarious nature of seasonal work, effectively restricts workers' ability to voluntarily leave employment and is a direct violation of FL.04 (withholding of identity documents). A follow-up note is required for immediate rectification and the implementation of a compliant document handling procedure.
**Severity: Critical**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Compliance Area: HAR-01 Harassment & Abuse** During the onsite verification conducted on 12-14 March 2024, the audit team reviewed the factory's policy documentation (REF: POL-095-03, Rev.2) and conducted confidential interviews with a cross-section of 37 production operators, predominantly from the seasonal workforce engaged for the current peak order cycle. While the written policy was found to be adequate, multiple interview respondents, particularly female operators in the finishing section, described an environment of sustained verbal abuse and intimidation by two identified line supervisors. Specific and consistent allegations included the use of derogatory language, public humiliation for missing production targets, and threats of dismissal or assignment to undesirable tasks for those who raised concerns. The factory management was unaware of these systematic issues, indicating a failure in the implementation of their grievance procedure and a lack of effective channels for workers to report abuse without fear of retaliation. **Severity: Moderate** A follow-up note is required to verify the immediate suspension of the implicated supervisors pending investigation and the rollout of mandatory, effective training for all managerial staff.

[Emergency Preparedness]
During the walkthrough conducted on 23 October 2023, it was observed that the posted emergency evacuation plans at the main production hall and dormitory building were outdated, reflecting a factory layout from the previous year and not accounting for the current seasonal workforce surge which has increased occupancy by approximately 35%. Interviews with 12 randomly selected operators from the cutting and sewing sections revealed that no site-wide emergency drill had been conducted in the past 12 months, with three new hires stating they had received no orientation on assembly points. This lapse in maintaining current documentation and conducting regular drills presents a significant risk, especially given the high-density occupancy and the acknowledged language barriers within the transient workforce, failing to meet the requirements of Section 7.3 of the Workplace Safety Standard. Severity: Moderate. Corrective Action Plan (CAP) reference: EP-095-01.

Factory 096 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: newly established factory with 100-200 workers, young workforce, still building compliance systems
============================================================

[Child Labor]
**Compliance Area: C.01 - Prohibition of Child Labor.** During the document review on [Date of Audit], it was noted that the factory's hiring policy, while present, lacks a detailed procedural annex for age verification, relying solely on national ID card checks. Subsequent physical inspection of the production floor and worker interviews conducted on [Date +1] revealed no visual evidence of underage workers among the approximately 120 personnel observed. However, during confidential interviews with a sample of 15 production workers, one individual, hired through a subcontractor for packaging three months prior, appeared visibly young and, when questioned, provided an inconsistent account of their age and educational background, though they possessed an ID stating they were 18. The factory management, upon being alerted, could not immediately produce verified age documentation for this worker, citing incomplete records from the subcontractor. This represents a failure in the due diligence process to effectively screen and monitor all personnel, creating a risk of non-compliance. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Wages & Benefits]
**Compliance Area: 3.0 Wages & Benefits (Ref: WB-096-2024-01)**
**Background & Observations:** The factory, being newly established, is in the process of formalizing its payroll systems. A review of wage records for the audit period covering March 2024 indicated a basic wage structure that meets the local minimum wage requirement. However, an in-depth cross-check of time and attendance records against payroll registers for the weeks of March 11-17 and March 18-24 revealed inconsistencies in the calculation of overtime (OT) premiums. Specifically, for a sample of 27 production line workers, overtime hours worked beyond the standard 48-hour workweek were compensated at the standard hourly rate, rather than at the legally mandated rate of 1.5 times the regular rate, as confirmed during confidential worker interviews conducted on April 10, 2024, with 8 employees from the cutting and sewing departments. This constitutes a failure to adhere to local labor law, Article 65, regarding remuneration for overtime work. The management acknowledged this as a systemic oversight due to the manual calculation process currently in use. **Follow-up Required:** The factory must implement a corrected payroll procedure and provide evidence of retroactive payment for the identified shortfalls. **Severity: Moderate**

[Contracts & Human Resources]
**Compliance Area 3.0: Contracts & Human Resources (Ref: CHR-096-01)**
A review of personnel files and worker interviews conducted on 23 October 2023 indicates fundamental deficiencies in the contractual framework and hiring documentation processes. The inspection of a sample of 15 personnel files for production workers hired between May and August 2023 revealed that only 4 files contained a signed employment contract, with the remaining 11 containing only a basic application form without stipulating terms of employment, remuneration, or working conditions. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with 8 randomly selected workers from the sewing section, 6 of whom confirmed they had never been provided with nor signed a formal contract, stating they were only given verbal briefings on their daily production targets and wage rates. The absence of written contracts for the majority of the sampled workforce constitutes a failure to establish the basic terms of employment as required by local labor law and undermines the transparency of the employer-employee relationship. The management representative acknowledged the gap, citing the rapid scaling of operations and the nascent state of the HR department as contributing factors, and committed to developing a standardized contract template for implementation. **Severity: Moderate.**

[Harassment & Abuse]
**Audit Area:** HARASSMENT & ABUSE (Code: SA.03)
**Reference:** F096-HR-20241027-01
**Date of Inspection:** October 27, 2023

During the review of policies and worker interviews, a significant gap was identified in the operationalization of the factory's stated zero-tolerance policy towards harassment and abuse. While a basic policy document exists in the administrative office, the audit team's observations and interviews with a cross-section of 18 workers from the cutting, sewing, and finishing departments conducted on October 26-27, 2023, revealed a lack of effective implementation. Multiple workers, all of whom were under 25 years of age, reported experiencing or witnessing frequent use of derogatory language and verbal intimidation by certain line supervisors, particularly during peak production periods to meet quotas; however, none were aware of a confidential or trusted mechanism to report such incidents without fear of reprisal. The factory's nascent human resources department has not conducted any formal training on respectful workplace behavior or the harassment policy for management or the workforce since operations began. This constitutes a failure to establish and communicate clear procedures to prevent and address abusive practices, creating an environment where misconduct can go unremediated. **Severity: Moderate** A corrective action plan must detail the establishment of a confidential reporting channel, mandatory training for all personnel, and a documented investigative procedure, with evidence of implementation required for follow-up verification within 90 days.

[Occupational Safety & Health]
During the site inspection conducted on 23 October 2023, it was observed in the cutting section that two industrial fabric cutting machines (serial #s CT-097 and CT-101) lacked functional emergency stop devices, with one device being physically missing and the other non-operational. Worker interviews with four operators from the cutting team revealed a general lack of training on lockout/tagout procedures, with one worker stating, "We just switch it off at the power point if there is a problem, but we were not shown a specific safety procedure." This is compounded by the absence of a documented machine safety procedure (ref. OSH Clause 4.3.1) and represents a significant immediate risk to personnel. The factory's nascent safety management system has not yet adequately addressed basic machine guarding requirements, necessitating immediate corrective action.
Severity: Critical

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding Reference: EP-096-04**
**Compliance Area: Emergency Preparedness (A.7.01)**
**Date of Inspection: October 26, 2023**

During the walk-through assessment of the facility’s emergency preparedness protocols, it was noted that, while the factory has installed a basic grid of fire extinguishers and illuminated exit signs, the overall emergency response system remains underdeveloped and inadequately practiced. A review of documentation revealed that the required biannual comprehensive emergency drills, inclusive of full evacuation procedures, have not been conducted since the factory’s operational commencement eight months ago; the only drill logged was a partial tabletop exercise held in March 2023. This was corroborated during confidential interviews with a sample of 17 production workers from various departments, the majority of whom expressed uncertainty regarding the location of secondary exits or assembly points, with several newer hires unaware of any formal alarm signal. The absence of a documented and practiced emergency action plan, coupled with the workforce's demonstrated lack of procedural familiarity, constitutes a significant failure in establishing a state of readiness for potential incidents. **Severity: Moderate** - Immediate corrective action required to implement and drill a full emergency evacuation plan within 30 days, with follow-up verification scheduled for Q1 2024.

[Working Hours & Overtime]
**Compliance Area 4.0: Working Hours & Overtime (Code: WH-01)**
During the review of attendance and payroll records for the period of March 1-31, 2024, it was observed that the factory's production schedule regularly exceeded the legal and code limits. Inspector analysis of six randomly selected worker timecards from the cutting and sewing departments indicated an average workweek of 72 hours, with consecutive workdays extending up to 14 days without the required day of rest. This pattern was corroborated in interviews conducted on April 10, 2024, with 8 of 15 production workers, who stated that mandatory overtime is common to meet shipment deadlines and that refusals are informally discouraged. While management acknowledges the nascent state of their planning and capacity building systems, the current practices represent a systemic failure to control working hours. A review of overtime compensation calculations confirmed proper payment at the correct premium rate, however, the frequency and duration of overtime work are excessive and not managed appropriately. **Severity: Critical.**

Factory 097 - Audit Report
Factory Profile: small family-owned factory with 50-100 workers, limited HR capacity, first time being audited
============================================================

[Emergency Preparedness]
**Audit Finding EP-2023-097-04: Emergency Preparedness & Fire Safety**

**Background & Scope:** The audit scope for emergency preparedness at the facility included a review of documented procedures, visual inspection of fire safety equipment and egress routes, and verification of worker training and awareness. The inspection was conducted on October 26, 2023, and included interviews with seven production workers, one supervisor, and the factory owner.

**Observations & Evidence:** While a basic fire extinguisher was noted in the main office, the primary production hall, which houses approximately 85 workers and significant fabric inventory, was found to have no accessible fire extinguishers. Furthermore, the designated primary emergency exit at the rear of the building was obstructed by a permanent storage rack containing bulk rolls of polyester fabric, effectively blocking the exit path. During worker interviews, four of the seven workers stated they had never participated in an emergency drill and were unsure of the location of the nearest assembly point. The owner confirmed that no formal emergency drill had been conducted in the past 12 months, citing operational disruptions.

**Conclusion:** The factory has failed to establish and maintain basic minimum requirements for emergency preparedness. The obstruction of a primary exit constitutes a serious life safety hazard, and the lack of firefighting equipment and worker training further exacerbates the risk. A follow-up review is required within 30 days to verify corrective actions.
**Severity: Critical**
